Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
The Impossibility of Infinite Temporal Regress
Sanusi refutes infinite temporal regress using the concept of completion of number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite past is logically impossible according to Sanusi's argument about the completion of an infinite number.

Core Idea
An infinite completed series of past events is a logical contradiction.

This lesson examines Sanusi's refutation of an infinite temporal regress (tasalsul). He argues that supposing an endless chain of past events leads to an impossibility: having an infinite number of events that have been completed. Understanding this argument is crucial for establishing that the world must have a beginning, which points to the necessity of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise, logical style of Sanusi.
The passage contains two distinct arguments.
2
Identify the two arguments
Look for where the first argument ends and the second begins. The first uses the concept of 'completion of number' (faragh al-'adad); the second mentions eternal existence (al-wujud al-azali).
The second argument is incomplete in the text.
3
Understand 'completion of number'
Sanusi argues that 'completion of number' requires both ends of a series to terminate. An infinite series has no first term, so it cannot be completed. Therefore, an infinite past is impossible.
This is the core logical move.
4
Reflect on the second argument
Consider why an infinite regress would force eternal existence to coexist with non-existence. Even though the text cuts off, think about the impossibility of an actual infinite.
The argument reinforces the first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلانُ الدَّورِ والتسلسل . وتقديرها حوادث لا مبدأ لها يؤدي إلى فراغ ما لا نهاية له عدداً قبل ما وُجِدَ منها الآنَ ، لكن فراغ العددِ يستلزم انتهاءَ طرفيهِ ، ففراغ ما لا نهاية لهُ مِنْ عدد الحوادث محال ، فما توقَّفَ عليهِ مِنْ وجودِ الحوادثِ الآن يجبُ أنْ يكون محالاً ، فيلزم أن تكونَ عدماً مع تحقق وجودها . وأيضاً : يلزم على وجودِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها أنْ يقارنَ الوجود الأزليَّ عدمُهُ وأن يستحيل عندَ تطبيق ما فرغ منها بدون زيادة على
The refutation of circularity and infinite regress. And supposing them to be events with no beginning leads to the completion of an infinite number before what exists of them now, but the completion of number necessitates that both its ends terminate, so the completion of an infinite number of events is impossible. So that on which the existence of events now depends must be impossible, and thus they would be non-existent despite the certainty of their existence. And also: it follows from the existence of events with no first that the eternal existence would be accompanied by its non-existence, and that it would be impossible when matching what has been completed of them without increase...
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage refutes the possibility of an infinite past (tasalsul). The author argues: if events had no beginning, then before the present event there would have been an infinite number of past events that have been completed. But 'completion of number' (faragh al-'adad) requires that both ends of the series terminate—i.e., the series must have a first and last term. Therefore, completing an infinite number is impossible. Thus, the supposition that the existence of current events depends on an infinite past is false; it would imply the events are non-existent while they are actual. A second argument: if events have no first, then eternal existence (al-wujud al-azali) would be accompanied by non-existence (since the eternal would have to coexist with a non-first event? The text cuts off, but the idea is that an infinite regress entails paradoxical accompaniment of existence and non-existence).

Key Terms
دور
تسلسل
حوادث
فراغ العدد
أزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think an infinite past is possible because time can extend backward without end.
Correction: Sanusi shows that an actually infinite number of past events would have to be completed up to the present, but an infinite series cannot be completed because it lacks a first term.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue results from supposing events with no beginning?
A) The events would be eternal
B) The number of past events would have no completion
C) The completion of an infinite number is impossible
D) The world must have a beginning

Key Takeaway
Sanusi argues that an infinite past is impossible because it would require the completion of an infinite number, which is logically contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are certain that you exist right now, and that your existence is caused by events before you. Is it possible that this chain of causes goes back forever without a first cause? How does Sanusi's argument confront you with the necessity of a Creator?
?
If an infinite past is impossible, then time had a beginning. What does that mean for your own life? Are you living as if your time is limited and accountable, or as if you have an infinite future to sort things out?
?
The argument forces you to choose: either the universe is absurd (an infinite past is contradictory) or there is a First Cause. Have you sincerely confronted this choice, or do you avoid it by assuming time stretches back forever? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed time had a beginning?

Causality and Created Things

Applying the Proof of Mutual Hindrance to Human Actions
Sanusi uses the proof of mutual hindrance to argue that God alone creates human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof of mutual hindrance is used to establish that God alone creates all human actions, and why human powers are affirmed as concurrent but not causal.

Core Idea
The proof against multiple gods also proves God creates every human action.

Sanusi now extends the same logical proof he used against multiple gods to the realm of human actions. He argues that just as only one Creator can exist, that same Creator is the sole efficient cause of all actions, including our own. He then addresses a natural question: if God creates everything, why do we experience a difference between voluntary and involuntary movements? The passage provides the answer with a clear distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Sanusi connects this argument back to the proof of mutual hindrance.
The phrase 'بِهذا الدليل بعينه' signals continuity from the previous discussion.
2
Identify the two key claims
The passage makes two main points: (1) God is the creator of all human actions, with no effect from human powers; (2) Human powers exist concurrently, proven by the distinction between involuntary and voluntary movement.
These are not separate arguments; the second supports the first.
3
Understand the distinction between movements
Contrast 'حركة الاضطرار' (involuntary, like shivering) and 'حركة الاختيار' (voluntary, like walking). The passage says we perceive a necessary difference between them, which requires the existence of a power in the human that is concurrent with the action.
This distinction is used to affirm human power as real but non-causal.
4
Reflect on the Ash'ari concept of kasb
Connect this passage to the idea of acquisition (kasb): humans do not create their actions but 'acquire' them while God creates them. The concurrent power is the basis for moral responsibility.
This passage is a classic formulation of the Ash'ari position on free will and predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا الدليل بعينه ـ أعني : دليل التمانع ـ نستدل على أَنَّهُ جلَّ وعلا هو الموجدُ لأفعال العبادِ ، ولا تأثيرَ لقُدَرِهم الحادثة فيها ، بل هي موجودة مقارنة لها
وإِنَّما قلنا بوجودِ قُدَرِ مقارنة ؛ لما نجده مِنَ الفرقِ الضروري بينَ حركة الاضطرار وحركة الاختيار .
And by this very proof — I mean, the proof of mutual hindrance (dalīl al-tamānuʿ) — we argue that He, exalted and majestic, is the creator of the actions of servants, and that their acquired powers (qudaruhum al-ḥāditha) have no effect in them. Rather, they are existent concurrent (muqārina) with them. And indeed we have said about the existence of concurrent powers due to what we find of the necessary distinction between involuntary movement and voluntary movement.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage applies the proof of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) to a different domain: human actions. Sanusi asserts that God alone is the creator of all human actions, and the created human powers (qudar ḥāditha) are not causally effective in producing those actions; they merely coexist (muqārina) with the actions. He then explains why we affirm the existence of such concurrent powers: because we observe a necessary distinction between involuntary movements (like shivering) and voluntary movements (like walking). If there were no powers in humans, we would not experience this distinction. So human powers are real but are not the efficient cause; they are created by God at the moment of action and are concurrent with it, not causing it. This is the classic Ashʿarī position on human acquisition (kasb).

Key Terms
التمانع (al-tamānuʿ)
أفعال العباد (afʿāl al-ʿibād)
القدر الحادثة (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)
المقارنة (al-muqārana)
حركة الاضطرار (ḥarakat al-iḍṭirār)
حركة الاختيار (ḥarakat al-ikhtiyār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of mutual hindrance only applies to the existence of multiple gods and has no bearing on human actions.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly says 'by this very proof' he argues for God's sole creation of human actions, showing the proof's broader application.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi affirm the existence of concurrent powers in humans?
A) Because we see a necessary distinction between forced and voluntary movements.
B) Because humans can create their own actions.
C) Because the Quran says so.
D) Because otherwise humans would have no power at all.

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual hindrance establishes not only God's oneness but also His exclusive role as creator of all human actions, with human powers serving only as non-causal accompaniments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that God alone creates every action you perform, including those you think are your own. Is there a particular action or habit you have been prideful about, as if you achieved it by your own power? How does this lesson strip you of that pride and throw you back on Allah's mercy?
?
If your power is merely concurrent and has no real effect, then every time you choose to obey or disobey, what role does your will actually play? Does this teaching make you more aware of Allah's absolute sovereignty over your choices, or does it tempt you to think 'I am just a puppet'? Answer honestly: what changes in your relationship with Allah when you internalize this?
?
The passage distinguishes involuntary and voluntary movements to affirm that you do have some power—but that power does nothing. When you stand in prayer, or give charity, or control your anger, are you acting as if your effort is what earns you reward, or are you acting as if Allah is the only real agent? What would it look like for you to live today in constant awareness that your actions are created by Allah and your power is just a gift?

Kasb: The Foundation of Moral Responsibility
Sanusi defines kasb and refutes fatalism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī doctrine of kasb (acquisition) and how it refutes fatalism (Jabr) while preserving human responsibility.

Core Idea
Kasb is concurrent power without effect – the basis for reward and punishment.

Sanusi introduces the term kasb, which expresses how divine power and human power relate to actions. This concept is crucial because it distinguishes the Sunni position from both fatalism and libertarian free will. Let us unpack its definition and why denying it leads to innovation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of kasb
Start by reading the Arabic passage for the definition. Notice that kasb is described as the relation of the created power to the object of power 'concurrently without effect'.
The key is 'min ghayr ta'thir' – without any causal influence.
2
Understand the role of kasb
Kasb is the locus of religious obligation (taklif) and a marker for reward and punishment. This means human actions are not meaningless; they carry moral weight.
3
Identify the refutation of Jabr
Sanusi immediately nullifies the doctrine of Jabr, which denies any human power. Note the two reasons given: (1) denial of the necessary difference between voluntary and involuntary, (2) annulment of the basis of obligation.
Jabr is thus called an innovation (bid'a).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ الكلام على الكسب ] وعن تعلُّق هذه القدرة الحادثة بالمقدور مقارنةً لهُ مِنْ غير تأثير .. عبَّرَ أهل السنَّةِ رضي الله عنهم بالكسب ؛ وهو مُتعلَّقُ التكليف الشرعي ، وأمارة على الثواب والعقاب . فبطل إذاً مذهب الجبر ؛ وهو إنكار القدرة الحادثة ؛ لما فِيهِ مِنْ جَحْدِ الضرورة ، وإبطالِ محلّ التكليف ، وأمارة الثواب والعقاب ، ومِنْ هنا كانَ بدعة . ومذهب القدريَّةِ ؛ وهو كونُ العبدِ يخترعُ أفعاله على وَفْقِ مرادِهِ بالقدرة التي خلق الله تعالى لهُ ؛ لما علمْتَ مِنْ دليل الوحدانية ، واستحالة شريك معَ اللهِ تعالى أيّاً كانَ . ويلزمُ فيهِ أيضاً إحالةُ ما عُلِمَ إمكانُهُ ؛ إذ الأفعالُ يصح تعلُّقُ القدرةِ القديمةِ بها قبلَ تعلُّق القدرة الحادثة ، فلو منَعَتها القدرة الحادثةُ للزِمَ مَا ذُكِرَ وترجيحُ المرجوح . قالوا : لم يزل يقدر عليها ؛ بأنْ يَسلُبَ القدرة الحادثة . قلنا : فقد لزمَ إذاً ألا يقدر عليها مع وجودِ القدرةِ الحادثة ! وأيضاً : مِنْ أصلِكُم وجوب مراعاة الأصلح ، ولا يمكن سلبها عندكم بعد التكليف .
[Discussion on Kasb] And regarding the relation of this newly-arrived power to the object of power concurrently without effect... the people of the Sunna (may God be pleased with them) expressed it as 'kasb' (acquisition). It is the locus of religious obligation (taklīf sharʿī) and a marker for reward and punishment. Thus the doctrine of Jabr (compulsion) is nullified; it is the denial of the newly-arrived power, because it involves the denial of the necessary [difference], and the annulment of the locus of obligation and the marker for reward and punishment, and thus it is an innovation (bidʿa). And the doctrine of Qadariyya (free will); it is that the servant invents his actions according to his desire by the power that God created for him; [it is refuted] because of what you know from the proof of unity (tawḥīd) and the impossibility of a partner with God, whoever it may be. And there also follows from it the impossibility of what is known to be possible, because actions can be related to the eternal power before the relation of the newly-arrived power. So if the newly-arrived power prevented them, then what was mentioned would entail and the preferring of the less likely. They said: He (God) has not ceased to have power over them by removing the newly-arrived power. We said: Then it would follow that He is not able over them while the newly-arrived power exists! And also: from your own principle is the obligation to observe what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ), and it is not possible to remove it according to you after obligation.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage covers the core of the Ashʿarī doctrine of kasb (acquisition). The author first defines kasb as the relation of the human's created power to the action, concurrent with the action but without causal effect. Kasb is the basis for religious responsibility and a sign for reward and punishment. Then he refutes two extremes: Jabr (compulsion/fatalism) which denies any human power, thus negating the necessary difference between voluntary and involuntary acts, rendering obligation meaningless and making it an innovation (bidʿa). The other extreme is Qadariyya (free will), which claims that humans invent their own actions independently. This is refuted by the tamānuʿ argument: there can be only one Creator, so humans cannot create their own actions. Additionally, if human power could prevent God's power from acting, then we would have two effective agents, which is impossible. The author then presents an objection from the Qadariyya: God could always act by removing the human power before the action. The answer is that if God removed the human power, then the action would not occur with that power, but the question is about the action occurring with the power present. Also, using the Qadariyya's own principle of al-aṣlaḥ (God must do what is most beneficial for humans), they hold that God cannot remove human power after obligation because that would be unjust. Thus their objection is self-defeating.

Key Terms
الكسب
القدرة الحادثة
التكليف
الجبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the doctrine of Jabr considered a bidʿa?
A) Because it denies the existence of God
B) Because it denies the necessary difference and annuls obligation
C) Because it affirms human power
D) Because it was invented by the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
Kasb is the Sunni middle path: humans have power concurrent with action, but only Allah creates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that your power is concurrent with action but not causal. How does this reshape your understanding of personal responsibility? Identify one area of your life where you have blamed 'fate' for your actions, and commit to a concrete step to reclaim accountability in light of this lesson.
?
Sanusi argues that denying human power annuls the very basis for obligation and reward. Examine your own heart: Are there times you secretly wish your sins were not your fault? How does this lesson challenge that desire? What will you do the next time you catch yourself avoiding responsibility?
?
If kasb truly means that your choices are yours even though Allah creates the act, then every action carries weight. How will you live differently tomorrow knowing that your smallest deed is both from Allah and your own? Write one practical resolution to be more mindful of your thoughts and intentions.

Causality and Created Things

Refuting the Qadariyya: Humans Cannot Create Actions
Sanusi refutes Qadariyya free will and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī refutation of the Qadariyya claim that humans independently invent their own actions.

Core Idea
Only Allah creates; human power cannot prevent divine power.

Having established the middle ground of kasb, Sanusi now takes on the opposite extreme: the Qadariyya claim that humans invent their own actions. He deploys arguments from divine unity (tawḥīd) and the impossibility of two effective agents, and then answers a possible objection using the Qadariyya's own principle of al-aṣlaḥ.

Guided Path
1
Read the Qadariyya position
The Qadariyya hold that the servant invents his actions according to his desire using the power created by Allah. Understand this claim before the refutation.
2
Follow the proof from tawḥīd
Sanusi invokes the known proof of divine unity: there cannot be two creators. If humans create their actions, that would imply a partner with Allah.
This is a direct application of the tamānuʿ argument.
3
Understand the second argument: impossible prevention
Actions can be related to eternal power before the created power. If created power could prevent eternal power, that would entail impossibility and preferring the less likely (tarjīḥ al-marjūḥ).
4
Analyze the objection and response
The Qadariyya respond that God could always act by removing the created power. Sanusi counters that then God would not be able to act while the power exists, and also the principle of al-aṣlaḥ forbids removing power after obligation.
This uses the opponent's own principle against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ الكلام على الكسب ] وعن تعلُّق هذه القدرة الحادثة بالمقدور مقارنةً لهُ مِنْ غير تأثير .. عبَّرَ أهل السنَّةِ رضي الله عنهم بالكسب ؛ وهو مُتعلَّقُ التكليف الشرعي ، وأمارة على الثواب والعقاب . فبطل إذاً مذهب الجبر ؛ وهو إنكار القدرة الحادثة ؛ لما فِيهِ مِنْ جَحْدِ الضرورة ، وإبطالِ محلّ التكليف ، وأمارة الثواب والعقاب ، ومِنْ هنا كانَ بدعة . ومذهب القدريَّةِ ؛ وهو كونُ العبدِ يخترعُ أفعاله على وَفْقِ مرادِهِ بالقدرة التي خلق الله تعالى لهُ ؛ لما علمْتَ مِنْ دليل الوحدانية ، واستحالة شريك معَ اللهِ تعالى أيّاً كانَ . ويلزمُ فيهِ أيضاً إحالةُ ما عُلِمَ إمكانُهُ ؛ إذ الأفعالُ يصح تعلُّقُ القدرةِ القديمةِ بها قبلَ تعلُّق القدرة الحادثة ، فلو منَعَتها القدرة الحادثةُ للزِمَ مَا ذُكِرَ وترجيحُ المرجوح . قالوا : لم يزل يقدر عليها ؛ بأنْ يَسلُبَ القدرة الحادثة . قلنا : فقد لزمَ إذاً ألا يقدر عليها مع وجودِ القدرةِ الحادثة ! وأيضاً : مِنْ أصلِكُم وجوب مراعاة الأصلح ، ولا يمكن سلبها عندكم بعد التكليف .
[Discussion on Kasb] And regarding the relation of this newly-arrived power to the object of power concurrently without effect... the people of the Sunna (may God be pleased with them) expressed it as 'kasb' (acquisition). It is the locus of religious obligation (taklīf sharʿī) and a marker for reward and punishment. Thus the doctrine of Jabr (compulsion) is nullified; it is the denial of the newly-arrived power, because it involves the denial of the necessary [difference], and the annulment of the locus of obligation and the marker for reward and punishment, and thus it is an innovation (bidʿa). And the doctrine of Qadariyya (free will); it is that the servant invents his actions according to his desire by the power that God created for him; [it is refuted] because of what you know from the proof of unity (tawḥīd) and the impossibility of a partner with God, whoever it may be. And there also follows from it the impossibility of what is known to be possible, because actions can be related to the eternal power before the relation of the newly-arrived power. So if the newly-arrived power prevented them, then what was mentioned would entail and the preferring of the less likely. They said: He (God) has not ceased to have power over them by removing the newly-arrived power. We said: Then it would follow that He is not able over them while the newly-arrived power exists! And also: from your own principle is the obligation to observe what is most beneficial (al-aṣlaḥ), and it is not possible to remove it according to you after obligation.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage covers the core of the Ashʿarī doctrine of kasb (acquisition). The author first defines kasb as the relation of the human's created power to the action, concurrent with the action but without causal effect. Kasb is the basis for religious responsibility and a sign for reward and punishment. Then he refutes two extremes: Jabr (compulsion/fatalism) which denies any human power, thus negating the necessary difference between voluntary and involuntary acts, rendering obligation meaningless and making it an innovation (bidʿa). The other extreme is Qadariyya (free will), which claims that humans invent their own actions independently. This is refuted by the tamānuʿ argument: there can be only one Creator, so humans cannot create their own actions. Additionally, if human power could prevent God's power from acting, then we would have two effective agents, which is impossible. The author then presents an objection from the Qadariyya: God could always act by removing the human power before the action. The answer is that if God removed the human power, then the action would not occur with that power, but the question is about the action occurring with the power present. Also, using the Qadariyya's own principle of al-aṣlaḥ (God must do what is most beneficial for humans), they hold that God cannot remove human power after obligation because that would be unjust. Thus their objection is self-defeating.

Key Terms
القدرية
الوحدانية
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first argument Sanusi uses against the Qadariyya?
A) The argument from the necessity of kasb
B) The proof of divine unity (tawḥīd) and impossibility of a partner
C) The argument from al-aṣlaḥ
D) The argument from the impossibility of removing power

Key Takeaway
The Qadariyya error is attributing creative power to humans, which contradicts tawḥīd and leads to logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something praiseworthy, do you instinctively feel 'I did that' or 'Allah enabled me'? Which feeling is more dominant? How can you shift your heart to the second?
?
If your every action is created by Allah, what does that mean for your pride in your achievements? Does this truth make you more humble or does it tempt you to become passive? How will you actively rely on Allah in your next decision?
?
The Qadariyya wanted to protect human responsibility but fell into shirk. Are there areas in your life where you act as if you are the sole creator of your success, forgetting that Allah is the true agent? Will you repent and attribute all power to Him alone?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Refutation of Generated Acts (Tawallud)
Sanusi refutes the doctrine of generated acts (tawallud) by Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the doctrine of tawallud is logically impossible according to Sanusi and how it contradicts established principles of causality.

Core Idea
Created power cannot produce effects outside its own locus.

Sanusi continues his discussion of causality by refuting the Qadariyya's doctrine of tawallud, which claims that human acts can generate effects in other loci. Drawing on the principle that a created power cannot affect its own locus, he shows that tawallud leads to absurdities like acts without agents. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to understand the overall argument.
Pay attention to the strong language against the Qadariyya.
2
Understand the principle
Identify the key premise: the impossibility of a created power affecting its own locus leads to the impossibility of affecting another locus through its object.
This is the backbone of the refutation.
3
Recognize the absurdities
Notice the three absurdities Sanusi lists: an effect between two causes, an act without an agent, and an agent without will or knowledge.
These follow from the Qadariyya's own premises.
4
Consider the additional refutation
Observe that the majority agree that satiety and thirst do not generate from eating and drinking, which further undermines tawallud.
This is a common-sense argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
[ في إبطال القول بالتولُّدِ ]
وإذا عرفت استحالة تأثير القدرة الحادثة في محلها
بطل لذلك أيضاً تأثيرها بواسطة مقدورها في غير محلها : كرمي الحجرِ والضرب بالسيف ونحو ذلكَ ممَّا يُوجَدُ عادةً بواسطة حركة اليد مثلاً ، وهو المسمَّى بالتولد عند القدرية مجوس هذهِ الأمَّةِ ، مع ما فيهِ على مذهبهم مِنْ وجودِ أثرٍ بينَ مُؤثرين ، ووجودِ فعلٍ مِنْ غيرِ فاعل ، أو فاعل مِنْ غيرِ إرادة ولا علم بالمفعول ، ونحو ذلك مِنَ الاستحالاتِ المذكورة في المطوّلات
واتَّفق الأكثرُ على عدمِ تولَّدِ الشبع والرِّي ونحوهما عنِ الأكل والشرب وشبههما ، وذلك مما ينقضُ أيضاً على القائلين بالتولدِ ، وبالله التوفيق
Chapter. [On refuting the doctrine of tawallud]. When you know the impossibility of the effect of the originated power in its own locus, it is also nullified thereby its effect through its object in a different locus, such as the throwing of a stone and the striking with a sword and the like of that which is usually found by means of the movement of the hand for example. And it is what is called tawallud by the Qadariyya, the Magians of this community, along with what is in it according to their school of an effect between two causes, and an act without an agent, or an agent without will or knowledge of the effect, and the like of those impossibilities mentioned in the extended works. And the majority agreed on the non-generation of satiety and thirst and the like from eating and drinking and their likes, and that also is a refutation against the proponents of tawallud. And by Allah is success.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine of tawallud (generated acts) held by the Qadariyya, whom Sanusi calls the 'Magians of this community'. The argument proceeds as follows: since it is impossible for a created power (qudra ḥāditha) to produce an effect in its own locus (maḥalluhā), it follows that it cannot produce an effect through its object (maqdūr) in a different locus. Examples include throwing a stone or striking with a sword, where the act is usually found by a means such as hand movement. Sanusi then points out the absurdities that follow from their position on their own premises: an effect existing between two causes, an act without an agent, or an agent without will or knowledge of the effect. Finally, he notes that the majority agree that satiety and thirst do not generate from eating and drinking, which also refutes the proponents of tawallud. The explanation follows the argumentative order: first the principle, then examples, then absurdities, then an additional refutation from common agreement.

Key Terms
تولد
قدرة حادثة
محل
مقدور
فاعل
إرادة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the doctrine of tawallud is a rational explanation of causality.
Correction: Sanusi shows it leads to logical absurdities and is refuted by the inability of created power to affect another locus.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the doctrine of tawallud impossible?
A) Because it implies multiple gods.
B) Because it leads to an effect without a cause.
C) Because it suggests a created power can produce an effect outside its own locus.
D) Because it denies divine will.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of tawallud is impossible because a created power cannot produce effects beyond its own locus, leading to logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting the Ray Theory of Vision
Sanusi refutes the ray theory with four arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical flaws in the ray theory of vision and how Sanusi systematically refutes it.

Core Idea
The ray theory of vision collapses under its own absurd consequences.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a common philosophical theory about how vision works — that rays from the eye, passing through illuminated air, enable sight. He does not just reject it; he shows it leads to impossible outcomes. By examining his refutations, we learn the importance of testing theories against observable reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
Understand the theory: rays from the eye connect to illuminated air, like a crystal helping to see objects inside it.
Notice the analogy with crystal.
2
Identify the first refutation
Sanusi argues that if only the ray's width matters, one would only see objects the size of the pupil, which is false.
3
Notice the second refutation
He points out we see equally well in dark and bright air, contradicting the need for illuminated air.
This shows that illumination of air is not the cause.
4
Examine the third refutation
The individual atom (jawhar fard) is not seen even though the ray connects to it, disproving the theory.
This is a counterexample.
5
Reflect on the fourth refutation
A large object appears small at a distance despite full ray contact and facing, showing the theory fails.
Size perception is not determined by ray connection alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : إنَّما ذلكَ لاتصال الشعاع بالهواء وهو مضيء ، فأعانَ على رؤية ما قابلَهُ كالبلور المُعِين بإشراقه على رؤية ما فيه . قلنا : فيلزمُ أَلا يَرى مِنَ الهواء إلا قدر حدقتِهِ ، وأيضاً : فنحن نرى والهواء مظلم ما نراه والهواء مشرقٌ . ومما ينقضُ عليهم : عدم رؤية الجوهر الفردِ معَ اتصال الشعاع بهِ ، ولا ينالُهُ مِنْ ذلكَ وحدَهُ إِلا ما ينالُهُ معَ غيرِهِ ، ورؤية الكبيرِ معَ البعدِ صغيراً معَ اتصال الشعاع والمقابلة لجميعه
They said: That is only because the ray connects to the air and it is luminous, so it assists in seeing what faces it, like a crystal that helps by its brightness to see what is inside it. We said: Then it would follow that one would not see from the air except the size of his pupil, and also: we see in dark air and we see in bright air. And among the refutations against them: the non-seeing of the individual atom (al-jawhar al-fard) despite the ray connecting to it, and it obtains from that alone only what it obtains from it with another, and the seeing of the large object as small at a distance despite the ray connecting and facing all of it.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-response pair in the debate on the mechanism of vision. The opponent claims that rays from the eye, when passing through illuminated air, enable vision, similar to how a crystal's brightness helps see objects within it. The author refutes this first by noting a necessary consequence: if only the ray's width matters, one would only see objects the size of the pupil, which is absurd. Second, he points out that we see equally in dark and bright air, contradicting the need for illuminated air. Third, he raises the counterexample of the individual atom (jawhar fard): since the ray connects to it, it should be seen, but it is not; moreover, the atom alone gives no different result than with others. Fourth, he notes that a large object is seen small at a distance even with full ray connection and facing, showing the theory's failure.

Key Terms
الشعاع
الجوهر الفرد
المقابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ray theory of vision is plausible and consistent with experience.
Correction: Sanusi shows it leads to absurd consequences and contradicts observable facts like seeing in dark air and invisibility of atoms.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, one refutation of the ray theory is:
A) we see in dark air
B) we cannot see atoms
C) the ray is always straight
D) the pupil size determines vision

Key Takeaway
The ray theory of vision is invalid because it leads to logical contradictions and conflicts with observed reality.

?
Sanusi did not merely reject the ray theory; he provided four specific counterexamples. In your spiritual life, do you actively seek evidence that contradicts your comfortable assumptions, or do you avoid it? If you discovered that a cherished practice or belief rested on unsound reasoning, would you have the courage to abandon it? What would that look like for you starting today?

Refutation of the Geometric Angle Explanation
Sanusi refutes the claim that rays form an acute angle to explain seeing large objects small.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the geometric angle explanation for the small appearance of distant objects is invalid, based on logical consequence and observation.

Core Idea
Geometry alone cannot explain vision; observation disproves the angle theory.

Sanusi now tackles a specific scientific objection: that vision works through geometric angles. The opponent claims rays form an acute angle to the object, causing it to appear small. Sanusi shows this leads to an absurd consequence that contradicts direct observation, proving the theory false.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim
Read the first part of the passage where the opponent explains why distant objects appear small: the ray passes through an acute angle of a triangle whose base is the object, standing as a straight line at right angles, which is shorter than other lines, thus the extra distance prevents seeing the ends.
Notice the geometric reasoning: triangle, base, acute angle, right angles.
2
Identify the key geometric detail
The opponent asserts that the central ray is shorter because it forms right angles with the base, while other rays are longer and miss the edges due to increased distance.
This is the crux of their argument: the extra distance blocks the edges.
3
Follow the author's refutation
Read the author's response: if the object moves slightly further by that extra distance, it should not be seen at all, but observation shows it is still visible. This logical consequence contradicts the theory.
The refutation uses a conditional: if the extra distance prevents seeing edges, then moving the object by that distance should make it invisible entirely.
4
Understand the role of observation
The author appeals to 'al-mushāhada' (observation) as the decisive refutation. The geometric explanation fails because it conflicts with what we actually see.
This highlights the importance of empirical evidence in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : إنَّما ذلكَ لأنَّ الشعاعَ نفذَ مِنْ زاويةٍ حادةٍ لمثلث قاعدتُهُ المرئيُّ ، فقامَ خطّاً مستقيماً بوسط القاعدة على زوايا قائمة ، ومعلوم أنَّهُ أصغرُ ممَّا يقومُ عليها مِنْ سائر الخطوطِ فزيادة ذلك البعدِ لغيرِهِ منعَتْ مِنْ رؤية طرفي المرئي . قلنا : فيلزمُ إذا انتقل المرئيُّ إلى مقدارِ تلك الزيادةِ مِنَ البعد ألا يُرى ، والمشاهدة تكذبه .
They said: That is only because the ray penetrated from an acute angle of a triangle whose base is the seen object, so it stood as a straight line in the middle of the base at right angles, and it is known that it is smaller than the other lines that stand on it, so the increase of that distance for other lines prevented seeing the ends of the seen object. We said: Then it would follow that if the seen object moves to the amount of that increase of distance, it would not be seen, but observation contradicts it.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage continues the debate on vision. The opponent attempts to explain why distant large objects appear small: because the ray passes through an acute angle of a triangle with the object as base, forming a straight line at the center, which is shorter than other lines, so the extra distance prevents seeing the edges. The author refutes this by showing a logical consequence: if the object moves slightly further by that amount, it should disappear entirely, but observation shows it still visible. Hence the explanation is invalid.

Key Terms
الشعاع
الزاوية الحادة
المثلث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The geometric angle theory correctly explains why distant objects appear small.
Correction: Sanusi shows this theory leads to a false prediction: if the object moves slightly further, it should disappear, which does not happen. Therefore the theory is invalid.

Checkpoint

A) It would appear larger
B) It would not be seen
C) It would appear inverted
D) The ray would break

Key Takeaway

Refuting the Ray Theory: Accidents and the Absurdity of Seeing Tastes
Sanusi refutes ray theory using accidents and tastes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the example of accidents and tastes to refute the claim that vision requires a physical ray from the eye to the object.

Core Idea
If vision required ray contact, then tastes and smells would be visible.

Sanusi continues his refutation of the ray theory with a clever dialectical argument. He points out that we see accidents (like motion and rest) without any ray touching them, so ray contact cannot be necessary. Then he turns the opponent's own reasoning against them, showing that it would force absurd conclusions like tastes being visible.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialectical exchange
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the back-and-forth between Sanusi and the opponent.
Pay attention to the logical structure: refutation, opponent's response, counter-response.
2
Identify the key refutation
Sanusi first refutes by pointing out that we see accidents (akwan) without rays touching them. This directly challenges the necessity of ray contact.
Accidents are non-color attributes like motion and rest.
3
Trace the reductio ad absurdum
When the opponent defines the visible as what rays touch or inheres in what they touch, Sanusi shows this would make tastes and smells visible, which is false.
This is a classic reductio: if the premise leads to an absurd conclusion, the premise must be false.
4
Examine the final counterexample
The opponent tries to restrict to 'what accepts sight', but Sanusi gives the example of seeing a distant object without its color, undermining color as essential to vision.
This shows that color is not necessary for sight, so the ray theory fails on another front.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما ينقضُ عليهم : رؤية الأكوانِ معَ أَنَّ الأشمَّةَ لم تتصل بها . قالوا : المرئي ما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ ، أو قام بما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ قلنا : فيلزمُ أنْ تُرى الطعوم والروائح ؛ لقيامها بما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ . قالوا : إنَّما ذلك فيما يقبلُ الرؤية . قلنا : ها هو البعيدُ يُرى دونَ لونِهِ
And among the refutations against them: the seeing of accidents (al-akwān) while the rays did not connect to them. They said: The seen is what rays connected to, or what is established in what rays connected to. We said: Then it would follow that tastes and smells would be seen, because they are established in what rays connected to. They said: That is only for what accepts sight. We said: Behold, the distant object is seen without its color.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage raises another refutation: we see accidents (akwan, such as motion and rest) without rays touching them, so ray connection is not necessary. The opponent responds that the seen is either what rays touch or what inheres in what they touch. The author turns this against them: tastes and smells inhere in objects touched by rays, so they would be visible—but they are not. The opponent then restricts vision to things that accept sight. The author gives a counterexample: a distant object is seen without its color, showing color is not essential to vision.

Key Terms
الأكوان
الطعوم
الروائح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision requires the eye to emit a ray that physically contacts the object.
Correction: Seeing accidents and counterexamples show that ray contact is not necessary for vision.

Checkpoint

A) we see tastes and smells
B) we see accidents without rays
C) we see colors only
D) rays cannot penetrate air

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the ray theory by showing that accidents are seen without ray contact and that the theory would absurdly imply the visibility of tastes and smells.

Refuting Emission and Facing in Vision Theory
Sanusi refutes emission and facing conditions with examples and dialectical exchange.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses examples like the sun disc, fire, and mirror to refute the conditions of emission and facing in the kalām theory of vision.

Core Idea
Seeing oneself in a mirror disproves the need for direct facing.

Sanusi continues his criticism of the kalām theory of vision by attacking two key conditions: that vision occurs through emission of rays and that the object must face the eye. He presents several empirical counterexamples and engages in a dialectical exchange with the opponent. This lesson walks through those arguments and their logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the arguments presented.
Notice the back-and-forth structure in the mirror argument.
2
Identify the first refutation
Observe how the examples of the sun disc and fire at a distance disprove the necessary connection of rays.
The sun disc is seen without the birds below it, contradicting emission.
3
Follow the mirror argument
Understand how seeing oneself in a mirror refutes the condition of facing, and analyze the opponent's responses and Sanusi's rebuttals.
The opponent claims reflection; Sanusi shows that implies the mirror itself would not be seen.
4
Consider the final point
Reflect on the logical implication that if facing were required, one could only see objects the size of oneself, which is false.
The opponent's final appeal to rays assisting is left unaddressed here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما ينقض عليهم : رؤيةً قُرْص الشمس مع عدم رؤيةِ ما دونَها مِنَ الطير إذا علا في الجو ، ورؤية النار على البعدِ دون ما دونَها ، وأيضاً : الانبعاث إنَّما يكون عن اعتماد إلى جهة ، والسبر يبطله ثم لزوم المقابلة يَبطُلُ برؤيةِ الإنسانِ نفسَهُ في المرآةِ والماء . قالوا : لم تتشبَّثِ الأشعة فيهما لعدم التضريس ، فانعكست إلى الرائي . قلنا : فيلزم ألا يرى المرآة والماء لعدم قاعدة الأشعة قالوا : إنَّما رأى صورة منطبعةً ، لا نفسَهُ قلنا : فيلزم ألا تبعد ببعدِهِ ، ولا تتحرك بحركتِهِ . وممَّا يلزم على اشتراط المقابلة : ألا يرى الرائي إلا قدْرَ ذاتِهِ ؛ إذ لا يقابل أكبر منها . قالوا : الشعاعُ أعان على ذلك
And among what refutes them: the seeing of the sun's disc without seeing what is below it of birds when it rises in the air, and the seeing of fire at a distance without what is below it, and also: emission only occurs through inclination to a direction, and probing (al-sabr) invalidates it. Then the requirement of facing is invalidated by a person seeing himself in a mirror and water. They said: The rays did not attach in them due to lack of roughness, so they reflected to the observer. We said: Then it would follow that the mirror and water would not be seen due to lack of base of rays. They said: He only saw an impressed image, not himself. We said: Then it would follow that it would not move away with his moving away, nor move with his movement. And from what follows upon requiring facing: that the observer would not see except the size of himself, since nothing larger than him faces him. They said: The ray assists in that.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents multiple refutations of the ray theory. First, we see the sun's disc but not birds below it, and fire at a distance but not what is below it, disproving necessary ray connection. Second, the theory of emission requires an inclination to a direction, but probing (sabr) shows it false. Third, the requirement that the object face the eye is invalidated by seeing oneself in a mirror and water, where the reflection is not directly facing. The opponent claims rays reflect due to smoothness, but the author argues that then the mirror and water themselves would not be seen. The opponent then says it's an impressed image, but the author points out that such an image would not move with the observer. Finally, if facing were required, one could only see objects the size of oneself, which is false. The opponent's final appeal to rays assisting is left unrefuted in this passage.

Key Terms
الانبعاث
المقابلة
الانعكاس
الصورة المنطبعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mirror argument is often thought to support the reflection theory, implying that rays are emitted and reflected.
Correction: Sanusi uses the mirror case to show that the condition of facing is false; he further argues that if rays reflected, the mirror itself would not be seen, and the image moves with the observer, contradicting an impressed image.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the condition of facing (muqabala)?
A) By the example of the sun disc
B) By seeing oneself in a mirror
C) By seeing fire at a distance
D) By the atom argument

Key Takeaway
Sanusi uses empirical examples and logical reasoning to dismantle the conditions of emission and facing, showing their incoherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see your own reflection every day without a second thought. Did you ever consider that this simple act dismantles a whole philosophical theory about vision? How often do you accept what you see without questioning the underlying mechanisms? Will you now be more attentive to the assumptions hidden in your everyday perceptions?
?
Sanusi used empirical examples to argue that direct facing is not needed for vision. When you look at a reflection, you are seeing something that is not physically opposite you. ?

Four Theories on the Proof-Conclusion Connection
Sanusi enumerates and evaluates four theories on how evidence relates to its result.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four major theological perspectives on how a proof (dalil) relates to its conclusion (natija) in Islamic theology, and why Sanusi considers the rational connection view as correct.

Core Idea
The link between proof and conclusion is not merely logical—it reflects deep theological commitments.

Sanusi opens a foundational question: what kind of connection exists between evidence and its result? Is it a fixed rational necessity, a customary habit of God, or something else? The answer divides the main theological schools. In this passage, he presents the four possibilities, names the schools that hold each view, and declares his preferred position while acknowledging the validity of others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question posed
Read the opening sentence that presents four alternatives for the nature of the connection (customary, rational, by generation, by necessitation).
Each alternative corresponds to a different theological school.
2
Recognize the four schools
Sanusi lists three schools explicitly: al-Ash'ari (customary), Imam al-Haramayn (rational), Mu'tazila (generation). The fourth (necessitation) is implied.
The fourth school is not named but often associated with philosophers.
3
Understand Sanusi's judgment
He declares Imam al-Haramayn's rational view as 'the correct one' (al-sahih), but the footnote suggests 'more correct' since al-Ash'ari's view is also sound.
Sanusi's evaluation is nuanced, not absolute.
4
Notice additional details
Al-Qadi al-Baqillani had two opinions, and al-Ghazali followed al-Ash'ari. The Mu'tazila made an exception for recollective nazar.
These details show the complexity of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل الربط بين الدليل والنتيجة عادي فيمكن تخلفه ، أو عقلي فلا يمكن عندَ نفْيِ الآفاتِ العامَّةِ كالموتِ ونحوِه التخلُّفُ ، أو بالتولد ؛ بمعنى : أنَّ القدرة الحادثةَ أَثَّرَتْ في وجودِ النتيجة بواسطة تأثيرها في النظر ، أو بالإيجاب ؛ بمعنى : أنَّ النظرَ عِلَّةٌ أَثَرَتْ في وجودِ المعلول ؟ أربعة مذاهب : الأَوَّلُ : مذهب الأشعري ، والثاني : مذهب إمام الحرمين ، وهو الصحيحُ (۱) ، وللقاضي القولانِ ، والثالثُ : مذهب المعتزلة ، واستثنوا مِنْ ذلكَ النظر التذكُّري ، فقالوا فيه بقولِ (۱) قال العلامة الحامدي في ( حاشيته على شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ( ص ۱۸ ) : ( الأولى : « الأصح » ؛ لأن قول الأشعري صحيح ، لا فاسد ) ، وبقول الشيخ الأشعري قال حجة الإسلام الغزالي
And is the connection between the evidence and the result customary, such that it can fail? Or rational, such that it cannot fail given the absence of general impediments like death and the like? Or by generation (tawallud), meaning that the originated power (of the subject) effects the existence of the result through its effect on nazar? Or by necessitation (ijab), meaning that nazar is a cause that effects the existence of the effect? [There are] four schools: the first: the school of al-Ash'ari; the second: the school of Imam al-Haramayn, and it is the correct one (1) – and al-Qadi had two opinions; the third: the school of the Mu'tazila, and they excepted from that recollective nazar, saying about it the opinion of (1) al-Allama al-Hamidi said in his gloss on Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra (p. 18): 'the primary [expression] is 'the more correct', because the opinion of al-Ash'ari is correct, not corrupt'; and by the opinion of Shaykh al-Ash'ari spoke Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a classification of four theological schools on the nature of the connection between a proof (dalil) and its conclusion (natija). Sanusi presents the question: Is the connection (1) customary (adi) – meaning it could possibly fail (e.g., God could create the conclusion without the proof)? Or (2) rational (aqli) – meaning given the proof in sound conditions, the conclusion must follow, unless general impediments like death or insanity prevent understanding? Or (3) by generation (tawallud) as the Mu'tazila hold – the power (qudra) of the subject, through its effect on nazar, produces the result? Or (4) by necessitation (ijab) – nazar itself is a cause that produces the effect? Sanusi then lists the four positions: (i) al-Ash'ari's: customary connection; (ii) Imam al-Haramayn's: rational connection, which Sanusi declares the correct view (though the commentator notes 'the more correct' because al-Ash'ari's view is also sound); al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) had two opinions; (iii) the Mu'tazila's: generation, except they exclude 'recollective nazar' (tadhakkuri) from this rule; (iv) the fourth is not explicitly enumerated here but implied by the question: the necessitation view, associated with some philosophers. Sanusi ends by noting that al-Ghazali followed al-Ash'ari's opinion. The footnote clarifies that calling Imam al-Haramayn's view 'correct' might better be 'more correct', since al-Ash'ari's is also valid.

النتيجة
عقلي

الأشعري
إمام الحرمين
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The connection between proof and conclusion is purely logical and universal across all schools.
Correction: Sanusi shows that Islamic theologians debated this connection, with some viewing it as customary (allowing exceptions) or dependent on metaphysical causation, not as a fixed logical rule.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which school's view on the proof-conclusion connection is considered correct?
A) Al-Ash'ari's (customary)
B) Imam al-Haramayn's (rational)
C) The Mu'tazila's (generation)
D) The philosophers' (necessitation)

Key Takeaway
The nature of the link between proof and conclusion is a key theological dividing line, and Sanusi favors the rational connection view while acknowledging the validity of the customary view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your faith, do you treat your reasoning as a guarantee of truth, or do you leave room for God to act beyond your proofs? Which of the four views most closely matches your actual trust?
?
Sanusi declares the rational connection 'the correct view,' yet he also honors al-Ashʿarī's view as sound. How do you handle theological disagreement among respected scholars—does it unsettle your certainty or deepen your humility?
?
If the connection between proof and conclusion is merely customary, then your sincere study of theology does not compel God to give you certainty. Does that thought make you cling more tightly to your own reasoning, or drive you to rely more directly on God's grace?

The Three Modes of Causal Influence
Sanusi enumerates and defines the three modes of influence: choice, nature, and cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three modes of causal influence according to Sanusi: influence by choice, by nature, and by cause, and how they are derived from a binary distinction.

Core Idea
Every causal influence is either by choice, by nature, or by necessary cause.

Sanusi begins by asserting that all modes of influence are confined to three types. He then provides a logical argument based on two binary divisions: whether the agent can refrain or not, and if not, whether the effect depends on conditions. This classification is crucial for distinguishing God's free agency from natural and necessary causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage thoroughly
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi states the three modes first, then gives the reasoning.
2
Understand the first binary division
Identify that the first division is between agents that can refrain from their effect and those that cannot. The first category is the 'free agent' (فاعل مختار).
3
Understand the second binary division
For agents that cannot refrain, the second division is between those whose effect depends on conditions (nature) and those whose effect is unconditional (cause). See the examples of fire and hand-key.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this classification matters for theology: God is a free agent, while natural causes are conditional, and necessary causes are unconditional but not free.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقدَّمَ انحصار جهاتِ التأثير في أوجه ثلاثة؛ وهي التأثير بالاختيار، والتأثير بالطبيعة، والتأثيرُ بالعِلَّةِ، وأَنَّ وَجْهَ الحصرِ : أَنَّ كلَّ مُؤثرٍ إِمَّا أن يصح منه الترك لأثره؛ كالكاتب مثلاً للكتابة، والمتحرك غير المرتعش مثلاً لحركتِهِ عندَ القدري، لا عند السُّني القائل بعدمِ تأثيرِ القدرةِ الحادثة، أو لا، والأوَّلُ : الفاعلُ المختارُ، ويلزمُهُ أنْ يكونَ حيّاً عالماً قادراً مريداً، والثاني : إما أن يَتوقَّفَ اقتضاؤُهُ على شرط وانتفاء مانع كما يقولُ الطبائعي في إحراقِ النارِ ونفع الأدوية مثلاً، فإنَّهُ قد يمنع منها مانع، أو لا، كما يقولُ الفيلسوفي في حركة اليد مع حركة المفتاح مثلاً، فإنه يستحيل أن يمنعَ من حركة المفتاح أوِ الخاتم الكائنين في اليد عند حركته مانع، والأوّلُ : الطبيعةُ، والثاني : العِلَّةُ
The confinement of the modes of influence to three aspects has preceded: they are influence by choice, influence by nature, and influence by cause. And the reason for the enumeration is: every influencer either can refrain from its effect—like the writer, for example, from writing, and the mover that is not trembling, for example, from its motion according to the Qadari, not according to the Sunni who says the originated power has no effect—or cannot. The first is the free agent, and it necessarily follows that it be living, knowing, powerful, willing. The second: either its exigency depends on a condition and absence of an obstacle, as the naturalist says concerning the burning of fire and the benefit of medicines, for example, for a hindrance may prevent them; or not, as the philosopher says concerning the movement of the hand with the movement of the key, for example, for it is impossible for any hindrance to prevent the movement of the key or the ring both located in the hand when it moves. The first is nature, and the second is the cause.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the possible modes of causal influence. Sanusi states that influence is confined to three types: influence by choice, by nature, and by cause. He then gives the rationale for this enumeration based on two binary divisions. First: every influencer either can refrain from its effect or cannot. If it can refrain, it is a free agent (فاعل مختار), which necessarily possesses life, knowledge, power, and will. The example is a writer who can choose not to write. If it cannot refrain, then its effect is either conditional (depends on a condition and absence of an obstacle) or unconditional. The conditional is nature (طبيعة), e.g., fire burning can be prevented by wetness. The unconditional is cause (علة), e.g., the movement of the hand necessarily causes the movement of a key in the hand, with no possible hindrance. This classification is foundational for distinguishing God's free agency from natural and necessary causation.

Key Terms
تأثير بالاختيار
تأثير بالطبيعة
تأثير بالعلة
فاعل مختار
حصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'influence by choice' applies to all voluntary actions, but Sanusi specifies that the free agent must be living, knowing, powerful, and willing.
Correction: Influence by choice requires that the agent possess life, knowledge, power, and will; natural causes and necessary causes lack these attributes.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes 'influence by nature' from 'influence by cause'?
A) Nature can be prevented by obstacles, cause cannot.
B) Nature requires a living agent, cause does not.
C) Cause is always by choice, nature is not.
D) Nature is only in fire.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi grounds the three modes of causal influence in a binary distinction between agents that can refrain and those that cannot, with further subdivision for the latter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you rely on natural causes (e.g., medicine) as if they have independent power, or do you see them as instruments that depend on God's will? How would this change your attitude when you face a situation where the cause fails?
?
Sanusi says that a free agent (God) must be living, knowing, powerful, and willing. When you pray, do you truly believe that God is perfectly free and not constrained by any cause? Or do you silently assume that your actions force His response?
?
If all created causes are either nature or cause (not free agents), then nothing in creation can act independently. Does this truth sit uncomfortably with your sense of personal autonomy? How can you reconcile your daily choices with the fact that true free agency belongs to God alone?

Definitions of Origin and Branch
Sanusi defines origin and branch as technical terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definitions of 'origin' (asl) and 'branch' (far) as used in kalam causal reasoning.

Core Idea
In kalam, an origin is not a cause—it is merely a habitual antecedent.

Before engaging with proofs about causality and God, Sanusi clarifies two key terms: origin and branch. These terms are used to describe relationships in the observable world without assuming metaphysical causation. Understanding these definitions prevents confusion between natural causation and divine agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic and translation slowly. Notice how origin is defined as that from which something else arises habitually, without effect.
2
Distinguish origin from cause
The definition explicitly says 'without any effect from it originally'. This is crucial—origin does not imply causation in the metaphysical sense.
Contrast with modern notions of causality.
3
Examine the example
Water is given as an origin for plants. Understand that water does not truly cause plants to grow; it is merely the habitual precursor.
4
Connect to broader kalam
Realize that this terminology supports the occasionalist worldview where only God is the true agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : ( أصلِهِ وفرعِهِ ) يعني بالأصل : ما نشأ عنه غيرُهُ بحَسَبِ مجرى العادة مِنْ غيرِ تأثيرِ لهُ أصلاً ، وبالفرع : الغير الناشئ ؛ كالماء للنبات ونحو ذلك . قوله : ( وأنَّ الجميع مفتقر إلى فاعل مختار ) يعني : لأنَّ الطبيعة والعِلَّةَ لا يُخصِّصانِ مِثْلاً عن مِثْلِ ، والعالم كله متماثل ، ومع ذلكَ قدِ اختص كلُّ جزء منه بما لم يثبت لمماثله ، وقد سبق تقرير ذلكَ فاعل ذاتِكَ ، والحال واحدٌ ، ولهذا المعنى استغنى عن ذكر ذلك هنا ، وهو مندرج في التشبيه بقوله : ( كافتقارك ) . ولُهُ : ( وأنَّ جميعَهُ عاجز ) يعني : ومِنْ هذا المعنى وجبَ أنْ يكون صانع العالم ليسَ شيئاً منهُ ؛ لوجوب عموم العجز لجميعِه ، فلا يكون فاعلُهُ جِرْماً ولا قائماً بهِ ، وإلا لعجز كعجزه ، وسيأتي لذلك مزيد بيان إن شاء الله تعالى
And his saying: (its origin and its branch) he means by origin: that from which another arises according to the course of habit without any effect from it originally, and by branch: the arising other; like water for plants and the like. His saying: (and that all are in need of a free agent) he means: because nature and cause do not specify one similar over another, and the whole world is similar, and despite that, each part of it is specified with what is not established for its similar, and that has been previously established... and it is included in the analogy with his saying: (like your need). And to him: (and that all of it is incapable) he means: from this meaning it is necessary that the Maker of the world is not a part of it; because of the necessity of universal incapacity for all of it, so its agent is not a body nor subsisting in it, otherwise it would be incapable like its incapacity, and there will come for that more explanation if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct but related points: First, the definitions of asl (origin) and far (branch) as used in causal language—asl is that from which something arises habitually without intrinsic effect, far is the resulting thing. Second, the proof that all things are in need of a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār). The argument: nature and cause cannot differentiate between two similars, yet the world—which is entirely similar in its material nature—has distinct parts, so the differentiation must come from a free agent. The passage then adds that all parts of the world are inherently incapable (ʿājiz), which implies the Maker cannot be part of the world (neither a body nor inhering in it), because if He were, He would share the incapacity. This is a standard kalam proof for God's transcendence and non-corporeality.

Key Terms
أصل
فرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'origin' implies real causal power.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly says 'without any effect from it originally', making it clear it is merely habitual.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, does an origin have any real effect on what arises from it?
A) Yes, it has some effect
B) No, it has no effect at all
C) It depends on the situation
D) Only in the case of water

Key Takeaway
In kalam, an origin is a habitual antecedent with no causal power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself assuming that because something happens regularly, it must be the cause? How does Sanusi's definition challenge your everyday thinking about cause and effect?
?
If water does not truly cause plants to grow, what does that imply about your reliance on natural processes? Where are you placing your trust—in habits or in the Creator?
?
When you see a sequence like water and plant, do you immediately attribute power to the material? How can you train your heart to see the true Agent behind all appearances and submit only to Him?

Proof of the Need for a Free Agent and the Maker's Transcendence
Sanusi argues for a free agent and Maker's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key kalam arguments: that all creation needs a free agent, and that the Maker cannot be part of creation.

Core Idea
The world's differentiation proves a free agent, and its universal incapacity proves the Maker is not part of it.

Having defined origin and branch, Sanusi now presents two powerful arguments. First, since nature cannot differentiate between similar things, the observed differences in the world point to a free agent who chooses. Second, because every part of the world is inherently incapable, the Maker cannot be a part of the world—otherwise He would share that incapacity. These arguments establish both God's existence and His transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the argument that nature does not specify one similar over another, and since the world's parts are similar yet distinct, a free agent is needed.
Understand the problem of indistinguishable opposites.
2
Understand the specification argument
The world is composed of similar particles, yet each particle has unique properties (e.g., location, time). Nature alone cannot produce this specification, so it must come from a free agent.
3
Read the second proof
Read the claim that all of creation is incapable, and therefore the Maker cannot be a part of it.
Incapability means lacking power to create or sustain.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
If the Maker were a body or inherent in a body, He would be subject to the same incapacity as other parts, which is impossible. Thus He is not part of the world.
This is a classic proof for God's non-corporeality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

The Impossibility of Beginningless Accidents
Sanusi proves the impossibility of an infinite regress of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite regress of accidents is logically impossible according to classical kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
An infinite series cannot be completed; thus accidents must have a beginning.

Sanusi presents a concise but powerful proof that accidents—temporary attributes of substances—cannot stretch back infinitely. The argument hinges on the idea that if there were an infinite number of past accidents, then an infinite number would have been 'completed' before the present, which is a contradiction. Let's walk through this logical demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Try to grasp the overall argument before diving into details.
Notice the key terms: حوادث (accidents), لا أول لها (without beginning), فراغ العدد (completion of number).
2
Identify the key concepts
Identify the three main concepts: 'beginningless accidents', 'completion of an infinite number', and the conclusion that such a thing is impossible.
The proof uses the idea that if a number is completed, it must have two ends—a first and a last.
3
Understand the logical structure
Trace the logical steps: Premise (beginningless accidents imply infinite series completed before now) → Implication (completion requires two ends) → Contradiction (infinite series has no first end) → Conclusion (beginningless accidents are impossible).
This is a classic kalam argument for the temporal origin of the universe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلان حوادث لا أول لها. وتقديرها حوادث لا مبدأ لها يؤدي إلى فراغ ما لا نهاية له عدداً قبل ما وُجِدَ منها الآن، لكن فراغ العدد يستلزم انتهاء طرفيه، ففراغ ما لا نهاية له من عدد الحوادث محال، فما توقف عليه من وجود الحوادث الآن يجب أن يكون محالاً، فيلزم أن تكون عدماً مع تحقق وجودها.
Refutation of beginningless accidents. And considering them as accidents with no beginning leads to the completion of an infinite number before what exists of them now, but the completion of a number necessitates the termination of its two ends. So the completion of an infinite number of accidents is impossible. Therefore, what the existence of accidents now depends on must be impossible, and it follows that they would be non-existent while their existence is realized.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) against the possibility of beginningless accidents—accidents that have no first in an infinite regress. The argument uses the concept of 'completing an infinite number' (faragh ma la nihaya lahu). If accidents had no beginning, then before the present moment, an infinite number of accidents would have to have already occurred, meaning the infinite series would be completed. But a completed number necessarily has two ends (a first and a last). Since an infinite series by definition has no first end, its completion is impossible. Therefore, the assumption that accidents are beginningless is impossible; otherwise, they would have to be both existent and non-existent (a contradiction). This is a classic kalam argument for the temporal origin of the universe.

Key Terms
حوادث
لا أول لها
فراغ العدد
تسلسل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think an infinite past is possible without contradiction.
Correction: This proof shows that an actual infinite series of events cannot be completed, because it would have no first end. Thus a beginningless past is logically impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what is impossible?
A) An infinite number of accidents before the present.
B) The existence of any accident.
C) The beginning of the universe.
D) The subsistence of motion in a substance.

Key Takeaway
An infinite regress of accidents is impossible because it would require completing an infinite number, which is logically contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the universe cannot have an infinite past, then every moment of your existence—from your first breath to this very second—points directly to a Creator who initiated it all. Does your daily life reflect this truth, or do you live as if the world has always been here and will always be here without needing Allah? Commit to one concrete action today that reminds you of your contingent existence.
?
This proof shows that assuming an infinite past leads to contradiction. Yet many people live as if the world is self-sufficient and eternal. Where in your own heart have you unconsciously relied on the permanence of this world—its systems, your health, your relationships—rather than on the Eternal? Write down one such reliance and repent of it.
?
The argument forces you to accept that everything after the first moment is contingent. Consider the most fleeting thought or desire you had today—it is an accident in need of a cause. Are you living in constant awareness that every accident in your life is sustained by Allah's will, or do you attribute things to 'chance' or 'nature' as if they were self-sufficient? Tonight, before sleeping, recall three events of your day and consciously attribute them to Allah's direct creation.
