Refutation of the Eternity of Bodies via Accidents
Sanusi refutes the claim that bodies are eternal because they are never free from accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the argument that bodies are eternal because they are never free from accidents, and why the opponent's assumption of a first accident is the key flaw.

Core Idea
No first accident means no proof of body's eternity from accidents.

In this passage, Sanusi dismantles an opponent's argument for the eternity of bodies. The opponent claims that since bodies are never without accidents, they must themselves be eternal. Sanusi cleverly accepts the premise but rejects the conclusion by denying that accidents have a first beginning. Let's see how he does it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's claim, Sanusi's concession, then his objection.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The opponent's argument has two premises: (1) bodies are never free from accidents, (2) if so, bodies are eternal. Sanusi refutes step 2 by showing it only follows if accidents have a first beginning.
3
Understand the refutation's core
Sanusi asserts that accidents have no first beginning; each accident is preceded by another ad infinitum. Therefore, the type of accidents is eternal, and bodies being never free from an eternal type does not prove they are eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حادث ، قولكم : ( لأنَّه لا يعرى عنها ) مُسلَّمٌ ، وقولكم : ( فيكونُ حادثاً مثلها ) ممنوع ؛ لأنَّ ذلك إنَّما يلزم لو كانَتِ الحوادث التي لازمَتِ الأجرام لها مبدأ يُفتتَحُ بهِ عددُها ، ونحن نقولُ : لا مُفتَحَ لتلك الحوادثِ ، بل ما مِنْ حادث إلا وقبلَهُ حادث لا إلى أوَّل ، فلم يلزم مِنْ قدمِ الأجرام على هذا التقديرِ عُرُؤُها عنِ الحوادثِ اللازمة لها ؛ لأنَّ نوعها الذي لا تنفكُ عنه الأجرام قديم
Accident? Your statement: 'Because it is never free from them' is granted, and your statement: 'So it is an accident like them' is prevented; because that would only follow if the accidents that accompany bodies had a beginning with which their number is opened. But we say: There is no beginning for those accidents; rather, there is no accident except that before it is an accident without [going back to] a first. So it does not follow from the eternity of bodies on this assumption that they are free from the accidents that are necessary for them, because their type from which bodies are inseparable is eternal.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument. The opponent claims that bodies are eternal (qadīm) because they are never free from accidents (ḥawādith). The author first concedes (musallam) the premise that bodies are never free from accidents. However, he denies (mamnūʿ) the conclusion that bodies must therefore be accidents themselves. The reasoning is that if the accidents had a first beginning, then the bodies would be free from them before that beginning, and thus bodies would be contingent. But the author asserts that accidents have no first beginning; every accident is preceded by another accident ad infinitum. Therefore, the eternity of bodies does not entail that they are ever free from accidents, because the type (nawʿ) of accidents that is inseparable from bodies is itself eternal. So the opponent's argument fails because it assumes a beginning for accidents, which is false.

Key Terms
حوادث
أجرام
قدم
نوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a body is never free from accidents, it must be temporal because accidents are temporal.
Correction: Accidents as a type can be eternal, so the body's inseparable accidents do not imply its temporality.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the claim that bodies are eternal because they are never free from accidents?
A) He denies that bodies are never free from accidents.
B) He accepts the premise but denies the conclusion because accidents have no beginning.
C) He argues that bodies are not eternal because they have accidents.
D) He says that accidents are not necessary for bodies.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's argument for the eternity of bodies fails because it assumes a first beginning for accidents, which does not exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that the opponent's argument fails because it assumes a first accident. In your own spiritual life, do you sometimes assume that your sins or bad habits have a 'first' cause that you can point to, thereby excusing yourself from immediate repentance? If there is no 'first' accident, how does that challenge you to break the cycle without waiting for a perfect starting point?
?
This lesson shows that being constantly accompanied by something does not prove one's origin. What things in your life—like peer pressure or worldly desires—do you allow to define who you are, as if their presence proves something about your identity? Are you willing to look beyond what accompanies you to find the truth of your creation and purpose?
?
Sanusi's argument forces you to think about infinity and beginnings. Is your faith built on comfortable assumptions that everything must have a neat beginning, or are you ready to accept that some aspects of Allah's creation (like the type of accidents) are eternal? How does this reshape your understanding of Allah's absolute power and your own dependency on Him?

Proof that Beginningless Accidents Are Impossible
Sanusi proves that an infinite regress of past accidents is impossible because it entails completing an infinite series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's first proof that an infinite past series of accidents is impossible because it would require completing an infinite sequence.

Core Idea
An infinite past cannot be completed — that is a contradiction.

Sanusi presents a logical argument against the idea of accidents with no beginning. He shows that if there were beginningless accidents, an infinite number of them would have already occurred, which is impossible because it involves completing an infinite series. This lesson walks through that proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage from the source once through, then read the English translation. Notice the logical structure and key terms like 'حوادث لا أول لها' and 'فراغ ما لا نهاية له'.
The Arabic is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the central contradiction
The author argues that beginningless accidents would imply that an infinite number of events have been completed up to the present. But 'completion of an infinite' is a contradiction. Identify the phrase where he states this.
Look for 'جمع بين الفراغ وعدم النهاية'.
3
Trace the consequence
If the completion of an infinite is impossible, then our existence now would also be impossible because it depends on that impossible completion. Understand the dependency argument.
The author says 'يلزم عليه أن يكون وجودنا ... محالاً'.
4
Note the nuance about types and individuals
The author clarifies that this argument targets beginningless accidents, not endless ones. Also, he relates it to the position that only individual accidents are created while types are eternal.
This connects to the concept of 'قدم نوعي' (eternity of type).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب مِنْ أوجه : (۱) الأوّلُ : أَنَّهُ يلزم على وجودِ حوادث لا أول لها : أن يكون دخل في الوجودِ وفرغَ مِنْ حركاتِ الأفلاكِ وأشخاص الحيوان ونحوها على الترتيب واحداً بعد واحد.. عدد لا نهاية له ! والجمعُ بينَ الفراغِ وعدم النهايةِ جمع بين متناقضين ، فيكون محالاً على الضرورة ، ويلزمُ عليهِ : أنْ يكونَ وجودنا ووجود سائر الحوادثِ الآنَ محالاً لتوقفه على المحالِ ، وهو فراغ ما لا نهاية له ، وإلى هذا الجواب أشرنا في العقيدة بقولنا : ( يؤدّي . . . ( إلى آخرِهِ ، و(مِنْ) في قولنا : ( مِنْ وجودِ الحوادث ( لبيان ( ما ) الموصولة قبلها ، والضمير في ( عليه ( يعودُ على ( فراغ ما لا نهاية له ) ؛ أي : فما توقف على فراغ ما لا نهاية له الذي اتضحَتِ استحالته . . يجب أن يكون محالاً ; وعليه : فأشخاص العالم حادثة عندهم ، وأنواعها قديمة ، فهو قول بالقدم النوعي لأشخاصها فقط ، مع التسليم بحدوث أفرادها ، وسيبين المصنف المحالات العقلية اللازمة على إثبات هذا لأنَّ ما توقَّفَ على المحالِ محالٌ ; ضرورةَ أَنَّ المتوقِّفَ لا يُوجَدُ بدونِ المتوقفِ عليه ، والمتوقفُ في قضيتنا هو وجود الحوادث الآن واسم ( تكونَ ( في قولِهِ : ( فيلزم أن تكون عدماً ) يعود على الحوادث الموجودة الآن ; حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها مستحيل ، بخلاف حوادث لا آخر لها
And the answer is from aspects: (1) The first: That the existence of accidents with no first necessitates that there would have entered into existence and been completed from the movements of the spheres and the individuals of animals and the like in order one after another... a number with no end! And combining completion and the absence of an end is combining two contradictories, so it is impossible by necessity. And it follows from it that our existence and the existence of all other accidents now would be impossible because it depends on the impossible, which is the completion of what has no end. And to this answer we alluded in the Creed by our saying: 'it leads...' to the rest of it. And (min) in our saying 'from the existence of accidents' is to indicate (what) the relative pronoun before it. And the pronoun in 'upon it' refers to 'completion of what has no end'; i.e., whatever depends on the completion of what has no end, whose impossibility is clear, must be impossible. And upon this: the individuals of the world are accidents according to them, and their types are eternal, so it is a statement of the eternity of the type for its individuals only, while conceding the occurrence of its individuals. And the author will show the intellectual impossibilities that follow from affirming this, because that which depends on the impossible is impossible; necessarily, the dependent is not found without that on which it depends. And the dependent in our case is the existence of accidents now. And the noun 'be' in his saying 'it would necessitate that they be non-existence' refers to the accidents existing now. Accidents with no first are impossible, unlike accidents with no last.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (wajh) that beginningless accidents are impossible. The author argues that if there were an infinite series of past accidents, then at the present moment an infinite number of motions, animal individuals, etc., would have been completed (farigha) in sequence. But 'completion of an infinite' is a contradiction because an infinite by definition cannot be completed. Therefore, the existence of such a series is impossible. Consequentially, the existence of everything that depends on the completion of that infinite series—including our own existence now—would also be impossible, because whatever depends on the impossible is itself impossible. The author then clarifies that in his Creed (al-ʿAqīda) he alluded to this argument. He also explains a grammatical point about the word 'min' and the pronoun 'upon it'. He notes that for those who hold that only individuals are created but types are eternal, the necessary impossibilities will be shown. The passage concludes by restating that beginningless accidents are impossible, but not endless ones (like those of paradise).

Key Terms

فراغ ما لا نهاية له
متناقضين
محال
قدم نوعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any infinite series (past or future) is impossible.
Correction: The author only rules out an infinite past series (beginningless), not an infinite future series (endless). The contradiction arises from completing an infinite, which only applies to past series.

Checkpoint

A) That they would exist forever without end.
B) That they would require the completion of an infinite number of events, which is contradictory.
C) That they are accidents and thus must have a beginning.
D) That they depend on the existence of bodies.

Key Takeaway
Beginningless accidents are impossible because they would require completing an infinite series, which is logically contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What does that say about your entitlement to life? Are you living as if your next breath is a right rather than a gift from the One who brought the world into being?
?
The argument forces you to admit that the past is finite and had a beginning. That beginning demands a Creator. If you truly believed this, would you change anything about how you spend your time? What would you stop doing? What would you start doing?
?
This proof shows that an infinite regress of events is impossible. But you may still live as if causes and effects go back forever, never truly relying on the First Cause. In what area of your life—finances, relationships, habits—do you act as though there is no ultimate dependency? How would acknowledging your absolute dependence on Allah change one decision you will make today?

The Impossibility of an Infinite Past
Sanusi argues that infinite past series would render present existence impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an infinite series of past events would logically prevent the existence of any present event, and how Sanusi uses analogy to demonstrate this.

Core Idea
Infinite past series leads to a logical absurdity: present existence would be impossible.

In this passage, Sanusi employs a compelling analogy to show that an infinite chain of past events cannot exist. He compares the movement of the celestial sphere in a given time to a promised dirham that requires an infinite number of predecessors. If those predecessors were infinite, the promised event could never occur. He then extends this argument to our own existence and that of plants and animals, concluding that since we do exist, the premise of an infinite past must be false. This argument is a key component of the kalam cosmological argument for a beginning of the universe.

Guided Path
1
Read the Analogy
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi sets up the comparison between celestial movement and a promised dirham.
The dirham analogy is designed to make the abstract concept of infinity tangible.
2
Understand the Impossibility
Reflect on why an infinite series of predecessors would make the specific event (movement in a specific time, or receiving the dirham) impossible. The key point is that you cannot traverse an infinite series to reach the present.
This shows that an actual infinite in the past leads to a contradiction.
3
Apply to Our Existence
Sanusi extends the argument to our present existence and that of crops and animals. If our ancestors were infinite, we could never come to exist. Recognise that this strengthens the case against an infinite past.
The argument is not just about celestial spheres; it applies to all chains of causal dependence.
4
Reflect on the Flow
Trace the logical flow: infinite past → impossibility of a specific event → impossibility of present existence. See how the analogy serves as a clear demonstration.
The flow diagram in the lesson can help visualize the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ممَّا لا نهاية له ؛ فالحركةُ للفَلَكِ في الزمانِ المعيَّن نظير الدرهم الموعود به في الزمان المخصوص ، والحركاتُ التي لا تتناهى قبلها نظير الدراهم التي لا تتناهى قبل ذلك الدرهم ، فيكون وجود الحركةِ للفَلَكِ في هذا الزمانِ مثلاً مستحيلاً ، كما استحال وجود الدرهم الموعودِ بهِ في الزمانِ المعيَّنِ للشخص ، وكذا يلزمُ أنْ يكونَ وجودنا في هذا الزمان ووجود سائر الحيواناتِ والزروع مستحيلاً ؛ لتوقفنا على وجودِ آباء قبلَنا لا نهاية لهم ، وتوقف الزروع على بذور قبلها لا نهاية لها ولا خبر في فضيحتهم كالعِيانِ
From what has no end: the movement of the celestial sphere in a specific time is analogous to the promised dirham in a specified time, and the movements that are infinite before it are analogous to the dirhams that are infinite before that dirham. Thus the existence of the movement of the celestial sphere in this time, for example, would be impossible, just as the existence of the promised dirham in the specified time for the person is impossible. Likewise it necessarily follows that our existence in this time and the existence of other animals and crops would be impossible, because our existence depends on the existence of ancestors before us who have no end, and crops depend on seeds before them that have no end, and there is no doubt about their refutation being as clear as day.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a proof by analogy that an infinite series of past events is impossible. Sanusi compares the movement of the celestial sphere in a given time to a promised dirham that is supposed to be given at a specific time. If there are infinite movements before it, just as there are infinite dirhams before that promised one, then the existence of that specific movement would be impossible, just as it is impossible for the promised dirham to exist because it would require traversing an infinite series. He then extends this to our own existence and the existence of plants and animals: if we depended on an infinite chain of ancestors or seeds, our present existence would be impossible. Since we do exist, the premise of an infinite past is false. He concludes that the opponent's position is as clear as day in its absurdity.

Key Terms
حوادث لا أول لها
متناهية
غير متناهية
الحركة الفلكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think an infinite future and an infinite past are equally impossible.
Correction: Sanusi's argument focuses on the past because an infinite past would require traversing an actual infinite, leading to a contradiction for the present. An infinite future does not pose the same logical problem because it is not traversed.

Checkpoint

A) A promise to give dirhams forever
B) The movement of the celestial sphere and a promised dirham
C) The existence of ancestors
D) The growth of crops

Key Takeaway
An infinite series of past events is logically impossible because it would prevent the existence of any present event, as shown by the analogy of the promised dirham and our own existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Second Proof: Conjunction of Existence and Non-Existence
Sanusi argues that beginningless events imply the conjunction of existence and non-existence in eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi demonstrates that beginningless events would lead to the impossible conjunction of eternal existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Beginningless events force eternal existence and eternal non-existence to coexist, which is impossible.

Sanusi presents a second argument against the possibility of events without a beginning. If events had no beginning, the genus of events would be eternal, but each individual event is preceded by an eternal non-existence. This would mean that the same thing—a particular event—would be both eternally existent and eternally non-existent, an impossibility. In this lesson, we will unpack this argument and consider an objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: an implication, its demonstration, and an objection.
The passage ends mid-objection; the full response is not given.
2
Understand the core implication
Sanusi claims that if events had no beginning, then eternal existence would be accompanied by its non-existence. Grasp why this is the conclusion he aims to prove.
This is the key claim: the conjunction of existence and non-existence in eternity.
3
Follow the demonstration step by step
Trace how Sanusi shows that a particular event would be eternal because the genus of events is eternal, yet its non-existence is also eternal. This yields a contradiction.
Focus on the reasoning: each event is preceded by eternal non-existence; all non-existences coexist in eternity; the genus is eternal; a specific event would then be eternal, but its non-existence is also eternal.
4
Consider the objection
Read the objection: 'Non-existence does not accompany any event; rather it is before all events.' Reflect on why this objection would imply that events have a beginning, thus self-destructing.
Sanusi's response is not fully given, but the implication is clear: if non-existence is before all events, then there was a time when no events existed, implying a first event.
5
Reflect on the impossibility
Understand why the conjunction of existence and non-existence is impossible by logical necessity. This argument builds on the earlier proof against infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : يلزم على وجودِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها أنْ يقارنَ الوجودَ الأزليَّ عدمُهُ هذا وجة ثانٍ لإبطالِ حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ، وتقريره : أن نقول : لو كانت الحوادث لا أوَّلَ لها لزم اجتماع الوجودِ الأزلي مع عدمِهِ . وبيانُ الملازمةِ : أَنَّ كلَّ حادثٍ مِنْ تلك الحوادث مسبوق بعدم لا أوَّلَ لهُ ، وتلك العدماتُ كلُّها مجتمعة في الأزلِ ؛ إذ لا ترتيب فيها ، وجنسُ الحوادثِ أزليّ أيضاً ؛ لأنَّها لا أَوَّلَ لها ، وذلكَ الجنسُ لا يتحقق وجودُهُ إلا في حادثٍ مِنْ أفرادِهِ ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ ذلكَ الحادث أزليّاً ، لكن عدمُهُ السابق عليه أيضاً أزلي ؛ لما سبق أنَّ عدم كل حادث أزلي ، فقد لزمَ مقارنةً وجودِ الشيء لعدمِهِ ، لأنَّهما أزليَّانِ معاً ، واجتماعُ وجودِ الشيء معَ عدمِهِ محال على الضرورة ، وفيه أيضاً مصاحبةُ السابق - وهو العدمُ - للمسبوق ؛ وهو الوجود الحادث ، وفيهِ الجمعُ بينَ متناقضينِ ؛ وهو الحدوث والأزليَّة . فإن قالوا : لا نُسلّمُ أنَّ العدمَ يصاحبه شيءٌ مِنَ الحوادثِ ، بل العدمُ قبلَ جميعها .. لزمَ أنْ يكونَ لجميع الحوادثِ أول ، وهم
Additionally, it necessarily follows from the existence of events with no beginning that eternal existence would be accompanied by its non-existence. This is a second argument for refuting events with no beginning. Its explanation: we say: if events had no beginning, then the conjunction of eternal existence with its non-existence would be necessary. The demonstration of the implication: every event among those events is preceded by a non-existence that has no beginning, and all those non-existences are together in eternity, because there is no order among them. The genus of events is also eternal because it has no beginning, and that genus is not realized in existence except in an individual event. Thus it necessarily follows that that individual event would be eternal. But its prior non-existence is also eternal, because as previously established, the non-existence of every event is eternal. So the conjunction of the existence of a thing with its non-existence has become necessary, because both are eternal together. And the conjunction of the existence of a thing with its non-existence is impossible by necessity. Also in this argument is the accompaniment of the prior—which is non-existence—with the posterior—which is the originated existence—and the combination of two opposites: origination and eternity. If they say: 'We do not concede that non-existence accompanies any of the events; rather non-existence is before all of them,' then it would necessarily follow that all events have a beginning, and they...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a second proof against the possibility of beginningless events (حوادث لا أول لها). Sanusi argues that if events had no beginning, then we would have the impossible situation where eternal existence (the genus of events) and eternal non-existence (the non-existence preceding each event) coexist. He explains: each event is preceded by an infinite chain of non-existence; all these non-existences are contemporaneous in eternity (since they are all prior and have no order). The genus of events itself is eternal because it has no starting point. But this genus only exists through a particular event. Therefore that particular event would be eternal. However, its non-existence is also eternal (since it is prior). Thus we have the same thing being both existent and non-existent in eternity, which is impossible. Additionally, the prior (non-existence) accompanies the posterior (existence), and the contradictory attributes of origination and eternity are combined. Then he considers an objection: if the opponent denies that non-existence accompanies any event and claims that non-existence is before all events (i.e., all events are preceded by non-existence collectively), then that would imply that all events have a beginning, which is what they deny. Thus the objection self-destructs.

Key Terms
حوادث لا أول لها
وجود أزلي
عدم أزلي
اجتماع الوجود والعدم
الملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think an infinite past is possible if time is infinite, but this argument shows a logical contradiction in the very concept of beginningless events.
Correction: Sanusi demonstrates that beginningless events lead to the impossible conjunction of existence and non-existence, regardless of one's intuition about infinite time.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does Sanusi identify in beginningless events?
A) That they would require an infinite regress of causes
B) That a particular event would be both eternal and originated
C) That they would imply a beginning
D) That they would require a creator

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's second proof shows that beginningless events entail the absurd coexistence of eternal existence and non-existence for the same thing.

The argument shows that beginninglessness leads to absurdity. Are there patterns in your life—sins, habits, excuses—that you treat as if they have always been there and always will be? What first step can you take to give them a real beginning of repentance?
?
Sanusi forces you to accept that only Allah's existence is not preceded by non-existence. Does your heart truly rest in that Eternal One, or are you seeking security in things that will inevitably cease? What practical change would align your trust with this lesson?

The Proof of Cutting and Matching Against Beginningless Events
Sanusi illustrates the proof of cutting and matching

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the proof of cutting and matching (burhan al-qat' wa al-tatbiq) to demonstrate the impossibility of an infinite regress of events.

Core Idea
Infinite regress leads to two numbers that are neither equal nor unequal—an absurdity.

Sanusi presents a sophisticated logical proof to refute the possibility of beginningless events. By imagining two counts of events starting from different points in time, he shows that the assumption of an infinite past leads to a contradiction in the relation between numbers. This proof, known as 'cutting and matching,' is a cornerstone of the argument for the temporal origination of the world. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage silently, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the hypothetical scenario Sanusi sets up.
The passage includes both the proof and a grammatical analysis. Focus first on the proof itself.
2
Identify the two counts and the contradiction
Notice that Sanusi describes two persons counting events from different starting points (Flood to eternity vs. now to eternity). What does he conclude about these two numbers? Why can't one be greater or equal?
The key is that both counts are infinite, so they cannot be exhausted, yet they differ in the events counted.
3
Understand the proof's structure
Sanusi explains that this proof is called 'cutting and matching' because we cut off the additional events (from Flood to now) to compare the two numbers. Then we match them, showing they are the same after the increase. This reveals the absurdity.
The proof relies on the impossibility of a number being both greater than and equal to another.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this proof refutes beginningless events. If events had no beginning, the two numbers would have an impossible relation. Therefore, events must have a beginning.
This is a classic reduction ad absurdum argument used in kalam.
5
Review the commentary
Read the commentary to deepen your understanding of the proof's logical steps and the technical terms like 'tatbiq' (application) and 'qat'' (cutting).
The commentary clarifies the grammatical structure of Sanusi's own explanation.

(١) في ( د ، هـ) : ( فيمتنع أن يكون أحدهما . . . )
Before the last, and 'the most' (al-akthar) is what corresponds to it. If we suppose now two persons, one counting events from the Flood to eternity, and the other counting them from now to eternity, it would be impossible according to their doctrine for one of the two numbers to be exhausted by counting before the other! So it is impossible for one of them to be more than the other (1). Thus it has become clear to you: that it necessarily follows from the existence of events without a beginning that there would exist two numbers between which there is neither equality nor inequality. And my saying: 'and that it is impossible' is coordinated with 'that it accompanies' which is the subject of 'necessarily follows', and the attached pronoun in 'from them' refers to the events, and 'without increase' is a circumstantial accusative from the subject of 'had emptied', and his saying: 'upon itself' is attached to 'application', and 'application' is placing one thing upon another, and the intended meaning here is considering one of the two numbers together with the other, and the relative pronoun 'what' in my saying: 'what is known' is the subject of 'is impossible'. And the object of application (al-mutabbaq) from the events is its counterpart in our example: what we supposed of the number of events from the time of the Flood to eternity, and the object applied to (al-mutabbaq 'alayh) is what we supposed of the number of events from now to eternity, and it is in reality the same as the object of application but after an increase of events upon it; and that is what is from the Flood to now. And because of our cutting in this proof of the applied from the increase of events in order to consider it with itself after its increase, it is called the proof of cutting and application (burhan al-qat' wa al-tatbiq). (1) In (d, h): 'so it is impossible for one of them to be...'
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by cutting and matching (burhan al-qat' wa al-tatbiq) to refute the possibility of an infinite regress of events. The author begins by defining terms: 'the one before the last' and 'the most' (i.e., the greater number) correspond to each other. He then sets up a hypothetical: two persons count events, one from the Flood to eternity, the other from now to eternity. On the philosophers' assumption of beginningless events, it is impossible for one count to be exhausted before the other, because both counts are infinite. Consequently, neither number can be greater than the other, yet they are not equal (since one includes the events from the Flood to now). This leads to a contradiction: two numbers that are neither equal nor one greater. The conclusion is that beginningless events would require two numbers that are neither equal nor unequal, which is absurd. The author then provides a grammatical and logical analysis of the proof's formulation, explaining technical terms: 'tatbiq' (application) means placing one thing upon another, here considering one number with another. The 'mutabbaq' (object of application) is the number from Flood to eternity, and the 'mutabbaq 'alayh' (object applied to) is the number from now to eternity, which is actually the same as the first but with the additional events from Flood to now. By 'cutting off' this added segment before the application, we compare the two numbers without the increase; after the increase, they are still the same. Thus the proof is named 'cutting and application'.

Key Terms
برهان القطع والتطبيق
حوادث لا أول لها
تطبيق
مساواة
مفاضلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of cutting and matching is circular because it assumes events are finite.
Correction: The proof does not assume finitude; it shows that the assumption of infinitude leads to an absurdity, thus proving finitude by contradiction. The proof is a reduction ad absurdum, not a circular argument.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence that Sanusi derives from the assumption of beginningless events?
A) That there would be two numbers that are equal.
B) That there would be two numbers that are neither equal nor one greater.
C) That one count would be exhausted before the other.
D) That the two counts would be identical.

Key Takeaway
The proof of cutting and matching shows that an infinite regress of events would necessarily involve two infinite numbers that are neither equal nor unequal, which is impossible.

?
Sanusi's proof reveals that the universe cannot be self-existent or eternal. When you look at the world around you—its order, its events—are you truly convinced that none of it could be self-caused? How does this affect your reliance on Allah in every moment, even the mundane?
?
The proof forces you to choose: either accept an absurd infinite regress or affirm a Creator. If you already affirm the Creator, does your daily life reflect that conviction, or do you still live as if things just happen without divine involvement? The Fourth Method: Refuting Beginningless Events
Sanusi presents a fourth refutation of the philosophers' claim of beginningless events

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's fourth refutation of the philosophers' claim of beginningless events, particularly the principle that the validity of a judgment follows the validity of its subject and how this leads to the impossibility of an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If the necessary conclusion is impossible, so is its premise.

Sanusi presents a fourth logical argument against the possibility of an infinite regress of events. He establishes a key principle: the validity of any judgment depends on the validity of what it is about. Applying this to the philosophers' claim, he shows that the judgment that an infinite past exists at every event leads to absurdity, and therefore the claim itself is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing too deeply, then read the English translation. Get a sense of the logical flow.
Notice that the passage is incomplete; it ends mid-sentence.
2
Identify the key principle
Find the principle 'صحة الحكم تتبع صحة المحكوم به' (the validity of the judgment follows the validity of the subject of judgment). Understand that this means if the premise is true, the judgment about it is valid.
This principle is foundational for the argument.
3
Follow the logical steps
Trace the reasoning: if events without beginning exist, then at every event it is valid to judge that an infinite past (emptiness) preceded it. This leads to absurdities like an eternal preceding an eternal or a finite number becoming infinite.
The absurdities are mentioned but not fully elaborated.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Since the judgment of an infinite past is impossible, its necessary condition (the existence of beginningless events) is also impossible. Therefore, all events have a beginning and nothing is eternal.
The passage cuts off at 'either it is...' — the proof of impossibility is incomplete.

1 The validity of the judgment follows the validity of the subject of judgment. And that it is valid for every event the establishment of a judgment of the emptying of what has no end before it, and so on without a beginning in the judgments, and from its necessities is the precedence of a subject of judgment with emptiness, so it would necessitate that an eternal precede an eternal. And if it is answered with finitude in the judgments, it would necessitate that what is finite is not finite by the increase of one! This is a fourth way also for refuting the philosophers, and its exposition: that we say: If events without a beginning existed, it would necessitate that at every event the existence of a judgment of the emptying of what has no end is valid, and the entailment is apparent because the validity of the judgment follows the validity of the subject of judgment, and the subject of judgment — which is the emptying of what has no end before every event — is valid according to their principle (1), so the existence of the judgment thereof at every event is necessarily valid. But this judgment is impossible for what we will mention now of the proof of that. So its necessary condition (malzum) — which is the existence of events without a beginning — is impossible, because of the necessity of the impossibility of the necessary condition upon the impossibility of its necessary concomitant. Thus all events have a beginning, and there is no existence of their genus nor of any of them in eternity, and that is the required. And the explanation of the impossibility of the existence of the judgment: that if it existed, it would not be free: either it is...
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage introduces a fourth method for refuting the philosophers who claim events can be without a beginning. Sanusi first asserts a principle: 'the validity of the judgment follows the validity of the subject of judgment' — meaning if the premise (the subject) is true, then the judgment about it is valid. He then applies this: if events had no beginning, then at every event it would be valid to judge that an infinite period (empty of events before it) has passed, because the premise itself states that before each event there is an infinite past. This leads to absurd consequences: either we have an infinite regress of judgments (an eternal preceding an eternal), or if we try to limit the judgments, we end up with a finite number that becomes infinite by adding one more. Thus, the judgment that infinite time has passed is impossible. Since the judgment is impossible, its necessary condition (the existence of beginningless events) is also impossible. Therefore, all events have a beginning, and no part of them is eternal. The passage then begins to detail the impossibility of the judgment but cuts off with 'either it is...' (imma an). The argument would likely continue with the standard proof that such a judgment would lead to a contradiction.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The judgment itself (the act of judging) is being questioned, rather than the logical validity of the judgment based on the premise.
Correction: Sanusi is not questioning whether anyone actually makes such a judgment; he is arguing that such a judgment would be logically impossible due to absurd consequences.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's fourth refutation shows that the claim of beginningless events leads to an impossible judgment, thus the claim itself is false.

Sanusi's Fourth Refutation of an Infinite Past
Sanusi presents a fourth way to refute infinite past events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's fourth way of refuting the philosophers' claim that past events can be infinite, and why the necessary 'judgment of infinite elapsed time' leads to an impossibility.

Core Idea
An infinite past series would require an impossible eternal-to-eternal precedence.

Sanusi presents a fourth logical argument against the possibility of an infinite chain of past events. This argument is a conditional proof that shows accepting an infinite past leads to a contradiction involving a 'judgment' that cannot exist. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional premise
Sanusi begins with a conditional: if there were events without a beginning, then at every event a judgment of an infinite time having elapsed would be valid. Recognize that this is based on the philosophers' own assumptions.
The entailment relies on 'validity follows validity'.
2
See why that judgment is impossible
Sanusi argues that such a judgment cannot exist. He divides the possibilities: either the judgment has a beginning or it does not. Both are shown to be false.
Focus on the 'no beginning' case as it reveals the core contradiction.
3
Analyze the 'no beginning' case
If the judgment has no beginning, then before each instance of judgment there must be events. This leads to the genus of the judged (eternal) preceding the genus of the judgment (also eternal), which is absurd.
Eternals cannot be prior to each other.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the necessary concomitant (the judgment) is impossible, what entails it (infinite past events) must also be impossible. Thus all events have a beginning.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا طريق رابع أيضاً للرد على الفلاسفة ، وتقريرها : أن نقول : لو وُجِدَتْ حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها لَلَزِمَ أنْ يصح عندَ كلِّ حادث وجود حكم بفراغ ما لا نهاية له ، والملازمة ظاهرةٌ ؛ لأنَّ صحة الحكم تتبع صحة المحكوم به ، والمحكوم به ـ وهو فراغ ما لا نهاية له قبلَ كُلِّ حادث - صحيح على أصلهم ، فوجود الحكم بذلك عند كل حادث صحيح ضرورةً اللكن هذا الحكم مستحيل ؛ لما نذكرُهُ الآنَ مِنَ البرهان على ذلك ، فيكون ملزومه ـ وهو وجود حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها - مستحيلاً ؛ لوجوب استحالة الملزوم عند استحالة لازمه ، فالحوادث إذا كلُّها لها أوّل ، ولا وجود لجنسِها ولا لشيء منها في الأزلِ ، وهو المطلوب ذلك وبيان استحالة وجودِ الحكم : أنَّهُ لو وُجِدَ لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ لهُ أوَّل ، أو لا ، والتالي باطل بقسميه ، فالملزوم ـ وهو وجود الحكم - باطل أيضاً ، والملازمة ظاهرة وأما بطلان التالي : فإنَّما يستبينُ ببطلان كلّ واحدٍ مِنْ قسميه ، فنقولُ : أما كونُ الحكم لا أَوَّلَ لَهُ فباطل ؛ لأنَّ مِنْ ضرورة هذا الحكم أنْ تسبقَ كلَّ فردٍ مِنْ أفرادِهِ حوادث ؛ ليحكم عليها بالانقضاء ، فيلزم أنْ يسبق جنسُ المحكوم عليه - وهو أزلي - جنس الحكم ؛ وهو أزلي أيضاً ، وسبق الأزلي على الأزلي محال ضرورةً .
This is also a fourth way to refute the philosophers, and its formulation: that we say: If there existed events without a beginning, then it would be necessary that at every event it is valid to have a judgment of the elapsing of an infinite [time] before it. The entailment is apparent, because the validity of a judgment follows the validity of what is judged upon, and what is judged upon – which is the infinite [time] elapsing before every event – is valid according to their principle, so the existence of a judgment of that at every event is necessarily valid. But this judgment is impossible, due to what we will mention now as the proof of that. Thus its implicans – which is the existence of events without a beginning – is impossible, because when its necessary concomitant is impossible, the implicans is necessarily impossible. Therefore, all events have a beginning, and neither their genus nor any individual of them exists in eternity, and that is what was sought. And the explanation of the impossibility of the existence of the judgment: that if it existed, it could not be but either having a beginning or not. The alternative is false in both its divisions, so the implicans – which is the existence of the judgment – is also false, and the entailment is apparent. As for the falsehood of the alternative, it is made clear by the falsehood of each of its two divisions. So we say: As for the judgment having no beginning, it is false, because it is necessary from this judgment that before every individual of its individuals there should be events to be judged as having elapsed, so it would follow that the genus of what is judged upon – which is eternal – precedes the genus of the judgment – which is also eternal – and the precedence of an eternal over an eternal is necessarily impossible.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a proof: Sanusi presents a fourth refutation of the philosophers' claim that events (حوادث) can exist without a beginning. The argument is a conditional proof. It begins by stating the conditional: if there were events without a first, then at every event it would be valid to have a judgment (حكم) that an infinite time has elapsed before it. The entailment is justified by the principle that the validity of a judgment follows the validity of what is judged (صحة الحكم تتبع صحة المحكوم به). According to the philosophers' own view, what is judged – an infinite elapsed time before each event – is valid, so the judgment is valid. But Sanusi asserts that this judgment is actually impossible, and he will prove it. Therefore, since the necessary concomitant (لازم) is impossible, what entails it (ملزوم) – the existence of events without a beginning – is also impossible. Thus all events have a beginning; none exist in eternity. Then Sanusi begins the proof of the impossibility of the judgment. The judgment, if it exists, must either have a beginning or not. Both alternatives are false. First, he shows that the judgment cannot be beginningless. If it had no beginning, then before each instance of judgment there must be events to be judged, leading to an eternal (genus of what is judged) preceding an eternal (genus of the judgment), which is absurd because precedence among eternals is impossible.

حوادث
حكم
فراغ ما لا نهاية له
ملزوم
لازم
أزلي
سبق الأزلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Sanusi's argument is circular, assuming what it seeks to prove.
Correction: It is not circular; it is a reductio that derives a contradiction (eternal preceding eternal) from the philosophers' own assumption of infinite events, without assuming a beginning.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's fourth way, what follows from the assumption of an infinite series of past events?
A) That at every event a judgment of infinite elapsed time is valid
B) That the series must have a beginning
C) That the judgment of infinite elapsed time is impossible
D) That the philosophers deny the validity of judgments

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's fourth way shows that an infinite past series would require the genus of an eternal (judged events) to precede another eternal (judgment), which is impossible; therefore, all events have a beginning.

?

Contradiction of a Finite Judgment on an Infinite Past
Sanusi proves impossible a judgment with a beginning on an infinite past, using an example from celestial motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a judgment with a beginning cannot apply to an infinite series, as it leads to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Adding one finite to another finite can never yield infinite.

Sanusi continues his proof by considering the second alternative: that the eternal judgment might have a beginning. He shows this leads to a clear mathematical absurdity. To make the argument concrete, he frames an example using the eternal motions of the celestial sphere, which were accepted by the philosophers of his time. This lesson walks through the contradiction and the illustrative example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the two parts: a general proof and an example.
The text is cut off at the end; focus on the completed reasoning.
2
Understand the initial contradiction
Sanusi claims that if judgment had a beginning, we would have a finite number (the number of judgments) that becomes infinite by adding one. Identify why this is contradictory: adding a finite to a finite must remain finite.
3
Analyze the celestial example
Consider the example of motions of the celestial sphere. Today we judge that an infinite number of motions have elapsed before today. Then another judgment on the previous motion, and so on. If these judgments began, they form a finite series. But then adding one more judgment (today's) should not yield an infinite count.
The example illustrates the abstract contradiction in a concrete setting.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask: why does this matter for theology? It shows that the eternal attributes (like God's knowledge or will) cannot have a beginning when related to an infinite past. This strengthens the case for the eternity of God's attributes.
This connects to broader discussions of divine essence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما كون الحكم له أوّل فباطل أيضاً ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ أنْ يُوجَدَ عددٌ متناه في نفسِهِ ، لكن زدنا عليه واحداً فصار الجميع غير متناه ! وبطلان هذا اللازم ظاهرُ ؛ لأنَّ زيادة الواحدِ على عددٍ ما زيادة شيء متناه ، والفَرْضُ أنَّ المزيدَ عليهِ متناه أيضاً ، فيكون مجموعهما متناهياً ضرورةً ، فالحكم بأنَّ المجموع غير متناه واضح البطلان وأما بيانُ لزوم هذا المحال على تقدير انتهاء الحكم : فلنفرض مثالاً على أصلهم يتضحُ فيهِ ذلكَ ؛ وذلكَ أنْ نَفَرِضَ في حركاتِ الفلكِ مثلاً وجود حكم في يومنا هذا بانقضاء ما لا نهاية لهُ مِنَ الحركاتِ قبلَهُ ، ثم كذلكَ حكم آخرُ في الحركة التي تلي حركة يومنا هذا قبله ، ثم هكذا توالَتِ الأحكامُ ؛ فإِنْ فُرِضَ تواليها أبداً بحيثُ
As for the judgment having a beginning, it is also false, because upon it would follow that there exists a number finite in itself, but we added one to it, so the whole becomes infinite! The falsehood of this necessary consequence is apparent, because the addition of one to a number is the addition of something finite, and the assumption is that what is added to is also finite, so their sum must be finite. So the judgment that the sum is infinite is clearly false. As for the explanation of the entailment of this absurdity on the assumption that the judgment has an end: let us suppose an example according to their principle to clarify it; and that is that we suppose in the motions of the celestial sphere, for example, the existence of a judgment on this our day of the elapsing of an infinite number of motions before it, then likewise another judgment on the motion that precedes the motion of our day, then thus the judgments follow in succession; if their succession is supposed perpetually such that...
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage continues the proof that the judgment is impossible by examining the second alternative: that the judgment has a beginning. Sanusi argues this leads to a contradiction: we would have a finite number (the number of judgments) but adding one to it makes it infinite. But adding a finite to a finite must yield a finite, so calling the sum infinite is clearly absurd. To demonstrate the entailment, Sanusi gives a concrete example using the motions of the celestial sphere (حركات الفلك), which according to the philosophers are eternal. He imagines a judgment today that an infinite number of motions have elapsed before today. Then another judgment on the motion immediately before today's motion, and so on, forming a succession of judgments. If this succession goes on for an infinite time (أبداً), then the total number of judgments would be infinite? Actually, the argument is cut off at the page end, but the point is that the number of judgments would be finite if they have a beginning, yet when we consider them together, they would be infinite—a contradiction. The example illustrates that if judgments have a beginning, the number of 'infinite past' judgments would be finite, but then adding one more (the judgment on today) would not produce an infinite sum if all are finite. The text ends with 'بحيثُ' (such that), so the full argument is not here, but the core contradiction is clear.

Proof: Adding One Motion Cannot Create an Infinite Chain
Sanusi concludes a proof that adding one motion to a finite set cannot yield an infinite chain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why adding one finite motion to a finite set cannot produce an infinite chain, and how this refutes the idea of an endless series of contingent events.

Core Idea
Finite plus finite is finite; infinite chains cannot be built by adding finite units.

Sanusi presents a tight logical argument to close the door on the possibility of an infinite sequence of motions. He takes a concrete finite segment — say a thousand motions — and shows that both before and after a certain motion the counts are finite. Adding one motion cannot turn a finite into an infinite. This is a key move in kalam for proving the existence of a beginning to the universe.

Guided Path
1
Understand the finite scenario
The author posits a finite set of motions (e.g., a thousand). Recognize that both the set before and after a certain motion are finite. This is crucial.
The example of a thousand motions makes the argument concrete.
2
Follow the contradiction
If adding one motion could make the finite infinite, then a finite set plus one would be infinite, which is absurd. See how the argument forces the opponent to admit that no actual infinite can exist.
3
Generalize the argument
The author says this applies to all claimed endless temporal sequences. See how this proof knocks down the hypothesis of an infinite regress.
This is a standard kalam argument for the temporal finitude of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زيادة هذه الحركةِ الواحدة فيها ؛ لأنَّ ما قبل هذه الحركة متناه ، وإلا لَوُجِدَ الحكم عليه بعدم النهايةِ ، والفَرْضُ انقطاعه (۱) ، وما بعدها متناه أيضاً ؛ إذ أعلاه ألف حركة ، ولا ريبَ أَنَّها متناهيةٌ . فإذاً ؛ لا سبب لعدم النهاية في جميع الأحكام إلا زيادة تلكَ الحركة الواحدة ، فقد لزمَ أنَّ ما يتناهى ؛ وهو ما قبل تلك الحركة الواحدةِ وما بعدَها مِنَ الحركاتِ .. صارَ لا يتناهى بسبب زيادة حركة واحدة فيه ؛ وهي الحركةُ التي تلي الألف قبلَها ، وإِن شئتَ فاقتصر على ذكر ما قبل هذه الحركة فإنَّهُ يتناهى ، وقد صار لا يتناهى عند زيادة تلك الحركةِ عليهِ ، وهو أقرب وأظهرُ (۲) ، والله أعلم . ولا يخفى عليك إجراء مثل هذا في سائر ما قالوا بهِ مِنْ حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ، وبعد هذا البيانِ لا يبقى عليك إشكال في لفظ العقيدة ، وبالله التوفيق (۳) ، ولا حول ولا قُوَّةَ إلا بالله .
The addition of this one motion to it, because what is before this motion is finite, otherwise the judgment of non-finiteness would apply to it, and the assumption is its cessation (1); and what is after it is also finite, since its highest point is a thousand motions, and there is no doubt that they are finite. So there is no cause for non-finiteness in all judgments except the addition of that one motion. So it necessarily follows that what is finite — that is, what is before that one motion and what is after it of motions — becomes non-finite due to the addition of one motion to it, which is the motion that follows the thousand before it. And if you wish, restrict yourself to mentioning what is before this motion, for it is finite, and it becomes non-finite when that motion is added to it, and this is closer and clearer (2). And God knows best. And it is not hidden from you to apply something similar to all that they have said about contingent things with no beginning. After this explanation, no difficulty remains for you concerning the phrase 'the creed'. And with God is success (3), and there is no power and no strength except with God.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a concluding proof demonstrating that the assumption of an infinite chain of temporal motions leads to a contradiction. The author takes a specific finite segment of motion (e.g., a thousand motions) and shows that both before and after a certain motion, the sets are finite. Adding a single motion cannot turn a finite set into an infinite one; therefore, the thesis that there can be an infinite series of contingent events is false. The author then generalizes the argument to all claimed endless temporal sequences and asserts that after this clarification, any confusion about the creed is removed. The proof relies on the impossibility of generating an actual infinite by adding finite increments.

Refutation of Infinite Regress (Tasalsul)
Sanusi proves the impossibility of infinite regress in causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof that an infinite regress of causes is impossible and why the first cause must be eternal.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible because it would require an endless chain of contingent beings with no beginning.

Sanusi now tackles a crucial argument for the existence of a necessary being: the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes. If every being were contingent, we would need an infinite chain of originators, which is absurd. This lesson walks through his reductio ad absurdum proof, showing that the only alternative is a self-sufficient, eternal first cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Sanusi uses a conditional structure.
2
Follow the reductio argument
Assume for argument's sake that the originator of the world is contingent. What follows? Trace the logical steps.
The argument leads to two impossible alternatives.
3
Notice the two impossible outcomes
The contingent originator would need another originator. If that originator is the original thing itself, circularity occurs. If it is another, infinite regress occurs. Both are impossible.
4
Understand why infinite regress is impossible
Infinite regress means an endless chain of contingent beings with no first cause. Sanusi calls this 'a void with no end' – rationally unacceptable.
Even opponents concede this point.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Since infinite regress and circularity are impossible, the only alternative is an eternal, uncaused first cause – Allah.
This argument is central to Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حادثاً؛ إذ لا واسطة بينهما في حق كل موجود، لكن كونه حادثاً محال؛ لأنَّهُ يُوجِبُ افتقاره إلى محدثِ، لما عرفتَ من وجوب افتقار كل حادث إلى محدث، ثم ننقل الكلام إلى محدثه، فيكون حادثاً كالأول، فيفتقر أيضاً إلى محدث، فإن كان محدثه الأول الذي كان أثراً له.. لزم الدور، وإن كان غيره لزم في الغير ما لزم فيه وتسلسل، والتسلسل محال؛ لما عرفت استحالة حوادث لا أول لها، والخصوم القائلون بذلك سلموا أن التسلسل في الأسباب والمسببات مستحيل. وإلى إبطال التسلسل أشرت بقولي في العقيدة: (لما في الأول - أعني: التسلسل - من فراغ ما لا نهاية له) أعني: وقد مر بيان استحالته، والباء في قوله: (بالعدد) بمعنى (في) أي: ما لا نهاية له في عدده.
It would be contingent; for there is no intermediate between them in the right of every existing thing, but its being contingent is impossible because it necessitates its need for an originator, as you know from the obligation of every contingent needing an originator. Then we transfer the speech to its originator, so it becomes contingent like the first, so it also needs an originator. If its originator is the first which was its effect, then circularity follows; and if it is other than it, then what follows in the other is what follows in it, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. And infinite regress is impossible because you know the impossibility of contingents with no beginning. And the opponents who say that have conceded that infinite regress in causes and effects is impossible. And towards refutation of infinite regress I pointed by my saying in the Creed: 'because in the first – meaning infinite regress – there is a void (farāgh) with no end.' I mean: and the explanation of its impossibility has passed. And the ba in his saying 'bi-al-ʿadad' is in the sense of 'fī', i.e., that which has no end in its number.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against infinite regress (tasalsul) in causal chains. The argument begins by assuming, for reductio, that the originator of the world were itself contingent (ḥādith). If so, it would require an originator (muḥdith). That originator would either be the original thing (circularity) or something else. If something else, that other would also be contingent and need another originator, leading to an infinite regress. The author states that infinite regress is impossible because it would imply an infinite series of contingent events with no beginning, which is impossible. He notes that even the opponents (those who deny a necessary being) concede that infinite regress in causes and effects is impossible. Then the author refers to his own creedal statement where he mentioned 'a void with no end' as the absurdity in infinite regress. He explains that 'bi-al-ʿadad' (by number) means 'fī al-ʿadad' (in number), i.e., an endless number. Thus the proof shows that the first cause must be eternal (qadīm), not contingent.

Key Terms
تسلسل
حادث
محدث
دور
فراغ ما لا نهاية له

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress of causes is possible because time or matter is infinite.
Correction: Sanusi argues that an infinite regress of contingent beings would require an infinite chain with no first cause, which is rationally impossible. Even opponents of a necessary being concede this.

Checkpoint

A) Because it leads to circularity.
B) Because it would imply an infinite chain of contingents with no beginning, which is impossible.
C) Because the opponents denied it.
D) Because the author said so in his creed.

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress of contingent causes is impossible because it would entail an endless series with no beginning, which is logically absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refutation of Circularity (Dawr)
Sanusi refutes circular causation by showing it leads to self-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why circular causation (dawr) is logically impossible and how this refutation supports the proof for an eternal, uncaused Creator.

Core Idea
Circular causation makes a thing prior and posterior to itself—an absurd impossibility.

Sanusi now tackles a potential objection: what if the causal chain loops back on itself? He demonstrates that any circular causal chain forces a thing to be both prior and posterior to itself, which is impossible. This solidifies the argument that the chain must end in an eternal, uncaused Maker, a conclusion so clear that no rational person has ever denied it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of circularity (dawr)
Read the opening sentence where Sanusi defines circularity as a thing being prior and posterior to itself. This paradoxical definition sets up the proof.
The term 'dawr' literally means 'turning' or 'cycle'.
2
Follow the proof for self-priority
Trace the logical steps that show how A must be prior to itself because A is prior to its maker (which is an effect of A) and that maker is prior to A. This forces A to be prior to its own prior, hence prior to itself by two degrees.
Pay attention to the two degrees of priority: A is prior to its maker, and its maker is prior to A.
3
Understand the necessity of the eternal Maker
Read the concluding sentence: because the proof of the Maker's eternity is so clear and free of doubt, no rational person has ever claimed the Maker of the world is contingent. This connects the refutation of circularity to the main argument.
This conclusion shows the strength of the proof for qidam al-Ṣāniʿ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا إبطالُ الدَّورِ: فإليهِ أشرتُ بقولي في العقيدة : (وفي الثاني يعني : الدور - مِنْ كونِ الشيء الواحدِ سابقاً على نفسه مسبوقاً بها ) ، أمَّا لزوم سبقيتِهِ على نفسِهِ : فلأنَّ صانعَهُ أثرٌ لَهُ ، فيجبُ أنْ يتقدَّمَ على صانعه ؛ لوجوبِ سبقِ المؤثر على أثرِهِ ، لكنَّهُ هو أيضاً أثر لصانعه فيجب أن يتقدَّمَ صانعُهُ عليهِ لعين ما ذُكِرَ ، فلزمَ أَنْ يتقدَّمَ على نفسِهِ بمرتبتين ؛ لأنَّهُ مُقدَّمٌ على صانعِهِ المقدَّم على نفسه ، والمقدَّم على المقدَّم على الشيء مُقدَّم على ذلكَ الشيء ضرورةً ، ولذلك أيضاً يجب أنْ يتأَخَّرَ عن نفسِهِ بمرتبتين ، وهو الذي عنيتُ بقولي : ( مسبوقاً بها ( ، وذلكَ لأنَّهُ أثر لصانعِهِ ، فيتأخَرُ عنه ، وصانعَهُ أَثرُ لَهُ ، فيتأَخَّرُ عنه ، والمؤخَّرُ عن المؤخّر عن الشيء مؤخّرٌ عن ذلك الشيء ضرورةً . وبالجملة : فاللازم في الدورِ أنْ يتقدَّمَ حصولُ الشيء على حصولِ نفسه بمرتبتين ، وأن يتأخَّرَ حصوله عن حصول نفسه بمرتبتين ، والتقدم والتأخر على ما ذُكِرَ متلازمان ، ولظهور برهان قدم الصانع وانتفاء الشبهة فيه .. لم يقل أحدٌ مِنَ العقلاء بحدوثِ صانع العالم
As for the refutation of circularity (dawr): I pointed to it by my saying in the Creed: 'And in the second – meaning circularity – [it consists] of the single thing being prior to itself and posterior to it.' As for the necessity of its priority over itself: because its maker is an effect of it, so it must be prior to its maker, due to the necessity of the cause (muʾaththir) preceding its effect. But it is also an effect of its maker, so its maker must be prior to it for the same reason. Thus it follows that it is prior to itself by two degrees, because it is prior to its maker which is prior to itself, and what is prior to that which is prior to a thing is necessarily prior to that thing. And likewise it must also be posterior to itself by two degrees, which is what I meant by my saying 'and posterior to it.' That is because it is an effect of its maker, so it is posterior to it, and its maker is an effect of it, so it is posterior to it. And what is posterior to that which is posterior to a thing is necessarily posterior to that thing. In summary, what necessarily follows in circularity is that the attainment of a thing is prior to its own attainment by two degrees, and posterior to its own attainment by two degrees. And priority and posteriority as mentioned are mutually concomitant. And due to the clarity of the proof of the eternity of the Maker (qidam al-Ṣāniʿ) and the removal of any doubt about it, no rational person has said that the maker of the world is contingent.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof against circular causation (dawr). Sanusi refers to his creedal statement that circularity involves a thing being both prior and posterior to itself. He then unpacks the proof: In a circular causal chain (A causes B, B causes A), A is an effect of B, so B must be prior to A (since cause precedes effect). But B is also an effect of A, so A must be prior to B. This forces A to be prior to itself, and specifically by two degrees: A is prior to B, and B is prior to A, so A is prior to that which is prior to A, hence prior to A itself. Similarly, A is posterior to itself by two degrees. Thus circularity entails self-priority and self-posteriority, which is impossible. The author concludes that because the proof of the eternity of the Maker is so clear and free of doubt, no rational person has ever claimed that the Maker of the world is contingent (i.e., occurred in time). This establishes the necessity of a beginningless creator.

Key Terms
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Circular causation could be a valid way to explain existence without an ultimate first cause.
Correction: Circularity is impossible because it forces a thing to be prior and posterior to itself, which is logically absurd. This demonstrates the necessity of a beginningless First Cause.

Checkpoint
What does circular causality (dawr) entail according to Sanusi?
A) A thing is both cause and effect of itself simultaneously.
B) A thing is prior to itself by one degree and posterior by one degree.
C) A thing is prior to itself by two degrees and posterior by two degrees.
D) A thing is neither prior nor posterior to itself.

Key Takeaway
Circular causation is impossible because it entails a thing being prior and posterior to itself, which is absurd; thus the causal chain must end in an eternal, uncaused First Cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi shows that circular reasoning forces a thing to be prior and posterior to itself—an impossibility. In your own spiritual life, are you aware of any beliefs or practices that rely on circular justification, where you try to make yourself the source of your own guidance or worth? How would breaking that cycle by affirming God's absolute priority change your daily choices?
?
The proof of the eternity of the Maker is so clear that 'no rational person has said that the maker of the world is contingent.' Have you ever allowed emotional attachments, cultural norms, or intellectual pride to obscure your acceptance of this rational truth? What would it cost you to fully submit your mind to this proof?
?
If every effect must have a cause outside itself, and circularity is impossible, then your very existence points to a necessary First Cause. Yet do you live as if you are self-sufficient? Examine one area today—your finances, your relationships, or your sense of purpose—where you are tempted to act as your own first cause. How can you intentionally depend on God in that area starting now?
