Causality and Created Things
intermediate
10 min
Refutation of the Naturalists' Objection on Delayed Effects
Sanusi presents and refutes the Naturalist objection regarding delayed effects of an eternal nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Naturalist claim that an eternal nature can produce the world with a temporal delay, by exposing the logical impossibility of the supposed obstacles or conditions.

Core Idea
Eternal nature cannot produce temporal effects without an infinite regress or contradiction.

The Naturalists argue that the world's Maker could be an eternal nature whose effects are delayed due to obstacles or missing conditions, just as fire burns only when contact and dryness are present. Sanusi dismantles this objection by analyzing the nature of the obstacle or condition itself, showing that it leads either to an infinite regress of originated obstacles or to the impossibility of an eternal obstacle ceasing. This lesson walks through both the objection and the refutation, revealing the logical necessity of a Creator who acts by will, not nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the structure: first the Naturalists' objection, then Sanusi's answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the Naturalists' objection
Identify their claim: an eternal nature can produce the world later due to a missing condition or an obstacle. They use the example of fire and burning.
They deny that the effect must be eternal if the cause is eternal.
3
Follow Sanusi's refutation: two cases for the obstacle
Sanusi shifts focus to the obstacle itself. He considers two possibilities: the obstacle is originated (hadith) or eternal (qadim). Trace the reasoning for each.
If originated, it requires another obstacle, leading to infinite regress. If eternal, it can never cease, so the world never exists.
4
Apply the same reasoning to conditions
Sanusi notes the condition (shart) must also be originated, leading to the same infinite regress.
This shows the Naturalist position cannot avoid the original argument.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why the objection fails and the original proof (that the world must be eternal or not exist at all) stands.
The only consistent view is that the Maker acts by will, not by a fixed nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراضُ مِنَ الطبائعيِّينَ على الدليل السابق ؛ وهو لزوم قدم العالم أو استمرار عدمه، وتقريرُهُ : أن قالوا : نختارُ أنَّ صانع الحوادثِ طبيعة ، وأنَّها قديمةٌ ، قولكم : ( فيلزمُ قدم تلك الحوادثِ ) غيرُ مسلَّم ؛ لأنَّ عدم المفارقةِ إنَّما يلزمُ في العلة مع معلولها لأنَّ تلازمهما لا يتوقَّفُ على شيء ، أما ملازمة الطبيعة مطبوعها فمتوقف على عدمِ الموانع ووجودِ الشرائط ، كما نقولُ مثلاً : تأثير النارِ بطبعها على مذهب الفلاسفة - أبعدهم الله - في احتراق الشيء .. يتوقف على وجود شرط ؛ وهو مسُّها مثلاً لذلك المحترق ، وانتفاء مانع ؛ وهو بلل ذلك الممسوس مثلاً ، أما إذا وُجِدَ مانعُها أو انتفى شرطُها فتُوجَدُ هي مع عدم مطبوعها الذي هو الاحتراق . قالوا : فإذا تقرَّرَ هذا فنقولُ : صانعُ هذه الحوادثِ طبيعةٌ قديمة ، لكن تأخَّرَ مطبوعها ولم يكن قديماً لمانع من وجودِهِ أزلاً أو فوات شرط ، فلما انتفى المانع ووُجِدَ الشرط فيما لا يزالُ . . وُجِدَتْ تلك الحوادث ، فلا يلزم على هذا قدمُ الحوادثِ ولا استمرار عدمها كما زعمتم . وجوابُهُ : أنَّا ننقلُ الكلام معهم إلى ذلك المانع مِنْ وجودِ الحوادثِ أوِ الشرط لها المتأخر وجودُهُ ، فنقولُ : ذلك المانع مِنْ تأثير الطبيعة في وجود الحوادث أزلاً لا يخلو : إما أن تُقدروه قديماً أو حادثاً : فإنْ كانَ حادثاً : افتقر إلى مُحدِثٍ ، والمحدِثُ على أصلكم طبيعةٌ قديمة ، فتحتاجون إلى تقديرِ مانع آخر منعَ مِنْ وجودِ هذا المانعِ الحادث أزلاً ، والمانعُ مِنْ تأثير الطبيعة قد اخترتُم أَنَّهُ حادث ، فيكون هذا المانع الثاني حادثاً ، ويفتقرُ أيضاً في تأخير وجودِهِ عن طبيعته القديمةِ إلى تقديرِ مانعِ آخرَ حادث ، ثم كذلك هذا المانع الآخرُ ، ويتسلسل ، فيلزم وجود حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ، وقد سبق برهان استحالته ، وإن منعوا التسلسل في الموانع الحادثة وجعلوا لها مبدأ . . لزم قدمُ حوادثِ العالم ؛ لعرُو الطبيعة المؤثرة فيها عن المانع ازلاً. وإنْ كانَ المانعُ مِنْ وجودِ العالم قديماً : لزم ألا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم حتى ينعدم مانعُهُ القديمُ ، لكن عدم القديم محال ، وقد سبق برهانه ، فوجود العالم المتوقفِ عليهِ محال. وهكذا نقولُ في الشرط المتأخّرِ وجوده عن الطبيعةِ : إِنَّهُ حادث فيفتقر إلى مُحدِثٍ ، والمحدِثُ على أصلهم طبيعة قديمة ، فيحتاجون أيضاً إلى تقديرِ مانع مِنْ وجودِ هذا الشرط أزلاً ، أو فواتِ شرط لم يُوجَدْ إلا فيما لا يزالُ ، وننقلُ الكلامَ إلى مانع الشرط وإلى شرط الشرط ، ويلزمُ ما لزمَ أَوَّلاً مِنَ التسلسلِ إِنْ قَدَّرْتَ الشروط أوِ الموانع.
An objection from the Naturalists against the previous proof, which is the necessity of the eternity of the world or the continuation of its non-existence. Its presentation: they said: We choose that the Maker of accidents is a nature, and that it is eternal. Your statement: 'The eternity of those accidents follows' is not granted; because the inseparability is necessary only in the cause with its effect, since their association does not depend on anything. As for the association of a nature with its product, it depends on the absence of obstacles and the presence of conditions, as we say for example: the effect of fire by its nature according to the school of the philosophers — may God distance them — in burning a thing depends on the existence of a condition, namely its contact with that combustible, and the absence of an obstacle, namely the moisture of that contacted thing. But if its obstacle exists or its condition is absent, then it exists while its product, which is burning, is absent. They said: When this is established, we say: The Maker of these accidents is an eternal nature, but its product was delayed and was not eternal due to an obstacle to its existence from eternity or the lack of a condition. When the obstacle ceased and the condition existed in what will be, those accidents existed. So the eternity of accidents does not follow from this, nor the continuation of their non-existence, as you claimed. Its answer: We shift the discussion with them to that obstacle to the existence of the accidents or the condition for them whose existence is delayed, and we say: That obstacle to the effect of the nature in causing accidents from eternity is not free: either you consider it eternal or originated. If it is originated, it requires an originator, and the originator according to your principle is an eternal nature. So you need to suppose another obstacle that prevented the existence of this originated obstacle from eternity, and the obstacle to the effect of the nature you have chosen as originated. Thus this second obstacle becomes originated, and it also in delaying its existence from its eternal nature depends on supposing another originated obstacle, and so on for this other obstacle, and there is an infinite regress, so the existence of accidents with no first arises. And the proof of its impossibility has preceded. And if they deny the infinite regress in originated obstacles and make them have a beginning, then the eternity of the accidents of the world follows, because the effective nature becomes free from the obstacle from eternity. And if the obstacle to the existence of the world is eternal, then it is necessary that nothing of the world exists until its eternal obstacle ceases, but the cessation of the eternal is impossible, and its proof has preceded, so the existence of the world which depends on it is impossible. And similarly we say regarding the condition whose existence is delayed from the nature: it is originated, so it requires an originator, and the originator according to their principle is an eternal nature. So they also need to suppose an obstacle to the existence of this condition from eternity, or the lack of a condition that did not exist except in what will be, and we shift the discussion to the obstacle of the condition and to the condition of the condition, and what first followed of infinite regress follows whether you consider the conditions or the obstacles.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection by the Naturalists (الطبائعيِّين) against a previous proof that argued for either the eternity of the world or the perpetual non-existence of accidents. The Naturalists claim that the Maker of events (الحوادث) could be an eternal nature (طبيعة) whose effects are delayed due to obstacles (موانع) or missing conditions (شروط). They illustrate with fire: its burning effect depends on contact and absence of moisture. So the world's events can appear later when obstacles are removed and conditions are met, without implying their eternity. The response then analyzes the obstacle or condition itself. If the obstacle is originated (حادث), it requires an originator, which on the Naturalists' view is an eternal nature. That leads to an infinite regress of originated obstacles, which is impossible (as previously proven). If they try to stop the regress by positing a first obstacle, then the nature would have been free from obstacles from eternity, resulting in eternal accidents. If the obstacle is eternal (قديم), then it can never cease, making the world's existence impossible. The same reasoning applies to the condition, which must be originated and leads to regress. Thus the objection fails, and the original proof stands.

Key Terms
طبيعة
طبائعييون
مانع
شرط
تسلسل
قدم
حادث
مطبوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A natural cause can produce its effect unconditionally, without needing any conditions or absence of obstacles.
Correction: Natural causes require specific conditions and absence of obstacles; but even when such conditions exist, they themselves must be explained, leading to an infinite regress or impossibility if the cause is eternal.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue would happen if the obstacle preventing the eternal nature from producing the world were eternal?
A) The world would have been created at the same time as the nature.
B) The world would never exist because an eternal obstacle cannot cease.
C) The obstacle would be originated and require an infinite regress.
D) The nature would not be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The Naturalists' objection fails because the supposed obstacle or condition for the world's creation must itself be explained, leading either to an infinite regress or to the impossibility of the world's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that Allah's actions are conditioned by something outside His will, such as time or circumstances? How does this lesson's refutation of conditions and obstacles strengthen your conviction that He is absolutely free and self-sufficient?
?
The Naturalists' argument fails because any delay in creation would require an endless chain of explanations. Does your own heart ever feel distant from Allah, as if a 'condition' is missing or an 'obstacle' is present? How does the knowledge that no such obstacle can exist for Allah's mercy challenge you to seek Him without hesitation?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's command is unconditional and immediate, what changes in the way you pray, repent, or trust His promises today?

Causality and Created Things

The Naturalist Dilemma: Two Possibilities for the Hindrance
Sanusi presents the two possibilities for the hindrance to the world's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why naturalists must posit a hindrance to explain the world's delayed creation and the two possible natures (originated or eternal) of that hindrance.

Core Idea
The hindrance to the world's creation must be either originated or eternal—both lead to impossible consequences.

Naturalists claim the world is caused by an eternal nature. To explain why that nature didn't produce the world from eternity, they must assume a hindrance. In this passage, Sanusi sets up the core dilemma: is that hindrance itself originated or eternal? Each option leads to a distinct impossibility, and the argument unfolds from this simple fork.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Start with the passage where Sanusi introduces the hindrance problem. Notice how he states the two possibilities: the hindrance is either originated (hadith) or eternal (qadim).
This fork is the backbone of the entire refutation.
2
Understand the naturalist position
Recognize that naturalists believe in an eternal nature that would have acted immediately. A hindrance is needed to delay creation.
3
Focus on the first scenario
Sanusi first tackles the scenario where the hindrance is originated. He shows this leads to an infinite regress of hindrances.
We will explore this step-by-step in the next lesson.
4
Preview the outcome
Note that both branches of the dilemma ultimately lead to impossibility—either infinite regress or an eternal hindrance that never ceases.
This sets up the detailed arguments in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنْ كانَ حادثاً : افتقر إلى مُحدِثٍ ، والمحدِثُ على أصلكم طبيعةٌ قديمة ، فتحتاجون إلى تقديرِ مانع آخر منعَ مِنْ وجودِ هذا المانعِ الحادث أزلاً ، والمانعُ مِنْ تأثير الطبيعة قد اخترتُم أَنَّهُ حادث ، فيكون هذا المانع الثاني حادثاً ، ويفتقرُ أيضاً في تأخير وجودِهِ عن طبيعته القديمةِ إلى تقديرِ مانعِ آخرَ حادث ، ثم كذلك هذا المانع الآخرُ ، ويتسلسل ، فيلزم وجود حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ، وقد سبق برهان استحالته ، وإن منعوا التسلسل في الموانع الحادثة وجعلوا لها مبدأ . . لزم قدمُ حوادثِ العالم ؛ لعرؤ الطبيعة المؤثرة فيها عن المانع ازلاً . وإنْ كانَ المانعُ مِنْ وجودِ العالم قديماً : لزم ألا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم حتى ينعدم مانعُهُ القديمُ ، لكن عدم القديم محال ، وقد سبق برهانه ، فوجود العالم المتوقفُ عليهِ محال . وهكذا نقولُ في الشرط المتأخّرِ وجوده عن الطبيعةِ : إِنَّهُ حادث فيفتقر إلى مُحدِثٍ ، والمحدِثُ على أصلهم طبيعة قديمة ، فيحتاجون أيضاً إلى تقديرِ مانع مِنْ وجودِ هذا الشرط أزلاً ، أو فواتِ شرط لم يُوجَدْ إلا فيما لا يزالُ ، وننقلُ الكلامَ إلى مانع الشرط وإلى شرط الشرط ، ويلزمُ ما لزمَ أَوَّلاً مِنَ التسلسلِ إِنْ قدَّرْتَ الشروط أوِ الموانع حادثة ، وعدم القديم إِنْ قُدْرَ مانع الشرطِ قديماً . وإلى هذا الاعتراض وجوابه أشرتُ بقولي : ( فإنْ أُجيبَ التأخُرِ في الطبيعة ... ) إلى آخرِهِ ، وإِنَّما خصصت هذا الجواب بالطبيعة لعدم تأتي تقديرِ المانع أو فواتِ الشرط في تأثيرِ العِلَّةِ فالدليل السابق ناهض فيها ، ولا يُتوهم عليه جواب
If it is originated (hadith), it is in need of an originator (muhdith), and the originator according to your principle is an eternal (qadim) nature. So you need to assume another hindrance (mani') that prevented this originated hindrance from existing eternally. And the hindrance from the effect of nature you have chosen to be originated. So this second hindrance is originated, and it also, in delaying its existence from its eternal nature, is in need of assuming another originated hindrance. Then likewise this other hindrance, and so on in an infinite regress (tasalsul). Thus the existence of events with no first (la awwala laha) becomes necessary, and the proof of its impossibility has already preceded. And if they deny infinite regress in originated hindrances and give them a beginning, then the eternity of the events of the world becomes necessary, because the effective nature is free from the hindrance eternally. And if the hindrance from the existence of the world is eternal: then it would be necessary that nothing of the world exist until its eternal hindrance ceases. But the cessation of the eternal is impossible, and its proof has already preceded. So the existence of the world, which depends on that, is impossible. And similarly we say concerning the condition (shart) whose existence is delayed relative to nature: it is originated, so it is in need of an originator, and the originator according to their principle is an eternal nature. So they also need to assume a hindrance from the existence of this condition eternally, or the absence of a condition that did not exist except in what never ceases. And we transfer the discussion to the hindrance of the condition and to the condition of the condition. And what followed first from infinite regress follows if you assume the conditions or hindrances to be originated, and the impossibility of the eternal if the hindrance of the condition is assumed to be eternal. And to this objection and its answer I pointed in my saying: 'If the delay in nature is answered...' to its end. And I specified this answer for nature only because the assumption of a hindrance or the absence of a condition is not feasible in the effect of the cause (illa); the previous proof is sufficient for it, and no answer can be imagined for it.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a naturalist argument about the creation of the world. The naturalists claim that the world is caused by an eternal nature, but they must explain why that nature did not produce the world eternally—they posit a hindrance (mani') that prevented it. The author examines two possibilities for this hindrance: it is either originated (hadith) or eternal (qadim). If the hindrance is originated, then it itself needs an originator, which according to them is an eternal nature; but then that nature must have had a hindrance preventing it from creating the hindrance eternally, leading to an infinite regress of originated hindrances, which has already been proven impossible. If they try to avoid infinite regress by giving the first hindrance a beginning, then before that beginning there was no hindrance, so the nature would have been acting, meaning the world would be eternal—also impossible. If the hindrance is eternal, then the world can never exist because an eternal hindrance cannot cease to exist. The same reasoning applies to a condition (shart) that is delayed in existence relative to nature: it must be originated, leading to the same infinite regress or impossibility. The author then references his own poetic line where he gave this answer, and notes that this answer is specific to the nature theory; for a true cause (illa), the previous proof (likely from the argument of dhari'a) suffices and no such objection can be raised.

Key Terms
حادث (originated)
قديم (eternal)
مانع (hindrance)
طبيعة (nature)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hindrance could be originated without further regress because the original nature creates it directly.
Correction: But the nature could have created it at any time, so there must be a reason (another hindrance) why it didn't create it earlier, leading to infinite regress.

Checkpoint
What is the immediate consequence if the hindrance to the world's creation is originated?
A) The world must be eternal.
B) An infinite regress of originated hindrances follows.
C) The hindrance must be eternal.
D) The nature must be originated.

Key Takeaway
An originated hindrance cannot be the first; it leads to an infinite regress of hindrances, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to trace the causes of something in your life—your existence, your faith, or even a habit—only to find an endless chain? Did you stop at a First Cause? What would it mean for you if you couldn't find one?
?
Sanusi shows that an infinite regress of originated things is impossible. Where in your own thinking do you rely on a chain of reasoning that ultimately rests on something uncaused? Are you willing to let that uncaused thing be the Creator?
?
The naturalist believes nature is eternal and creates. Is there any part of your life where you treat 'nature' or 'time' as an ultimate cause, avoiding the need for a Creator? How does this argument confront that assumption?

Causality and Created Things

Avoiding Regress Leads to Eternal World
Sanusi shows that avoiding infinite regress leads to the eternity of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that if naturalists try to avoid infinite regress by giving the originated hindrance a beginning, it implies the world is eternal, which is also impossible.

Core Idea
If the first hindrance has a beginning, then before it, nature had no hindrance—so the world would have existed eternally.

After showing that an infinite regress of originated hindrances is impossible, Sanusi considers the naturalist's possible escape: what if they deny the regress and claim the first hindrance simply began to exist at some time without a prior hindrance? In this lesson, we see that this escape leads to an equally impossible consequence—the eternity of the world, which contradicts observed contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the second scenario
Locate the line where Sanusi says: 'If they deny infinite regress...' and consider the alternative.
2
Understand the logic
If the first hindrance has a beginning, then before that moment, there was no hindrance. The nature would have been free to act, so it would have produced the world from eternity.
This makes the world eternal, which is against the naturalists' own claim that the world is originated.
3
Connect to previous proof
Sanusi refers to a previous proof that an eternal chain of events is impossible.
This is the same proof used against infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنْ كانَ حادثاً : افتقر إلى مُحدِثٍ ، والمحدِثُ على أصلكم طبيعةٌ قديمة ، فتحتاجون إلى تقديرِ مانع آخر منعَ مِنْ وجودِ هذا المانعِ الحادث أزلاً ، والمانعُ مِنْ تأثير الطبيعة قد اخترتُم أَنَّهُ حادث ، فيكون هذا المانع الثاني حادثاً ، ويفتقرُ أيضاً في تأخير وجودِهِ عن طبيعته القديمةِ إلى تقديرِ مانعِ آخرَ حادث ، ثم كذلك هذا المانع الآخرُ ، ويتسلسل ، فيلزم وجود حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ، وقد سبق برهان استحالته ، وإن منعوا التسلسل في الموانع الحادثة وجعلوا لها مبدأ . . لزم قدمُ حوادثِ العالم ؛ لعرؤ الطبيعة المؤثرة فيها عن المانع ازلاً . وإنْ كانَ المانعُ مِنْ وجودِ العالم قديماً : لزم ألا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم حتى ينعدم مانعُهُ القديمُ ، لكن عدم القديم محال ، وقد سبق برهانه ، فوجود العالم المتوقفُ عليهِ محال . وهكذا نقولُ في الشرط المتأخّرِ وجوده عن الطبيعةِ : إِنَّهُ حادث فيفتقر إلى مُحدِثٍ ، والمحدِثُ على أصلهم طبيعة قديمة ، فيحتاجون أيضاً إلى تقديرِ مانع مِنْ وجودِ هذا الشرط أزلاً ، أو فواتِ شرط لم يُوجَدْ إلا فيما لا يزالُ ، وننقلُ الكلامَ إلى مانع الشرط وإلى شرط الشرط ، ويلزمُ ما لزمَ أَوَّلاً مِنَ التسلسلِ إِنْ قدَّرْتَ الشروط أوِ الموانع حادثة ، وعدم القديم إِنْ قُدْرَ مانع الشرطِ قديماً . وإلى هذا الاعتراض وجوابه أشرتُ بقولي : ( فإنْ أُجيبَ التأخُرِ في الطبيعة ... ) إلى آخرِهِ ، وإِنَّما خصصت هذا الجواب بالطبيعة لعدم تأتي تقديرِ المانع أو فواتِ الشرط في تأثيرِ العِلَّةِ فالدليل السابق ناهض فيها ، ولا يُتوهم عليه جواب
If it is originated (hadith), it is in need of an originator (muhdith), and the originator according to your principle is an eternal (qadim) nature. So you need to assume another hindrance (mani') that prevented this originated hindrance from existing eternally. And the hindrance from the effect of nature you have chosen to be originated. So this second hindrance is originated, and it also, in delaying its existence from its eternal nature, is in need of assuming another originated hindrance. Then likewise this other hindrance, and so on in an infinite regress (tasalsul). Thus the existence of events with no first (la awwala laha) becomes necessary, and the proof of its impossibility has already preceded. And if they deny infinite regress in originated hindrances and give them a beginning, then the eternity of the events of the world becomes necessary, because the effective nature is free from the hindrance eternally. And if the hindrance from the existence of the world is eternal: then it would be necessary that nothing of the world exist until its eternal hindrance ceases. But the cessation of the eternal is impossible, and its proof has already preceded. So the existence of the world, which depends on that, is impossible. And similarly we say concerning the condition (shart) whose existence is delayed relative to nature: it is originated, so it is in need of an originator, and the originator according to their principle is an eternal nature. So they also need to assume a hindrance from the existence of this condition eternally, or the absence of a condition that did not exist except in what never ceases. And we transfer the discussion to the hindrance of the condition and to the condition of the condition. And what followed first from infinite regress follows if you assume the conditions or hindrances to be originated, and the impossibility of the eternal if the hindrance of the condition is assumed to be eternal. And to this objection and its answer I pointed in my saying: 'If the delay in nature is answered...' to its end. And I specified this answer for nature only because the assumption of a hindrance or the absence of a condition is not feasible in the effect of the cause (illa); the previous proof is sufficient for it, and no answer can be imagined for it.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a naturalist argument about the creation of the world. The naturalists claim that the world is caused by an eternal nature, but they must explain why that nature did not produce the world eternally—they posit a hindrance (mani') that prevented it. The author examines two possibilities for this hindrance: it is either originated (hadith) or eternal (qadim). If the hindrance is originated, then it itself needs an originator, which according to them is an eternal nature; but then that nature must have had a hindrance preventing it from creating the hindrance eternally, leading to an infinite regress of originated hindrances, which has already been proven impossible. If they try to avoid infinite regress by giving the first hindrance a beginning, then before that beginning there was no hindrance, so the nature would have been acting, meaning the world would be eternal—also impossible. If the hindrance is eternal, then the world can never exist because an eternal hindrance cannot cease to exist. The same reasoning applies to a condition (shart) that is delayed in existence relative to nature: it must be originated, leading to the same infinite regress or impossibility. The author then references his own poetic line where he gave this answer, and notes that this answer is specific to the nature theory; for a true cause (illa), the previous proof (likely from the argument of dhari'a) suffices and no such objection can be raised.

Key Terms
تسلسل (infinite regress)
حادث (originated)
قديم (eternal)
مانع (hindrance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first hindrance could be created by nature at a specific moment without needing a prior hindrance, and that doesn't make the world eternal.
Correction: If nature had no hindrance before that moment, it would have acted eternally, producing an eternal world. The moment of creation itself would be arbitrary unless preceded by a hindrance.

Checkpoint
What happens if naturalists give the first originated hindrance a beginning and deny infinite regress?
A) The hindrance becomes eternal.
B) The world becomes eternal.
C) Infinite regress still follows.
D) The nature becomes originated.

Key Takeaway
Avoiding infinite regress by positing a first hindrance simply shifts the problem to the pre-eternal activity of nature, making the world eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a seemingly neat explanation for a difficult question, only to realize it created a bigger contradiction in your beliefs? In your own life, when have you traded one problem for another rather than facing the root issue? This lesson shows that avoiding one problem (infinite regress) by positing a first hindrance leads to the impossibility of an eternal world. What 'first hindrances' have you settled for that actually brought you no closer to the truth, but only postponed a deeper reckoning?
?
The argument forces you to choose between two unacceptable conclusions: infinite regress or an eternal world. But the naturalist is forced to reject both. In your own thinking, are there areas where you hold onto two incompatible ideas because letting go of either would be too uncomfortable? What would it cost you to fully embrace the truth that this world is not self-sufficient, and that you are completely dependent on the Creator for every moment of your existence?

The Eternal Hindrance and the Condition Argument
Sanusi handles the eternal hindrance and extends the argument to conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that if the hindrance is eternal, the world can never exist, and also that the same reasoning applies to conditions (shart) tied to nature.

Core Idea
An eternal hindrance can never cease, so the world can never come to be—a clear impossibility.

Having dismantled the two possibilities for an originated hindrance, Sanusi turns to the second main branch: what if the hindrance itself is eternal? In this lesson, we see that an eternal hindrance can never cease to exist, making the world's existence impossible. Then Sanusi extends the same logic to conditions (shart) that are delayed relative to nature, showing that they too lead to the same dead ends: either an infinite regress of conditions or an eternal condition that never ceases, and thus the world cannot exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the eternal hindrance case
Find where Sanusi says: 'If the hindrance is eternal...' and understand the impossibility.
An eternal thing cannot change, so it can never cease.
2
Apply the same to conditions
Sanusi extends the argument to a condition whose existence is delayed. Note that it must be originated (since it appears later), leading to the same regress or eternal condition.
3
Note the author's remark
The author mentions that this entire answer is specific to the theory of nature. For a true cause (illa), the previous proof (from the argument of dhari'a) suffices and no such objection can be raised.
This clarifies the scope of the argument.

?

Are they not like the eternal hindrance that can never cease, or are they originated and lead to infinite excuses? How does this lesson expose the emptiness of your procrastination?
?
Think of a spiritual goal you have postponed because you were waiting for a certain condition to be met. Examine that condition—is it truly external and unavoidable, or is it just another layer of delay? What does Sanusi's argument teach you about the trap of always waiting for conditions to be perfect?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is the ʿilla (true cause) who creates without delay, then why do you act as if He is a mere nature that requires hindrances or conditions to act? How can you realign your expectation of His power from this lesson?

Denying Causal Efficacy of Elemental Mixtures
Sanusi denies causal efficacy of elemental mixtures and refutes Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari occasionalist rejection of natural causality in health and disease, and why Ibn Sina is criticized for upholding elemental causation.

Core Idea
God alone is the true cause; elemental mixtures have no effect.

Sanusi here confronts a key naturalist idea: that the balance of elements causes health. He asserts that all creation is directly from Allah, and that coincidence of events does not imply causation. This passage also levels a sharp critique at Ibn Sina for contradicting his Islamic claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Pay attention to the strong negation at the start and the subsequent examples.
Notice the structural flow: denial, counterfactual, principle, then refutation.
2
Identify the central claim
Sanusi denies that the composition or balance of elements causes health, disease, or any existence or corruption. Understand this as a rejection of natural causality.
The key phrase is 'لا تأثير له' (no effect).
3
Understand the refutation of Ibn Sina
Sanusi accuses Ibn Sina of following naturalists despite his Islamic claims. Reflect on why this is a serious charge in the context of Islamic theology.
Ibn Sina's medical poem is cited as evidence of his naturalistic views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس لاعتدال الطبيعة تأثير في الصحة ] وإذا عرفت هذا عرفت أنّ تركيب امتزاج العناصر التي يذكرها الأطباء والطبائعيونَ وانحلالها .. لا تأثيرَ لهُ في وجودِ شيء ولا في فساده ، ولا أنَّ باعتدال الطبائع تكون صحة الجسم ، ولا أنَّ بغلبة بعضها تكون الأمراض كما يزعمونَ ، بل لو كان الجسم بسيطاً لم يتركَّبْ إلا مِنْ نوع واحدٍ .. لقبلَ مِنَ الكون والفسادِ عند أهل الحق والسنَّةِ ما يقبلُهُ عند تركيبهِ مِنَ الأنواع ، واختياره جلَّ وعلا خلْقَ شيءٍ عندَ  َخلْقِهِ شيئاً آخر .. لا يدلُّ على أن لأحد مخلوقيْهِ أثراً في مخلوقِهِ الآخر ، لا بالاختيار ولا بغيره ، بل وجوده وعدمه فيما يتعلق بالتأثير سواء . ولقد ضلَّ ابن سينا وكَذَبَ ونهج منهج الطبائعيينَ معَ ادّعائِهِ الإسلام وتستره بظاهره في الدنيا .. حيثُ يقولُ في « رسالته الطبية : من الرجز]
BALANCE (i'tidal) OF NATURE HAS NO EFFECT ON HEALTH. And if you know this, you know that the composition (tarkib) and dissolution (inhilal) of the elements (anāsir) that the physicians and naturalists mention have no effect on the existence of anything nor on its corruption, nor that by balance of natures (tabā'i') the health of the body occurs, nor that by predominance of some of them diseases occur, as they claim. Rather, even if the body were simple (basīt), composed only of one type, it would be subject to generation (kawn) and corruption (fasād) according to the People of Truth and Sunna just as it is when composed of types. And His choice, exalted and high, to create a thing at the time of creating another thing does not indicate that one of His creatures has an effect on His other creature, neither by choice nor otherwise. Rather, its existence and non-existence are equal with respect to effect (ta'thīr). And indeed Ibn Sīnā went astray and lied, and followed the path of the naturalists despite his claim of Islam and his outward concealment in this world, when he says in his 'Medical Epistle' in rajaz meter:
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage asserts a strong occasionalist position: the mixture of elements (as per medicine and natural philosophy) has no causal effect whatsoever on existence, corruption, health, or disease. The author denies that a balance of humors causes health or that their predominance causes disease. To illustrate, he says that even if the body were simple (composed of a single element), it would still undergo generation and corruption just as it does when composed of multiple elements—this shows that composition is irrelevant. The fact that God chooses to create one thing at the same time as another does not imply that one created thing influences the other; they are independent. Finally, he condemns Ibn Sina for following the naturalists despite claiming Islam, quoting his medical poem.

Key Terms
اعتدال (balance)
تركيب (composition)
انحلال (dissolution)
عناصر (elements)
كون وفساد (generation and corruption)
تأثير (effect)
طبائع (natures)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That two events occurring together (like a mixture and health) implies causation.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that simultaneous occurrence does not indicate causation; all effects are directly from Allah.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say about a body that is simple (composed of only one type of element)?
A) It would be incorruptible.
B) It would be subject to generation and corruption just like a composite body.
C) It would be healthier than a composite body.
D) It would prove the naturalists' theory.

Key Takeaway
The balance or composition of elements has no inherent causal power; all effects are directly from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall ill and seek medical treatment, does your heart truly believe that the medicine has no effect, and that Allah alone creates health? Or do you secretly rely on the medication while verbally attributing healing to Allah? Take a moment to examine where your trust actually lies.
?
Sanusi calls Ibn Sina a liar for outwardly claiming Islam while believing in natural causation. Are there areas of your life where your actions and assumptions betray a belief in autonomous causes—like thinking 'this diet made me healthy' or 'I succeeded because of my effort'—while professing that Allah is the only Creator and Provider? What would it cost you to live as if this is literally true?
?
If you truly internalized that existence and non-existence are equal with respect to effect, how would it change your fear of losing something? Would you still cling to health, wealth, or relationships as if they have inherent power to give or take happiness? Ask Allah to purify your heart from hidden shirk in causality and to make you content with His decree alone.

Causality and Created Things
beginner
4 min
No Causal Effect in Elemental Mixture
Sanusi refutes elemental mixture's causal role in health, disease, and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi denies any causal role to elemental mixture in health, disease, or existence, affirming that all causation belongs to God alone.

Core Idea
Elemental mixture has no causal power; only God creates and changes.

Having established that God is the sole creator and sustainer, Sanusi now tackles a specific claim from physicians and naturalists: that the balance or mixture of elements (hot, cold, wet, dry) causes health or disease. He refutes this by arguing that even a simple body, composed of only one type, would still undergo generation and corruption by God's direct act. Thus, mixture is irrelevant to causation. This lesson unpacks his concise but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi rejects multiple claims in sequence: mixture's effect, balance causing health, dominance causing disease, and the implied causal link between simultaneous creations.
2
Identify the main refutations
The passage refutes: (1) mixture's effect on existence/corruption, (2) balance causing health, (3) imbalance causing disease. Then provides the simple body counterexample and clarifies that God's simultaneous creation does not imply causation.
Each refutation is a distinct target.
3
Understand the simple body argument
Sanusi asks you to imagine a body made of only one element (simple). Such a body would still undergo generation and corruption just as a mixed body does. This proves that mixture is not the cause of these changes.
This is a thought experiment that isolates the supposed cause.
4
Grasp the deeper principle
The passage ends with a general principle: God's choice to create one thing at the same time as another does not indicate any causal influence between them. This is an Ash'ari denial of secondary causality.
This aligns with the earlier lesson on God's exclusive causal agency.

Source Text

وإذا عرفت هذا عرفت أنّ تركيب امتزاج العناصر التي يذكرها الأطباء والطبائعيونَ وانحلالها .. لا تأثيرَ لهُ في وجودِ شيء ولا في فساده ، ولا أنَّ باعتدال الطبائع تكون صحة الجسم ، ولا أنَّ بغلبة بعضها تكون الأمراض كما يزعمونَ (١) ، بل لو كان الجسم بسيطاً لم يتركَّبْ إلا مِنْ نوع واحدٍ .. لقبلَ مِنَ الكون والفسادِ عند أهل الحق والسنَّةِ ما يقبلُهُ عند تركيبهِ مِنَ الأنواع ، واختياره جلَّ وعلا خلْقَ شيءٍ عندَ خلْقِهِ شيئاً آخر .. لا يدلُّ على أن لأحد مخلوقيْهِ أثراً في مخلوقِهِ الآخر ، لا بالاختيار ولا بغيره ، بل وجوده وعدمه فيما يتعلق بالتأثير سواء .
And if you know this, you know that the composition of the mixture of elements which the physicians and naturalists mention and its dissolution... has no effect in the existence of anything nor in its corruption, nor that by the balance of temperaments the health of the body occurs, nor that by the dominance of some of them illnesses occur as they claim; rather, if the body were simple, composed only of one type, it would accept from generation and corruption according to the people of truth and Sunnah what it accepts when composed of the types, and His choice, glorified and exalted, to create something at the creation of another thing does not indicate that any of His creatures has an effect in another of His creatures, neither by choice nor otherwise; rather, its existence and non-existence regarding effect are equal.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a direct refutation of the naturalist claim that elemental mixture and balance have causal power over health and disease. The author argues that composition and dissolution of elements have no effect whatsoever on existence or corruption. Even if the body were simple (unmixed), it would still undergo generation and corruption by God's direct act. The fact that God creates one thing at the time of creating another does not imply that the first creature causes the second. This is a standard Ash'ari denial of secondary causation, affirming that all causal efficacy belongs to God alone.

Key Terms
امتزاج العناصر
اعتدال الطبائع
الكون والفساد
تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the mixture of elements (hot, cold, wet, dry) in the body causally produces health or disease, and that a simple body would not undergo generation and corruption.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even a simple body would undergo generation and corruption, so mixture is not the cause. All causal efficacy lies with God, not with elements.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does the mixture of elements have no effect on health or disease?
A) Because the body is composed of four elements.
B) Because a simple body would still undergo generation and corruption, showing causation is from God alone.
C) Because physicians are ignorant of true causes.
D) Because God directly creates all changes without any habitual patterns.

Key Takeaway
Elemental mixture has no causal effect; only God's direct action brings about existence, change, and corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recover from an illness, do you truly attribute your healing to God's direct act or do you unconsciously credit the medicine or your body's natural balance? What would it mean to genuinely thank only God for your health?
?
Think of a pattern in your daily life—like the sun rising or food nourishing you. Do you ever catch yourself thinking these 'natural laws' have inherent power? How can you train your heart to see God's hand in every cause-effect sequence?
?
If you honestly believed that no creature—including your own efforts—has any causal effect, how would your reliance on God change? What would you pray for differently starting today?

Refuting Ibn Sina's Elemental Causation
Sanusi refutes Ibn Sina by quoting his poem proving elemental composition, then accusing him of following the naturalists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of Ibn Sina's reliance on the four-element theory of Hippocrates, and why he accuses Ibn Sina of lying and following the naturalists.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's own poem betrays his hidden naturalism despite his Islamic claims.

Sanusi does not merely critique Ibn Sina's philosophical position; he levels a severe personal accusation. By quoting Ibn Sina's own medical poem, Sanusi argues that Ibn Sina's belief in the four elements as causal agents contradicts his outward profession of Islam. This lesson examines how Sanusi uses Ibn Sina's words against him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the harsh tone and the quoted poem.
The poem is in rajaz meter, a simple meter often used for teaching.
2
Identify accusation and quote
Sanusi explicitly accuses Ibn Sina of lying and following the naturalists. He then quotes Ibn Sina's own poem. What point does the poem make about the body's composition?
3
Understand the logical flaw
The poem argues that because the body decomposes into elements at death, it must be composed of them. Sanusi rejects this as proof of causation. Why is decomposition not evidence that the elements cause life?
Sanusi denies that decomposition implies causal efficacy; it is merely a physical process.

The poem argues that because the body decomposes into elements at death, it must be composed of them. Sanusi rejects this as proof of causal efficacy; decomposition does not mean the elements cause generation or corruption. The accusation of 'lying' emphasizes that Ibn Sina's outward Islam contradicts his philosophical commitments.

Al-Tilimsani's Logical Dilemma on Mixture
Al-Tilimsani's logical dilemma against mixture causing new species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Tilimsani's argument that mixture cannot be the cause of new species because mere contact between bodies cannot negate their inherent forms.

Core Idea
Contact alone never destroys a form; only a true cause can.

Al-Tilimsani, a later Ash'ari scholar, sharpens the attack on natural causality by focusing on mixture. He presents a crisp dilemma: after mixing, either elements stay the same or they change. If they change, their forms must be removed—but contact is not sufficient for that removal. This argument exposes the logical gap in any theory that attributes new species to mixture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Get the overall gist of the dilemma before analyzing.
Notice the key terms: امتزاج, صور, معاني, تماس الأجسام.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
Al-Tilimsani sets up a clear either-or: either each element remains as it was after mixture, or it does not. If it does not, what causes its form to be negated?
The first branch (remaining unchanged) means no new species, so mixture fails. The second branch forces the question of causation.
3
Understand why contact cannot negate forms
The key premise: contact of bodies does not necessitate negation of their meanings (qualities). Al-Tilimsani gives the reason: lack of opposition and incompatibility (التضاد والتنافي). So mixture is not a sufficient cause.
This builds on the earlier refutation of nature and cause as efficient causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يدلُّ على أنَّ امتزاج العناصرِ لا أثرَ لهُ في حصولِ الأنواع المختلفة والأشخاص المتباينة سوى ما قدَّمناه في إبطال تأثير الطبيعة والعلة .. ما أشار إليهِ شرفُ الدين بنُ التَّلِمْساني في «شرح المعالم » قال : ( الامتزاج الموجِبُ لحصولِ الأنواع المختلفة والأشخاص المتباينة إذا حصل في العناصر لا يخلو : إما أن يبقى كلُّ عنصر على ما كان عليه أو لا ، فإن لم يبقَ فما الموجِبُ لانتفاء صورته التي كان عليها ؟! وتماس الأجسامِ لا يُوجِبُ نفْي ما فيها مِنَ المعاني(٢) ؛ لعدم التضاد والتنافي
It indicates that the mixture of elements has no effect in the occurrence of different species and diverse individuals beyond what we have presented in nullifying the effect of nature and cause. That is what Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani pointed out in his commentary on al-Ma'alim, saying: 'The mixture that necessitates the occurrence of different species and diverse individuals, when it occurs in the elements, is not free: either each element remains as it was or not. If it does not remain, what necessitates the negation of the form it had? And the contact of bodies does not necessitate the negation of what is in them of meanings, due to the lack of opposition and incompatibility.'
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument from Sharaf al-Din al-Tilimsani to disprove the causal efficacy of elemental mixture in generating new species and individuals. The argument is a dilemma: either the mixture leaves each element unchanged (in which case no new species arise), or it changes them. If it changes them, then something must have caused the removal of their original forms. Al-Tilimsani argues that mere contact of bodies cannot negate the inherent qualities (meanings) of the elements, because there is no necessary opposition between bodies that would cause such negation. Therefore, mixture is not the cause of new species; the real cause is God's direct creation.

Key Terms
امتزاج
صور
معاني
تماس الأجسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that mixture is physically impossible or that elements cannot mix at all.
Correction: Al-Tilimsani does not deny that mixture occurs; he denies that mixture has any causal effect in producing new species. The argument is about causality, not physical possibility.

Checkpoint
According to al-Tilimsani, what is the problem if each element does not remain as it was after mixture?
A) The mixture would be unnatural.
B) There is no cause for the removal of its form, since contact of bodies cannot negate qualities.
C) The elements would disappear.
D) The body would become simple.

Key Takeaway
Mixture alone cannot generate new species because contact cannot destroy the forms of elements; only Allah's direct creation can bring about new species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe a natural process producing a result, do you instinctively attribute the effect to the process itself, or do you recognize Allah as the true cause? How does Al-Tilimsani's argument challenge your default thinking?
?
Think of a situation where you felt changed by an external event—a relationship, a success, or a failure. Did you assume that event had the power to change you, or did you see it as an occasion for Allah's creation? What would it take for your heart to fully rely on Allah alone as the changer of forms?

Refuting the Generation of Species by Elemental Mixture
The author refutes the claim that mixture of elements generates species, citing al-Tilimsani and adding a further argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the kalam tradition argues that the mixture of elements cannot produce new species or individuals, and you will be able to explain the logical contradictions in the idea that mixing opposites generates a third quality.

Core Idea
Mixing elements cannot create new species; contact alone lacks the power to change forms.

This lesson tackles a key philosophical challenge to the Islamic doctrine of creation: the claim that the mixture of fundamental elements (fire, air, water, earth) can generate different species and individuals. The author cites the earlier refutation of nature as a cause and then presents a detailed argument from Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani, along with his own addition, to show that mixture cannot be the source of biological diversity. We will follow the logical structure of the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the full Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the source passage carefully. Notice that it opens with a reference to a previous refutation, then quotes al-Tilimsani, and finally adds the author's own point.
The passage is dense; take your time to absorb each logical move.
2
Understand the core dilemma
The argument presents a dichotomy: after mixture, each element either retains its original form or it does not. Trace how both options lead to impossibility.
If the form remains, no new species arises; if it is lost, what caused the loss?
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objector offers the example of hot and cold water mixing to produce lukewarm. The response shows that this scenario leads to a logical contradiction whether the interaction is instantaneous or successive.
Pay attention to the concepts of 'effector' and 'effect' in the same moment.
4
Examine the author's additional argument
The author adds that even if we assume a cycle of existence and non-existence, it leads to infinite regress, so the third quality never actually obtains.
This strengthens the refutation by covering a possible loophole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يدل على أن امتزاج العناصر لا أثر له في حصول الأنواع المختلفة والأشخاص المتباينة سوى ما قدمناه في إبطال تأثير الطبيعة والعلة.. ما أشار إليه شرف الدين بن التلمساني في «شرح المعالم» قال: (الامتزاج الموجب لحصول الأنواع المختلفة والأشخاص المتباينة إذا حصل في العناصر لا يخلو: إما أن يبقى كل عنصر على ما كان عليه أو لا، فإن لم يبق فما الموجب لانتفاء صورته التي كان عليها؟! وتماس الأجسام لا يوجب نفي ما فيها من المعاني؛ لعدم التضاد والتنافي مع تعدد المحال؛ فإنه إن اتحد محلها لزم تداخل الأجرام، وهو محال؛ إذ لو جاز ذلك لجاز وجود جملة العالم في حيز خردلة! وإن لم تنتف صورتها وجب بقاء الأمر فيها على ما كان قبل الامتزاج فإن قالوا: الماء الحار إذا لاقى الماء البارد مثلا كسر الحار من سورة البارد، والبارد من سورة الحار، فتحصل كيفية ثالثة وهي كونه فاترا. قلنا: تأثير إحدى الكيفيتين في الأخرى: إن كان في زمن واحد لزم أن يجامع وجود كل واحد منهما عدمه؛ ضرورة أن المؤثر لا بد وأن يكون حاصلا حال حصول أثره، فيكون كل واحد منهما من حيث كونه مؤثرا موجودا، أو من حيث كونه أثرا معدوما! وإن كان على التعاقب وجب وجود الأول حال عدمه؛ لتحقق إعدامه الثاني، وهو محال باتفاق (انتهى قلت: ولو فرض وجود الأول بعد عدمه وأعدم الثاني.. لزم أيضا أن يوجد الثاني بعد عدمه ليعدم الأول، ويتسلسل، فلا تحصل الكيفية الثالثة أبدا.
It indicates that the mixture of the elements has no effect in the generation of different species and diverse individuals except what we have previously presented in invalidating the effect of nature and the cause.. That which Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani indicated in Sharh al-Ma'alim, he said: (The mixture that necessitates the generation of different species and diverse individuals, when it occurs in the elements, is not free: either each element remains as it was or not. If it does not remain, then what necessitates the negation of its form that it had? The contact of bodies does not necessitate the negation of the meanings in them, due to the absence of opposition and mutual exclusion with multiple loci; for if their locus were one, the interpenetration of bodies would be necessary, which is impossible; for if that were possible, the existence of the entire world within the space of a mustard seed would be possible! And if their form is not negated, then the state must remain in it as before mixture. If they say: hot water when it meets cold water, for example, breaks the heat from the vehemence of the cold, and the cold from the vehemence of the heat, so a third quality results, which is lukewarm. We say: The effect of one of the two qualities upon the other: if it is in one moment, then it necessitates that the existence of each one of them coexists with its non-existence; necessarily, the effector must be present at the time of its effect, so each one of them, in terms of being an effector, is existent, or in terms of being an effect, is non-existent! And if it is successive, then the existence of the first at the moment of its non-existence is necessitated, due to the actualization of its eliminating the second, and that is impossible by agreement. (End.) I said: And if the existence of the first after its non-existence is assumed and it eliminates the second, then it is also necessary that the second exist after its non-existence to eliminate the first, and so it goes on infinitely, so the third quality never obtains.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophical claim that the mixture of elements (elements being fire, air, water, earth) can generate different species and individuals. The author first states that mixture has no effect beyond what was already covered in the earlier refutation of nature and cause. Then he quotes Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani's argument from Sharh al-Ma'alim. The argument proceeds as follows: If mixture occurs, each element either retains its original form or not. If it does not retain its form, what caused the loss? Contact between bodies cannot remove forms because there is no opposition or mutual exclusion between forms when they are in different loci (if they were in the same locus, that would require interpenetration, which is impossible). If the form is retained, then the state before mixture remains, so no new species arises. The objector gives the example of hot and cold water mixing to produce lukewarm. The response is a classic kalam argument: if the effect is instantaneous, then each quality must be both existent (as cause) and non-existent (as effect) at the same moment, a contradiction. If successive, then the first quality would have to exist at the moment it is being removed by the second, again a contradiction. The author then adds his own point: even if we suppose the first quality returns after being removed and then removes the second, this leads to infinite regress, so the third quality never appears. Thus, mixture cannot explain the origin of species.

Key Terms
امتزاج العناصر
صور
الأجسام
الكيفيات
التنافي
تداخل الأجرام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mixing opposites (e.g., hot and cold) produces a genuinely new third quality (e.g., lukewarm) through the interaction of their qualities.
Correction: The author shows that this scenario leads to logical contradictions (simultaneous existence and non-existence) or infinite regress, so no third quality is actually generated.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what would be the consequence if the effect of one quality on another is instantaneous?
A) The third quality would appear smoothly.
B) Each quality would have to be both existent and non-existent at the same moment.
C) The qualities would remain unchanged.
D) Interpenetration of bodies would occur.

Key Takeaway
The mixture of elements cannot generate new species or individuals because it leads to logical contradictions or infinite regress; contact alone is insufficient to change forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that physical processes (like mixing) automatically produce results without considering the direct power of Allah? After seeing the logical contradictions in this argument, can you still hold that assumption without falling into the same error? Commit to reminding yourself at least once today that every effect is directly from Allah, not from the mixture itself.
?
The objector used the example of hot and cold water to defend their view. When you encounter a seemingly convincing example from everyday experience that supports a worldview contrary to tawheed, how will you respond? Will you examine it critically as Ibn al-Tilimsani did, or accept it passively? Write down one such example you face and deconstruct it using the reasoning from this lesson.
?
This argument shows that even the simplest physical change (like water becoming lukewarm) cannot be explained by the mixture alone without logical contradiction. If you truly believe this, how should it change the way you think about the food you eat, the medicine you take, or the interactions you have? Will you see them as mere mixtures or as direct creations of Allah each moment?

The Impossibility of Two Bodies Occupying One Place
Sanusi argues against interpenetration of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is rationally impossible for two bodies to occupy the same physical space, based on Sanusi's argument.

Core Idea
Two bodies cannot share one place; else the world would fit in a mustard seed.

Sanusi begins by addressing a foundational principle: the impossibility of interpenetration of bodies. This principle is crucial for the subsequent refutation of the philosophers' claim about mixing qualities. The argument is a simple but powerful reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the short Arabic passage slowly. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Understand the argument
The argument states: if two bodies have the same place, interpenetration occurs, which is impossible because it would allow the entire world to fit in a mustard seed.
3
Consider the alternative
If the forms are not annihilated, then the state remains as before mixing, which contradicts the claim that a new quality emerges.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself why this argument is important for the broader discussion on causality and the mixing of qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع تعددِ المَحال؛ فإنَّهُ إِنِ اتَّحد محلها لزم تداخل الأجرام، وهو محال؛ إذ لو جاز ذلك لجازَ وجود جملة العالم في حيَّزِ خردلة! وإنْ لم تنتف صورتها وجبَ بقاء الأمر فيها على ما كان قبل الامتزاج فإن قالوا: الماء الحارُّ إذا لاقى الماءَ البارد مثلاً كسر الحارُّ مِنْ سورةِ الباردِ، والباردُ مِنْ سورةِ الحار، فتحصل كيفية ثالثة كونه فاتراً. قلنا: تأثير إحدى الكيفيتين في الأخرى: إنْ كانَ في زمنٍ واحدٍ لزمَ أنْ يجامع وجودُ كلِّ واحدٍ منهما عدمَهُ؛ ضرورةَ أَنَّ المؤثرَ لا بدَّ وأن يكون حاصلاً حال حصولِ أثرِهِ، فيكونُ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما مِنْ حيثُ كونه مؤثراً موجوداً، أو مِنْ حيثُ كونه أثراً معدوماً! وإنْ كانَ على التعاقب وجبَ وجودُ الأوَّلِ حال عدمِهِ؛ لتحققِ إعدامِهِ الثاني، وهو محال باتفاق. (انتهى قلتُ: ولو فُرضَ وجودُ الأوَّلِ بعد عدمِهِ وأعدم الثاني .. لزم أيضاً أنْ يُوجَدَ الثاني بعدَ عدمِهِ ليُعدِمَ الأوَّلَ، ويتسلسل، فلا تحصل الكيفية الثالثة أبداً). (1) شرح معالم أصول الدين ( ص ٢٠١ )
With the multiplicity of places; if they had a single place, then interpenetration of bodies would be necessary, and that is impossible; because if that were permissible, then it would be permissible for the entire world to exist in the space of a mustard seed! And if their forms are not annihilated, then the state of affairs would have to remain as it was before mixing. For if they say: Hot water, when it meets cold water, for example, the hot breaks the intensity of the cold, and the cold breaks the intensity of the hot, so a third quality results, that of being lukewarm. We say: The effect of one of the two qualities on the other: if it is in a single moment, then it would be necessary that the existence of each of them coexists with its non-existence; necessarily because the effecter must be present at the time of the occurrence of its effect, so each of them from the perspective of being an effecter exists, or from the perspective of being an effect is non-existent! And if it is successive, then the existence of the first at the time of its non-existence would be necessary, due to the realization of its annihilation of the second, and that is impossible by agreement. (End. I say: And if it is assumed that the first exists after its non-existence and annihilates the second, it would also be necessary that the second exist after its non-existence to annihilate the first, and an infinite regress would follow, so the third quality would never occur.) (1) Commentary on the Landmarks of the Principles of Religion (p. 201)
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument against the philosophers' view on the intermixture of qualities. The author first states the impossibility of two bodies occupying the same place (interpenetration), using the vivid example that if it were possible, the entire world could fit in a mustard seed. Then he presents an objection: when hot and cold water mix, they produce lukewarm water, which might seem to involve interpenetration of qualities. The author refutes this by analyzing the temporal aspect. He argues: if the effect of one quality on the other occurs at a single moment, then each quality would have to be both existent and non-existent simultaneously (as effecter and effect), which is a contradiction. If the effect occurs successively, then the first quality would have to exist at the time of its non-existence (to annihilate the second), which is also impossible. The commentator then adds a further refinement: even if we assume the first quality exists after its non-existence to annihilate the second, we would need the second to exist again to annihilate the first, leading to an infinite regress, so the third quality never arises.

Key Terms
تداخل الأجرام (interpenetration of bodies)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if interpenetration of bodies were possible?
A) The world would become cold.
B) The entire world could fit in a mustard seed.
C) Bodies would multiply.
D) Qualities would mix.

Key Takeaway
The principle that no two bodies can occupy the same space is a rational necessity; violating it leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What assumptions do you hold about reality that, if logically examined, might lead to similar absurd consequences? Are you willing to abandon them?
?
This argument uses a clear reductio ad absurdum. Where in your own life do you resist following a logical argument to its uncomfortable conclusion, especially regarding matters of faith or practice?
?
If you truly believe that contradictory statements cannot both be true, how does that affect the way you approach doubts or competing worldviews? Will you commit to seeking proof like Sanusi did, or settle for comfortable ambiguity?

Refuting the Philosophers' Explanation of Lukewarm Water
Sanusi refutes the philosophers' temporal causation for mixing qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the philosophers' claim that mixing hot and cold water produces a lukewarm quality through temporal causation.

Core Idea
Any explanation of mixing qualities leads to contradiction or infinite regress.

Having established the impossibility of interpenetration, Sanusi now addresses the philosophers' objection that mixing hot and cold water produces a third quality. He analyzes whether the effect of one quality on the other occurs in a single moment or successively, showing both lead to logical absurdity. The commentator adds a further infinite regress argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the philosophers' argument and Sanusi's response. Note the two cases: simultaneous or successive effect.
2
Analyze the simultaneous case
If the effect occurs in one moment, each quality must be both existent (as effecter) and non-existent (as effect) at the same time, a contradiction.
3
Analyze the successive case
If the effect is successive, the first quality must exist at the time of its non-existence to annihilate the second, which is impossible.
4
Consider the commentator's addition
The commentator shows that even if we assume the first quality exists after non-existence, it leads to infinite regress, preventing the third quality from ever occurring.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that this refutation demonstrates that the philosophers' account of mixture is rationally impossible, upholding the principles of causation.

The philosophers' claim that mixing produces a new quality is refuted because both simultaneous and successive causation lead to absurdities.

?

?

?
This lesson shows that even the simplest physical process, if left to its own causes, leads to logical absurdity. How does this expose the emptiness of relying on means and secondary causes? In what specific situation today can you consciously shift your trust from the means to the One who alone causes things to be?

Refutation of Astrological Causality and Necessity
Sanusi refutes astrological determinism with rhetorical questions about celestial particularities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the argument from specification (takhṣīṣ) to refute astrological determinism and to establish that the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) is the only cause of the particularities of celestial motions.

Core Idea
The specificities of the cosmos cannot be explained by necessity; they require a free chooser.

This passage presents a powerful critique of astrological determinism. Sanusi lists several obvious particularities of celestial motion and configuration that cannot be explained by any necessary cause. These arbitrary specificities, he argues, point decisively to a free agent who specifies each thing as He wills. Reading this passage will help you grasp the foundational kalām argument from specification against naturalist causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and the translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the series of rhetorical questions that build the argument.
2
Identify the rhetorical questions
List each question about celestial motions (e.g., why the highest sphere moves only one way, why all motions are east–west, why each planet has its own sphere, etc.).
These questions highlight arbitrary specificities that cannot be due to necessity.
3
Understand the logical structure
See how the questions lead to the conclusion that there is no cause for specification except the free agent. The author then condemns those who attribute these specificities to other than Allah.
This is the classic argument from specification (takhṣīṣ) in kalām.
4
Reflect on the concluding condemnation and supplication
Consider the strong language used against those who deny the free agent. The supplication reinforces the lesson's practical intent.
The passage ends with a prayer for preservation from afflictions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا أصغر ؟ وما بالُ الأعلى منها يتحرَّكُ حركةً واحدةً مِنَ المشرقِ إلى المغرب ، وباقي الأفلاك تتحرَّكُ حركتين ؛ إحداهما : الحركة اليومية مِنَ المشرقِ إلى المغرب ، والأخرى : حركاتها في البروج مِنَ المغرب إلى المشرقِ ؟ وما بالُ الحركاتِ كلّها اختصت بما بين المشرق والمغرب ، ولم تكن فيما بين الجنوب والشِّمالِ مثلاً ؟ ولِمَ اختص كلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ السبعةِ السَّيَّارَةِ بفَلَكِهِ المخصوص معَ جواز أنْ يكونَ في غيرِهِ ؟ ولِمَ اختصَّتْ سائر الكواكب الثابتة بالفلكِ الثامن ولم تكن في غيرِهِ ؟ ولِمَ كانَ الفلك التاسع أطلس مِنَ الكواكب ؟(۱) ، ولِمَ كانَ بعض الكواكب أكبرَ مِنْ بعض ؟ ولِمَ كَانَ بعضُها يلي القطبَ الشَّماليَّ ، وبعضُها يلي القطب الجنوبي ، وبعضُها على سمتِ الرؤوس ، وبعضُها مائلاً عنه ؟ ولا مُوجِبَ للتخصيص بجميع ما ذُكِرَ على أصلكم وهل مذهبكم في إسنادِ ذلك إلى غيرِ الفاعل المختار الذي يخص ما شاءَ بما شاءَ إلا تلاعب لا يرضى بقوله إلا مسلوب العقل والإيمان ، ومَنْ لم ينفعهُ اللهُ بشيءٍ ممَّا تعبَ في تعلّمِهِ ، وصار يهذو هذيان المجانينِ وغيرِ المميَّرِ مِنَ الصبيانِ ، ولا حول ولا قُوَّةَ إلا باللهِ ؟! اللهم ؛ عافنا بفضلِكَ مِنْ كلّ آفةٍ في ديننا ودنيانا وآخرتنا يا أرحم الراحمينَ ، يا ذا الجلال والإكرام (۱) أطلس : أسود ، وقوله : ( من الكواكب ) أي : من أجل خلوه منها ، ففي الكلام حذف ، أو ضمن ( أطلس ( معنى : متجرّد أو عارٍ ، فعدى بـ ( من ) . حامدي » ( ص ۱۸۳ ) . ۲۷۵
And no smaller? And what about the highest of them moving one movement from east to west, while the rest of the spheres move two movements: one is the daily movement from east to west, and the other is their movements in the zodiac from west to east? And what about all movements being confined to what is between east and west, and not being between south and north, for example? And why was each of the seven planets assigned its own particular sphere, despite the possibility that it could be in another? And why were all the fixed stars assigned to the eighth sphere and not in another? And why was the ninth sphere empty of stars? And why are some stars larger than others? And why are some of them near the north pole, some near the south pole, some at the zenith, and some inclined from it? There is no cause for specification in all that has been mentioned according to your principle. And is your doctrine in attributing that to other than the free agent who specifies whatever He wills with whatever He wills anything but frivolity, whose statement none accepts except one stripped of intellect and faith, and whom Allah has not benefited with anything from what he labored in learning, and he becomes delirious like the delirium of madmen and non-discriminating children? And there is no power and no strength except with Allah! O Allah, preserve us by Your grace from every affliction in our religion, our worldly life, and our Hereafter, O Most Merciful of the merciful, O Possessor of majesty and honor. (1) Aṭlas: black, and His saying: 'of the stars' means because it is empty of them, so in the speech there is ellipsis, or aṭlas is given the meaning of stripped or bare, so it is transitive with 'min'.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of astrological/astronomical determinism, which attributes the movements and properties of celestial bodies to inherent necessity rather than to a free agent (الفاعل المختار). The author (likely Sanusi) presents a series of rhetorical questions that highlight the arbitrary particularities of the cosmos—e.g., why the highest sphere moves only one way while others move two, why all motion is east-west, why each planet has its own sphere, why the ninth sphere is starless, why stars differ in size and position. These specificities, the author argues, cannot be explained by natural necessity (as the opponents claim) but only by the will of a free chooser who specifies each thing as He wills. He then condemns those who attribute such determinations to anything other than God, calling it frivolity (تلاعب) that only a mindless or faithless person would accept. The passage ends with a supplication for preservation from afflictions. The footnote clarifies the word 'أطلس' (empty) and its grammatical construction.

Key Terms
الفاعل المختار
التخصيص
ربط المعتادات
الحركات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial motions and positions are necessary and cannot be otherwise due to the inherent nature of the spheres.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the particularities (e.g., why motions are east-west not north-south, why spheres differ) are arbitrary and thus require a free chooser, not necessity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the only explanation for the specific motions and positions of celestial bodies?
A) The inherent nature of the spheres
B) The influence of other celestial bodies
C) The will of a free agent who specifies them
D) Random chance

Key Takeaway
The argument from specification (takhṣīṣ) shows that the particularities of the cosmos are contingent and can only be attributed to the free will of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the night sky and see the stars and planets, do you ever catch yourself attributing their order to 'natural laws' that somehow run on their own? How does Sanusi's rhetorical question—'why this particular arrangement?'—shake that assumption? What will you do today to consciously remind yourself that every single detail of the cosmos is a direct choice of your Lord?
?
The passage describes those who deny divine specification as 'delirious like madmen and children.' Is there any area of your life where you still live as if things 'just happen'—a habit, a relationship, a setback—instead of seeing the hand of the Free Agent who specifies each matter? Write down one area and make a specific du'a to seek Allah's specification in it.
?
Sanusi ends with a heartfelt supplication for preservation from affliction. Think of a recent 'coincidence' or 'stroke of luck' in your life. Have you thanked Allah for His precise specification, or did you mentally credit chance? Tomorrow, before you sleep, recollect one such event and verbally testify that it was Allah's free choice, not impersonal forces.

The First Type of Shirk: Attributing Actions to Celestial Spheres
Ibn Dihaq identifies the first type of shirk as attributing influence to celestial spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Dihaq considers belief in astral influence as a form of shirk.

Core Idea
Believing celestial spheres cause events in the world is shirk.

Ibn Dihaq begins by defining one category of shirk: attributing actions to other than Allah. He then enumerates three types, starting with the belief that celestial spheres influence the lower world. This lesson examines that first type and its association with philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the definition of shirk here: adding actions to other than Allah.
2
Identify the first type
Find the description of the first type: attributing action to celestial spheres. Note the specific claims about their influence and the group that holds this belief.
The passage says this type is specific to the philosopher and his blind followers.
3
Reflect on why it's shirk
Ask yourself why believing in celestial influence is considered shirk. Consider the core idea that only Allah has true creative power.
This connects to the learning objective about understanding the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة في نقل كلام لابن دهاقٍ عمَّنْ يضيف الفعل لغيره تعالى ] قال ابنُ دهاقٍ في شرح الإرشادِ » حينَ تعرَّضَ لأصنافِ الشركِ : ( وصنف آخرُ مِنَ الشركِ ؛ وهو إضافة الفعل لغير الله سبحانه ) ، قالَ : ( وهذا الصنف ثلاثة أنواع أحدها : إضافة الفعل إلى الأفلاكِ ، وأنَّها تُؤثر في العالم السفلي تأثيرات في الأجسام والنبات والمركبات ، وأنَّ البعض يتولد عنِ البعض ، وهذا النوع يختص به الفيلسوفُ ومَنْ تابَعَهُ مِنْ عَامَّتِهم عُمْيُ عني الْقُلُوبِ عَمُوا عَنْ كُلَّ فَائِدَةِ لِأَنَّهُمْ كَفَرُوا بِاللَّهِ تَقْلِيدَا الثاني : ما أُضيفَ مِنْ أفعالِ بعض إلى بعض ؛ مِنْ أَنَّ النارَ تُحرِقُ والطعامَ يُشبع والثوبَ يستر ، إلى غير ذلكَ مِنْ ربط المعتاداتِ حتى ظنُّوها واجبةً ، وتلك ضلالةٌ تبعَ الفيلسوفي فيها كثيرٌ مِنْ عامَّةِ المسلمين ) . قلت : بل وكثيرٌ مِنَ المتفقهة المشتغلين بما لا يعنيهم مِنَ العلوم ، وعن مراشدهم عَمِينَ .
Benefit: In transmitting the statement of Ibn Dihaq concerning those who attribute action to other than Him, may He be exalted. Ibn Dihaq said in his commentary on al-Irshad, when he discussed the types of shirk: (And another type of shirk: it is attributing action to other than Allah, the Sublime). He said: (And this type is of three kinds: one of them: attributing action to the celestial spheres, and that they influence the lower world in effects upon bodies, plants, and compounds, and that some are generated from others; and this kind is specific to the philosopher and those who follow him from their common people—blind of hearts, blind to every benefit, because they disbelieved in Allah by blind following. The second: what is attributed of the actions of some to others, such as that fire burns, food satiates, the garment covers, and so on from linking habits until they thought them necessary, and that is a misguidance in which many of the common Muslims followed the philosopher). I said: Rather, many of the fuqaha' who occupy themselves with sciences that do not concern them, and they are blind to their guidance.
