Commentary
This passage is a classification of shirk according to Ibn Dihaq. He defines one type of shirk as attributing actions to other than Allah. He then breaks this type into three kinds, of which he first lists two. The first kind is attributing actions to the celestial spheres, believing they influence the lower world. This is the view of philosophers and their blind followers. The second kind is attributing effects to natural causes, such as fire burning, food satiating, etc., as if these are necessary connections. This is also misguidance followed by many common Muslims. The author of the benefit (qultu) adds that this is also found among many fuqaha' who occupy themselves with irrelevant sciences.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief in astrological influences is harmless and does not contradict tawhid.
Correction: Ibn Dihaq categorizes this as shirk because it attributes independent causal power to celestial bodies, detracting from Allah's exclusive sovereignty.

B) Attributing actions to celestial spheres

Key Takeaway
The first type of shirk in actions is attributing effects to celestial spheres, which is shirk because it denies Allah's exclusive causality.

The Second Type of Shirk: Attributing Actions to Natural Causes
Ibn Dihaq lists the second type of shirk as attributing necessary causal effects to natural objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Dihaq's claim that attributing necessary causal power to natural objects is also shirk.

Core Idea
Thinking fire burns by itself is shirk.

After discussing the astral type, Ibn Dihaq describes the second type: attributing actions to natural causes like fire, food, and clothing. He explains that this results from linking habitual occurrences and assuming necessity, which is a misguidance followed by many. The author adds that even some fuqaha' fall into this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second type
Read the Arabic and translation for the second type. Notice the examples given: fire burns, food satiates, garment covers.
These are everyday examples of cause and effect.
2
Analyze the explanation
Ibn Dihaq says people thought these are necessary (wajiba) because of habitual linking. Understand why he considers this shirk.
The core idea: only Allah is the true agent.
3
Consider the author's addition
The author adds that many fuqaha' are also blind to guidance. Reflect on why even scholars might fall into this.
This shows the pervasiveness of this misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة في نقل كلام لابن دهاقٍ عمَّنْ يضيف الفعل لغيره تعالى ] قال ابنُ دهاقٍ في شرح الإرشادِ » حينَ تعرَّضَ لأصنافِ الشركِ : ( وصنف آخرُ مِنَ الشركِ ؛ وهو إضافة الفعل لغير الله سبحانه ) ، قالَ : ( وهذا الصنف ثلاثة أنواع أحدها : إضافة الفعل إلى الأفلاكِ ، وأنَّها تُؤثر في العالم السفلي تأثيرات في الأجسام والنبات والمركبات ، وأنَّ البعض يتولد عنِ البعض ، وهذا النوع يختص به الفيلسوفُ ومَنْ تابَعَهُ مِنْ عَامَّتِهم عُمْيُ عني الْقُلُوبِ عَمُوا عَنْ كُلَّ فَائِدَةِ لِأَنَّهُمْ كَفَرُوا بِاللَّهِ تَقْلِيدَا الثاني : ما أُضيفَ مِنْ أفعالِ بعض إلى بعض ؛ مِنْ أَنَّ النارَ تُحرِقُ والطعامَ يُشبع والثوبَ يستر ، إلى غير ذلكَ مِنْ ربط المعتاداتِ حتى ظنُّوها واجبةً ، وتلك ضلالةٌ تبعَ الفيلسوفي فيها كثيرٌ مِنْ عامَّةِ المسلمين ) . قلت : بل وكثيرٌ مِنَ المتفقهة المشتغلين بما لا يعنيهم مِنَ العلوم ، وعن مراشدهم عَمِينَ .
Benefit: In transmitting the statement of Ibn Dihaq concerning those who attribute action to other than Him, may He be exalted. Ibn Dihaq said in his commentary on al-Irshad, when he discussed the types of shirk: (And another type of shirk: it is attributing action to other than Allah, the Sublime). He said: (And this type is of three kinds: one of them: attributing action to the celestial spheres, and that they influence the lower world in effects upon bodies, plants, and compounds, and that some are generated from others; and this kind is specific to the philosopher and those who follow him from their common people—blind of hearts, blind to every benefit, because they disbelieved in Allah by blind following. The second: what is attributed of the actions of some to others, such as that fire burns, food satiates, the garment covers, and so on from linking habits until they thought them necessary, and that is a misguidance in which many of the common Muslims followed the philosopher). I said: Rather, many of the fuqaha' who occupy themselves with sciences that do not concern them, and they are blind to their guidance.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a classification of shirk according to Ibn Dihaq. He defines one type of shirk as attributing actions to other than Allah. He then breaks this type into three kinds, of which he first lists two. The first kind is attributing actions to the celestial spheres, believing they influence the lower world. This is the view of philosophers and their blind followers. The second kind is attributing effects to natural causes, such as fire burning, food satiating, etc., as if these are necessary connections. This is also misguidance followed by many common Muslims. The author of the benefit (qultu) adds that this is also found among many fuqaha' who occupy themselves with irrelevant sciences.

Key Terms
شرك
إضافة الفعل
المعتادات
الفيلسوف
المتفقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural causes like fire have inherent power to produce effects independently.
Correction: Ibn Dihaq teaches that such belief is shirk because it ascribes action to other than Allah; only Allah creates effects.

Checkpoint
What is the second type of shirk according to Ibn Dihaq?
A) Attributing actions to celestial spheres
B) Attributing actions to natural causes like fire burning
C) Denying divine attributes
D) Worshipping idols

Key Takeaway
The second type of shirk in actions is attributing necessary causal effects to natural objects, which is shirk because it denies Allah's exclusive causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you eat and feel full, do you habitually thank the food or do you remember that Allah alone creates satiety? What can you do to make that remembrance instinctive?
?
You see a fire burning wood. In that very moment, is your heart truly resting on Allah as the sole cause, or does it subtly rely on the fire's nature? How would you train yourself to break that reliance?
?
If a garment covers your body, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'the fabric is covering me' instead of 'Allah is covering me through this garment'? Will you commit to catching that thought tomorrow and redirecting it to Allah?

Two Erroneous Beliefs About Natural Causality
Ibn Dihaq classifies two erroneous beliefs about natural agents and their rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two erroneous beliefs about natural agents mentioned by Ibn Dihaq and their rulings: disbelief for those who say they act by nature, and innovation for those who say they act by a power placed by Allah.

Core Idea
Attributing actions to nature is disbelief; attributing them to a power in creation is innovation.

Ibn Dihaq begins by detailing the various beliefs people hold regarding natural causes. He identifies two specific erroneous positions: one that leads to clear disbelief and another that is considered innovation but with disagreement on its severity. This lesson will help you distinguish between these two errors and understand why the second is widespread among the common people.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage where Ibn Dihaq states the two beliefs and their rulings. Pay attention to the distinction between 'by nature' and 'by a power placed by Allah'.
The phrase 'لا خلاف في كفره' indicates unanimous consensus on disbelief.
2
Understand the first belief
Whoever says that natural agents act by their own nature (tab') is a disbeliever without any difference of opinion. This is the most severe position.
3
Understand the second belief
Whoever says that natural agents act by a power (quwwa) that Allah placed in them is an innovator (mubtadi'), and there is disagreement about whether this constitutes disbelief.
The author notes that this belief is common among ignorant fuqaha' of his time.
4
Reflect on the author's comment
The author interjects that this second belief is widespread among the common fuqaha' and blind followers. Reflect on why this is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (وهم فيها على اعتقاداتٍ؛ فمَنْ قالَ : بطبعها تفعلُ . . فلا خلاف في كفرِهِ ، ومَنْ قالَ : بِقُوَّةٍ جعلها الله فيها . . كان مبتدعاً، وقدِ اختلف في كفره ) . قلت : وهذا القسم هو اعتقاد أكثرِ عامَّةِ المتفقهة في زماننا ، ومَنْ في معناهم من جهلة المقلدين . قالَ : ( ومَنْ قالَ : إِنَّ الأكل دليلٌ عقلى على الشبع دون أن يكونَ معتاداً . . كان جاهلاً بمعنى الدلالةِ العقليَّةِ ، ومَنْ علمَ أَنَّ الله سبحانَهُ ربط بعض أفعالِهِ ببعض ، فكلَّما فعل هذا فعل هذا باختياره ، وإذا شاءَ خَرْقَ هذهِ العادة فعلَ .. فهذا هو المؤمنُ الذي سَلِمَ مِنْ هذهِ الآفة بفضل الله سبحانَهُ ) ثم ذكرَ أنَّ النوع الثالثَ مِنْ هذا الصنفِ : ما تقوله المعتزلة ويعتقده أكثرُ مَنْ جهلَ هذا العلمَ مِنَ المسلمينَ : أنَّ العبدَ يُوجِدُ أفعاله بقدرة خلقها الله تعالى لهُ وأمرَهُ أنْ يتصرَّفَ بها في غير ما نهاه عنه ، وذكرَ خلافَ أهل السنَّةِ في تكفيرهم ، قالَ : ( والأظهرُ أنَّهم كافرون ) انتهى)
He said: (And they have various beliefs concerning them. Whoever says: they act by their nature—there is no difference of opinion in his disbelief. Whoever says: by a power that Allah placed in them—he is an innovator, and there is disagreement about his disbelief). I said: This category is the belief of most common fuqaha' in our time and those like them among ignorant blind followers. He said: (And whoever says that eating is an intellectual indication of satiety without it being habitual—he is ignorant of the meaning of intellectual indication. Whoever knows that Allah, the Sublime, has linked some of His actions to others, so whenever He does this, He does that by His choice, and if He wills to break the habit, He does—that is the believer who has been saved from this affliction by the grace of Allah, the Sublime). Then he mentioned that the third type of this category is what the Mu'tazila say and what most of those who are ignorant of this science among Muslims believe: that the servant creates his actions by a power that Allah created in him and commanded him to use it in other than what He forbade. And he mentioned the disagreement of Ahl al-Sunna regarding declaring them disbelievers. He said: (And the most apparent is that they are disbelievers). End.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage continues the exposition of the second kind of shirk (attributing actions to natural causes). Ibn Dihaq states that people hold different beliefs about these natural agents. If one believes they act by their own nature (tab'), that is disbelief without disagreement. If one believes they act by a power placed in them by Allah, that is innovation (bid'ah) with disagreement on whether it is disbelief. The author (qultu) notes that this latter view is widespread among fuqaha' in his time. Then Ibn Dihaq discusses those who claim that eating is an intellectual proof of satiety without that being a habit—they are ignorant of intellectual indication. The correct belief is that Allah links actions by His choice and may break habits. Then he presents the third type: the Mu'tazilite belief that the servant creates his own actions by a power Allah created in him. Ibn Dihaq mentions disagreement among Ahl al-Sunna on takfir, and states the stronger view is that they are disbelievers.

Key Terms
طبع
قوة
بدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that saying 'fire burns by a power from Allah' is acceptable and not problematic.
Correction: Ibn Dihaq clearly states that this belief is innovation (bid'ah) and there is disagreement about whether it constitutes disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Dihaq, what is the ruling on someone who says fire burns by its own nature?
A) They are innovators
B) They are disbelievers without disagreement
C) They are merely ignorant
D) They are Muslims

Key Takeaway
Attributing actions to nature is clear disbelief; attributing them to a power in creation is innovation, not mere theological difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that the cause of something happening is simply the physical properties of an object, forgetting the direct will of Allah? How can you train yourself to constantly acknowledge Allah's direct involvement in every event?
?
If Ibn Dihaq says the belief in a power placed by Allah is widespread among fuqaha', reflect on your own study circles: do you or people you know speak about natural causes in a way that resembles this innovation? What will you do to correct it?
?
When you experience a success or a setback, do you attribute it to a 'natural law' or to the immediate decree of Allah? Which belief would change your reaction to trials and blessings, and why does that matter for your faith today?

The Correct Belief: Allah's Habit and Intellectual Indication
Ibn Dihaq refutes the claim of necessary intellectual causality and affirms Allah's habit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct belief about causality: that Allah habitually links actions, and that understanding this as a habit (not a necessary intellectual indication) is the mark of true faith.

Core Idea
True believers know that causes and effects are linked by Allah's habit, not by necessity.

Ibn Dihaq contrasts two views on causality: one that falsely claims a necessary intellectual connection between cause and effect, and the correct view that Allah freely chooses to link actions. This lesson clarifies the distinction and why the latter is essential for sound faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on intellectual indication
Read Ibn Dihaq's statement about those who claim eating is an intellectual proof of satiety without it being habitual. Note how he labels such a person.
The term 'دلالة عقلية' (intellectual indication) is key.
2
Understand the error
Whoever thinks that eating necessarily indicates satiety by intellect alone, disregarding the habit (ada) of Allah, is ignorant of the meaning of intellectual indication.
Intellectual indication refers to conceptual entailment, not empirical regularity.
3
Learn the correct belief
The correct belief is that Allah links some actions to others by His choice. Whenever He wills, He does this and then that, and He can break the habit if He wills.
This is the belief of the saved believer.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
Ibn Dihaq concludes that the one who affirms Allah's habit and His power to break it is the true believer who has been saved from this affliction by Allah's grace.

?

The Mu'tazilite View of Human Action as Shirk
Ibn Dihaq presents the Mu'tazilite view of human action as the third type of shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite belief that humans create their own actions and why Ibn Dihaq considers it the third type of shirk, with the apparent ruling of disbelief.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own actions is shirk and disbelief.

Ibn Dihaq presents the third type of shirk within the category of attributing acts to other than Allah: the Mu'tazilite belief that the servant creates his own actions using a power created by Allah. This lesson explores that belief, the disagreement among Ahl al-Sunna on takfir, and the author's preferred position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Mu'tazilite view
Read Ibn Dihaq's description of the third type: the Mu'tazilite belief that the servant creates his own actions by a power from Allah.
The term 'تكوين' (creating) is used for the servant's action.
2
Understand the belief
The Mu'tazila and many ignorant Muslims believe that the servant brings his own actions into existence (yūjidu af‘alahu) using a power (qudra) created by Allah in him.
3
Consider the disagreement on takfir
Ibn Dihaq mentions that Ahl al-Sunna disagree on whether to declare such people disbelievers, but he states the stronger view is that they are disbelievers.
The phrase 'الأظهر أنهم كافرون' indicates his preferred position.
4
Reflect on the implications
This shows the gravity of attributing creative power to humans, even if that power is created by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something good, do you catch yourself thinking 'I did that' with a sense of self-reliance, forgetting that every act is created by Allah? Pause right now to recount one success from today and attribute it entirely to Allah's creation. How does that reshape your gratitude?
?
If you truly believe that your actions are created by Allah, why do you still feel pride in your deeds or despair over your failures? Consider a recent sin you committed: did you assume full responsibility for the act without acknowledging that Allah created it? How does this doctrine change the way you should approach repentance—not as fixing yourself, but as returning to the Creator of your acts?
?
The passage declares that the Mu'tazila are disbelievers for holding that humans create their actions. Look into your own heart: do you ever secretly believe that you have the ultimate power to control your choices, even while professing otherwise? Write down one area of your life where you act as if you are the independent creator of your own efforts, and then commit to verbally affirming before each action: 'Allah, you are the Creator of this act.'

Three Beliefs on Fire's Action and Their Rulings
Classification of three beliefs on fire's causation with rulings and a clarification on rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three theological positions on how fire causes burning, and the rulings of kufr and bid'ah associated with each.

Core Idea
Not all errors about causation are equal: some are kufr, some bid'ah, and some are matters of scholarly dispute.

This lesson examines a key topic in Islamic theology: the nature of causality in the created world. The scholar presents three distinct beliefs about how fire burns—ranging from outright disbelief to acceptable orthodox belief—and classifies the rulings on their adherents. Understanding these distinctions helps clarify the boundaries between kufr, bid'ah, and correct belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice that the text includes both the original author's statements and the commentator's interjections.
Pay attention to the phrases 'قال:' and 'قلت:' which indicate shifts between author and commentator.
2
Identify the three positions
Identify the three beliefs about fire's action: (1) fire acts by its nature, (2) fire acts by a power God placed in it, (3) the Mu'tazila view that the servant creates his own actions.
3
Note the rulings for each
For each position, note the ruling: unanimous kufr for the first, bid'ah with disagreement on kufr for the second, and the apparent view of kufr for the third.
The commentator points out that the second view is widespread among common jurists.
4
Understand the clarification on rational proof
The passage distinguishes between rational proof and customary connection. Claiming that eating is a rational proof of satiety (without custom) is ignorance; correct belief is that God customarily links actions.
This section guards against false understanding of rational proof in causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ( وهم فيها على اعتقاداتٍ ؛ فمَنْ قالَ : بطبعها تفعلُ . . فلا خلاف في كفرِهِ ، ومَنْ قالَ : بِقُوَّةٍ جعلها الله فيها . . كان مبتدعاً ، وقدِ اختلف في كفره ) . قلت : وهذا القسم هو اعتقاد أكثرِ عامَّةِ المتفقهة في زماننا ، ومَنْ في معناهم من جهلة المقلدين . قالَ : ( ومَنْ قالَ : إِنَّ الأكل دليلٌ عقلى على الشبع دون أن يكونَ معتاداً . . كان جاهلاً بمعنى الدلالةِ العقليَّةِ ، ومَنْ علمَ أَنَّ الله سبحانَهُ ربط بعض أفعالِهِ ببعض ، فكلَّما فعل هذا فعل هذا باختياره ، وإذا شاءَ خَرْقَ هذهِ العادة فعلَ .. فهذا هو المؤمنُ الذي سَلِمَ مِنْ هذهِ الآفة بفضل الله سبحانَهُ ) ثم ذكرَ أنَّ النوع الثالثَ مِنْ هذا الصنفِ : ما تقوله المعتزلة ويعتقده أكثرُ مَنْ جهلَ هذا العلمَ مِنَ المسلمينَ : أنَّ العبدَ يُوجِدُ أفعاله بقدرة خلقها الله تعالى لهُ وأمرَهُ أنْ يتصرَّفَ بها في غير ما نهاه عنه ، وذكرَ خلافَ أهل السنَّةِ في تكفيرهم ، قالَ : ( والأظهرُ أنَّهم كافرون ) انتهى
He said: (And they have concerning it various beliefs. So whoever says: 'It acts by its nature' – there is no disagreement about his disbelief. And whoever says: 'By a power that God placed in it' – he is an innovator, and there is disagreement about his disbelief.) I said: This category is the belief of most common jurists in our time, and those like them among ignorant imitators. He said: (And whoever says: 'Eating is a rational proof of satiety without it being customary' – he is ignorant of the meaning of rational proof. And whoever knows that God, exalted is He, has linked some of His actions with others – so whenever He does this, He does that by His choice, and if He wishes to break this custom, He does – then this is the believer who is saved from this affliction by the grace of God, exalted is He.) Then he mentioned that the third type of this category is what the Mu'tazila say and what most of those Muslims who are ignorant of this science believe: that the servant brings his actions into existence by a power that God the Exalted created for him and commanded him to use it in what He did not forbid him. And he mentioned the disagreement of Ahl al-Sunna regarding declaring them disbelievers. He said: (And the apparent view is that they are disbelievers.) End.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a classification and ruling on three theological positions regarding causation, specifically the action of fire. The author (quoted) presents the first two views: (1) those who say fire acts by its nature – unanimous disbelief; (2) those who say by a power God placed in it – innovation, with disagreement on whether it is disbelief. The commentator then identifies the second view as the belief of most common jurists today. Another quotation clarifies the distinction between rational proof and customary connection: understanding that eating indicates satiety only through custom is correct, while claiming a direct rational proof independent of custom is ignorance. The third view is the Mu'tazila position that the servant creates his own actions with power given by God. The author reports that Ahl al-Sunna disagree on takfir of the Mu'tazila, but the apparent view is that they are disbelievers. The commentary follows the structure: first two views, commentator's note, clarification on rational vs. customary, then third view and ruling.

البدعة
القدرة
المعتزلة
الدلالة العقلية
العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second position (belief that fire acts by a power God placed in it) is considered kufr by some, but the text says it is bid'ah with disagreement on kufr.
Correction: The text explicitly states: 'كان مبتدعاً، وقد اختلف في كفره', meaning he is an innovator, and there is disagreement about his disbelief. So it is not automatically kufr.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on those who say fire acts by its nature?
A) They are innovators (mubtadi').
B) There is no disagreement about their disbelief.
C) There is disagreement about their disbelief.
D) They are believers.

Key Takeaway
Correct belief about causality is that God customarily links causes and effects, and deviations from this are classified as kufr or bid'ah depending on the specific error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fire burn, do you consider that it is truly God Who causes the burning, or do you instinctively attribute it to fire's nature? Where in your daily life do you forget that God is the direct cause of all effects? How can you begin to train your heart to see His hand behind every cause today?
?
The lesson distinguishes between those who believe fire acts by its nature (kufr) and those who believe by a power God placed in it (bid'ah). How many of your own actions are performed with the assumption that you are the independent cause, forgetting that your power is created and sustained by Allah? What specific action can you take this week to remind yourself that all power belongs to Him alone?
?
The correct belief is to recognize that God has linked some actions with others by His custom, and He may break it at any moment. Do you live with this certainty in your heart? In what area of your life do you feel trapped by 'natural laws' or routine, and how can you start calling upon Allah to break that custom with His direct intervention?

The Bee's Action and Divine Causality
Sanusi presents an objection and answers with Sunni doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunni doctrine of divine creation and acquisition (kasb) answers the objection that the bee's intricate action implies its own knowledge or intelligence.

Core Idea
The bee's precision is directly from Allah, not from the bee itself.

Sanusi anticipates a common objection against the design argument: if a non-rational bee can produce such precise work, how can that indicate the Creator's knowledge? He answers by grounding the Sunni stance on causality and creation, showing that all actions, including the bee's, are Allah's acts. This passage clarifies the Sunni rejection of any causal influence from creatures and affirms that all precision in nature points directly to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the objection and the beginning of the answer.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the Sunni doctrinal foundation
Locate the sentence stating that Allah alone creates everything and that actions are attributed to Him in creation, while creatures only have acquisition (kasb) without real effect.
This is the core of the answer.
3
Apply the doctrine to the bee
Understand why the bee's hexagonal shape is not due to any effect or acquisition of the bee, but solely by Allah's creation and inspiration.
The passage specifically denies any effect or even acquisition for the bee.
4
Conclude with the Quranic verse
Read the Quranic verse (20:50) cited at the end. Reflect on how it shows that all of creation, including the bee's action, indicates Allah's tremendous knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أذكياء المهندسين بعد سبرٍ وبحث عظيم ، ومعلوم : أنَّ النحلةَ مِنَ الحيوانِ غير العاقل ، وقد صدرَ مِنْ فعلها ما صدرَ ، فكيف يصح معَ هذا أنْ يُستدَلَّ بإحكام الفعلِ واشتماله على دقائقِ الصنع على علمٍ صانعه ؟ فالجواب : أنَّكَ قد عرفتَ أنَّ مُعتقَد أهل السنَّةِ : أَنَّ الله جل وعلا منفرد بخلْقِ كلّ شيءٍ ، لا تأثير لغيره في شيءٍ أياً كانَ ، وأَنَّ الأفعال التي تتصف بها العقلاء وغيرُهم كلُّها منسوبة إلى الله جل وعلا خلقاً واختراعاً ، وإنْ كانَ بعضُها يُنسب إلى بعض مَنْ يَتَصفُ بها كسباً مِنْ غيرِ تأثير أصلاً، وسيأتي في فصلِ خلقِ الأفعالِ تفسير معنى الكسب . فليس في الوجودِ عند أهل السنَّةِ إلا الله جل وعلا موصوفاً بصفاتِهِ العليَّةِ ، وكلُّ ما سواهُ مِنَ الكائناتِ فهي أفعاله ، فالشكل المسدَّسُ الذي اتخذتْهُ النحلة إذاً ليس لها فيهِ تأثير أصلاً ، بل ولا كسب مِنْ غيرِ تأثیر ؛ لما يأتي في فضلِ إبطالِ التولُّدِ مِن امتناع تعلَّقِ القدرةِ الحادثة بغير محلها ، وإِنَّما وقوع ذلكَ الشكل بمجرَّدِ خَلْقِ اللهِ جَلَّ وعلا واختراعِهِ ، وألهم النحلَ لاتخاذِهِ مَسْكناً كما ألهم سائر الحيوانات لمصالحها، الَّذِي أَعْطَى كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلْقَهُ ثُمَّ هَدَى ﴾ [طه : ٥٠] ، فهو مِنْ جملة ما يدلُّ على عظيم علمهِ تعالى
The ingenious engineers after deep probing and great research, and it is known that the bee is from the non-rational animals, and from its action has issued what issued, so how is it valid with this that one should infer from the precision of the action and its inclusion of subtle points of craftsmanship to the knowledge of its maker? The answer: You have known that the belief of Ahl al-Sunna is that Allah Almighty is alone in creating everything, no effect of other in anything whatsoever, and that the actions which rational beings and others are described with are all attributed to Allah Almighty in creation and invention, even if some of them are attributed to some of those described with them as acquisition (kasb) without any effect at all, and it will come in the chapter on the creation of actions the explanation of the meaning of acquisition. So in existence according to Ahl al-Sunna there is only Allah Almighty described with His high attributes, and everything else among the existents is His acts. Therefore the hexagonal shape which the bee has taken has no effect for it therein at all, nor even acquisition without effect; because of what will come in the section on invalidating generation (tawallud) from the impossibility of attaching the originated power to other than its place. And indeed the occurrence of that shape is merely by the creation of Allah Almighty and His invention, and He inspired the bee to take it as a dwelling just as He inspired all animals for their benefits, He who gave each thing its creation then guided (Quran 20:50), so it is among the things that indicate His tremendous knowledge, exalted is He.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection states: Brilliant engineers (implying those who argue from design) after deep investigation claim that the bee's production of a hexagonal honeycomb with precise geometry indicates the knowledge of the bee's maker (God). But the objector says: The bee is a non-rational animal, so how can its action be used to argue for the knowledge of its maker? The answer begins by affirming the Sunni doctrine: Allah alone creates everything; no other being has any effect. All actions, whether of rational beings or not, are created by Allah. The term 'acquisition' (kasb) is used for actions attributed to creatures without any real causal effect, to be explained later. Therefore, in the Sunni view, only Allah and His attributes truly exist; all other things are His acts. The bee has no effect whatsoever in producing the hexagonal shape; even its acquisition is absent because originated power cannot attach to something outside its locus. The shape is purely Allah's creation and invention. Allah inspired the bee to build it as a dwelling, just as He inspires all animals for their needs, as per the Quranic verse 'He gave each thing its creation then guided' (20:50). Hence, the bee's product is one of the things that indicate Allah's tremendous knowledge, not the bee's.

إحكام الفعل
خلق
كسب
أهل السنة
فعل
إلهام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the bee's intricate work proves the bee's own intelligence or knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that all such precision is directly from God, not from the bee's capability. The bee has no effect or even acquisition in the act; it is merely an instrument of Allah's creation and inspiration.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni answer, what is the actual source of the bee's precise hexagonal shape?
A) The bee's own intelligence and skill
B) A combination of the bee's effort and natural laws
C) Allah's direct creation and inspiration without any effect from the bee
D) The bee's acquired knowledge through instinct

Key Takeaway
The bee's precise hexagonal shape is directly created by Allah and inspired in the bee, so it indicates Allah's knowledge, not the bee's.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the intricate design in nature—a bird's nest, a spider's web, or a honeycomb—do you instinctively credit the creature's skill, or do you immediately see it as Allah's direct creation? What does your instinct reveal about your heart's orientation?
?
If you truly believe that every action you perform—including your most praiseworthy achievements—is created by Allah and that you have no real causal effect, how does that reshape your sense of accountability and your relationship with pride? Can you genuinely attribute all success to Him alone, without any sense of personal entitlement?
?
The bee builds its honeycomb without understanding or intention, yet produces something of stunning precision. In what areas of your life are you acting without true understanding, merely following divine inspiration? Are you willing to surrender your own plans and trust that Allah's direct guidance will produce far better results than your own calculations?

Two Views on Divine Causation and the Meaning of Ta'lil
Sanusi presents two views on divine causation and defines ta'lil as correlation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two views on divine causation in Islamic theology and the technical meaning of ta'lil as necessary correlation.

Core Idea
Divine causation is not efficient causality but necessary correlation.

Sanusi presents a foundational dispute in kalam about what God creates when He brings about an attribute. The correct view, held by the investigators, resolves the question and leads to a precise definition of ta'lil. This lesson unpacks that dispute and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the technical terms maʿnā (meaning) and ḥāl (state).
2
Identify the two views
The passage presents two answers to the problem of what God creates when knowledge entails being-knowing. Find each view and note which one the investigators adopt.
The first view holds that God creates both the meaning and the state.
3
Understand the definition of ta'lil
After presenting the correct view, Sanusi defines ta'lil (causation) as mere correlation between two things in affirmation and negation. Reflect on how this differs from common notions of causality.
This definition is crucial for avoiding anthropomorphic ideas of God's action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك أنهم قد اختلفوا : إذا خلق الله تعالى في ذات الجوهر علماً مثلاً ، ولزم ذلك العلم ثبوت عالميته على القول بثبوت الحال .. فهل الصانع تعالى فعل المعنى والحال اللازمة له(١) ، أو فعل المعنى ، والمعنى لملازمته الحال وعدم تعقلها بدونه . . هو الذي أفاد ثبوت الحال ؟(٢) فذهب المحققون إلى الأول ، وهو الحق الذي لا شك فيه . ومعنى التعليل عند هؤلاء شاهداً وغائباً : ثبوت التلازم بينهما في طرفي النفي والإثبات لا أزيد .
And that is because they differed: when God Most High creates in the substance's essence a knowledge, for example, and that knowledge necessitates the establishment of its being-knowing according to the view of the existence of the state (ḥāl)... Is the Maker Most High the doer of the meaning (maʿnā) and the state (ḥāl) necessarily concomitant to it, or did He do the meaning, and the meaning, due to its inseparable connection to the state and the latter's inconceivability without it, is what conferred the existence of the state? The investigators (muḥaqqiqūn) adopted the first [view], which is the truth beyond doubt. And the meaning of causation (taʿlīl) according to these people, in the witnessed and the absent, is: the existence of correlation (talāzum) between them in the two sides of negation and affirmation, nothing more.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute among theologians regarding divine causation. When God creates an accident in a substance, such as knowledge, that knowledge entails the attribute (ḥāl) of being-knowing. The question is: does God create both the meaning (the accident) and its concomitant state, or does He create only the meaning, and the meaning itself produces the state? The author states that the investigators (the sound scholars) hold the first view: God creates both. Then he defines 'ta'lil' (causation) in this context: it is nothing but necessary correlation (talazum) in both affirmation and negation, not an efficient causal production. This definition is crucial because in the divine realm, causation is not like physical efficient causality but merely a necessary concomitance.

Key Terms
حال (ḥāl)
معنى (maʿnā)
تعليل (taʿlīl)
تلازم (talāzum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ta'lil implies that God creates an efficient cause which then produces an effect independently.
Correction: Ta'lil means only necessary correlation, not efficient causation. God directly creates both the cause and the effect; there is no intermediary causal power.

Checkpoint
According to the investigators, what does 'ta'lil' mean when applied to divine actions?
A) God creates the cause which then creates the effect.
B) It means necessary correlation between two things in affirmation and negation.
C) It means the effect is produced by the cause without God's direct involvement.
D) It means the cause is more fundamental than the effect.

Key Takeaway
In Ashʿarī theology, causation (taʿlīl) is defined as the necessary correlation between a meaning and its concomitant state, not as efficient production.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever unconsciously rely on created means as if they have independent causal power? How does the Ashʿarī definition of causation as mere correlation challenge that assumption in your daily life?
?
When you reflect on God's creation, do you truly believe that He is the sole creator of every effect, or do you still attribute some efficacy to created things? What would change in your worship if you internalized this lesson's definition of taʿlīl?
?
The investigators' view is that God creates both the meaning and the state. Are you willing to attribute every state you experience—your emotions, your knowledge, your very existence—directly to God's creative act at every moment, without leaving room for secondary causation? What might that require from you today?

Refuting the View that Meaning Necessitates State
Sanusi refutes the view that meaning necessitates state with temporal and dependence arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that a created meaning cannot independently necessitate a state, and why only the agent (God) can be the true cause.

Core Idea
A created meaning cannot be an independent cause; only God's attributes are truly efficient.

Sanusi continues his critique of alternative theories of causality. He now attacks the idea that the agent creates a meaning, and that meaning then automatically produces a state. This leads to absurdities about time and dependence, and ultimately denies that any created thing can be an efficient cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's view and Sanusi's rejection
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that Sanusi states the view and then declares it absolutely false.
2
Examine the two temporal scenarios
Notice Sanusi considers two possibilities: the cause precedes the effect in time, or they are simultaneous. Both lead to impossibility or arbitrariness.
Temporal precedence would mean a delay, impossible for eternal matters. Simultaneity removes causal priority.
3
Understand the dependence argument
Sanusi argues that the state's need for the meaning is stronger than the meaning's need for the state, so the agent should directly create the state.
The state is only conceivable via its meaning, so it is more dependent.
4
Refute the 'root' defense
Opponents claim the meaning is a root (aṣl) and therefore effects the state. Sanusi replies that being a root does not imply efficient causality; only God's attributes are efficient.
If being a root sufficed, then substances would independently create accidents, which is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما من قال من المتكلمين : إن الفاعل يفعل المعنى ، والمعنى يوجب الحال ، ولم يفعل الفاعل الحال أصلاً . . فقوله في ذلك باطل قطعاً ؛ لأن تلك العلة إن أثرت في ثبوت الحال مع التقدم .. لزم تأخر المعلول عن علته بالزمان ، وهو محال ، وإن أثرت في الثبوت مع مصاحبة وجودها له.. لزم عدم تقدم المؤثر على أثره (٣) ، ولزم التحكم ؛ إذ ليس إسناد وجود العلة للفاعل المختار وهي أفادت ثبوت الحال .. بأولى من إسناد ثبوت الحال للفاعل وهي أفادت ثبوت تلك العلة ، بل طلب الحال للمعنى أقوى من طلب المعنى له ؛ لأن الحال لا تعقل متميزة إلا باعتبار معناها ، بخلاف العكس. فإن أجابوا بترجيح العلة للتأثير لكونها أصلاً .. قيل لهم : لا ملازمة بين كون الشيء أصلاً وكونه مؤثراً ، وإنما يصح التأثير لمن وجبت له صفات الألوهية ؛ من كمال العلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة والوحدانية إلى غير ذلك من الصفات التي لا تليق إلا بالله جل وعلا ، ولو كان كون الشيء أصلاً لغيره يقتضي استقلاله بإثبات غيره له .. للزم أن يكون تعالى إنما أوجد الجواهر وهي تستقل الملازم بإيجاد الأعراض ! وذلك معلوم البطلان. وبالجملة : فهذا القول باطل ، وعلى تقدير صحته فإنما يصح باعتبار صفاتنا الحادثة هي وأحوالها ، فأمكن إسنادها إلى مؤثر ، وأما صفاته جل وعلا فكلها واجبة ، والواجب من لازمه وجوب القدم والبقاء ؛ إذ الوجوب نفي قبول الانتفاء ، وما لا يقبل الانتفاء فلا انتفاء له سابقاً ولا لاحقاً ، وفي ذلك تحقيق قدمه وبقائه ، فلم يصح إسناده لمقتض أصلاً ، فلا معنى للتعليل إن أطلق فيها إلا التلازم . وظاهر كلام المقترح : أن الخلاف جار أيضاً في تعليل الواجب ؛ فإنه قد قال في تقرير الجواب عن شبهة المعتزلة التي سبق تقريرها : ( من قال بأن التعليل معناه التلازم . . يقول : قد يتلازم الممكنان وقد يتلازم الواجبان ، ولا منافاة ، ومن قال بأن المعنى يوجب .. قال : الحكم لا يجب إلا باعتبار وجوب معناه ، فإنا قلنا : إنه لا يعقل ( وهو تعقل المعنى متميزاً إلا باعتبار حالها ) .
And as for those among the theologians who said: 'The agent does the meaning, and the meaning necessitates the state, and the agent did not do the state at all'... his statement in that is absolutely false; because if that cause produced effect in the state with temporal precedence, then the effect would be delayed from its cause in time, which is impossible. And if it produced effect in [its] existence while accompanying its existence, then there would be no precedence of the efficient over its effect, and arbitrariness would follow; since attributing the existence of the cause to the free agent while it conferred the existence of the state is not more likely than attributing the existence of the state to the agent while it conferred the existence of that cause. Rather, the state's need for the meaning is stronger than the meaning's need for it; because the state is not conceivable as distinct except by consideration of its meaning, unlike the reverse. For if they answer by giving preference to the cause for effect due to its being a root (aṣl)... it is said to them: There is no necessary connection between a thing being a root and its being an efficient; rather, effect is only valid for one to whom the attributes of divinity are necessary: perfect knowledge, power, will, life, unity, and other attributes befitting only God Almighty. And if a thing being a root for another necessitated its independence in proving that other for it, then it would follow that God Most High only created substances while the concomitant is independent in creating accidents! And that is known to be false. In summary: this statement is false, and even if it were true, it would only be valid by considering our created attributes and their states, so it is possible to attribute them to an efficient. As for His attributes Almighty, all of them are necessary (wājib), and the necessary entails the necessity of pre-eternity and everlastingness; because necessity is the negation of susceptibility to non-existence, and that which does not admit non-existence has no non-existence before or after, and in that is the realization of His pre-eternity and everlastingness. So it is not valid to attribute it to any necessitator at all. Hence, if 'causation' is used regarding them, it has no meaning except correlation. And the apparent meaning of the muqtaraḥ's statement is that the dispute also applies to the causation of the Necessary; for he said in the exposition of the answer to the Mu'tazila's doubt which was previously presented: 'He who says that causation means correlation... says: two possibles may correlate and two necessities may correlate, with no contradiction. And he who says that the meaning necessitates... says: the judgment is only necessary by considering the necessity of its meaning, for we said: it is not conceivable (and it is the conceiving of the meaning as distinct except by consideration of its state).'
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second view that the agent only creates the meaning, and the meaning then produces the state. Sanusi presents several arguments: (1) If the cause precedes the effect in time, that is impossible for eternal things. (2) If cause and effect are simultaneous, then the cause has no real effect, leading to arbitrariness because there is no reason to attribute the effect to the cause rather than vice versa. (3) The state's dependence on the meaning is stronger, so it is more fitting for the agent to be the direct creator of the state. (4) If the opponent says the cause is more fundamental, Sanusi replies that being more fundamental does not entail being an efficient cause; only the divine attributes of God — perfect knowledge, power, etc. — make something truly efficient. (5) If being a root made one independent, then substances would independently create accidents, which is false. Then he moves to the case of divine attributes: all of God's attributes are necessary (wājib), meaning they cannot cease to exist. For such attributes, there is no real causation; the only meaning of 'ta'lil' is necessary correlation. Finally, he notes that the muqtaraḥ's discussion shows that this dispute also applies to the Necessary Being, and he quotes a view that for necessary beings, causation is merely correlation. This entire section guards against attributing temporal causation to divine attributes and emphasizes that God's attributes are not caused by something else.

Key Terms
علة (ʿilla)
معلول (maʿlūl)
أصل (aṣl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a created meaning can act as an independent efficient cause.
Correction: Sanusi shows that only God with His attributes is truly efficient; a created meaning cannot necessitate a state independently.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject the view that the meaning necessitates the state when cause and effect are simultaneous?
A) Because it would imply the effect is delayed in time.
B) Because it would lead to arbitrariness in attributing causation.
C) Because the state is not real.
D) Because the agent cannot create the meaning.

Key Takeaway
A created meaning cannot independently necessitate a state; only God with His attributes is the true efficient cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes attribute the outcomes in your life—success, hardship, relationships—to secondary causes as if they have independent power? Where exactly in your daily reliance do you need to redirect your trust to Allah alone as the true agent? What concrete step will you take today to break that false attribution?
?
Sanusi argues that even if something is a 'root', it does not give it causal independence. In your own life, have you given any person, system, or habit an authority that belongs only to Allah's attributes? How can you actively remind yourself that they are merely vessels dependent on Him?
?
When you reflect on the concept that every state (ḥāl) is directly from Allah, what changes in your feeling of helplessness or control? Is there a specific situation where you have felt that an external cause 'made' you feel a certain way? How does this passage invite you to take responsibility before Allah instead?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Divine Attributes: No Causation, Only Correlation
Sanusi denies efficient causation for divine attributes, affirming only correlation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi denies efficient causation for God's attributes and holds that 'causation' in this context means necessary correlation.

Core Idea
God's attributes are necessary and uncaused; 'causation' is only correlation.

Having refuted the general view that a meaning necessitates a state, Sanusi now applies the argument to divine attributes. He shows that for necessary attributes—those that cannot cease to exist—there is no real causal relationship, only a logical correlation. This passage also clarifies that the dispute about causation applies even to the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the transition and conclusion
Start from 'وبالجملة' to see the summary of the previous argument and the shift to divine attributes.
Sanusi says the view is false, and even if true, only for created attributes.
2
Understand the nature of necessary attributes
Sanusi defines necessity as negation of non-existence, which entails pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqā').
Necessary: that which cannot cease to exist, so it has no beginning or end.
3
Deny external causation for divine attributes
Since necessary attributes cannot cease, they cannot be caused by anything external. 'Causation' (ta'lil) can only mean correlation (talāzum).
There is no efficient cause for God's attributes.
4
Examine the muqtaraḥ's dispute
Sanusi notes that the muqtaraḥ confirms the dispute also applies to the Necessary. Two views: causation as correlation vs. causation as meaning necessity.
Both views allow for necessary beings; the dispute is over how to interpret 'causation'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما من قال من المتكلمين : إن الفاعل يفعل المعنى ، والمعنى يوجب الحال ، ولم يفعل الفاعل الحال أصلاً . . فقوله في ذلك باطل قطعاً ؛ لأن تلك العلة إن أثرت في ثبوت الحال مع التقدم .. لزم تأخر المعلول عن علته بالزمان ، وهو محال ، وإن أثرت في الثبوت مع مصاحبة وجودها له.. لزم عدم تقدم المؤثر على أثره (٣) ، ولزم التحكم ؛ إذ ليس إسناد وجود العلة للفاعل المختار وهي أفادت ثبوت الحال .. بأولى من إسناد ثبوت الحال للفاعل وهي أفادت ثبوت تلك العلة ، بل طلب الحال للمعنى أقوى من طلب المعنى له ؛ لأن الحال لا تعقل متميزة إلا باعتبار معناها ، بخلاف العكس. فإن أجابوا بترجيح العلة للتأثير لكونها أصلاً .. قيل لهم : لا ملازمة بين كون الشيء أصلاً وكونه مؤثراً ، وإنما يصح التأثير لمن وجبت له صفات الألوهية ؛ من كمال العلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة والوحدانية إلى غير ذلك من الصفات التي لا تليق إلا بالله جل وعلا ، ولو كان كون الشيء أصلاً لغيره يقتضي استقلاله بإثبات غيره له .. للزم أن يكون تعالى إنما أوجد الجواهر وهي تستقل الملازم بإيجاد الأعراض ! وذلك معلوم البطلان. وبالجملة : فهذا القول باطل ، وعلى تقدير صحته فإنما يصح باعتبار صفاتنا الحادثة هي وأحوالها ، فأمكن إسنادها إلى مؤثر ، وأما صفاته جل وعلا فكلها واجبة ، والواجب من لازمه وجوب القدم والبقاء ؛ إذ الوجوب نفي قبول الانتفاء ، وما لا يقبل الانتفاء فلا انتفاء له سابقاً ولا لاحقاً ، وفي ذلك تحقيق قدمه وبقائه ، فلم يصح إسناده لمقتض أصلاً ، فلا معنى للتعليل إن أطلق فيها إلا التلازم . وظاهر كلام المقترح : أن الخلاف جار أيضاً في تعليل الواجب ؛ فإنه قد قال في تقرير الجواب عن شبهة المعتزلة التي سبق تقريرها : ( من قال بأن التعليل معناه التلازم . . يقول : قد يتلازم الممكنان وقد يتلازم الواجبان ، ولا منافاة ، ومن قال بأن المعنى يوجب .. قال : الحكم لا يجب إلا باعتبار وجوب معناه ، فإنا قلنا : إنه لا يعقل ( وهو تعقل المعنى متميزاً إلا باعتبار حالها ) .
And as for those among the theologians who said: 'The agent does the meaning, and the meaning necessitates the state, and the agent did not do the state at all'... his statement in that is absolutely false; because if that cause produced effect in the state with temporal precedence, then the effect would be delayed from its cause in time, which is impossible. And if it produced effect in [its] existence while accompanying its existence, then there would be no precedence of the efficient over its effect, and arbitrariness would follow; since attributing the existence of the cause to the free agent while it conferred the existence of the state is not more likely than attributing the existence of the state to the agent while it conferred the existence of that cause. Rather, the state's need for the meaning is stronger than the meaning's need for it; because the state is not conceivable as distinct except by consideration of its meaning, unlike the reverse. For if they answer by giving preference to the cause for effect due to its being a root (aṣl)... it is said to them: There is no necessary connection between a thing being a root and its being an efficient; rather, effect is only valid for one to whom the attributes of divinity are necessary: perfect knowledge, power, will, life, unity, and other attributes befitting only God Almighty. And if a thing being a root for another necessitated its independence in proving that other for it, then it would follow that God Most High only created substances while the concomitant is independent in creating accidents! And that is known to be false. In summary: this statement is false, and even if it were true, it would only be valid by considering our created attributes and their states, so it is possible to attribute them to an efficient. As for His attributes Almighty, all of them are necessary (wājib), and the necessary entails the necessity of pre-eternity and everlastingness; because necessity is the negation of susceptibility to non-existence, and that which does not admit non-existence has no non-existence before or after, and in that is the realization of His pre-eternity and everlastingness. So it is not valid to attribute it to any necessitator at all. Hence, if 'causation' is used regarding them, it has no meaning except correlation. And the apparent meaning of the muqtaraḥ's statement is that the dispute also applies to the causation of the Necessary; for he said in the exposition of the answer to the Mu'tazila's doubt which was previously presented: 'He who says that causation means correlation... says: two possibles may correlate and two necessities may correlate, with no contradiction. And he who says that the meaning necessitates... says: the judgment is only necessary by considering the necessity of its meaning, for we said: it is not conceivable (and it is the conceiving of the meaning as distinct except by consideration of its state).'

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second view that the agent only creates the meaning, and the meaning then produces the state. Sanusi presents several arguments: (1) If the cause precedes the effect in time, that is impossible for eternal things. (2) If cause and effect are simultaneous, then the cause has no real effect, leading to arbitrariness because there is no reason to attribute the effect to the cause rather than vice versa. (3) The state's dependence on the meaning is stronger, so it is more fitting for the agent to be the direct creator of the state. (4) If the opponent says the cause is more fundamental, Sanusi replies that being more fundamental does not entail being an efficient cause; only the divine attributes of God — perfect knowledge, power, etc. — make something truly efficient. (5) If being a root made one independent, then substances would independently create accidents, which is false. Then he moves to the case of divine attributes: all of God's attributes are necessary (wājib), meaning they cannot cease to exist. For such attributes, there is no real causation; the only meaning of 'ta'lil' is necessary correlation. Finally, he notes that the muqtaraḥ's discussion shows that this dispute also applies to the Necessary Being, and he quotes a view that for necessary beings, causation is merely correlation. This entire section guards against attributing temporal causation to divine attributes and emphasizes that God's attributes are not caused by something else.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine attributes are caused by God's essence or by an underlying meaning.
Correction: Sanusi says necessary attributes have no external cause; they are by definition uncaused. 'Causation' is only correlation.

Checkpoint
Why can't divine attributes have an efficient cause according to Sanusi?
A) Because they are created by another divine attribute.
B) Because they are necessary and thus cannot be produced or removed.
C) Because they are accidental.
D) Because they are only in our minds.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are necessary and thus have no efficient cause; 'causation' in this context means necessary correlation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat God's attributes as if they are caused by your actions—expecting mercy only when you are good, or wrath only when you sin—rather than seeing them as necessary and uncaused? How would your relationship with God change if you truly internalized that His attributes never fluctuate? This week, meditate on one attribute of God (e.g., His mercy) and remind yourself daily: this attribute is wājib, uncaused, and perfectly correlated with His essence.
?
When you face hardship, do you assume that God's justice or wisdom must be 'caused' by something you did or failed to do? How does Sanusi's teaching that divine attributes have no efficient cause free you from trying to manipulate God's responses? Commit to one situation where you will stop trying to 'earn' God's favor and instead rest in the fact that His attributes are necessary and unchanging.
?
Sanusi says that for created attributes, we can attribute them to an efficient cause, but for God's attributes, any 'causation' is only correlation. Is there an area of your life where you are using the same cause-and-effect logic for God that you use for creatures? What would it look like to trust that God's attributes correlate perfectly with His essence, even when you cannot see the connection? Write a prayer asking Him to help you live in the reality of His uncaused, perfect attributes.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
10 min
Refutation of Created Power Having Real Effect
Sanusi refutes the claim that created power causes effects and affirms absolute divine causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari theologians affirm that Allah alone is the true creator and that no created power can produce any effect independently or even dependently.

Core Idea
Allah is the sole creator; created power causes nothing.

In this passage, Sanusi builds on the previous discussion of divine attributes to tackle a controversial issue: do human actions or any created powers have genuine causal efficacy? Some theologians held that created power does affect actions, albeit within a predetermined measure. Sanusi shows why such a view is rationally untenable and affirms the exclusive divine causality. Let's follow his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Sunni position
Read the opening lines where Sanusi states the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah: Allah alone is the creator of every possible thing, and causal effect is a property exclusive to Him. Note the Qur'anic evidence he cites.
This sets the doctrinal foundation.
2
Understand the reported view
Sanusi mentions that some theologians like Imām al-Ḥaramayn reportedly claimed that created power does affect actions, but not independently. Recognize that this is a compromise view that Sanusi will refute.
3
Follow the logical dilemma
Examine the two-horned dilemma: either the effect is an essential attribute of the power or it is not. Sanusi shows both options lead to absurdities. Trace each horn carefully.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Consider the defense and its refutation
Sanusi anticipates an objection: 'The effect occurs only according to Allah's will.' He rejects this by showing that if effect is essential, it cannot depend on external conditions like divine will. Understand why this answer fails.
5
Reflect on the implications
Conclude that no created power can have any real causal efficacy. This reinforces tawhid and the Ash'ari stance on occasionalism. Note how this connects to the broader theology of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا عرفت أنَّ الصواب ما قالَهُ أهلُ السنَّةِ ، ودلَّ عليهِ ظاهرُ الكتاب والحديثِ ، وأجمعَ عليه السلف الصالح قبل ظهورِ البدع ؛ مِنْ أنَّ اللهَ تعالى هو الخالق بالاختيار لكل ممكن يبرز إلى الوجودِ ، ذاتاً كانَ أو قولاً لها أو فعلاً ، لا يشاركه تعالى في ملكِ الممكنات شيء أي شيء كانَ ، وأنَّ التأثير وإيجاد الممكناتِ خاصَّيَّةٌ مِنْ خواصه تعالى ، يستحيلُ ثبوتُهما لغيرِهِ ، قال تعالى : ﴿ إِنَّا كُلَّ شَيْءٍ خَلَقْتَهُ بِقَدَرٍ ﴾ ، وقال تعالى : ﴿ وَاللَّهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ إلى غير ذلكَ مِنَ الظواهر التي لا تنحصر . ما نُقِلَ عن إمام الحرمين في تأثير القدرة الحادثة ، وكذا ما نُقل عن القاضي والأستاذ . وما نُقِلَ عن إمام الحرمين مِنْ أنَّ له قولاً بأنَّ القدرة الحادثة تُؤثر في الأفعال ، لكن لا على سبيل الاستقلال كما تقولُ القدريَّةُ ، بل على أقدار قدَّرَها الله تعالى . فهو قول مرغوب عنه ، لا يصح القول به ، ولا تقليده في ذلكَ إنْ صح عنه ؛ لفسادِه قطعاً ، وعدم جريه على السنَّةِ عقلاً ونقلاً ؛ لأنَّ القدرة الحادثة على مقتضى هذا القول : إمَّا أنْ يكونَ مِنْ صفةِ نفسها إيجاد الفعل الذي تتعلَّقُ بهِ أو لا ؛ فإنْ كانَ الأوَّلُ لزمَ عندَ تعلُّقها بالفعل إمَّا سلب صفتِها النفسيَّةِ إنْ لم تُؤثر في الفعل ، وكان الموجدُ له هو الله تعالى ، أو غلبتها لقدرته تعالى إنْ كانَتْ هي التي أثَرَتْ في الفعل ، وفرضنا أنَّ اللهَ تعالى أرادَ أَنْ يُوجِدَ ذلكَ الفعلَ بقدرته ، وكلا الأمرين محال . ولا يدفع محذور ما لزمَ مِنَ العجز والغلبة في الثاني .. قوله : ( إنَّ تأثيرها إنَّما هو على وَفْقِ إرادتِهِ تعالى ) لأنَّ التأثيرَ إِذا قُدِّرَ أَنَّهُ صفةٌ نفسيَّةُ للقدرة الحادثة.. لم يمكن أن يتوقف ثبوتُهُ لها على شيء أصلاً . وإنْ كانَ الثاني - وهو أنَّ التأثير ليس صفةً نفسيَّةً للقدرةِ الحادثة - لزمَ أنْ تفتقر إلى معنى يقوم بها ويُوجِبُ لها التأثير ، وننقل حينئذٍ الكلام إلى ذلك المعنى الذي أوجب لها التأثير : هل ذلك أيضاً مِنْ صفة نفسِهِ ، أو لمعنى قامَ بهِ ؟ ويلزم التسلسل ، وقيام المعنى بالمعلنى . وكذا أيضاً لا يخفى فساد ما نُقِلَ عن القاضي والأستاذ : أنَّ القدرة
And if you know this, you will know that the correct view is what Ahl al-Sunnah said, and is indicated by the apparent meaning of the Book and the Sunnah, and upon which the righteous predecessors agreed before the emergence of innovations: that Allah Almighty is the creator by choice of every possible thing that emerges into existence, whether it be an essence, or a statement belonging to it, or an act; nothing shares with Him Almighty in the dominion of possibles, anything whatsoever; and that effect (al-ta'thīr) and bringing possibles into existence are a specific property (khāṣṣiyyah) of His attributes, and it is impossible for them to be established for other than Him. He Almighty said: {Indeed, We have created everything by measure} (54:49), and He Almighty said: {And Allah created you and what you do} (37:96), and other such clear proofs which are countless. [We have] what was transmitted from Imām al-Ḥaramayn concerning the effect of the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha), and likewise what was transmitted from al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh. And what was transmitted from Imām al-Ḥaramayn that he had a saying that the originated power does affect actions, but not independently as the Qadariyya say, rather according to measures (aqdār) that Allah Almighty has determined. So that is a rejected saying, it is not correct to utter it, nor to imitate him in that if it is authentic from him; because of its absolute corruption, and its lack of conformity to the Sunnah both rationally and by transmission. Because the originated power, according to this saying, either that the bringing into existence (ījād) of the act to which it is related is from its essential attribute (ṣifat nafshā) or not. If the first, then upon its relation to the act, there necessarily follows either the negation of its essential attribute if it does not affect the act, and the bringer into existence of it is Allah Almighty, or its being overcome by His power if it is the one that affected the act, while we have assumed that Allah Almighty willed to bring that act into existence by His power; and both matters are impossible. And the harmful consequence that follows from incapacity and being overcome in the second is not repelled by his saying: 'Its effect is only in accordance with His will,' because if the effect is assumed to be an essential attribute of the originated power, it cannot be that its establishment for it depends on anything at all. And if the second—that the effect is not an essential attribute of the originated power—then it necessarily requires a meaning (maʿnā) that subsists in it and necessitates for it the effect, and we then transfer the discussion to that meaning which necessitated the effect: is that also from its own attribute, or due to a meaning subsisting in it? And an infinite regress (tasalsul) is necessitated, and the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning. Likewise also the corruption of what was transmitted from al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh—that the power...
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of any view that attributes genuine causal efficacy to created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha). The author begins by stating the Sunni position: Allah alone is the creator of everything, including human actions, and causal effect (al-ta'thīr) is a property exclusive to Him. Then he addresses reports that Imām al-Ḥaramayn, al-Qāḍī, and al-Ustādh held that created power does affect actions, albeit not independently but by a measure determined by Allah. He declares this saying invalid and proceeds to disprove it with a logical dilemma. The dilemma: For the originated power (capability) that is claimed to produce an effect, either the effect is an essential attribute of that power or it is not. If it is essential, then whenever the power relates to an act, it must produce it; but we know that Allah may will that act and create it directly. Thus, if the power does not produce it (because Allah creates it instead), the power's essential attribute is negated—impossible. If the power does produce it, then it overcomes Allah's power, as it would produce an act that Allah willed to create Himself—also impossible. Both horns are absurd. Some might object that the power's effect is only in accordance with Allah's will, so no conflict occurs. The author replies: if effect is essential, it cannot depend on any external condition like divine will; it would necessarily occur whenever the power exists, regardless of will. If they instead choose the second horn—that effect is not essential—then the power needs some additional cause to give it the effect, and that cause itself would need another, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Thus, the view collapses. The author then begins to mention the similar corruption of al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh's view, but the page cuts off.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)
التأثير (al-ta'thīr)
صفة نفسية (ṣifat nafsiyya)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
المعنى (al-maʿnā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ash'aris completely deny any secondary causality, but they only deny necessary causal connection. The specific misconception here is that created power can have a real effect, even if dependent on Allah's will.
Correction: Sanusi demonstrates that attributing any real effect to created power leads to logical absurdities (either negating its essential attribute or infinite regress). The only coherent view is that Allah alone is the true cause.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, if the effect of originated power is an essential attribute (ṣifat nafsiyya), what happens when Allah wills to create the same act?
A) The power refrains from acting to avoid conflict.
B) Either the power loses its essential attribute or overcomes Allah's power.
C) The power acts in accordance with Allah's will without issue.
D) The power becomes non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The logical dilemma shows that no created power can have any real causal effect; Allah alone is the sole creator and the exclusive source of causal efficacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a difficult task, do you attribute the outcome to your own ability or to Allah's creation of that outcome? Which attribution changes how you feel before your next task?
?
This lesson argues that even the power you feel within yourself has no true effect. How would your daily prayers and reliance on Allah change if you fully internalized that every breath, move, and success is His direct creation?
?
Is there a part of your life—your career, relationships, or spiritual growth—where you act as if your effort alone can produce results? What would it mean to surrender that illusion and let Allah be the sole creator of every good in your life?

Refutation of al-Qadi and al-Ustadh on the Specific Description
Sanusi critiques al-Qadi and al-Ustadh on the specific description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the views of al-Qadi and al-Ustadh on the effect of human power on the act's specific description, and why al-Sanusi rejects them both.

Core Idea
Human power only touches the specific description, not existence — but what is that description?

In the previous lessons, al-Sanusi established that human power only affects the act's specific description, not its existence. Now he addresses two earlier scholars—al-Qadi and al-Ustadh—who held a similar view but disagreed on what that 'specific description' actually is. Their disagreement reveals deeper commitments about modes and mental considerations. Al-Sanusi, however, dismisses both positions as obviously corrupt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a shared claim, then the disagreement between al-Qadi and al-Ustadh.
Look for the terms 'ḥāl', 'wajh', and 'iʿtibār'.
2
Identify common ground and difference
Both scholars agree that the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) affects the most specific description (akhaṣṣ waṣf) of the act, not its existence. They differ on the ontological status of that description. Al-Qadi calls it a 'mode' (ḥāl); al-Ustadh rejects modes and calls it an 'aspect' (wajh) or 'consideration' (iʿtibār).
The disagreement reflects their broader views on the reality of modes.
3
Reflect on al-Sanusi's critique
Al-Sanusi states that the corruption of both views is 'not hidden' (lā yakhfā fasad). Ask yourself why he would consider the entire framing—that human power only affects the specific description—as fundamentally flawed.
This connects to the broader Ashʿarī doctrine that God alone creates all aspects of actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا أيضاً لا يخفى فساد ما نُقِلَ عن القاضي والأستاذ : أنَّ القدرة الحادثة تُؤثر في أخص وصْفِ الفعلِ لا في وجودِهِ ، إلا أنَّ القاضي يقولُ : إِنَّ أخصَّ وصْفِ الفعل حال ، والأستاذ ينفي الأحوال ، ويقولُ : إِنَّ أخص وصْفِ الفعل وجه واعتبار .
And likewise, the corruption of what was transmitted from al-Qadi and al-Ustadh is also not hidden: that the originated power effects the most specific description (akhaṣṣ waṣf) of the act, not its existence, except that al-Qadi says: the most specific description of the act is a mode (ḥāl), and al-Ustadh denies modes and says: the most specific description of the act is an aspect (wajh) and a consideration (iʿtibār).
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a critical evaluation of two earlier scholars, al-Qadi and al-Ustadh, who held a nuanced view that human power affects not the existence of the act but its most specific description. The author, al-Sanusi, states that the corruption of this view is obvious ('la yakhfa fasad'). However, within this shared framework, the two scholars disagree on the ontological status of that specific description. Al-Qadi considers it a 'mode' (ḥāl)—a state that is neither existent nor non-existent in the usual sense. Al-Ustadh rejects modes entirely and instead calls it an 'aspect' (wajh) or 'consideration' (iʿtibār), which is a mental or relational attribute. This disagreement reflects their broader theoretical commitments. Al-Sanusi appears to reject both positions, implying that any attempt to limit human power to only the specific description is fundamentally flawed.

Key Terms
أخص وصف
حال
وجه
اعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disagreement between al-Qadi and al-Ustadh is about whether human power plays any role in the act at all.
Correction: Both agree that human power affects the specific description, not existence. Their disagreement is about the nature of that description (mode vs. aspect).

Checkpoint
According to al-Sanusi's passage, how do al-Qadi and al-Ustadh differ regarding the most specific description of the act?
A) Al-Qadi says it is a cause, al-Ustadh says it is an effect.
B) Al-Qadi says it is a mode (ḥāl), al-Ustadh says it is an aspect (wajh).
C) Al-Qadi says it is created by God, al-Ustadh says it is created by the human.
D) Al-Qadi denies it, al-Ustadh affirms it.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sanusi rejects both al-Qadi's and al-Ustadh's attempts to nuance human causal efficacy; for him, God alone creates all aspects of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish a good deed, do you instinctively feel a sense of ownership or pride? How does Sanusi's categorical rejection of any human causal role challenge that feeling? What would it take for you to truly believe that God alone created that obedience?
?
Al-Qadi and al-Ustadh both attempted to carve out a space for human power while affirming God's sovereignty. Are there areas in your life where you subtly do the same—holding on to a sense of personal control while intellectually affirming that God does everything? Name one such area right now.
?
Sanusi dismisses these nuanced positions as obviously corrupt. Is there a belief or practice in your faith that you've been treating with intellectual nuance to avoid a hard truth—perhaps about divine decree or your own helplessness? What would it cost you to accept the plain, uncompromising stance?
