Al-Shahrastani's Two-Aspect Theory of Human Action
Al-Shahrastani distinguishes between divine creation and human acquisition using the specific description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Shahrastani's two-aspect theory of human action: how an act is entirely God's creation in its existence but is attributed to the human in its specific moral quality, which is the basis of moral responsibility.

Core Idea
An act's existence is from God; its moral quality is from the human.

Al-Shahrastani, a prominent Ash'ari theologian, refines the doctrine of acquisition (kasb) by distinguishing two aspects of every human act. On one hand, the act's existence as a created entity is solely from God; on the other, its specific quality—whether it is obedience or disobedience—is attributed to the human. This distinction preserves God's exclusive creative power while explaining human moral accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation side by side. Notice that the passage discusses two perspectives: movement as existence vs. movement as a particular moral act (e.g., prayer, theft).
Pay attention to the words 'من حيث' which signal the two different aspects.
2
Identify the two attributions
Find where the passage attributes the act to God (creation, knowledge, no inherence) and where it attributes it to the human (specific description, locus, attribution as 'he is praying').
God creates the movement; the human performs the specific act.
3
Understand the role of specific description
The passage states that the command and prohibition address the specific description (e.g., 'pray, do not steal'). That specific quality is what makes an act obedience or disobedience and what merits reward or punishment.
The existence of the act is neutral; only its moral quality matters for evaluation.
4
Reflect on the solution to a theological problem
Consider how this two-aspect theory solves the tension between God as the sole creator and human responsibility. Humans do not create the act's existence, but they are responsible for its moral quality through acquisition (kasb).
This aligns with Ash'ari theology that avoids attributing any share in creation to humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختار الشَّهْرَسْتانيُّ مذهبَ القاضي ، وفرَّقَ بين وجهي الاختراع والكسب : بأنَّ الحركة مِنْ حيثُ هي حركةٌ تُنسَبُ إلى فعلِ اللهِ تعالى إيجاداً واختراعاً ، ويلزمُ مِنْ ذلكَ علَمُهُ بهَا مِنْ جميع وجوهها وأَنَّهُ لا يفعلُ في ذاتِهِ ولا يتَصفُ بها اتصاف قيام ، فلا تُضافُ إِليهِ مِنَ العبدِ مِنْ حيثُ خصوصها ، فيُقالُ : أوجدها وأحدثها ، ولا يُقالُ : إِنَّهُ متحرك بها ، وتُنسَبُ إلى العبدِ مِنْ حيثُ خصوصها ؛ وهو كون تلك الصفةِ صلاةً أو غصباً أو سرقةً ، ولا تأثير لقدرةِ العبدِ إلا في ذلكَ الوجه ، ولا يُشترَطُ علمُهُ بالفعل مِنْ كلّ وجه ، وذاتُهُ محل فعلِهِ وكسبه ، وتكونُ صفةٌ لهُ ، فيُقالُ : إِنَّهُ مُتحرِّكُ وساكن ومُصلِّ وغاصب ، وإن اتصلَ بهِ أمرُ فوقع على موافقته.. سُمِّيَ طاعةً وعبادةً ، وإن اتصلَ بهِ نهي فوقع على خلافه.. سُمِّيَ معصيةٌ وجريمة ، وذلك الوجه هو المكلَّف به ، وهو الذي توجَّهَ الخطاب بهِ ، فقيل : صل ، ولا تسرق ، ولا تغصب ، وهو المقابل بالثواب والعقاب ، والمدح والذم ، لا مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّهُ موجود
And al-Shahrastani chose the doctrine of al-Qadi, and distinguished between the two aspects of creation (ikhtirāʿ) and acquisition (kasb): that movement, insofar as it is movement, is attributed to the act of God the Exalted as bringing into existence and creation; and from that follows His knowledge of it from all its aspects, and that He does not act in His essence nor is He described by it with a description of inherence, so it is not attributed to Him from the servant from the aspect of its particularity. Thus it is said: He brought it into existence and originated it, and it is not said: He is moving by it. And it is attributed to the servant from the aspect of its particularity, which is that the attribute be prayer, or usurpation, or theft. And there is no effect of the servant's power except in that aspect, and his knowledge of the act from all aspects is not required, and his essence is the locus of his act and acquisition, and it becomes an attribute for him, so it is said: he is moving and still and praying and usurping. And if a command attaches to it and it occurs in conformity to it, it is called obedience and worship; and if a prohibition attaches to it and it occurs contrary to it, it is called disobedience and crime. And that aspect is what one is obligated concerning, and it is what the address is directed to, so it is said: pray, do not steal, do not usurp. And it is what is met with reward and punishment, praise and blame, not insofar as it is existent.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of al-Shahrastani's position on the distinction between divine creation (ikhtirāʿ) and human acquisition (kasb) within the Ash'ari framework. He adopts al-Qadi's view that human power acts on the most specific description. The distinction is made on the basis of two perspectives on the same act (e.g., movement). From the perspective of its existence as a created entity, the act is entirely from God: He brings it into existence, knows it in all its aspects, and does not become qualified by it in the sense of inherence. For example, we say 'God created the movement,' but we do not say 'God is moving by it.' From the perspective of its specific description (khṣūṣiyya)—that it is prayer, theft, etc.—the act is attributed to the human servant. The servant's power only affects that specific aspect, not the existence. Consequently, the servant is described as 'praying' or 'stealing' because his essence is the locus (maḥall) of the act as acquired. This specific description is the subject of religious obligation (taklīf): it determines whether the act is obedience or disobedience, and it is what merits reward or punishment. The existent act qua existence is not the object of moral evaluation—only its specific quality is. This preserves God's exclusive role as creator while allowing human moral responsibility.

Key Terms
اختراع
كسب
أخص وصف
خصوص
طاعة
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Acquisition (kasb) means the human creates the act's existence or a part of it.
Correction: In al-Shahrastani's view, the human's power only affects the act's specific moral quality (e.g., being prayer or theft), not its existence. The existence remains entirely God's creation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Shahrastani, which aspect of an act is the subject of reward and punishment?
A) The act's existence as a created entity.
B) The act's specific description, such as being prayer or theft.
C) The act's universal essence.
D) The act's relation to God's will.

Key Takeaway
Al-Shahrastani resolves the tension between divine omnipotence and human responsibility by attributing the existence of acts to God and their specific moral qualities to human acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship like prayer, do you sense that its very existence is a gift from God while you are only responsible for its 'prayer-ness'? How would your awareness of God's presence change if you consciously separated the two aspects in your mind?
?
If your act's existence is entirely from God, then your sin is not an independent rebellion but a misdirection of God-given existence. Does that humble you about your disobedience or make you more accountable? What does it mean that God creates the motion of your hand as it steals or hits?
?
Al-Shahrastani says the servant's essence is the locus of the act. When you sin, do you feel that your very self becomes the site of that sin, and that God's knowledge encompasses it? How does that thought compel you to purify your intentions before your next action?

The Five Positions on Created Power
Sanusi enumerates four false positions on created power and affirms the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five positions on the effect of created power and why only the first is correct according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
Created power has a dependent effect; only Allah creates independently.

Sanusi now addresses a key dispute: Does the power Allah creates in humans actually affect their actions? Various sects and scholars have answered differently. In this passage, he enumerates five positions, declares four false, and affirms the Ash'ari middle path of acquisition (kasb). He also expresses astonishment that some respected scholars were attributed with erroneous views.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of positions
Read the Arabic passage and identify each of the five positions on created power. Note who each is attributed to: the second to Imam al-Haramayn, the third to al-Qadi, the fourth to the Jabriyya, the fifth to the Qadariyya.
The first position is not explicitly described here but is the Ash'ari view affirmed later.
2
Notice the author's evaluation
The author clearly states that all positions except the first are false. Underline the sentence: 'وهذه الأقوال كلها باطلة ما عدا الأول'.
3
Consider the amazement
Sanusi expresses amazement that the second position was attributed to Imam al-Haramayn, given his own writings support the Ash'ari doctrine. Ask: Why would Sanusi find this contradictory?
This highlights the importance of verifying attributions and not relying on isolated reports.
4
Connect to the theory of kasb
Reflect on how the correct position aligns with the Ash'ari theory of acquisition: the servant's power is created by Allah and has an effect that is dependent, not independent.
This resolves the tension between divine omnipotence and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : القولُ الذي حُكِي عن الإمام : أنَّ القدرة الحادثة تُؤثر في وجود الفعل على أقدار قدَّرَها البارى تعالى
والثالث : قول القاضى ومَنْ تابعَهُ : أَنَّها تؤثر في أخص وصفِ الفعل ، لا في وجودِهِ .
والرابع : مذهبُ الجبريَّةِ : أَنَّهُ لا قدرة أصلاً ، وإنَّما المخلوقُ للعبدِ المقدورُ فقط ؛ كالحركة والسكون مثلاً ، ولا زيادة عليه أصلاً ، وساوَوا بين المضطر كالمرتعش وبين المختار
والخامس : مذهبُ القدريَّةِ مجوس هذه الأمة : أنَّ القدرة الحادثة تُؤثر في وجودِ الفعل على سبيل الاستقلال .
وهذه الأقوال كلُّها باطلةٌ ما عدا الأوَّلَ ، وإِيَّاهُ اعتمدت في هذهِ العقيدة ، وهو الحقُّ الذي لا شكّ فيه ، وأنا أعجبُ مِنَ القولِ الذي نُقِلَ عن الإمام كيف يصحُ أنْ يقولهُ معَ ما أكثرَ في (الإرشاد) وغيرِهِ مِنَ الأدلَّةِ لتصحيح المذهب الحقِّ ؛ وهو مذهب الأشعري ، ومبالغتِهِ في النكير والتضليل لمَنْ يعتقد أنَّ للقدرة الحادثة تأثيراً ما ، وكذا ما نُقِلَ عن القاضي والأستاذ مع ما لهما في تواليفهما ممَّا يُضادُّهُ !
The second: the statement that was narrated from al-Imam: that the created power influences the existence of the act according to measures that the Creator, exalted, has determined. The third: the statement of al-Qadi and those who followed him: that it influences the most specific description of the act, not its existence. The fourth: the doctrine of the Jabriyya: that there is no power at all, and that what is created for the servant is only the predetermined act, like motion and stillness for example, and no increase upon it at all, and they equated between the compelled person like the one trembling and the free chooser. The fifth: the doctrine of the Qadariyya, the Magians of this community: that the created power influences the existence of the act independently. And all these statements are false except the first one, and it is what I have relied upon in this creed, and it is the truth about which there is no doubt. And I am amazed at the statement narrated from al-Imam: how can he say it, given his many proofs in (al-Irshad) and other works to confirm the correct doctrine—which is the Ash'ari doctrine—and his extreme criticism and misguidance of those who believe that the created power has any effect, and likewise what has been narrated from al-Qadi and al-Ustadh, given what they have in their writings that contradicts it!
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and evaluation of five positions on the effect of the created power (al-qudra al-hāditha). The author lists four false positions: the second (attributed to Imam al-Haramayn) says the created power influences the existence of the act according to predetermined measures; the third (al-Qadi and followers) says it influences only the most specific description of the act, not its existence; the fourth (Jabriyya) denies any power for the servant, equating the compelled with the free; the fifth (Qadariyya) claims the created power influences the act independently. The author declares all these false except the first, which he affirms as the truth he relies on. He then expresses amazement that the second position could be attributed to Imam al-Haramayn, given his own arguments in al-Irshad supporting the Ash'ari doctrine and his strong condemnation of those who believe the created power has any effect. He likewise questions the attributions to al-Qadi and al-Ustadh, given their own writings contradict such views.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة (al-qudra al-hāditha)
الجبرية (al-Jabriyya)
القدرية (al-Qadariyya)
الاستقلال (al-istiqlāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris deny any effect of created power, leaving no human role.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm a dependent effect (kasb): the created power influences the act but not independently; Allah creates the act and the power simultaneously.

Checkpoint
Which of the five positions mentioned does the author affirm as true?
A) The second position
B) The third position
C) The first position
D) The fifth position

Key Takeaway
The correct position is that created power influences the act in a dependent manner, not independently, affirming both divine omnipotence and human responsibility through acquisition (kasb).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute a success to your own effort, do you genuinely believe that your power was entirely dependent on Allah? Or is there a part of your heart that feels you did it 'on your own'? What would it mean to truly internalize that your power influences only according to Allah's determination?
?
The author expresses amazement at how Imam al-Haramayn could say something that contradicts his own works. Are there areas in your life where your words or beliefs contradict your actions? Which contradiction is most urgent for you to resolve today?
?
If you were to be honest with yourself, do you sometimes fall into the Jabriyya trap by using 'everything is from Allah' as an excuse for negligence? Or do you lean toward Qadariyya by claiming credit for your achievements? Which tendency is more dangerous for your soul, and what one concrete step can you take this week to align with the truth of dependent agency?

Proof of Concurrent Temporally Originated Power
Sanusi proves concurrent temporally originated power and explains its basis in accident nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument for the existence of a temporally originated power concurrent with its effect, based on the necessary distinction between forced and voluntary movement, and why this concurrence is grounded in the nature of accidents.

Core Idea
The distinction between forced and voluntary movement proves that power appears at the time of action.

Sanusi begins a concise refutation of the Jabriyya determinists by demonstrating that voluntary movement necessarily implies a power that is present at the time of the action. He then clarifies that this power is an accident, and its concurrence with the effect follows from the transient nature of accidents. This passage lays the foundation for understanding human agency within Sunni kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two distinct parts: the proof from movement distinction and the explanation about accidents.
The passage is dense; take it slowly.
2
Identify the proof
Find the key phrase 'الفرق الضروري بين حركة الاضطرار وحركة الاختيار' and understand how this distinction leads to affirming concurrent powers.
3
Understand the nature of the power
Sanusi states that concurrence is not because it is a power, but because it is an accident. Explore why accidents have the ruling of non-existence.
Accidents do not persist, so they must be concurrent with their effect.
4
Apply to refutation
Consider how this argument refutes the Jabriyya who deny human power entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الردُّ على الجبرية بإثباتِ القدرة الحادثة . وإنما قلنا بوجودِ قُدَرٍ مقارنة؛ لما نجدهُ مِنَ الفرقِ الضروري بين حركة الاضطرار وحركة الاختيار . أمَّا مقارنة القدرة الحادثة لمقدورها فهو الذي عليهِ إمام الحرمين ، ونص عليه كثيرٌ مِنْ أئمَّةِ السَّنَّةِ ، وهذا الحكم ليسَ ثابتاً لها مِنْ حيثُ كونها قدرةً ، بل مِنْ حيث إنَّها عرضُ ، ومِنْ أحكام العرض : انعدامه
The refutation of the Jabriyya by proving the temporally originated power. And we only said that there exist concurrent powers because of what we find of the necessary distinction between forced movement and voluntary movement. As for the concurrence of the temporally originated power with its effect, that is what Imam al-Haramayn held, and many of the imams of the Sunnah stated it. And this ruling is not established for it inasmuch as it is a power, but inasmuch as it is an accident. And among the rulings of an accident is its non-existence.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage begins a refutation of the Jabriyya (determinists) by affirming the existence of a temporally originated power (qudra haditha) that is concurrent with its effect. The proof is based on the necessary distinction between involuntary and voluntary movement. Since voluntary movement implies a power that is present at the time of the action, unlike forced movement where no such power is present. Sanusi then notes that the concurrence of the power with its effect is the position of Imam al-Haramayn and many other Sunni imams. He clarifies that this property of concurrence is not due to the power being a power per se, but because it is an accident (arad). Accidents, by their nature, are characterized by non-existence (they do not subsist for two consecutive moments), so they must be concurrent with their effect since they cannot precede it.

Key Terms
قدرة حادثة
قدر مقارنة
حركة اضطرار
حركة اختيار
عرض
انعدام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The temporally originated power is an independently subsisting entity.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the power is an accident, and its concurrence with the effect stems from the nature of accidents (i.e., they do not persist), not from its being a power.

Checkpoint
Why is the concurrence of the temporally originated power with its effect attributed to its being an accident rather than a power?
A) Because accidents do not persist and must be concurrent with their effect.
B) Because power is eternal.
C) Because the Jabriyya deny concurrence.
D) Because Imam al-Haramayn said so.

Key Takeaway
The necessary distinction between forced and voluntary movement proves that a temporally originated power exists concurrently with its effect, and this concurrence follows from the fact that the power is an accident.

Does this awareness change how you prepare for an action, especially before committing a sin? Will you now pause to seek Allah's help for the power to obey, knowing that it is not in your own hands?
?
Sanusi proves that the power in your actions is an accident—fleeting and dependent. If your power is recreated every instant, what does that mean for your sense of ownership over your deeds? How can you use this truth to humble yourself and increase your reliance on Allah in every moment?

Objection: Power May Precede Its Object – The Knowledge Analogy
Objection that power may precede its object, with analogy to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that power can precede its object by analogy with knowledge, and the initial response that challenges this analogy.

Core Idea
If power is not a cause, it could be replaced before its object – just like knowledge of a future event.

The author presents an objection from a hypothetical proposer who challenges the doctrine that power must be concurrent with its object. The objector argues that since power is not a cause, it could exist beforehand and be replaced, using the analogy of knowledge about a future event. The author begins a response, but the text is incomplete. In this lesson, we will examine the objection and the start of the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'قال المقترح' to 'فالمقارن متعلق بالوجود'. Understand the objector's main claim: that power is not a cause, so it may precede its object.
The objector's argument hinges on the idea that power does not necessitate the object's existence.
2
Examine the knowledge analogy
The objector compares power to knowledge of a future event. Identify how the analogy is supposed to work: knowledge can be renewed, so power can be replaced.
The analogy is described from 'وهذا كما لو علم إنسان' to 'فالمقارن متعلق بالوجود'.
3
Consider the beginning of the answer
Read the response starting from 'ولو قدر وجود ضد العلم'. The author seems to challenge the analogy by considering opposites of knowledge. The text cuts off, so consider what the full argument might be.
The response is incomplete; we only have the opening phrase.
4
Evaluate the strength of the objection
Think about whether the objector's analogy is valid. Does the replacement of powers undermine the necessity of concurrent existence? Reflect on how kalam theologians might respond.
This connects to the broader discussion of causality in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المقترح : ( وهذا عندي فيه نظر ؛ مِنْ حيثُ إِنَّ امتناع التقدُّم إذا لم يكن مأخوذاً إلا مِنْ حيثُ استحالة بقائها ، فالقدرة في التحقيقِ ليست علة لوجودِ المقدور ولا مُؤثّرةً فيه ، فإذا لم يكنْ مِنْ حكمها . وجود المقدور فيجوز وجودها قبل وقوع المقدور وتُعدَمُ ويُوجَدُ مثلها ، فالمقارنة متعلقة ، والسابقة متعلقة . ويصح أنْ يُقالَ : كانَتْ تلك القدرة مُتعلّقةً بِهِ قبل عدمِها ، ثمَّ انتفَتْ فانتفى تعلقها ووُجِدَ مثلها ، وهذا كما لو علم إنسان وجود زيدٍ غداً وقتَ طلوع الفجرِ مثلاً بإنباء صادقٍ ، ثم قدَّرْنا تجدُّدَ علمه بوجودِهِ في الوقتِ المعلومِ إلى حالة وجودِ المعلومِ في الوقت الذي أخبر عنه. . فإنَّ المقارنَ مُتعلّق بالوجودِ ، والسابق مُتعلّق بالوجودِ في الزمن المخصوص ، فالمعلومُ مُتعلَّق لهما ، وأحدهما مُتقدِّم والآخرُ مُتأخّرُ . ولو قُدْرَ وجود ضدّ العلمِ ؛ مِنْ ذهول أو غفلةٍ أو جهل أو شكٍّ حال
The proposer said: (This, in my view, requires consideration; insofar as the impossibility of precedence is taken only from the impossibility of its remaining, then power in reality is not a cause for the existence of the object nor an effecter in it. So if it is not among its rulings to require the existence of the object, then it is permissible for it to exist before the occurrence of the object and then perish, and a similar one comes into existence. So the concurrent [power] is related, and the preceding [power] is related. And it is correct to say: That power was related to it before its nonexistence, then it ceased and its relation ceased, and a similar one came into existence. This is like if a person knows the existence of Zayd tomorrow at dawn, for example, through a truthful report, then we suppose a renewal of his knowledge of his existence at the known time until the state of the known's existence at the time about which he reported. For the concurrent [knowledge] is related to existence, and the preceding [knowledge] is related to existence at the specific time; so the known is related to both, one preceding and the other subsequent. And if the existence of the opposite of knowledge—such as heedlessness, forgetfulness, ignorance, or doubt—were supposed at the state of...
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (اعتراض) from a hypothetical proposer (المقترح) and begins the author's response. The objector argues that the proof that power cannot precede its object depends on the impossibility of its remaining for two instants. But if power is not a cause or effecter of its object, it need not exist at the same time. Therefore, it could exist earlier, perish, and then a similar power could arise at the moment of the action. Each power would be related to the same object, one preceding, one concurrent. The objector draws an analogy with knowledge: if someone knows that Zayd will exist tomorrow, that knowledge is related to Zayd's existence at the specific time. That knowledge may cease, and new knowledge may arise at the moment of existence, both related to the same known event. Similarly, he argues, power can be related in two ways. The author's response (beginning after the objection) seems to challenge this analogy, but the text is incomplete, cutting off mid-sentence at 'the state of (ḥāl)'. The full answer is not available in this window.

Refutation of the Doctrine of Tawallud (Generation of Effects)
Sanusi refutes tawallud by logical deduction and lists its absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the doctrine of tawallud (generation of effects) is rationally impossible according to Sanusi, and what logical contradictions it entails.

Core Idea
Created power cannot produce effects outside its own locus—tawallud is absurd.

In this passage, Sanusi builds on his earlier proof that a created power cannot affect its own locus. He now extends that conclusion to show that it cannot generate effects in another locus through an intermediary. This refutes the Mu'tazilite doctrine of tawallud, which they used to explain secondary causation. Sanusi highlights several logical contradictions that follow from their view.

Guided Path
1
Recall the earlier conclusion
Start by remembering that created power cannot affect its own locus. This is the foundation for the refutation.
2
Identify the doctrine of tawallud
Recognize that the Qadariyya claim effects can be generated through an intermediary, called tawallud. Examples: throwing a stone, striking with a sword.
The examples show effects that seem produced indirectly.
3
List the absurdities
Sanusi states that tawallud leads to contradictions: an effect between two agents, an act without an agent, or an agent without will and knowledge.
Contemplate why each of these is impossible.
4
Consider the majority consensus
The majority of scholars reject generation even for natural processes like satiety from eating. This further weakens the doctrine.
This shows the doctrine is not universally accepted.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Connect the conditional premise, the examples, the absurdities, and the consensus to see the complete refutation.
Sanusi's argument is deductive and cumulative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصلُ في إبطال القول بالتولد. وإذا عرفت استحالة تأثير القدرة الحادثة في محلها .. بطل لذلك أيضاً تأثيرها بواسطة مقدورها في غير محلها؛ كرمي الحجرِ والضرب بالسيفِ ونحو ذلكَ ممَّا يُوجَدُ عادةً بواسطة حركة اليد مثلاً (۱) ، وهو المسمَّى بالتولدِ عند القدرية مجوس هذهِ الأُمَّةِ ، معَ ما فيهِ على مذهبهم مِنْ وجودِ أَثرِ بينَ مُؤثرينِ ووجودِ فعلٍ مِنْ غيرِ فاعل ، أو فاعل مِنْ غيرِ إرادة ولا علم بالمفعول، ونحو ذلكَ مِنَ الاستحالاتِ المذكورة في المطولات . واتَّفق الأكثرُ على عدم تولدِ الشبع والرَّيِّ ونحوهما عنِ الأكل والشرب وشبههما ، وذلك مما ينقصُ أيضاً على القائلين بالتولد ، وبالله التوفيق.
A chapter on refuting the claim of generation (tawallud). And when you know the impossibility of the effect of the originated power (al-qudrah al-ḥādithah) upon its locus... then consequently its effect through its object of power (maqdūr) upon other than its locus is also nullified; like the throwing of a stone, striking with a sword, and similar things that usually occur through the movement of the hand, for example—and this is what is called generation (tawallud) among the Qadariyya, the Magians of this community—besides what is involved in their doctrine of the existence of an effect between two agents, the existence of an act without an agent, or an agent without will and knowledge of the effect, and similar impossibilities mentioned in the lengthy works. And the majority have agreed on the non-generation of satiety, thirst-quenching, and the like from eating, drinking, and their likes; and that also counts against the proponents of generation. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (ibṭāl) of the doctrine of tawallud (generation of effects), which was held by the Mu'tazila and Qadariyya. The argument begins with a conditional: if it is established that a created power (al-qudrah al-ḥādithah) cannot produce an effect in its own locus (maḥall), then it logically follows that it cannot produce an effect outside its locus through something it has power over (maqdūr). Sanusi gives examples such as throwing a stone or striking with a sword, where the movement of the hand (the locus of power) is followed by the stone's movement or the sword's cutting, which are outside the locus. The Mu'tazila call this secondary effect 'tawallud'. Sanusi then condemns them as the 'Magians of this community' for attributing causal efficacy to creatures. He adds that their doctrine entails multiple absurdities: an effect between two agents, an act without an agent, or an agent without will and knowledge of the effect—all of which are impossible. He further notes that the majority of scholars reject generation in cases like satiety from eating, which undermines the theory. The refutation concludes by invoking divine assistance.

Key Terms
التولد
القدرة الحادثة
محل
مقدور
القدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that when a person throws a stone, the person's power directly creates the stone's motion or the sword's cutting.
Correction: Sanusi argues that created power cannot produce effects outside its locus; the apparent generation is an illusion; only Allah creates all effects.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is one of the absurdities necessitated by the doctrine of tawallud?
A) The existence of an effect between two agents
B) The impossibility of a created power affecting its locus
C) The absence of a creator for natural processes
D) The denial of human free will

Key Takeaway
Sanusi demonstrates that the doctrine of tawallud is logically impossible because it entails contradictions such as an effect between two agents, an act without an agent, or an agent without will and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a task, do your thoughts immediately turn to Allah as the creator of that success, or do you credit your own skill and effort? How does this lesson expose the subtle tawallud in your heart?

Sanusi calls those who believe in tawallud 'Magians of this community.' Are there areas in your life where you treat created means—your job, your wealth, your relationships—as if they have independent power to bring you benefit or harm? What would it take to uproot that belief and see Allah as the sole active agent?
?
You often feel full after eating and satisfied after drinking. Yet the majority of scholars deny that eating generates satiety. How can you train your soul to see Allah as the direct giver of satisfaction, not the food or drink? What practical step will you take today to live this tawheed in your eating?

Causality and Created Things

The Ash'ari View on Created Power
Sanusi defines the Ash'ari view on created power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari view that created power has no causal effect and is merely concurrent with its object, like knowledge.

Core Idea
Ash'ari: created power has no effect; it is like knowledge.

Sanusi begins by stating the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the Ash'aris) on the nature of created power. This view denies that human power has any causal role in bringing about actions—rather, it is simply a concurrent attribute with no effect. This is a foundational principle of Ash'ari occasionalism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari passage
Read the Arabic text for the Ash'ari position carefully. Notice the key phrase 'لا تأثير لها في شيء من الممكنات' (has no effect on anything among contingent things).
This is the core claim.
2
Analyze the analogy with knowledge
Sanusi compares the relation of power to its object to the relation of knowledge to its object. Knowledge does not affect its object; it simply corresponds to it. Similarly, created power does not cause its maqdūr.
This analogy emphasizes the lack of causal influence.
3
Understand the locus restriction
The passage adds that created power is only related to what occurs in its own locus (maḥall). What is outside the locus has no relation to power at all.
This further limits the scope of human power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب أهل الحقِّ على ما سبق : أنَّ القدرة الحادثة لا تأثير لها في شيءٍ مِنَ الممكناتِ ، وهي تتعلق بمقدورها تعلقاً مِنْ غيرِ تأثير ، بل نسبته كنسبة العلم الذي يتعلَّق بمعلومِهِ ولا يُؤثر فيهِ ، إلا أنَّ القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلَّق بمقدور إلا في محلها ، وما خرجَ عن محلها فلا نسبة بينَهُ وبينَ القدرة لا بتأثير ولا غيره
والمعتزلة قد سبق أن مذهبهم أنَّ العبد مخترع أفعاله ، ووافقوا أنَّ القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلَّق مباشرة إلا بالمقدور الذي في محلها ، غير أنَّهم يرون أنَّ ما في محلّها سبب يُوجَدُ بهِ ما هو خارج عن محلّها ، وزعموا أنَّ السبب والمسبَّبَ مقدورانِ للعبدِ ، إلا أنَّ أحدهما مباشرةً ، والآخر ـ وهو المسبب ـ بواسطة إيقاع السبب ، ولم يذكروا تولداً في محلّ القدرة الحادثة إلا العلم النظريَّ ؛ فإِنَّ النظر عندَهم يُولِّدُهُ في محل القدرة عليهِ
The madhhab of Ahl al-Haqq, as has preceded, is that the created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) has no effect (ta'thīr) on anything among contingent things (al-mumkināt), and it is related to its object of power (maqdūr) in a relation without effect, rather its relation is like the relation of knowledge (al-'ilm) which is related to its object of knowledge (ma'lūm) and does not affect it, except that created power is not related to an object of power except in its locus (maḥall), and what is outside its locus has no relation between it and power, neither by effect nor otherwise. And al-Mu'tazila, as has preceded, their madhhab is that the servant (al-'abd) is the inventor (mukhtari') of his actions; and they agreed that created power is not directly related except to the object of power which is in its locus, but they held that what is in its locus is a cause (sabab) by which what is outside its locus comes into existence; and they claimed that the cause and the effect (al-musabbab) are both objects of power for the servant, except that one of them is directly, and the other—that is, the effect—is by means of bringing about the cause; and they did not mention tawallud (generation) in the locus of created power except for theoretical knowledge (al-'ilm al-naẓarī); for indeed theoretical reflection (al-naẓar), in their view, generates it in the locus of power over it.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the Ash'ari position (madhhab ahl al-ḥaqq) on created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha). According to the Ash'aris, created power has no effect (lā ta'thīr lahā) on any contingent thing (mumkināt). Instead, it is related to its object of power (maqdūr) in a relationship that does not involve causing that object to exist. The author compares this to knowledge: knowledge relates to its object without affecting it. So created power is merely concurrent with the act, not causing it. The one exception: created power is only related to what occurs in its own locus (maḥall), not to things outside that locus. For example, the power in a hand is related to the hand's motion, but not to the effect of that motion on another object. The author then contrasts the Mu'tazilite view: they hold that the servant is the inventor (mukhtari') of his actions. While they agree that created power directly relates only to what is in its locus, they claim that the act in the locus is a cause (sabab) that brings about an effect (musabbab) outside the locus. Both the cause and the effect are considered under the servant's power: the cause directly, the effect indirectly by means of performing the cause. The author adds a nuance: the Mu'tazila only claim tawallud (generation) in the case of theoretical knowledge (al-ʿilm al-naẓarī). For them, theoretical reflection (al-naẓar) generates knowledge within the same locus (the mind), not outside it. This is their sole exception to the principle that generated effects occur outside the locus. Thus the passage lays out the key point of dispute: for Ash'aris, only God creates all actions; for Mu'tazila, humans create their own acts via causality.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة (created power)
مذهب أهل الحق (madhhab Ahl al-Haqq)
محل القدرة (locus of power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that created power has causal effect like God's power.
Correction: The Ash'ari position clearly denies any effect, likening it to knowledge, which does not affect its object.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position as stated in the passage, what is the relationship of created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) to its object of power (maqdūr)?
A) It causes the object to exist directly.
B) It has no effect; it is related like knowledge to its object.
C) It is a necessary cause but requires divine permission.
D) It generates the object outside its locus.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view denies any causal role for created power, limiting it to a non-effective relation within its own locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your actions are created by Allah alone, or do you attribute some efficacy to your own ability? How would this belief change how you rely on Allah?
?
If created power has no effect, then what is the purpose of your efforts? How does this understanding affect your sense of responsibility and your humility before Allah?
?
Are you comfortable with the idea that your choices are not causally effective? What does this teach you about trust in Allah's sole power and the need to constantly seek His help?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
5 min
The Mu'tazilite View on Created Power and Causality
Sanusi describes the Mu'tazilite theory of causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite view that humans create their actions and that the act in the locus is a cause for effects outside the locus, with the exception of theoretical knowledge.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila: humans invent actions; cause and effect are under human power.

After presenting the Ash'ari position, Sanusi turns to the Mu'tazilite view. The Mu'tazila agree that power directly acts only in its locus, but they argue that the act in the locus is a cause that produces effects outside. They also maintain that only theoretical knowledge is generated (tawallud) within the same locus.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite passage
Read the Arabic text for the Mu'tazilite view. Notice the key phrases 'العبد مخترع أفعاله' (the servant is the inventor of his actions) and 'السبب والمسبب مقدوران للعبد' (cause and effect are both objects of power for the servant).
This establishes human agency.
2
Identify the cause-effect structure
The Mu'tazila hold that the act in the locus is a cause (sabab) for something outside the locus (musabbab). Both are under human power: the cause directly, the effect indirectly by bringing about the cause.
This is the core of their causal theory.
3
Examine the exception for theoretical knowledge
The passage notes that the Mu'tazila only acknowledge tawallud (generation) in the case of theoretical knowledge: reflection (naẓar) generates knowledge in the same locus (the mind). This is their sole exception to the principle that generated effects occur outside the locus.
This nuance distinguishes their view on knowledge from other effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب أهل الحقِّ على ما سبق : أنَّ القدرة الحادثة لا تأثير لها في شيءٍ مِنَ الممكناتِ ، وهي تتعلق بمقدورها تعلقاً مِنْ غيرِ تأثير ، بل نسبته كنسبة العلم الذي يتعلَّق بمعلومِهِ ولا يُؤثر فيهِ ، إلا أنَّ القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلَّق بمقدور إلا في محلها ، وما خرجَ عن محلها فلا نسبة بينَهُ وبينَ القدرة لا بتأثير ولا غيره
والمعتزلة قد سبق أن مذهبهم أنَّ العبد مخترع أفعاله ، ووافقوا أنَّ القدرة الحادثة لا تتعلَّق مباشرة إلا بالمقدور الذي في محلها ، غير أنَّهم يرون أنَّ ما في محلّها سبب يُوجَدُ بهِ ما هو خارج عن محلّها ، وزعموا أنَّ السبب والمسبَّبَ مقدورانِ للعبدِ ، إلا أنَّ أحدهما مباشرةً ، والآخر ـ وهو المسبب ـ بواسطة إيقاع السبب ، ولم يذكروا تولداً في محلّ القدرة الحادثة إلا العلم النظريَّ ؛ فإِنَّ النظر عندَهم يُولِّدُهُ في محل القدرة عليهِ
The madhhab of Ahl al-Haqq, as has preceded, is that the created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) has no effect (ta'thīr) on anything among contingent things (al-mumkināt), and it is related to its object of power (maqdūr) in a relation without effect, rather its relation is like the relation of knowledge (al-'ilm) which is related to its object of knowledge (ma'lūm) and does not affect it, except that created power is not related to an object of power except in its locus (maḥall), and what is outside its locus has no relation between it and power, neither by effect nor otherwise. And al-Mu'tazila, as has preceded, their madhhab is that the servant (al-'abd) is the inventor (mukhtari') of his actions; and they agreed that created power is not directly related except to the object of power which is in its locus, but they held that what is in its locus is a cause (sabab) by which what is outside its locus comes into existence; and they claimed that the cause and the effect (al-musabbab) are both objects of power for the servant, except that one of them is directly, and the other—that is, the effect—is by means of bringing about the cause; and they did not mention tawallud (generation) in the locus of created power except for theoretical knowledge (al-'ilm al-naẓarī); for indeed theoretical reflection (al-naẓar), in their view, generates it in the locus of power over it.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the Ash'ari position (madhhab ahl al-ḥaqq) on created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha). According to the Ash'aris, created power has no effect (lā ta'thīr lahā) on any contingent thing (mumkināt). Instead, it is related to its object of power (maqdūr) in a relationship that does not involve causing that object to exist. The author compares this to knowledge: knowledge relates to its object without affecting it. So created power is merely concurrent with the act, not causing it. The one exception: created power is only related to what occurs in its own locus (maḥall), not to things outside that locus. For example, the power in a hand is related to the hand's motion, but not to the effect of that motion on another object. The author then contrasts the Mu'tazilite view: they hold that the servant is the inventor (mukhtari') of his actions. While they agree that created power directly relates only to what is in its locus, they claim that the act in the locus is a cause (sabab) that brings about an effect (musabbab) outside the locus. Both the cause and the effect are considered under the servant's power: the cause directly, the effect indirectly by means of performing the cause. The author adds a nuance: the Mu'tazila only claim tawallud (generation) in the case of theoretical knowledge (al-ʿilm al-naẓarī). For them, theoretical reflection (al-naẓar) generates knowledge within the same locus (the mind), not outside it. This is their sole exception to the principle that generated effects occur outside the locus. Thus the passage lays out the key point of dispute: for Ash'aris, only God creates all actions; for Mu'tazila, humans create their own acts via causality.

Key Terms
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila)
السبب (cause)
المسبب (effect)
التولُّد (tawallud / generation)
العلم النظري (theoretical knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Mu'tazilites believe power directly affects things outside the locus.
Correction: They agree that direct power is limited to the locus, but they introduce causality: the act in the locus is a cause for effects outside.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite view in the passage, what is the relationship between the act in the locus and the effect outside the locus?
A) The act directly causes the effect by a necessary connection.
B) The act is a cause (sabab) that brings about the effect.
C) The act and effect are unrelated; God creates both.
D) The effect is generated (tawallud) within the locus.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite view holds that humans create their actions, and the act within the locus serves as a cause for effects outside, except for theoretical knowledge which is generated in the same locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish something through your own effort, do you truly believe it is solely your doing, or do you sense that Allah's power is behind it? What evidence from your life makes you think one way or the other? This lesson shows the Mu'tazilite view; how does it challenge you to re-evaluate your own assumption?
?
The Mu'tazilites said that only theoretical knowledge is generated from reflection. Think about the last time you studied and 'produced' an understanding. Did you feel that knowledge was your own creation? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that all true knowledge comes from Allah, so that your learning becomes an act of worship?
?
If you were to adopt the Mu'tazilite view for a day, how would your reliance on your own plans and causality change? Would you feel more in control or more anxious? What does that reveal about your heart's attachment to your own power versus trust in Allah's decree?

Definition and Refutation of Tawallud
Sanusi defines, traces origin, and refutes tawallud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite doctrine of tawallud and why Sanusi refutes it based on the impossibility of a single effect having two agents.

Core Idea
A single effect cannot come from two distinct agents.

Sanusi begins by defining tawallud, the Mu'tazilite theory of generated causation. He traces its roots to the philosophers' concept of natural causes and then refutes it using a logical argument: one effect cannot have two causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of tawallud
Read the Arabic definition carefully: 'إيجاد حادث بواسطة مقدور بالقدرة الحادثة'. Notice what constitutes a generating cause and its effect.
The definition involves a created power producing an effect through a cause.
2
Understand the philosophical origin
The passage explains that the Mu'tazila took this idea from the philosophers' natural causes, which can be impeded, unlike rational causes. See how the Mu'tazila adapted it and changed terminology.
3
Grasp the refutation argument
Sanusi argues that a single effect cannot be produced by two agents: the generating cause and the servant's power. This shows the doctrine is logically incoherent.
This is the key argument: one effect, one agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فحقيقة التولُّدِ عندَهم : إيجاد حادث بواسطة مقدور بالقدرة الحادثة.
وهذا المذهبُ إنَّما أخذوهُ مِنْ مذهب الفلاسفة في الأسبابِ الطبيعية ، فإنَّهم زعموا أنَّ الطبيعةَ تُؤثر في مفعولها ما لم يمنعها مانع ، وليسَتْ عندَهم كالعلل العقليَّةِ الموجبة الأحكام لذواتها ؛ إذْ لا يجوز أن يمنعها مانع ، فأخذ المعتزلة ذلك ولقبوه تولداً ، ولم يجعلوا حكم السبب المولدِ بمثابة العلَّة العقلية ؛ لجواز أنْ يمتنع التولُّدُ لمانع ، ثم غيَّروا العبارة كي لا يظهر مأخذهم ؛ فقالوا : هو فعلُ فاعل السبب ! وهذا إذا حُقَّقَ لم يكن له حاصل ؛ لأنَّ الأثر الواحد يمتنعُ أنْ يكونَ ثابتاً لمؤثرين ، فمِنْ ضرورة تأثير السبب فيهِ امتناع تأثيرِ القدرةِ فيهِ وقول القائل : ( هو يؤثر فيه بواسطة السبب ( يؤول حاصلُ القولِ بهِ إلى أنَّهُ فعل سببه كما أنَّ البارئ عندَهم فعل العبد وهو مخترع لفعله ، ولم يكن فعله فعلاً للهِ عزَّ وجلَّ ، إلا أنَّهم يمنعونَ إضافتهُ اللَّهِ
The reality of tawallud (generation) according to them is: bringing into existence an originated thing by means of an object of power through created power. And this doctrine, they only took it from the doctrine of the philosophers (al-falāsifa) concerning natural causes (al-asbāb al-ṭabī'iyya); for they claimed that nature (al-ṭabī'a) affects its effect (maf'ūl) as long as no impediment (māni') prevents it, and it is not with them like rational causes (al-'ilal al-'aqliyya) that necessitate the judgments (al-aḥkām) by their essences (dhawātihā), since it is not permissible for an impediment to prevent them. So al-Mu'tazila took that and named it tawallud, and they did not make the ruling of the generating cause (al-sabab al-muwallid) like the rational cause, due to the possibility that generation may be prevented by an impediment. Then they changed the expression so that their source would not appear; so they said: it is the act of the agent of the cause! And this, when investigated, has no result; because one effect (al-athar al-wāḥid) cannot be established for two agents (mu'aththirayn); therefore, from the necessity of the cause's effect in it is the impossibility of power's effect in it. And the saying of the one who says: 'It affects it by means of the cause' amounts in the conclusion to saying that it is the act of its cause, just as the Creator, according to them, is the act of the servant while the servant is the inventor of his act, and his act was not an act of God Almighty, but they forbid its attribution to God.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage defines and critiques the Mu'tazilite concept of tawallud (generation). It begins with the definition: 'The reality of tawallud according to them is: bringing into existence an originated thing by means of an object of power through created power.' In other words, a human performs an act (the cause) and as a result, another event (the effect) is generated—e.g., motion of the hand generates motion of a key. The author then traces the origin: the Mu'tazila took this idea from the philosophers (falāsifa) who believed in natural causes. The philosophers held that nature (ṭabī'a) produces its effect unless an impediment (māniʿ) prevents it—unlike rational causes which necessarily produce their effects and cannot be impeded. The Mu'tazila adopted this model, calling it tawallud, and allowed that the generation might be blocked by an impediment. To hide their borrowing, they changed terminology, saying: 'It is the act of the agent of the cause'—meaning the servant is the agent of the cause, and the effect is the act of that agent by means of the cause. The author refutes this: when examined, the claim has no substance. The key argument: a single effect (al-athar al-wāḥid) cannot be produced by two distinct agents. If the cause (e.g., the hand's motion) truly produces the effect, then the servant's power (qudra) cannot also produce it. If the effect is attributed to the servant's power, it cannot also be attributed to the cause. So the Mu'tazilite position amounts to saying the effect is the act of its cause, not of the servant's power directly. Moreover, they claim the servant is the inventor of his act, yet they deny that God creates the act—but they also forbid attributing the act to God. The passage thus concludes that the doctrine is incoherent: an effect must have a single agent, and the Mu'tazila cannot consistently maintain both that the servant invents his act and that the act is generated by a cause.

Key Terms
التولُّد (tawallud / generation)
السبب المولد (generating cause)
الفلاسفة (philosophers)
الأسباب الطبيعية (natural causes)
العلل العقلية (rational causes)
الأثر الواحد (single effect)
المؤثران (two agents)
فاعل السبب (agent of the cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawallud is a valid causal chain where a cause produces an effect through God's power.
Correction: Sanusi shows it is impossible because one effect cannot have two agents: the cause and the human's power cannot both be effective.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation in the passage, why is the Mu'tazilite doctrine of tawallud invalid?
A) Because it contradicts the Qur'an and Sunnah.
B) Because a single effect cannot come from two different agents.
C) Because the philosophers themselves rejected it.
D) Because the Mu'tazila could not define it clearly.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes tawallud by demonstrating that a single effect cannot be produced by two distinct agents, exposing the Mu'tazilite doctrine as logically incoherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something difficult, do you secretly attribute the success to your own skill or effort, forgetting that God alone creates every outcome? How can this lesson on tawallud change your internal narrative? Will you pause today and consciously attribute every good to Allah, even if it came 'through' your actions?
?
Think of a sin you committed and regretted. Did you consider yourself the sole agent of that sin, or did you remember that God creates all actions, including your wrong choices? Does the impossibility of two agents for a single effect undermine your tendency to claim ownership of your good deeds while distancing yourself from sinful acts? What would it look like to sincerely repent, acknowledging both your responsibility and Allah's complete control?
?
The Mu'tazila changed their wording to conceal borrowing from philosophers. Are there areas in your faith where you rationalize or adjust expressions to fit modern sensibilities rather than accept divine truth as taught? Will you examine your speech and beliefs this week, removing any borrowed rationalizations that contradict the oneness of Allah's creative power?

Definition and Critique of Mu'tazili Tawallud
Sanusi defines tawallud and refutes its philosophical origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili doctrine of tawallud, its philosophical origin, and Sanusi's refutation based on the impossibility of a single effect having two agents.

Core Idea
Tawallud is a borrowed philosophical theory that contradicts Mu'tazili own principles.

Sanusi begins by defining the Mu'tazili concept of tawallud (generation), then traces its roots to Greek natural philosophy. He then exposes the internal inconsistency: the theory claims an effect is produced by a cause and also by the agent's power, which is impossible. Finally, he shows that tawallud forces the Mu'tazila to attribute evil acts to Allah, which they sought to avoid.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of tawallud
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Locate the technical definition of tawallud as given by the Mu'tazila: bringing about an event through an object of power using a created power.
Note the key terms: تولد (tawallud), سبب (sabab), قدرة (qudra).
2
Identify the philosophical origin
Sanusi asserts that this doctrine was borrowed from Greek philosophers' theory of natural causes. Notice how the Mu'tazila modified the terminology to hide their borrowing.
The philosophers believed nature acts unless prevented; the Mu'tazila applied this to human actions.
3
Analyze Sanusi's critique
Sanusi presents two main criticisms: (1) a single effect cannot have two agents (cause and power), and (2) tawallud leads to attributing generated acts, including evil, to Allah, contradicting the Mu'tazili goal of avoiding such attribution.
Reflect on how this critique uses logical and theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد التولد
فحقيقة التولد عندهم: إيجاد حادث بواسطة مقدور بالقدرة الحادثة.
وهذا المذهب إنما أخذوه من مذهب الفلاسفة في الأسباب الطبيعية، فإنهم زعموا أن الطبيعة تؤثر في مفعولها ما لم يمنعها مانع، وليست عندهم كالعلل العقلية الموجبة الأحكام لذواتها؛ إذ لا يجوز أن يمنعها مانع، فأخذ المعتزلة ذلك ولقبوه تولدا، ولم يجعلوا حكم السبب المولد بمثابة العلة العقلية؛ لجواز أن يمتنع التولد لمانع، ثم غيروا العبارة كي لا يظهر مأخذهم؛ فقالوا: هو فعل فاعل السبب! وهذا إذا حقق لم يكن له حاصل؛ لأن الأثر الواحد يمتنع أن يكون ثابتا لمؤثرين، فمن ضرورة تأثير السبب فيه امتناع تأثير القدرة فيه. وقول القائل: (هو يؤثر فيه بواسطة السبب) يؤول حاصل القول به إلى أنه فعل سببه كما أن البارئ عندهم فعل العبد وهو مخترع لفعله، ولم يكن فعله فعلا لله عز وجل، إلا أنهم يمنعون إضافته إلى الله تعالى لرومهم في أصلهم قطع نسبة القبائح إليه، ومذهبهم في التولد يلزمهم ما فروا منه من نسبة فعلها إليه، وكون المتولد فعل فاعل السبب .. قد نقل إمام الحرمين في الشامل اتفاق المعتزلة عليه.
Definition of tawallud (generation). The reality of tawallud according to them is: bringing into existence an event by means of an object of power (maqdūr) through a created power. And this doctrine they took from the doctrine of the philosophers regarding natural causes, for they claimed that nature influences its effect as long as no obstacle prevents it, and it is not in their view like rational causes which necessitate judgments by their essences, because it is not possible for an obstacle to prevent them. So the Muʿtazila took that and called it tawallud, and they did not make the ruling of the generative cause like that of the rational cause, because it is possible that generation is prevented by an obstacle. Then they changed the wording so that their source would not appear; they said: it is the act of the doer of the cause! But when this is examined, it has no substance, because a single effect cannot be established for two agents. From the necessity of the cause's influence on it comes the impossibility of the influence of the power on it. And the saying of the speaker: 'It influences it by means of the cause' amounts in the summary of the statement to the fact that it is the act of its cause, just as the Creator according to them is the act of the servant while the servant is the inventor of his act, and his act was not an act of Allah Almighty. However, they prevent its attribution to Allah the Exalted because of their seeking in their principle to sever the attribution of evil deeds to Him. And their doctrine of tawallud necessitates for them what they fled from, namely the attribution of its act to Him. And that the generated act is the act of the doer of the cause... the Imam of the Two Sanctuaries (al-Juwayni) has transmitted in al-Shāmil the agreement of the Muʿtazila upon it.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation of the Mu'tazili doctrine of tawallud (generation). The author (Sanusi) first gives the technical definition: tawallud is causing an event indirectly through an object of power (maqdūr) using a created power. He then traces the origin of this doctrine to the Greek philosophical theory of natural causes, where nature produces effects unless impeded. The Mu'tazila adopted this and called it tawallud, but they did not treat the generative cause as necessary, allowing for prevention. Sanusi points out that they changed terminology to hide the philosophical borrowing, claiming the effect is 'the act of the doer of the cause'. He then critiques this: a single effect cannot have two agents (the cause and the power). If the cause influences it, then the servant's power cannot influence it. Moreover, saying 'influences by means of the cause' reduces to saying it is the act of the cause itself, just as the Mu'tazila hold that the Creator is the act of the servant (i.e., the servant creates his own acts), thus denying divine involvement. But they deny attribution to Allah to avoid assigning evil to Him, yet their tawallud theory forces them to attribute the generated evil acts to Him anyway. Finally, Sanusi cites Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni's report that the Mu'tazila are agreed on this doctrine.

Key Terms
تولد (tawallud)
سبب (sabab)
قدرة (qudra)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)
فاعل السبب (fa'il al-sabab)
علة عقلية ('illa 'aqliyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawallud is a neutral causal theory compatible with Islamic theology.
Correction: Sanusi shows it is borrowed from Greek philosophy and leads to theological contradictions, such as attributing evil acts to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, from where did the Mu'tazila borrow the concept of tawallud?
A) From the Ash'ari theory of acquisition (kasb)
B) From Greek philosophers' idea of natural causes
C) From the Qur'anic verses on creation
D) From the Hanbali emphasis on divine attributes

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes tawallud by demonstrating that it is philosophically borrowed and logically incoherent, leading to the very attribution of evil to Allah that the Mu'tazila sought to avoid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil or suffering in the world, do you find yourself instinctively looking for secondary causes to protect God in your mind? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of that instinct, even if it seems pious?
?
Sanusi shows that the Mu'tazila borrowed an idea from Greek philosophy and dressed it in Islamic terms. Are there ideas you hold about God's actions — how He 'causes' good and evil — that you might have absorbed from your culture or intellect without realizing they clash with core Islamic teachings?
?
If a single effect cannot have two agents, then every event in your life — both the good and the difficult — must be directly from Allah alone. Does your heart truly accept that, or do you sometimes hide behind secondary causes to avoid feeling His direct presence in hardship?

Internal Mu'tazili Disagreements on Tawallud
Sanusi reports two Mu'tazili positions on generated acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Mu'tazila did not have a unified position on generated acts (tawallud), as al-Nazzam rejected it outright and even proponents like Hafs al-Fard limited its scope.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila were not unified on generated effects (tawallud).

You may think that the Mu'tazila all agreed on generated acts, but this passage shows internal disagreement. Sanusi reports two divergent views to challenge that assumption. Al-Nazzam denied tawallud entirely, while Hafs al-Fard accepted it only under certain conditions. This fragment will deepen your understanding of the complexity within Mu'tazili thought on causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note the phrase 'قال المقترح' indicating the proposer is speaking.
2
Distinguish two views
Identify al-Nazzam's position and Hafs al-Fard's position. Notice how they differ on the agent of generated events.
Al-Nazzam attributes generated events to God via natures; Hafs al-Fard attributes some to the cause-maker based on choice.
3
Recognize the additional disagreement
The passage ends with a note that the Mu'tazila disagreed on the timing of power attachment to the generated effect. This shows further fragmentation.
This is a separate point of internal dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المقترح: (ولا يصح؛ فقد ذهب النظام منهم إلى أن المتولدات مضافة إلى البارئ سبحانه لا على معنى أنه فعلها، ولكن بمعنى أنه خلق الأجسام على طبائع وخصائص تقتضي حدوث الحوادث الناشئة عنها، ولم يقل: إنها فعل لفاعل سببها. وذهب حفص القرد: إلى أن ما يقع مباينا لمحل القدرة على قدر اختيار المسبب.. فهو فعل فاعل السبب؛ كالقطع والفصد والذبح، وما لا يقف على قدر اختيار المسبب؛ كالهوي عند الاندفاع ونحوه.. فليس من فعله. واختلفوا في وقت تعلق القدرة بالمتولد.
The proposer said: 'And it is not valid; for al-Nazzam among them went to the view that the generated things are attributed to the Creator, Exalted is He, not in the sense that He did them, but in the sense that He created bodies with natures and properties that necessitate the occurrence of the events arising from them, and he did not say that they are the act of the doer of the cause.' And Hafs al-Qird went to the view that what occurs separate from the locus of power, upon the measure of the choice of the cause-maker, is the act of the doer of the cause, like cutting, phlebotomy, and slaughter; and what does not stop upon the measure of the choice of the cause-maker, like falling when falling down and the like, then it is not from his act. And they disagreed regarding the time of the attachment of the power to the generated effect.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage reports two Mu'tazili positions on tawallud, presumably as an objection to Sanusi's previous claim of agreement. The first figure, al-Nazzam, completely rejected tawallud: he attributed generated events to God, not as His direct acts, but as consequences of the natures He placed in bodies. Thus the doer of the cause is not the agent of the generated effect. The second figure, Hafs al-Fard (here spelled al-Qird), partially affirmed tawallud: if the effect occurs in a separate locus and depends on the choice of the cause-maker (like cutting or slaughter), it is the act of the cause-maker. But if it does not depend on choice (like falling when pushed), it is not his act. The passage concludes by noting that the Mu'tazila also disagreed on when the created power attaches to the generated effect, a point of further fragmentation.

Key Terms
نظام (al-Nazzam)
حفص القرد (Hafs al-Qird/Fard)
متولد (mutawallad)
فاعل السبب (fa'il al-sabab)
اختيار (ikhtiyār)
محل القدرة (maḥall al-qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila agreed on the theory of generated acts (tawallud).
Correction: This passage shows that al-Nazzam rejected tawallud entirely, and even Hafs al-Fard accepted it only conditionally. The Mu'tazila also disagreed on when the power attaches to the generated effect.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was al-Nazzam's view on generated events (mutawalladāt)?
A) They are the act of the doer of the cause
B) They are directly created by God as new acts
C) They are attributed to God as consequences of the natures He created
D) They are not real events

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila were internally divided on the theory of generated acts, with al-Nazzam denying it and Hafs al-Fard accepting it only under specific conditions, and they further disagreed on the timing of power attachment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you act, do you ever assume you fully own the outcome? This lesson shows that even the Mu'tazila debated whether effects are truly your act. Reflect on a recent action where you felt proud of the result. How much of that result was truly within your choice, and how much was beyond your control? Commit to a specific humble acknowledgment of God's sovereignty in your actions today.
?
Al-Nazzam attributed generated events to God's creation of natures, not to the human agent. Does this challenge your sense of responsibility for unintended consequences of your actions? Think of a time when you unintentionally harmed someone. Did you consider that harm your sin? What does this lesson teach you about accountability for what you did not directly choose? Decide now on a concrete way to seek forgiveness for such unintended harms.

The Mu'tazilite Debate on Power and Generated Acts
Sanusi presents two Mu'tazilite positions on when power relates to a generated act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazilite positions on when the power (qudra) ceases to be effective over a generated act (mutawallad).

Core Idea
Power over a generated act ends either at the cause or at the act itself.

The Mu'tazilites debated whether a generated act remains under the agent's power until its cause occurs, or until the act itself exists. This lesson presents their two contrasting views and explains the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the two distinct opinions presented.
The passage uses concise technical language.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the first group's view: power remains until the cause occurs. Then the second group's view: power continues until the generated act itself exists.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the timing matters for the theory of tawallud (causal generation). How does each position affect the agent's responsibility?
This helps connect to broader theological debates on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في وقتِ تعلقِ القدرة بالمتولدِ فقال قوم منهم : لا يزال مقدوراً إلى حين وقوع سببه ، فيجب ثبوته فينقطع أثرُ القدرة فيه . وقال آخرونَ : إنَّما ينقطع كونه مقدوراً إذا وقع المتولد ووُجِدَ ، لا عند وقوع سببه فقط
And they differed regarding the time of attachment of power to the generated act. A group of them said: It remains within power until the occurrence of its cause, so its subsistence becomes necessary and the effect of power in it ceases. And others said: Its being within power only ceases when the generated act occurs and exists, not merely at the occurrence of its cause.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage presents a disagreement among Mu'tazilites about the temporal relationship between power (qudra) and generated acts (mutawalladat). The first opinion holds that the generated act remains within the agent's power only until the cause (sabab) occurs; once the cause happens, the generated act becomes necessary and the power over it ceases. The second opinion holds that the power continues until the generated act actually occurs; it does not cease at the mere occurrence of the cause. This is a technical discussion about the nature of causal efficacy in the theory of tawallud.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra)
المتولد (al-mutawallad)
السبب (al-sabab)
تعلق (ta'alluq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate is about whether power exists at all for generated acts.
Correction: The debate is about the specific moment when power ceases to be effective, not about its existence.

Checkpoint
According to the first group mentioned, when does the power over a generated act cease?
A) At the moment the generated act is intended.
B) At the occurrence of the cause.
C) At the actual existence of the generated act.
D) Power never ceases.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites disagreed on whether power over a generated act ends at the occurrence of its cause or at the actual existence of the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that once you set a cause in motion the outcome is already determined, do you still feel fully responsible for the result? How does that shape your reliance on Allah and your motivation to act?
?
Consider a recent situation where you tried to produce a good result. At what moment did you feel your ability to influence it ended—after the first step, or only when the result actually happened? Which of the two Mu'tazilite opinions does your lived experience lean toward, and why?
?
Are you more inclined to think your power over outcomes ends when the cause is set (like Opinion 1) or that your influence continues until the result occurs (like Opinion 2)? How does that belief affect your du'a, your patience, and your sense of hope or helplessness?

Mu'tazilite Dispute on Colors and Tastes as Generated Acts
Sanusi discusses Mu'tazilite views on generated acts and their consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite disagreement over whether colors and tastes can be generated acts and the theological consequences of Thumama's denial of an agent.

Core Idea
Denying agency in generated acts undermines the proof of a Creator.

This passage presents a dispute within Mu'tazilite thought: can colors and tastes be generated acts? Two prominent figures, Thumama ibn Ashras and Mu'ammar, offered conflicting views. The author highlights the dangerous implication of Thumama's position—that it invalidates the classical proof for God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without focusing on translation. Notice the structure: it first states the dispute, then gives Thumama's view, then Mu'ammar's.
The passage is short and dense; let the words sink in.
2
Understand Thumama's position and its consequence
Thumama claims generated things have no agent. The author says this necessarily invalidates the proof for a Creator. Why would that be? Think about how the proof relies on every event needing an agent.
This is a key logical link.
3
Examine Mu'ammar's view
Mu'ammar holds that all accidents except will arise from the natures of bodies. This reduces God's role to only creating will, not other accidents.
Both positions are considered problematic from an Ash'ari perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفوا في الألوان والطعوم : هل يجوزُ أن تقعَ مُتولّدةً أم لا ؟ وذهب ثمامةُ بنُ أشرسَ : إلى أنَّ هذه المتولَّداتِ لا فاعل لها . ويلزمه بطلان الدليل على إثباتِ الصانع وذهب معمرُ : إلى أنَّ جميع الأعراض واقعةٌ بطباع الأجسام إلا الإرادة
And they differed regarding colors and tastes: whether they can occur as generated or not? And Thumama ibn Ashras held that these generated things have no agent. And [this] necessitates the invalidity of the proof for establishing the Maker. And Mu'ammar held that all accidents occur by the natures of bodies except will.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage presents another Mu'tazilite disagreement: whether colors and tastes can be generated acts. Thumama ibn Ashras claimed that such generated things have no agent at all – a denial of any efficient cause. The author points out that this entails the invalidity of the proof for establishing the existence of a Creator (since the proof often relies on the need for an agent for contingent events). Then Mu'ammar is cited as holding that all accidents except will arise from the natures of bodies, which is a materialist position. These views are considered problematic from an Ash'ari perspective.

Key Terms
الألوان
الطعوم
المتولدات
فاعل
معمر
ثمامة بن أشرس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage endorses Thumama's or Mu'ammar's views.
Correction: The author presents these views as problematic and points out their theological consequences, such as invalidating the proof of a Creator.

Checkpoint
What consequence does the author indicate follows from Thumama's view that generated things have no agent?
A) It strengthens the proof of a Creator.
B) It invalidates the proof of a Creator.
C) It is neutral regarding the Creator.
D) It leads to a different proof.

Key Takeaway
Thumama's denial of an agent for generated acts leads to invalidation of the proof for a Creator, while Mu'ammar's naturalism reduces divine agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a color or taste, do you automatically think of natural causes and forget that Allah is directly creating that experience? How can you train yourself to see His hand in every moment?
?
In what areas of your life do you act as if things happen 'by themselves' without a divine agent? How would your trust in Allah deepen if you truly believed He is the direct agent of every event, from a fruit's taste to your heartbeat?
?
Thumama's view undermined the proof for a Creator. What subtle assumptions do you hold that might weaken your certainty in Allah's existence or attributes? Are you willing to examine them and seek correction?
