The Four Generated Things in Mu'tazilite Kalam
Sanusi lists the four mūliddāt and defines two by their effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of generated things (mūliddāt) as defined by the Mu'tazilites and their role in causal theory.

Core Idea
Generated things are secondary effects caused by human actions, not direct creations.

The Mu'tazilites developed a theory of causality that distinguishes between direct acts and their generated effects. This passage enumerates four types of such generated things: pressure, contiguity, looking, and impact. Let's explore each one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to identify the four generated things.
Notice that two of them are defined by their effects: nazar generates knowledge, wahy generates pain.
2
Understand each category
Go through each of the four: i'timad, mujawara, nazar, and wahy. Consider what each might mean in the context of causal theory.
I'timad refers to pressure or leaning, mujawara to contiguity with conditions, nazar to looking, wahy to impact.
3
Reflect on the broader significance
Think about why these four are considered 'generated' rather than directly created by God. This relates to the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud.
The conditions for mujawara are not explained here, but they are important in the theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمولدات عندهم أربعة (۱) : الاعتماد ، والمجاورة على شرائط معتبرة عندهم ، والنظرُ المولد للعلم ، والوَهْيُّ المولد للألم . وقد اختلف أبو هاشم والجبائي : في أنَّ المولد الاعتماد أو الحركة؟ فذهب الجبائي إلى الثاني ، وذهبَ ابنُه أبو هاشم إلى الأَوَّلِ والاعتمادات عندَهم راجعةٌ إلى شدَّةِ العضلاتِ ، وقوَّةِ ارتباط الأعصاب على الأعضاءِ ، وكلُّ ذلكَ مِنْ مذاهب الطبائعيينَ الضَّالِّينَ المضلين
And the generated things according to them are four (1): i'timad (pressure/leaning), mujawara (contiguity) upon conditions considered by them, nazar (looking) which generates knowledge, and wa-hy (weakness?) which generates pain. And Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i disagreed: whether the generator is i'timad or movement? Al-Jubba'i held the second, and his son Abu Hashim held the first. And i'timadāt (pressures) according to them are reducible to the intensity of muscles and the strength of the connection of nerves upon limbs, and all that is from the doctrines of the materialists, the misguided misguiders.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four types of generated things (mūliddāt) according to those Mu'tazilites who accept tawallud: (1) i'timād (often translated as 'pressure' or 'leaning'), (2) mujāwara (contiguity) with certain conditions, (3) naẓar (looking) which generates knowledge, and (4) wahy (weakness or impact) which generates pain. Then it notes a disagreement between Abu Hashim and his father al-Jubba'i: whether the generator is i'timād or movement; al-Jubba'i says movement, Abu Hashim says i'timād. The author then critiques the materialist underpinnings of the concept of i'timād, explaining that the Mu'tazilites reduce it to physical muscle tension and nerve connections, which he denounces as the doctrines of misguided materialists (ṭabā'i‘iyyīn).

Key Terms
المولدات (al-mūliddāt)
الاعتماد (al-i'timād)
المجاورة (al-mujāwara)
النظر (al-naẓar)
الوهي (al-wahy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) I'timad
B) Mujawara
C) Nazar
D) Wahy

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites identified four types of generated events: pressure, contiguity, looking, and impact.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states that looking (النظر) generates knowledge. What are you currently looking at that is generating knowledge of good deeds, and what are you looking at that generates knowledge of sin? Will you commit today to turn your gaze away from what does not benefit your faith?
?
Consider that impact (الوهي) generates pain. How do you respond to physical pain in your life? Do you see it as a generated effect with a cause, or as a direct test from Allah? How might that perspective change your patience and gratitude?

The Dispute over I'timad and the Materialist Critique
Sanusi reports a dispute and critiques the materialist account of i'timad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the intra-Mu'tazilite dispute about whether i'timad or movement is the generator of generated things, and why the author criticizes the materialist basis of i'timad.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite concept of i'timad is rooted in materialist philosophy, which the author denounces.

After enumerating the generated things, Sanusi highlights a disagreement between two leading Mu'tazilites: Abu Hashim and his father al-Jubba'i. The issue is whether the generator of other generated things is i'timad or movement. He then exposes the materialist foundations of their theory of i'timad, condemning it as misguided.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the disagreement
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the specific question: is the generator i'timad or movement?
Al-Jubba'i held movement, Abu Hashim held i'timad.
2
Understand the materialist reduction
The author explains that the Mu'tazilites reduce i'timad to physical muscle tension and nerve connections. Consider why this is problematic from an Islamic theological perspective.
This reduction implies a purely naturalistic explanation.
3
Reflect on the theological critique
The author denounces this as the doctrine of the 'materialists' (ṭabā'i‘iyyīn), calling them misguided and misguiding. Think about how this fits into the broader Ash'ari critique of Mu'tazilite causality.
This shows the author's rejection of any explanation that excludes divine agency.

Timing of a Generated Act Leaving Divine Power
Mu'tazili dispute on timing of generated act leaving divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazili views on when a generated act ceases to be within divine power.

Core Idea
When does an act leave God's power? Mu'tazila disagreed.

The Mu'tazila debated whether a generated act remains within divine power until its cause occurs or only when the act itself comes into existence. This distinction affects how we understand God's ongoing creative power. Let's examine their arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation.
The passage is short but packs two distinct positions.
2
Identify the two positions
Note the first group: the act leaves power when its cause occurs. The second group: only when the act itself occurs.
The difference is about the moment of leaving divine power.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this timing matters. Does it imply a limitation on divine power?
The Mu'tazila are trying to preserve both God's power and secondary causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال قوم من منهم : لا يزال مقدوراً إلى حين وقوع سببه ، فيجب ثبوته فينقطع أثرُ القدرة فيه . وقال آخرونَ : إنَّما ينقطع كونه مقدوراً إذا وقع المتولد ووُجِدَ ، لا عند وقوع سببه فقط . واختلفوا في الألوان والطعوم : هل يجوزُ أن تقعَ مُتولّدةً أم لا ؟ وذهب ثمامةُ بنُ أشرسَ : إلى أنَّ هذه المتولَّداتِ لا فاعل لها . ويلزمه بطلان الدليل على إثباتِ الصانع وذهب معمرُ : إلى أنَّ جميع الأعراض واقعةٌ بطباع الأجسام إلا الإرادة .
Some people said: 'It (a generated act) remains within divine power until the time of its cause's occurrence, so its existence becomes necessary, thus the effect of power over it ceases.' Others said: 'It only ceases to be within power when the generated act actually occurs, not merely when its cause occurs.' They also differed concerning colors and tastes: is it permissible for them to occur as generated acts or not? Thumama ibn Ashras held that these generated acts have no agent. This leads to the invalidation of the proof for establishing the Creator. Ma'mar held that all accidents occur by the nature of bodies except the will.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage presents two disputes among the Mu'tazila. First, they differ on the timing of when a generated act leaves divine power: one group says it leaves when its cause occurs, another says only when the act itself occurs. Second, they differ on whether qualities like colors and tastes can be generated acts. Thumama denies any agent for them, which the author notes would undermine the proof for God's existence. Ma'mar reduces all accidents to natural properties except will.

سبب

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila disagreed on whether a generated act leaves divine power at the occurrence of its cause or only when it actually exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if once you set a cause in motion, God's power over the outcome ceases, ignoring that He alone brings the act into existence? How would your daily trust in God change if you internalized that your deeds remain within His power until they actually happen?
?
When you plan your day, do you rely more on your own efforts (causes) or on the One who holds power over every generated outcome—even the smallest? What would it look like to consciously acknowledge God's continuous power over your actions?
?
Are there areas in your life where you feel a false sense of security because you think the cause guarantees the result, forgetting that God could cut off the generation at any moment? Repent of that reliance and realign your heart to humble dependence on His ongoing power.

Dispute on Generated Colors and Tastes
Mu'tazili dispute on generated colors/tastes and extreme views of Thumama and Ma'mar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili disagreement over whether colors and tastes can be generated acts, and the extreme views of Thumama and Ma'mar.

Core Idea
Are colors and tastes generated? Thumama said no agent; Ma'mar said nature does all.

The Mu'tazila also debated whether accidents like colors and tastes can occur as generated acts. Two extreme positions emerged: Thumama denied any agent for them, and Ma'mar attributed all accidents to the nature of bodies except will. These views have serious theological consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the two positions.
Notice the phrasing: 'هل يجوز أن تقع متولدة' and the names Thumama and Ma'mar.
2
Analyze Thumama's view
Thumama held that generated acts have no agent. Consider what this implies: if no agent, then there is no proof for a Creator.
The author points out that this leads to invalidating the proof for God's existence.
3
Analyze Ma'mar's view
Ma'mar attributed all accidents to the nature of bodies except the will. This reduces divine action to natural processes.
Note the exception: will is not from nature.
4
Reflect on the stakes
Both views challenge the standard theological framework. Thumama removes agency, Ma'mar naturalizes accidents.
This shows the diversity within Mu'tazili thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال قوم من منهم : لا يزال مقدوراً إلى حين وقوع سببه ، فيجب ثبوته فينقطع أثرُ القدرة فيه . وقال آخرونَ : إنَّما ينقطع كونه مقدوراً إذا وقع المتولد ووُجِدَ ، لا عند وقوع سببه فقط . واختلفوا في الألوان والطعوم : هل يجوزُ أن تقعَ مُتولّدةً أم لا ؟ وذهب ثمامةُ بنُ أشرسَ : إلى أنَّ هذه المتولَّداتِ لا فاعل لها . ويلزمه بطلان الدليل على إثباتِ الصانع وذهب معمرُ : إلى أنَّ جميع الأعراض واقعةٌ بطباع الأجسام إلا الإرادة .
Some people said: 'It (a generated act) remains within divine power until the time of its cause's occurrence, so its existence becomes necessary, thus the effect of power over it ceases.' Others said: 'It only ceases to be within power when the generated act actually occurs, not merely when its cause occurs.' They also differed concerning colors and tastes: is it permissible for them to occur as generated acts or not? Thumama ibn Ashras held that these generated acts have no agent. This leads to the invalidation of the proof for establishing the Creator. Ma'mar held that all accidents occur by the nature of bodies except the will.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage presents two disputes among the Mu'tazila. First, they differ on the timing of when a generated act leaves divine power: one group says it leaves when its cause occurs, another says only when the act itself occurs. Second, they differ on whether qualities like colors and tastes can be generated acts. Thumama denies any agent for them, which the author notes would undermine the proof for God's existence. Ma'mar reduces all accidents to natural properties except will.

Key Terms
متولدة
فاعل
طباع الأجسام
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thumama's view denies God's existence entirely.
Correction: Thumama denies secondary agents for generated acts, not the First Cause; his view arguably implies a direct divine action.

Checkpoint
According to Ma'mar, which accident is an exception to the rule that all accidents occur by the nature of bodies?
A) Colors
B) Tastes
C) Will
D) No exception

Key Takeaway
Some Mu'tazila, like Thumama and Ma'mar, held extreme views on generated accidents, with implications for divine agency and causality.

?

The Four Generated Acts and the Dispute on I'timad
Lists the four generated acts and records the dispute between Jubba'i and his son

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four generated acts recognized in Kalām theory and the key dispute between Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i regarding i'timad.

Core Idea
The generated acts are four: i'timad, contiguity, vision, and painful blow — but what exactly is generated by i'timad is debated.

Kalām scholars who affirm causality through generated acts enumerate four such acts. This passage from Sanusi presents the standard list, then records a subtle but important disagreement between the leading Mu'tazilite al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim over whether the generated act is i'timad itself or the resulting movement. Let's unpack the list and the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four acts
First read the Arabic and translation. Note the four terms: i'timad, mujawara, nazar, wahy. Each has a specific meaning in Kalām.
These are technical terms; don't confuse i'timad with 'intention'.
2
Understand the conditions and effects
The passage adds that mujawara has conditions, nazar generates knowledge, wahy generates pain. This specifies the causal relationship.
The conditions for mujawara are not detailed here but are discussed elsewhere.
3
Examine the dispute on i'timad
Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i differed on whether the generated act is i'timad itself or the movement it causes. This is a key nuance in Kalām causal theory.
This dispute shows that even within a school, there are subtle disagreements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المولدات عندَ مَنْ يقولُ بها أربعة : الاعتماد ، والمجاورة ، والنظرُ ، والوهي ] والمولدات عندهم أربعة (۱) : الاعتماد ، والمجاورة على شرائط معتبرة عندهم ، والنظرُ المولد للعلم ، والوَهْيُّ المولد للألم . وقد اختلف أبو هاشم والجبائي : في أنَّ المولد الاعتماد أو الحركة؟ فذهب الجبائي إلى الثاني ، وذهبَ ابنُه أبو هاشم إلى الأَوَّلِ
Generated acts according to those who affirm them are four: i'timad (leaning/pressure), mujawara (contiguity), nazar (speculative reasoning/vision), and wahy (painful blow). And generated acts according to them are four (1): i'timad, mujawara under certain conditions, nazar which generates knowledge, and wahy which generates pain. Abu Hashim and al-Jubba'i disagreed: is the generated act i'timad or movement? Al-Jubba'i held the second, and his son Abu Hashim held the first.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage first gives a bare list of four generated actions: i'timad, mujawara, nazar, wahy. Then it repeats the list with explanation: i'timad and mujawara have conditions, nazar generates knowledge, wahy generates pain. Then it records the disagreement between al-Jubba'i and his son over whether the generated act is i'timad (the pressure) or the movement (the motion caused).

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: I'timad is often misunderstood as simply 'intention' or 'reliance' in a figurative sense.
Correction: In Kalām, i'timad is a technical physical action like pressing or leaning, which generates a movement or effect.

Key Takeaway
The four generated acts are i'timad, mujawara, nazar, and wahy, but the precise nature of i'timad's generated effect is disputed between al-Jubba'i and his son.

Core Idea
Reducing i'timad to muscle strength is naturalism, not theology.

The author now turns to a specific Mu'tazili doctrine regarding i'timad (reliance or leaning), which they attempted to explain purely in physical terms. This passage shows how the author dismisses such explanations as a form of materialist naturalism, far from sound theological reasoning. Let's examine his concise critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

The passage is a single sentence, but it packs a doctrinal rejection.
2

Generation in God's Acts: Mu'tazila Internal Dispute
Mu'tazila debate whether generation applies to God's own acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila disagreement on whether God's own acts can generate effects, and the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
God's power is universal; can it still generate specific effects?

The Mu'tazila are known for their rational theology, but they do not always agree among themselves. This passage presents a second internal dispute: whether the concept of generation (تولد) applies to God's own actions. One group rejects it due to the universality of divine power, while another accepts it by analogy with created causality. Both positions spring from the same rationalist framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two distinct groups and their arguments.
Pay attention to the term 'قادريت' (universal power) and 'سبب مولّد' (generating cause).
2
Identify the two positions
Locate the first group (جماعة) and their reasoning based on the universality of God's power. Then locate the second group (آخرون) and their analogy-based argument.
The second group's argument uses the principle that a cause, once present, must produce its effect unless hindered.
3
Reflect on the author's comment
The author says the second view is 'أقرب إلى قياس مذهبهم' (closer to the analogy of their school). Consider what this implies about the author's own inclination.
This is a rare moment where the author evaluates the internal debate.
4
Connect to broader theology
Think about how this dispute relates to divine transcendence and causality. Does accepting generation in God's acts compromise His uniqueness?
The first group is concerned with preserving the universality of God's power, while the second is concerned with causal consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفت المعتزلة : هل يجوز أن يكون في أفعال الباري تعالى تولد ؟ فصارَتْ جماعةٌ : إلى منعِهِ ؛ لعموم قادريَّتِهِ تعالى ، وامتناع أنْ تتعلق بشيءٍ في محلها ، وإنَّما تتعلَّق بما خرجَ عن محلّها ، ونسبتها إلى جميع ما خرج عن محلها نسبةً واحدةً وصارَ آخرونَ : إلى أنَّ التولُّدَ معقول في أفعالِهِ تعالى ؛ فإِنَّ السببَ المولّدَ لمَّا جازَ وقوعُهُ مِنَ اللهِ عزَّ وجلَّ .. لم يجز أنْ ينتفي تأثيره في مُسببه إلا لمانع ، وليسَ صدورُهُ مِنَ الصانع مانعاً ، وإلا لمنع في الشاهدِ ، فلزمَ أَنْ يُولَّد ، وهذا القولُ أقرب إلى قياس مذهبهم هـذا حاصل مذاهبهم في التولُّدِ
Then the Mu'tazila differed: is it permissible that generation occurs in the acts of God Most High? A group held its prohibition due to the universality of His power, and the impossibility that it (the power) attaches to something in its own locus; rather it attaches to what exits its locus, and its relation to all that exits its locus is uniform. Another group held that generation is intelligible in His acts; for when the generating cause is possible to occur from God, it is impossible that its effect on its generated outcome be negated unless there is a hindrance, and its occurrence from the Creator is not a hindrance—otherwise it would be a hindrance in the witnessed realm—therefore generation must occur. And this view is closer to the analogy of their school. This is the summary of their doctrines on generation.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage records a second Mu'tazili internal disagreement: whether God's own acts can generate effects. One group denies it because God's power is universal and cannot be limited to generating from something in its own locus; it applies equally to all exits. Another group affirms it by analogy: if a cause can originate from God, its effect cannot be absent without a hindrance, and God's agency is not such a hindrance, so generation holds. The author comments that the latter is closer to the school's own reasoning.

Key Terms
تولد
قادريت
سبب مولّد
مسبّب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this dispute is about whether God Himself is affected by generation.
Correction: The dispute is about whether God's acts (His creation) can have generated effects—not about God being generated or affected.

Checkpoint

C) Because generated acts are impossible for any agent
D) Because they deny causality entirely

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are internally divided on whether God's acts can generate secondary effects, with the author favouring the affirmative view as more consistent with their principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson forces you to examine: Is your view of God's power so universal that it cannot be tied to specific outcomes? Or do you believe God's causation includes specific generated effects? How would your trust in God change if you truly believed every detail is directly connected to His power?
?
The second group argues that if a cause from God is possible, its effect must follow unless there is a hindrance, and God's action is not a hindrance. Do you live as though God's actions are hindered by your circumstances? Reflect on a time you doubted God's ability to bring about a specific good outcome. What does that doubt imply about your belief in the necessity of His effects? Are you willing to accept the full implications of God's absolute causality, even when it challenges your expectations?
?
This debate among the Mu'tazila is not just historical—it touches your daily life. When you pray for something, do you expect a direct cause-effect link from God? Or do you think God's power operates uniformly, detached from specific outcomes? This lesson asks you: Is your relationship with God one of trust in His specific, generating power, or do you limit Him to a distant, uniform power? Make a concrete commitment today to identify one area where you can more consciously attribute effects directly to God's causation.

Refutation of generation doctrine and first necessary implication
Sanusi refutes the generation doctrine by recalling earlier proof and stating an implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the generation doctrine by showing that it implies a single effect from two agents, which is impossible.

Core Idea
The generation doctrine forces a single effect from two agents, which is absurd.

In the previous chapter, Sanusi established by apodictic proof that all events are directly from God and that nothing else has any real effect. Now he applies that conclusion to refute the generation doctrine (tawallud). He points out one logical consequence: their view requires that a single event has two agents—the created power and the generating cause. This is impossible and thus refutes their position.

Guided Path
1
Recall the earlier proof
Before reading this passage, remember the earlier chapter's proof that all events are attributed to God alone and nothing else has any effect. This is the foundation of the refutation.
Sanusi says this refutation 'has become clear' from that chapter.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Sanusi first reminds us of the earlier proof, then states the first necessary implication.
The implication is 'lazim'—a logical consequence they must accept.
3
Identify the two agents in the implication
The two agents are: (1) the created power (qudra ḥāditha) of the human agent, and (2) the object of that power which is the 'generating cause' (sabab muwallid). According to them, the effect is caused by both.
This creates a contradiction because one effect cannot have two true agents.
4
Reflect on why this refutes tawallud
Ask yourself: if all events truly come from God alone, then the generation doctrine's claim that created powers produce effects is false. The implication shows their position leads to an absurdity.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ ردَّ مذهبهم في التولُّدِ قدِ اتَّضحَ في الفصل الذي قبل هذا ، وهو ما قامَ مِنَ البرهان القطعي على إسنادِ الحوادثِ كلّها للباري جلَّ وعزّ ، وأنَّهُ لا تأثير لكلِّ ما عداه جملةً وتفصيلاً في شيء منها ، وإلى هذا المعنى أشرنا في أوَّلِ هذا الفصل بقولنا : ( وإذا عرفت استحالة تأثير القدرة الحادثة . . . ) إلى آخرِ الكلام ، ثم أشرنا في هذا الفصل إلى لوازم تلزمهم ممَّا يخص القول بالتولد : فمنها : أنَّهُ يلزمُهم وجود أثرٍ واحدٍ عن مُؤثرين ؛ وهما القدرة الحادثة ، ومقدورُها الذي هو السبب المولَّدُ ؛ لأنَّهمُ ادَّعَوا أَنَّ الحادث واجب عندَ سببه المولدِ ، ومقدور للفاعل بالقدرة الحادثة أيضاً
Know that the refutation of their doctrine of generation has become clear in the chapter before this, which is what was established by apodictic proof that all events are attributed to God Almighty, and that there is no effect for anything other than Him, collectively and individually, in any of them. And to this meaning we pointed at the beginning of this chapter by our saying: 'And when you know the impossibility of the effect of created power...' to the end of the speech. Then we pointed in this chapter to necessary implications that follow from them regarding the doctrine of generation. Among them: that it necessitates for them the existence of a single effect from two agents: namely, the created power and its object which is the generating cause; because they claimed that the event is necessary upon its generating cause and also within the agent's power (by created power).
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage is the author's refutation. He says the earlier chapter already established by decisive proof that all events come from God and nothing else has any true effect. So that refutes the generation doctrine. Here, he adds one logical consequence of their position: it implies that one effect (the generated event) proceeds from two agents—the created power (of the human) and the generating cause itself (which is also an object of power). This is impossible because the effect would have two independent sources.

Key Terms
برهان قطعي
تأثير
قدرة حادثة
أثر واحد عن مؤثرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The generation doctrine does not deny that God is the ultimate cause; it only claims that secondary causes have real causal efficacy.
Correction: Sanusi's argument shows that attributing any real effect to creatures violates the earlier proof that all events are directly from God. The first implication reveals a logical impossibility.

Checkpoint
What is the first necessary implication (lazim) of the generation doctrine according to Sanusi?
A) That an event can occur without a cause
B) That one effect has two agents
C) That God is not omnipotent
D) That humans have no free will

Key Takeaway
The generation doctrine is refuted by the earlier proof that all events are from God alone, and it further leads to the impossibility of a single effect from two agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that no created thing—including your own power—has any independent effect, how would the way you plan your daily tasks change? What would you do differently tomorrow morning?
?
When you succeed at something, do you ever catch yourself feeling proud of your own ability as if you were the real cause? How would this lesson reorient your gratitude after every achievement?
?
The generation doctrine makes you think your efforts and the causes you set in motion guarantee results. Are you willing to surrender that sense of control and rely solely on God's direct creation of outcomes, even when it feels unsettling?

Mu'tazila Disagreement on Tawallud in God's Actions
Reports the Mu'tazila disagreement on tawallud in God's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions among the Mu'tazila regarding whether generated causation (tawallud) can occur in God's actions, and the reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila disagreed: can God's actions involve generated causes?

This passage from Sanusi reports an internal Mu'tazilite debate about the nature of divine causality. While some Mu'tazilites argued that God's universal power precludes generated effects, others insisted that if a generating cause comes from God, its effect must follow unless there is an impediment. Understanding this disagreement reveals the breadth of Mu'tazilite thought on causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the author presents two opposing views among the Mu'tazila: one prohibits tawallud in God's acts, the other permits it.
The second view is described as 'closer to the analogy of their school'.
2
Analyze each group's reasoning
For the first group, note the argument based on the universality of God's power. For the second group, note the argument about the generating cause and the absence of an impediment.
Think about how each argument relates to the concept of causality in the physical world (shāhid).
3
Consider the author's preference
The author states that the second view is closer to the Mu'tazilite school's own principles. Reflect on why that might be, given that Mu'tazila generally emphasized rational causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفت المعتزلة : هل يجوز أن يكون في أفعال الباري تعالى تولد ؟ فصارَتْ جماعةٌ : إلى منعِهِ ؛ لعموم قادريَّتِهِ تعالى ، وامتناع أنْ تتعلق بشيءٍ في محلها ، وإنَّما تتعلَّق بما خرجَ عن محلّها ، ونسبتها إلى جميع ما خرج عن محلها نسبةً واحدةً وصارَ آخرون : إلى أنَّ التولُّدَ معقول في أفعالِهِ تعالى ؛ فإِنَّ السببَ المولّدَ لمَّا جازَ وقوعُهُ مِنَ اللهِ عزَّ وجلَّ .. لم يجز أنْ ينتفي تأثيره في مُسببه إلا لمانع ، وليسَ صدورُهُ مِنَ الصانع مانعاً ، وإلا لمنع في الشاهدِ ، فلزمَ أَنْ يُولَّد ، وهذا القولُ أقرب إلى قياس مذهبهم هـذا حاصل مذاهبهم في التولُّدِ
Then the Mu'tazila disagreed: Is it permissible that there be generated causation (tawallud) in the acts of God Most High? A group held to its prohibition, due to the universality of His omnipotence (qādriyya) Most High, and the impossibility that it (i.e., the power) attaches to something in its locus, but rather it attaches to what is outside its locus, and its relation to all that is outside its locus is one relation. And others held that generated causation (tawallud) is intelligible in His acts Most High; for when the generating cause (sabab muwallad) is possible to occur from God Mighty and Majestic, it is not permissible that its effect on its caused (musabbab) be negated except for an impediment, and its occurrence from the Maker is not an impediment, otherwise it would prevent in the witnessed (shāhid), so it necessarily generates (yuwallad). And this statement is closer to the analogy of their school. This is the summary of their positions on generated causation (tawallud).
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage reports the disagreement among the Mu'tazila on whether generated causation (tawallud) can occur in God's acts. The author first states the two positions: one group prevents it based on the universality of God's power, which attaches only to what is outside its locus (meaning effects are directly from God, not mediated by created causes). The other group deems tawallud intelligible in God's acts, arguing that if the generating cause occurs from God, its effect cannot be negated unless there is a specific impediment—and God's acting as the source is not an impediment. The author notes that the second view is closer to the Mu'tazila's own principles (measured analogy, qiyās). The passage concludes that this sums up their positions on tawallud.

Key Terms
تولد (tawallud)
السبب المولد (al-sabab al-muwallad)
قادرية (qādriyya)
شاهد (shāhid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila uniformly accepted tawallud in God's acts.
Correction: This passage shows there was a disagreement: some prohibited it, others affirmed it.

Checkpoint
What reason did the first group of Mu'tazila give for denying tawallud in God's actions?
A) Because God's power is limited
B) Because God's power is universal and attaches only to what is outside its locus
C) Because generating causes are impossible in the witnessed world
D) Because tawallud requires an impediment

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila were divided: one group denied tawallud in God's acts based on the absoluteness of His power, while the other affirmed it, arguing that a cause from God must produce its effect unless there is an impediment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you trace the events of your day—blessings and hardships alike—do you see them as directly from God or through a chain of causes? How does the Mu'tazila debate force you to examine your own assumptions? Will you pause tomorrow to consciously attribute every outcome to His direct power, regardless of the means?
?
Have you ever dismissed a theological position because you assumed all adherents thought alike? This passage reveals a significant split within the Mu'tazila. Are you willing to revisit your own preconceptions about groups you disagree with? What would it take for you to engage with differing views with genuine curiosity and humility?
?
The second group's argument hinges on the absence of an impediment to God's action. In your life, where do you perceive impediments to His decrees—in your doubts, your impatience, your lack of trust? Will you surrender those perceived barriers and affirm that His will must be effective, and take a concrete step today to rely more fully on His power?

First Necessary Consequence: One Effect from Two Agents
The author states the first necessary consequence: one effect from two agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first necessary consequence that refutes the Mu'tazila's theory of tawallud and why it leads to the impossibility of one effect from two independent agents.

Core Idea
One effect cannot come from two independent agents.

This passage presents the first of several necessary consequences (lāzim) that the author uses to refute the Mu'tazila's theory of tawallud. The Mu'tazila claim that generated events are both necessary at their generating cause and object of power for the agent. This leads to the impossible conclusion that a single effect has two distinct agents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the compact style: the author states the consequence and then gives the reason.
The passage begins with 'فمنها' indicating this is one of a series of consequences.
2
Identify the two agents
The passage names two agents: the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) and its object of power which is the generating cause (al-sabab al-muwallad). Understand what each refers to in the Mu'tazila framework.
The Mu'tazila hold that the agent's power creates the cause, which then necessarily produces the effect.
3
Understand the contradiction
The problem is that if the effect is necessary from the generating cause and also an object of the agent's power, then one effect has two independent sufficient causes. This is rationally impossible.
Think about why two independent sufficient causes cannot produce the same effect without conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمنها : أنَّهُ يلزمُهم وجود أثرٍ واحدٍ عن مُؤثرين ؛ وهما القدرة الحادثة ، ومقدورُها الذي هو السبب المولَّدُ ؛ لأنَّهمُ ادَّعَوا أَنَّ الحادث واجب عندَ سببه المولدِ ، ومقدور للفاعل بالقدرة الحادثة أيضاً
Among them (the necessary consequences): that there follows for them the existence of one effect from two agents; they are the originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) and its object of power (maqdūruhā) which is the generating cause; because they claimed that the originated event is necessary at its generating cause, and is also an object of power for the agent through the originated power.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage presents the first necessary consequence (lāzim) that refutes the Mu'tazila's view on tawallud. If tawallud is accepted, then a single effect (the generated event) would be produced by two agents: (1) the originated power (of the human agent) and (2) the generated cause itself (which is also an object of power). The Mu'tazila claim the event is both necessitated by the generating cause and an object of power for the agent. This implies one effect has two independent causes, which is impossible in their own principles.

Key Terms
أثر (athar)
مؤثر (mu'aththir)
قدرة حادثة (qudra ḥāditha)
سبب مولَّد (sabab muwallad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think two causes can cooperate to produce a single effect.
Correction: The argument is that each cause is claimed to be independently sufficient, leading to conflict or redundancy. Two independent sufficient causes for one effect are rationally impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the first necessary consequence, what two agents would produce the same effect if tawallud is true?
A) The human power and the divine power
B) The human power and the generating cause
C) The generating cause and the effect itself
D) The will and the power

Key Takeaway
The first necessary consequence shows that tawallud leads to the absurdity of one effect having two independent causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever attribute outcomes to multiple independent causes—like your effort, luck, or natural laws—while forgetting that Allah alone creates every event? How does this lesson expose the flaw in that thinking?
?
When you succeed in something, do you subconsciously feel that your power and the means you used are both independently effective? What would it mean for your reliance on Allah if you truly internalized that only He is the Creator of all effects?
?
This passage argues that one effect cannot have two true causes. Are there areas in your faith where you are still dividing causation between Allah and secondary causes, despite knowing He is al-Khāliq? What step can you take today to purify your tawheed in causality?

Action Without an Agent: The Second Necessary Consequence
The author presents the second consequence of tawallud: action without an agent, with a hypothetical example and objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing an act to a deceased agent is impossible and how this refutes the doctrine of tawallud.

Core Idea
An act cannot be attributed to an agent who lacks the prerequisites of action.

In this passage, the author continues his critique of tawallud by presenting a vivid example: an archer who dies before his arrow strikes. The example shows that if tawallud were allowed, actions would be attributed to a dead person, contradicting the essential prerequisites of action. This lesson unpacks the argument and the subsequent objection and answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the hypothetical example.
2
Understand the archer hypothetical
Notice the sequence: archer shoots, dies before arrow hits, arrow strikes a living being causing wound and death. The author claims these effects are acts of the archer.
This is the scenario that leads to absurdity.
3
Identify the prerequisites of action
The author lists life, will, power, and knowledge as the prerequisites (muṣaḥḥiḥāt) for a valid act. A dead archer lacks all of them.
These are necessary for an act to be truly attributed to an agent.
4
Follow the objection and answer
The objection states that an act requires an agent but not necessarily at the same time. The answer: attribution is impossible when the agent cannot be an agent at the time of the act.
This reinforces that the agent must exist and possess the prerequisites at the moment of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : وجود الفعل بلا فاعل أو بدونِ إرادة وشعور بالفعل ؛ فإنَّ مَنْ رمى سهماً واخترمَتْهُ المنيَّةُ قبل وصول السهم إلى الرَّميَّةِ ، ثم اتَّصل بها وصادف حيّاً .. فإنَّهُ يحصل بِهِ جرحٌ ، ولا يزال سارياً إلى أنْ يفضيَ إلى الزُّهوقِ مثلاً ، فهذهِ السراياتُ والآلام أفعال للرامي ، وقد رمَّتْ عظامُهُ ! ولا مزيدَ في الفسادِ على نسبة قتل إلى مَيْتِ مع انتفاء مُصححاتِ الفعل منه ؛ وهي الحياة والإرادة والقدرة والعلم ، ولو جاز وقوع الفعلِ مِنْ مَيْتٍ لبطلَتْ دلالةُ الفعل على كون الفاعل حيّاً . ثم وجود الفعل حالةَ عدم الفاعل يمنعُ أيضاً الاستدلال بوجودِ الحوادث على وجود الصانع وإن قالوا : الفعلُ يدلُّ على فاعل ولا يلزم منه وجود الفاعل حالةَ وجود فعله • فالجواب : أَنَّهُ لا بدَّ مِنْ إضافة الفعل إلى الفاعل ، ويمتنع صدوره صحة مضافاً إليه في حالةِ امتناع كونِهِ فاعلاً ؛ إذ الصدور منه يقتضي ذلك ، والامتناع ينافي الصحة
And among them: the existence of an act without an agent, or without will and consciousness of the act. For indeed, one who shoots an arrow and death cuts him off before the arrow reaches the target, then it connects with it and strikes a living being — then a wound occurs by it, and it continues spreading until it leads to death, for example. So these spreadings and pains are acts of the archer, while his bones have rotted! And there is no greater corruption than attributing a killing to a dead person along with the absence of the prerequisites (muṣaḥḥiḥāt) of the act from him, which are life, will, power, and knowledge. And if the occurrence of an act from a dead person were permissible, the indication of the act that the agent is living would be invalidated. Then, the existence of the act in the state of the absence of the agent also prevents the inference from the existence of events to the existence of the Maker. And if they say: 'The act indicates an agent, and it does not require the existence of the agent at the time of the act's existence.' The answer: that the attribution of the act to the agent is necessary, and it is impossible for it to occur validly attributed to him in the state of his being impossible to be an agent; for occurrence from him necessitates that, and impossibility contradicts validity.
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage presents the second necessary consequence and the objections against it. The author argues that if tawallud is accepted, an act could be attributed to someone who is no longer alive (e.g., an archer who dies before the arrow hits). The subsequent wound and death would be acts of the dead archer, which is absurd because the prerequisites (life, will, power, knowledge) are absent. This would undermine the proof from acts for a living agent. Then the objection: the act indicates an agent but does not require the agent to exist at the same time as the act. The answer: attribution of the act to the agent is necessary, and it is impossible for it to be validly attributed when the agent cannot be an agent (e.g., dead). The occurrence from him requires his existence as an agent; impossibility contradicts the validity of attribution.

Key Terms
فعل (fiʿl)
فاعل (fāʿil)
مصححات الفعل (muṣaḥḥiḥāt al-fiʿl)
إرادة (irāda)
قدرة (qudra)
علم (ʿilm)
حياة (ḥayāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The act of injury from the arrow is not the act of the archer because he is dead at the time of injury.
Correction: According to tawallud, the act is still attributed to the archer because the causal chain started from him. The author argues this is absurd because the prerequisites of action are absent.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say attributing the arrow's effect to a dead archer is corrupt?
A) Because the arrow is no longer under the archer's control
B) Because the archer lacks the prerequisites of action: life, will, power, and knowledge
C) Because the archer did not intend to kill that person
D) Because the death occurred after the archer's death

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that the prerequisites of action (life, will, power, knowledge) are necessary for valid attribution, and tawallud's allowance of acts without an agent leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you attributed a good deed to yourself long after you stopped actively pursuing it, ignoring that you now lack the will or life to sustain it? How does this lesson expose that self-deception?
?
Which of the four prerequisites—life, will, power, knowledge—is most absent from your current worship? What concrete step will you take today to restore it before your deeds lose their valid attribution to you?
?
If acts are only validly attributed to a living, knowing, willing agent, then what does your spiritual laziness or ignorance say about your status as an agent of tawḥīd? Will you continue to produce dead acts?

Third Consequence: Death Generated from Pain
Third necessary consequence: death generated from pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third logical consequence that the Ash'ari use to refute the Mu'tazili theory of tawallud: if generated causation holds, then death following a series of pains would be attributed to the human agent who caused the initial pain.

Core Idea
If tawallud is true, death becomes a human act.

The author presents the third consequence in a series that shows the absurdity of tawallud. If generated causation is accepted, then death that results from a sequence of pains would itself be generated from the same human agent who produced the pain. But only God has power over life and death, so this consequence is theologically impossible. This fragment begins the argument but cuts off mid-comparison.

Guided Path
1
Read the incomplete passage
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice that the sentence is incomplete—it ends at 'كنسبة' (like the relation of). This signals that the full argument continues on the next page.
The footnote in the source indicates editorial continuation.
2
Identify the core claim
The claim is that death consequent upon pains (al-mawt al-mustaʿqab li-l-ālām) would be generated (mutawallid) from the agent of the pain (fāʿil al-alam). Understand that 'generated' here means an indirect effect of the agent's act.
3
Understand why this is a refutation
Consider the implication: if death is generated from the human agent, then humans create death. Since only Allah has power over death, this consequence contradicts Islamic theology and thus invalidates tawallud.
The Mu'tazila argue that acts generate effects, but the Ash'ari show this leads to unacceptable attributions.
4
Compare with previous consequences
This is the third consequence. Reflect on how each consequence independently refutes tawallud by showing an impossible attribution. All three together form a cumulative case against generated causation.
The first consequence concerned the arrow and the second (not in this fragment) likely dealt with a similar pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يلزمهم : أنْ يكونَ الموتُ المستعقَبُ للآلام مُتولّداً عن فاعل الألم (۱)؛ فإنَّ نسبة تعقُبِ الآلام المتوالية المتعاقبة إلى فعله كنسبة
And among what follows them: that death consequent upon pains be generated (mutawallid) from the agent of the pain; for the relation of the succession of consecutive and sequential pains to his act is like the relation of...
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage presents the third necessary consequence (lāzim) that refutes the Mu'tazila theory of tawallud. If generated causation is accepted, then death that results from a sequence of pains would itself be attributed to the human agent who caused the initial pain. The author argues that the relation of the successive pains to the agent's act is comparable to something (the text cuts off). The implication is that death would be an act of the human agent, which is impossible because only God has power over death. This completes the set of logical consequences that make tawallud untenable.

Key Terms
موت (mawt)
ألم (alam)
متولّد (mutawallid)
فاعل الألم (fāʿil al-alam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Death is always a direct act of God and never connected to human actions.
Correction: While only God creates death, the sequence of pain leading to death is a natural process. The Ash'ari argue that attributing generated causation to humans would make humans the creators of death, which is false.

Checkpoint
According to the third necessary consequence, what would be generated from the agent of pain?
A) The pain itself
B) The death that follows the pains
C) The will to cause pain
D) The arrow's wound

Key Takeaway
The third consequence shows that tawallud would attribute death to humans, which is impossible because only Allah has power over life and death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If death can only come from Allah, how does this change the way you view every pain and suffering you experience? Are you living each moment as a direct gift from Him, or do you take it for granted?
?
The Mu'tazila theory would make humans indirectly responsible for death through their actions. But if only Allah gives life and takes it, what does that mean for how you handle suffering? Do you turn first to Allah or to worldly explanations?
?
This argument shows that attributing death to anyone other than Allah is false. Is there any area of your life where you subtly believe that someone or something other than Allah has ultimate control over your life or death? What would change if you truly internalized that only Allah holds this power?

Impossibility of an Act from a Dead Agent
Sanusi argues against act without a living agent, refutes objection, and derives a necessary consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that an act cannot be attributed to a dead agent, and why this is crucial for proving the existence of a living Creator.

Core Idea
An act without a living agent destroys the proof for God.

Sanusi presents one of his key arguments against the theory of generated acts (tawallud). He uses the vivid example of a shooter who dies before his arrow hits, showing that the subsequent effects cannot be attributed to him. This argument not only refutes tawallud but also upholds a fundamental theological principle: only a living, knowing, and powerful being can be the source of acts. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense, logical style of Sanusi.
2
Understand the example and its implications
The shooter example illustrates why an act cannot be from a dead person. Identify the prerequisites (life, will, power, knowledge) that are absent after death.
This example is designed to show absurdity.
3
Examine the objection and Sanusi's response
The opponent says the act indicates a doer but not necessarily at the time of the act. Sanusi replies that valid attribution requires the doer to be capable at that moment.
Focus on the logical connection between attribution and possibility.
4
Consider the consequence for al-Jubba'i
Sanusi draws a necessary consequence: if pains generate death, then the agent of pain must also be agent of death, which breaks consensus and implies power over life.
This ties the argument to broader theological commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : وجود الفعل بلا فاعل أو بدونِ إرادة وشعور بالفعل ؛ فإنَّ مَنْ رمى سهماً واخترمَتْهُ المنيَّةُ قبل وصول السهم إلى الرَّميَّةِ ، ثم اتَّصل بها وصادف حيّاً .. فإنَّهُ يحصل بِهِ جرحٌ ، ولا يزال سارياً إلى أنْ يفضيَ إلى الزُّهوقِ مثلاً ، فهذهِ السراياتُ والآلام أفعال للرامي ، وقد رمَّتْ عظامُهُ ! ولا مزيدَ في الفسادِ على نسبة قتل إلى مَيْتِ مع انتفاء مُصححاتِ الفعل منه ؛ وهي الحياة والإرادة والقدرة والعلم ، ولو جاز وقوع الفعلِ مِنْ مَيْتٍ لبطلَتْ دلالةُ الفعل على كون الفاعل حيّاً . ثم وجود الفعل حالةَ عدم الفاعل يمنعُ أيضاً الاستدلال بوجودِ الحوادث على وجود الصانع. وإن قالوا : الفعلُ يدلُّ على فاعل ولا يلزم منه وجود الفاعل حالةَ وجود فعله. فالجواب : أَنَّهُ لا بدَّ مِنْ إضافة الفعل إلى الفاعل ، ويمتنع صدوره صحة مضافاً إليه في حالةِ امتناع كونِهِ فاعلاً ؛ إذ الصدور منه يقتضي ذلك ، والامتناع ينافي الصحة. ومما يلزمهم : أنْ يكونَ الموتُ المستعقَبُ للآلام مُتولّداً عن فاعل الألم؛ فإنَّ نسبة تعقُبِ الآلام المتوالية المتعاقبة إلى فعله كنسبة تعقُبِ الموتِ له ، وهذا الإلزام لا يتأَنَّى لهم دَفَعُهُ ، ولم يتأتّ للجبائي أن ينفصل عنه إلا بالتجاسر على خرق إجماع الأمة ، فنسب الموتَ إلى فاعل الألم ! وقد أجمعَتِ الأُمَّةُ على أنَّ البارى تعالى هو الذي يُحيي ويميتُ ، وهو قد نسب الإماتة إلى غيرِهِ ، ويلزمُهُ : أَنْ يكونَ قادراً على الإحياء أيضاً على الجملةِ ؛ لأنَّهُ ضدُّهُ ، والقدرة على الشيء عندهم قدرة على ضدّهِ !
And among them (the arguments against tawallud) is: the existence of an act without a doer or without will and consciousness of the act. For if someone shoots an arrow and death befalls him before the arrow reaches the target, then it (the arrow) connects and strikes a living being, then a wound occurs from it, and it continues spreading until it leads to death, for example. These spreadings and pains are acts of the shooter, even though his bones have rotted! There is no greater corruption than attributing a killing to a dead person while the prerequisites of the act are absent from him, namely life, will, power, and knowledge. If the occurrence of an act from a dead person were permissible, then the indication of the act that the doer is living would be nullified. Also, the existence of an act in the absence of a doer prevents the inference from the existence of events to the existence of the Maker. If they say: 'The act indicates a doer, but the existence of the doer at the time of his act is not necessarily implied.' The answer: It is necessary to attribute the act to the doer, and it is impossible for it to issue from him validly as attributed to him in a state where it is impossible for him to be a doer, because issuance from him requires that (possibility), and impossibility contradicts validity. And among the necessary consequences for them is that the death consequent upon pains would be generated from the agent of the pain. For the ratio of the succession of successive and consecutive pains to his act is like the ratio of the succession of death after it. This entailment cannot be repelled by them, and al-Jubba'i could not extricate himself from it except by daring to break the consensus of the nation, for he attributed death to the agent of the pain. The nation has unanimously agreed that the Exalted Creator is the One who gives life and causes death, but he (al-Jubba'i) attributed causing death to other than Him. It follows necessarily for him that he (the agent) would also be able to give life in general, because it is its opposite, and power over a thing according to them is power over its opposite!
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This passage is one of Sanusi's arguments against the theory of tawallud (generated acts). He starts by denying the possibility of an act occurring without a living agent, using the example of a shooter who dies before his arrow hits. The arrow continues to cause harm, but these effects cannot be attributed to the dead shooter because a dead person lacks the prerequisites for action: life, will, power, and knowledge. If an act could proceed from a dead person, the very indication that an act has a living doer would be invalidated, and the argument from contingent events to the existence of a Creator (who must be living) would collapse. He then anticipates an objection: that the act indicates a doer but does not require his existence at the moment of the act. Sanusi replies that attribution of the act to the doer is necessary, and it is impossible for an act to be validly attributed to one who is fundamentally incapable of being a doer at that moment. Issuance requires possibility; impossibility contradicts validity. Finally, he draws a necessary consequence for those who accept tawallud: the death that follows successive pains would have to be generated from the same agent who caused the pains. Since the relation between the pains and the agent is the same as between death and the agent, they cannot avoid this conclusion. Al-Jubba'i (a Mu'tazilite) tried to escape by attributing death to the agent of the pain, but this breaks the consensus that only God gives life and causes death. Moreover, if that agent can cause death, he must also be able to give life, since power over a thing implies power over its opposite in their view.

Key Terms
فعل
فاعل
تولد
حياة
إرادة
قدرة
علم
موت
الجبائي
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that an effect can be attributed to an agent even after the agent's death, as long as the agent initiated the chain of events.
Correction: Sanusi shows that this destroys the indication of the act that the doer is living, which is essential for proving that God is living. An act requires the agent to be alive, knowing, willing, and powerful at the moment the act occurs, not just at the start.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue would be invalidated if an act could proceed from a dead person?
A) The possibility of miracles
B) The indication of the act that the doer is living
C) The existence of the soul after death
D) The consensus of the nation

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of an act from a dead agent confirms that every act requires a living, knowing, willing agent, which in turn points to the living Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that your actions are only valid while you are alive and conscious, how does that change the way you live each moment? Are there deeds you postpone, assuming you can still claim them after death?
?
Sanusi shows that attributing an act to a dead person destroys the proof for a living Creator. Is there any part of your life where you rely on a dead cause—a past intention, a forgotten commitment—instead of actively engaging with the living God?
?
The objection that an act can be attributed to an agent even when he is absent might comfort you when you sin and think 'I wasn't myself' or 'I didn't mean it.' How does Sanusi's reply expose the false sense of security in that excuse, and what will you do to take full responsibility for your actions now?

Why Regularity Does Not Prove Causation
Sanusi refutes the argument from observed regularities and lists counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the argument from regularities for tawallud, and see that customary connections do not entail causal influence.

Core Idea
Regular conjunction does not prove causation; custom is not necessity.

The Mu'tazilites argued that since effects follow our purposes, our acts must generate them. Sanusi counters that mere regularity does not prove influence, and then sharpens his point by listing phenomena that both sides agree are not generated—even though they follow purposes. This lesson walks you through the argument and its supporting examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' argument and Sanusi's core response
First, read the passage to see the structure: opponents claim that observed regularities between acts and effects prove generation. Then note Sanusi's immediate answer that regular connection does not imply influence.
Pay close attention to the phrase 'ارتباط شيء بشيء بحسب مجرى العادة' – it's the key to the refutation.
2
Examine the counterexamples
Sanusi lists several phenomena that occur according to purposes but are not generated, such as satiety after eating, heat from friction, and fear from frightening. Notice how the opponents themselves agree these are not generated, which shows inconsistency in their position.
3
Reflect on the logical principle
Ask yourself: What is the underlying principle here? How does this refutation protect the Ash'ari view that all effects are directly from Allah?
This principle distinguishes customary regularity from necessary causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا على التولُّدِ : بأنَّا نجد المسبّباتِ واقعةً على حسَبِ القُصُودِ والدواعي. والجواب : أنَّ ارتباط شيءٍ بشيءٍ بحسب مجرى العادة وإن اطَّرِدَ .. لا يدلُّ على أنَّ لأحدهما تأثيراً في الآخرِ ، فارتباط الأصل ، كما أنَّ المقدور المباشَرَ بالقدرة الحادثة كذلكَ المقيس عليه والفرع مستويانِ عندنا في عدم الدلالة على التأثير. وأيضاً ممَّا ينقض عليهم هذهِ الحُجَّةَ : أَنَّا نجد أموراً واقعةً على حسَبِ الدواعي والقُصُودِ ، وقد ساعدونا على عدم تولدها ؛ منها : الشبع والرّيُّ عند الأكل والشرب ، والسقم والبرء والموتُ عند معظم المعتزلة ، والحرارة عندَ احتكاك جسم بجسم على تحامل واعتماد وسقط الزنادِ عند الاقتداح ، وفهم المخاطبِ وخجلُ الخَجِل ووجل الوَجِل ؛ عند الإفهام والتخجيل والتخويف ، وبعضُهم التزم التولد
They argued for tawallud (generation) by saying: We find the caused things occurring according to purposes and motives. The answer: That the connection of one thing with another according to the customary course (habit) even if it is regular does not indicate that one of them has influence over the other. So the connection of the original (act) is just as the directly caused thing (al-maqdur al-mubashar) by the originated power, and likewise the analogized thing (the branch) is equal in our view in the lack of indication of influence. Also, among what refutes this argument for them is that we find things occurring according to motives and purposes, and they have agreed with us on their not being generated: among them: satiety and thirst-quenching upon eating and drinking; illness, recovery, and death according to most Mu'tazilites; heat upon the friction of a body with a body with force and pressure; the falling of a spark upon striking (fire-making); and the understanding of the addressee, the bashfulness of the bashful, and the fear of the fearful upon making understood, shaming, and frightening. And some of them committed to generation (in some cases).
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage presents the opponents' argument for tawallud (generation of effects) and Sanusi's refutation. The opponents claim that we observe caused effects occurring in accordance with our purposes and motives, implying that human acts generate their effects. Sanusi's answer is that mere regular conjunction (customary connection) does not prove causal influence between two things. The original act (e.g., a movement) and its supposed effect are equal in his school: neither implies influence over the other. He then strengthens his refutation by listing phenomena that also occur according to purposes and motives but that the opponents themselves agree are not generated: satiety after eating, quenching thirst after drinking, illness, recovery, and death (according to most Mu'tazilites), heat from friction, sparks from striking, and psychological effects like understanding, bashfulness, and fear that follow from communicative or social acts. Some Mu'tazilites admitted generation in a limited sense, but the list shows inconsistency in their position.

مسببات
قصد
داعية
ارتباط
عادة
تأثير
قدرة حادثة
أصل
فرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two events regularly occur together, one must cause the other.
Correction: Sanusi shows that regularity alone does not prove causal influence; things can be habitually connected without one causing the other (e.g., eating and satiety).

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the regular connection between two things indicate?
A) That one necessarily influences the other
B) That they are both created directly by God
C) Nothing about influence
D) That they are generated by the same agent

Key Takeaway
Sanusi establishes that customary regularity does not prove causal efficacy, preserving the Ash'ari doctrine that all effects are directly from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you eat and feel full, do you genuinely attribute that fullness to Allah's direct creation, or do you unconsciously think the food itself caused it? How can you cultivate a moment of awareness before every meal to acknowledge His sole agency?
?
Think of a time when you felt fearful or bashful because of someone's words or actions. Did you blame that person for your feeling? According to this lesson, what is the real source of that feeling? Will you consciously redirect your recognition to Allah next time?
?
Sanusi says that regular conjunction does not prove influence. In your daily life, what 'regular conjunctions' do you rely on as if they were necessary causes (e.g., medicine curing illness, alarm waking you)? What would change if you truly believed that Allah creates each effect independently at every moment?

Refutation of al-Jubba'i's Attribution of Death to a Creature
Sanusi refutes al-Jubba'i's attribution of death to a creature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes al-Jubba'i by invoking consensus and the Mu'tazilite principle of opposites to show the absurdity of attributing death to a created agent.

Core Idea
Attributing death to a creature implies granting it power over life, violating consensus.

In this passage, Sanusi confronts a Mu'tazilite claim that a human agent who causes pain also causes death. He shows that this not only breaks the consensus of the community that God alone gives life and death, but also, by Mu'tazilite logic, forces the absurd conclusion that a creature can give life. This is a classic kalam argument using internal consistency and consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the strong language: 'لم يتأتّ للجبائي أن ينفصل عنه إلا بالتجاسر على خرق إجماع الأمة'.
2
Identify the two key moves
First, Sanusi appeals to consensus (إجماع) that God alone causes life and death. Second, he applies the Mu'tazilite principle that power over a thing implies power over its opposite.
This shows internal inconsistency in al-Jubba'i's view.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument is considered decisive: al-Jubba'i cannot escape without breaking consensus or accepting that a creature can give life, both unacceptable.
Sanusi's refutation is tight and leaves no room for rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعقُبِ الموتِ له ، وهذا الإلزام لا يتأَنَّى لهم دَفَعُهُ ، ولم يتأتّ للجبائي أن ينفصل عنه إلا بالتجاسر على خرق إجماع الأمة ، فنسب الموتَ إلى فاعل الألم ! وقد أجمعَتِ الأُمَّةُ على أنَّ البارى تعالى هو الذي يُحيي ويميتُ ، وهو قد نسب الإماتة إلى غيرِهِ ، ويلزمُهُ : أَنْ يكون قادراً على الإحياء أيضاً على الجملةِ ؛ لأنَّهُ ضدُّه ، والقدرة على الشيء عندهم قدرة على ضدّهِ !
Following death upon him? This objection they cannot possibly repel, and al-Jubba'i could not disentangle himself from it except by daring to break the consensus of the community, so he attributed death to the agent of pain! The community has unanimously agreed that the Creator, exalted, is the one who gives life and causes death, and he attributed causing death to other than Him. And it is necessary for him that he be capable of giving life as well in general, because it is its opposite, and ability over a thing according to them is ability over its opposite.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific Mu'tazilite position attributed to al-Jubba'i regarding the generation of death. Sanusi argues that the claim that a human agent, who causes pain, also causes death, leads to attributing to a creature a power that belongs exclusively to God. The consensus of the umma is that God alone gives life and death. By claiming that the agent of pain is the one who causes death, al-Jubba'i violates this consensus. Furthermore, if the agent has power over death, then by parity of reasoning he would also have power over its opposite, life, since according to Mu'tazilite principles power over a thing implies power over its opposite. This is absurd.

إجماع
البارى
الإماتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite view does not entail granting the power to give life.
Correction: Sanusi shows that by the Mu'tazilite principle that power over a thing implies power over its opposite, attributing death to a creature necessarily implies that creature also has power over life.

Checkpoint

A) That the agent is God
B) That the agent is also capable of giving life
C) That death is not an action of any agent
D) That the agent of pain is not the cause of death

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes al-Jubba'i by showing that attributing death to a creature both violates consensus and, by Mu'tazilite logic, implies the creature also has power over life, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you face uncertainty about your health or the life of someone you love, do you truly entrust the outcome to God alone, or do you find yourself placing ultimate reliance on doctors, medicine, or your own efforts? How can this lesson's emphasis on God's exclusive power over life and death reshape your daily trust in Him?
?
Al-Jubba'i dared to break the consensus of the Muslim community out of adherence to his own reasoning. Is there an area of your faith where you prioritize your own logic over the clear teachings of the scholars and the community? What would it cost you to humbly submit to that consensus?
?
If you genuinely believed that every moment of your life is directly sustained by God and that no creature can cause your death without His permission, how would that change the way you approach fear, risk, and your plans for the future? What is one concrete step you can take this week to live out this tawhid more deeply?

The Mu'tazila Argument for Tawallud and the Ash'ari Response
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazila argument for generated acts and responds that correlation does not imply causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument for generated acts based on observed regularities and the Ash'ari critique that such regularity does not imply causal influence.

Core Idea
Constant conjunction does not prove causation – it is merely God's custom.

Sanusi now examines one of the Mu'tazila's key arguments for the reality of generated acts (tawallud). They point to our everyday experience: effects seem to follow in a regular way from our intentions and motives. The Ash'ari response, presented here, is a foundational rejection of necessary causal connections. This lesson unpacks the argument and its refutation, clarifying how the Ash'ari view preserves divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: Mu'tazila claim (احتجوا) followed by the Ash'ari response (الجواب).
2
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
The Mu'tazila argue from empirical observation: we find effects (المسببات) occurring according to intentions and motives (القصود والدواعي). This constant conjunction, they claim, proves that the agent's act generates the effect.
This is an argument from induction – a common philosophical move.
3
Identify the Ash'ari response
The response denies that even constant association implies causal influence (تأثير). It is merely the course of habit (مجرى العادة). God's custom creates the regularity, not any created power.
This is the core of Ash'ari occasionalism.
4
Examine the extension to original and generated acts
Sanusi argues that both the original direct act (الأصل) and the generated branch (الفرع) are equal in lacking evidence of influence. The agent's power over the direct act does not entail real causal influence; it is also a habitual accompaniment.
This refutes the Mu'tazila analogy that generated acts must be caused by the agent like direct acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا على التولُّدِ : بأنَّا نجد المسبّباتِ واقعةً على حسَبِ القُصُودِ والدواعي والجواب : أنَّ ارتباط شيءٍ بشيءٍ بحسب مجرى العادة وإن اطَّرِدَ .. لا يدلُّ على أنَّ لأحدهما تأثيراً في الآخرِ ، فارتباط الأصل ، كما أنَّ المقدور المباشَرَ بالقدرة الحادثة كذلكَ المقيس عليه والفرع مستويانِ عندنا في عدم الدلالة على التأثير (۱)
They argued for generation (tawallud) by saying: We find effects occurring according to intentions and motives. The response: That a thing is associated with another thing according to the course of habit, even if it is constant, does not indicate that one of them has an influence on the other. So the connection of the original (direct act), just as the directly intended act is measured by the created power, so the analogical object and the branch (generated act) are equal in our view in lacking indication of influence.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage gives the Mu'tazila's main argument for generated acts: we observe that effects follow upon intentions and motives, and this constant conjunction suggests causal dependence. Sanusi provides the Ash'ari response: such regular association is merely habitual and does not prove that one thing has any real influence over another. He then distinguishes between the direct act (الأصل), which is under the agent's power directly, and the branch or generated act (الفرع). Both are equal in that neither provides evidence of genuine causal influence; the regular sequence is simply God's custom.

Key Terms
التولّد
المسبّبات
الارتباط
العادة
الأصل
الفرع
التأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'ari's denial of causal influence means they deny the reality of cause and effect.
Correction: Ash'ari affirm cause and effect as habitual sequences (God's custom), not as necessary causal connections. They do not deny the observed regularity, only its necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, why does the constant conjunction of A and B not prove that A influences B?
A) Because A and B could be reversed
B) Because it is merely habitual and does not entail real influence
C) Because A is always the cause
D) Because the Mu'tazila admit it is not constant

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazila argument for tawallud is that empirical regularity does not imply real causal influence; both direct and generated acts are under God's direct creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a consistent pattern in your life—like a repeated success or setback—do you instinctively attribute it to your own actions or to God's habit? How does this lesson challenge the way you interpret your daily experiences?
?
Think of a routine you rely on, such as waking up each morning. Do you truly believe that God could interrupt that habit at any moment? What would change in your mindset if you lived with that awareness?
?
The Mu'tazila argument seems logical: effect follows cause. But this lesson says it is only God's custom. Do you feel a resistance to that idea? Where in your life do you subtly deny God's direct control by assuming 'natural' causes?
