Refuting the Mu'tazila on Tawallud with Counterexamples and Inconsistency
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila by listing counterexamples and rebutting their excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila on tawallud by pointing out their own inconsistent denials of generation and by exposing the absurd consequences of their partial acceptance.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's own examples undercut their theory of generated acts.

Sanusi continues his critique of the Mu'tazila's theory of tawallud (generated acts). He now employs a powerful refutation: he lists cases where the Mu'tazila themselves admit that no generation occurs despite apparent causal connections. This inconsistency, when combined with the absurd implication of partial generation, leaves their position untenable. Additionally, he anticipates and refutes their possible excuse of non-constancy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the counterexamples
Read the list of phenomena where the Mu'tazila deny generation: satiety, thirst-quenching, illness, heat from friction, etc. Notice that these occur according to motives and intentions, yet the Mu'tazila agree no generation happens.
2
Understand the partial admission
Some Mu'tazila admit generation in satiety and heat. Note how Sanusi uses this to force an absurd conclusion: if one generated act is a body (fire from flint), then by similarity all bodies would be generated, which is impossible.
3
Examine the latent fire objection
The Mu'tazila might claim fire was latent in the flint. Sanusi dismisses this as absurd because there is no fire before striking, as seen in the marakh tree example.
This shows the generated thing is not latent but appears anew.
4
Refute the constancy excuse
The Mu'tazila might say they deny generation in the first list because the association is not constant. Sanusi retorts that the same non-constancy applies to their own examples (throwing, wounding), so their criterion fails.
This undermines their entire theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً ممَّا ينقض عليهم هذهِ الحُجَّةَ : أَنَّا نجد أموراً واقعةً على حسَبِ الدواعي والقُصُودِ ، وقد ساعدونا على عدم تولدها ؛ منها : الشبع والرّيُّ عند الأكل والشرب ، والسقم والبرء والموتُ عند معظم المعتزلة ، والحرارة عندَ احتكاك جسم بجسم على تحامل واعتماد وسقط الزنادِ عند الاقتداح ، وفهم المخاطبِ وخجلُ الخَجِل ووجل الوَجِل ؛ عند الإفهام والتخجيل والتخويف (٢) ، وبعضُهم التزم التولد في الشبع والرّيِّ والحرارةِ عند الأكل والشرب والاحتكاك ، وهو قولُ غير معظمهم والمحصلين منهم ، وأُلزِمَ هذا البعضُ أنْ تكونَ الأجسام متولّدةً معَ أَنَّها ليسَتْ مِنْ جنس مقدورنا بإجماع ؛ وذلك لأنَّ سقط النارِ عند الاقتداح يقعُ على حسب الدواعي ، فإذا تولَّد لزمَ أَنْ تتولد سائر الأجسام ؛ لتماثلها ، فإن زعموا أَنَّ النارَ كَانَتْ كامنة في الجسم فتحرَّكَتْ ، وأنَّ المتولد حركة هذا هَوَساً لا يرضى بقولِهِ عاقل ؛ فإنَّ الحجر والزناد ليس فيهما قبل القدح شيء ، وكذلكَ المَرْخُ إذا نُشِرَ بالمنشارِ فلا نار فيهِ ، وعندَ حكِّهِ تظهر النار (١) وإنْ أجابوا عن قولهم بعدم التولُّدِ في تلك الأمور التي ألزموها : بأنَّهم إنَّما قالوا فيها بعدم التولُّدِ لعدم اطرادِها .. قيلَ لهم (٢) : وكذلك ثبت عدم الاطرادِ فيما ادعيتُموهُ مُولَّداً ؛ كالرمي والجَرْحِ ورفعِ الثقيل وشيله (۳) ، وغيرِ ذلكَ ممَّا وقعَ فيهِ النزاعُ .
And also from what refutes this argument for them: we find things occurring according to motives and intentions, and they agree with us that they are not generated. Among them: satiety and thirst-quenching from eating and drinking, and illness and recovery and death according to most Mu'tazila, and heat from friction of one body against another with pressure and leaning, and the falling of a spark from striking a flint, and the understanding of the listener, the blushing of the embarrassed, and the fear of the fearful from informing and causing embarrassment and frightening. And some of them held that generation occurs in satiety and quenching and heat from eating, drinking, and friction, which is the view of the majority and the investigators among them. And this partial group was forced to accept that bodies would be generated, while they are not of the same genus as what we can produce by consensus. This is because falling of fire upon striking occurs according to intentions; if it is generated, then all bodies must be generated due to their similarity. If they claim that the fire was latent in the body and then moved, and that the generated thing is the motion of this body, this is absurd that no rational person would accept; for the stone and flint have nothing in them before striking, and likewise the marakh tree when grated with a saw has no fire in it, and when rubbed fire appears. And if they answer regarding their claim of non-generation in those things that they were forced to admit, by saying that they said they are not generated because they are not constant, it is said to them: Similarly, non-constancy is established in what you claim to be generated, such as throwing, wounding, lifting a heavy object, and other things about which there is dispute.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage provides a second refutation of the Mu'tazila's argument for tawallud. Sanusi lists several examples where the Mu'tazila themselves admit that no generation occurs despite the presence of motives and intentions: satiety, thirst-quenching, illness, heat from friction, etc. This shows that they do not consistently apply their principle. Some Mu'tazila do claim generation in satiety, etc., but then they are forced to the absurd conclusion that bodies are generated, because the falling of fire from flint is a body, and if it is generated by the agent's act, then all bodies would be generated by analogy, which is impossible. The Mu'tazila might respond that they deny generation in those cases because the association is not constant (sometimes eating does not cause satiety). Sanusi retorts that the same lack of constancy applies to the examples they do claim as generated, like throwing and wounding; sometimes a thrower fails to hit, etc. So their criterion fails.

Key Terms
النقض
الدواعي
التولّد
الاطراد
التماثل
الهوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe all apparent causes produce generated effects.
Correction: Sanusi shows that they themselves deny generation in many cases like satiety, heat, etc., revealing inconsistency.

Checkpoint
What forced implication do the Mu'tazila face if they admit generation in satiety and heat?
A) That all bodies are generated
B) That the agent is God
C) That generation is impossible
D) That the agent has no power

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that their own inconsistent application of tawallud and the absurd consequence of partial generation undermine their entire theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a theological or intellectual position that, if you applied it consistently, would lead to an absurdity? What did you do when you realized that inconsistency?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to excuse their inconsistency by appealing to 'non-constancy'. Are there areas in your own life where you selectively apply principles to avoid uncomfortable conclusions? Will you examine one such area today?
?
Sanusi's refutation exposes the danger of building a system on human reasoning alone. In what matters do you trust your own intellect over revealed truth, and what might that cost you?

Refutation of Latent Fire in Stone
Sanusi refutes the claim that fire is latent in stone and flint.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that fire is latent in stone and flint is rationally and empirically untenable.

Core Idea
Fire is not hidden in objects; generation claims lead to absurdity.

The author addresses a specific objection about the generation of fire. Some claim fire is latent in stone and simply becomes manifest when struck. Sanusi shows this view is inconsistent and leads to absurd consequences. He then provides clear observational evidence that fire is not pre-existing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target view
Read the passage to find which group's opinion is being refuted. Note the context: they deny generation in cases like satiety and heat, but are compelled to accept it here.
The group is some Mu'tazilites, but the text says 'other than the majority and the verifiers'.
2
Understand the reductio argument
See how the author argues that if fire were generated from striking, then all bodies would have to be generated due to similarity, which is impossible.
3
Examine the observational refutation
The author then refutes the latency claim directly: observation shows no fire in stone, flint, or markh wood before friction. This is simple empirical proof.
Note the specific example of the markh tree cut with a saw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الشبع والرّيِّ والحرارةِ عند الأكل والشرب والاحتكاك ، وهو قولُ غير معظمهم والمحصلين منهم ، وأُلزِمَ هذا البعضُ أنْ تكونَ الأجسام متولّدةً معَ أَنَّها ليسَتْ مِنْ جنس مقدورنا بإجماع ؛ وذلك لأنَّ سقط النارِ عند الاقتداح يقعُ على حسب الدواعي ، فإذا تولَّد لزمَ أَنْ تتولد سائر الأجسام ؛ لتماثلها ، فإن زعموا أَنَّ النارَ كَانَتْ كامنة [؟] جسم لا وجود جسم في كان الجسم فتحرَّكَتْ ، وأنَّ المتولد حركة هذا هَوَساً لا يرضى بقولِهِ عاقل ؛ فإنَّ الحجر والزناد ليس فيهما قبل القدح شيء ، وكذلكَ المَرْخُ إذا نُشِرَ بالمنشارِ فلا نار فيهِ ، وعندَ حكِّهِ تظهر النار
Regarding satiety, thirst, and heat from eating, drinking, and friction — this is the view of other than the majority of them and the verifiers among them. This particular one was compelled that bodies would be generated, despite the fact that they are not of the kind of our capability by consensus. That is because the falling of fire when striking occurs according to motives; so if it were generated, it would necessitate that all other bodies be generated, because of their similarity. If they claim that fire was latent [؟] a body no existence body in was the body and thus moved, and that what is generated is a movement — this is raving that no rational person would accept. For the stone and flint have nothing in them before striking, and likewise the markh tree, when cut with a saw, has no fire in it, and when it is rubbed, fire appears.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection concerning the generation of fire. The author first mentions a group (some Mu'tazilites) who deny generation in cases like satiety and heat from eating and friction, but then are compelled to admit that bodies would be generated from fire, even though bodies are not in our power. The argument is that if fire were generated from striking, then by analogy all similar bodies would be generated, which is absurd. The author then refutes the claim that fire was latent in the stone and moved: this is dismissed as irrational, since observation shows no fire in the stone or flint before striking. The example of the markh tree (a type of wood) similarly shows that fire is not pre-existing but appears only upon rubbing. Thus the claim of latency is false.

Key Terms
تولد (generation)
اقتداح (striking fire)
المرخ (markh tree)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fire is hidden (latent) in objects like stone and flint, and becomes manifest when struck.
Correction: Observation shows no fire in these objects before friction; the generation argument also leads to absurd consequences.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against the claim that fire is latent in stone?
A) Fire is not generated because it is not in our power.
B) If fire were latent, then all bodies would be generated due to similarity, which is absurd.
C) The stone and flint contain fire but it becomes visible only when struck.
D) The markh tree produces fire without striking.

Key Takeaway
The claim that fire is latent in stone is false because it leads to absurd generation of all bodies and contradicts direct observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The analogy in the argument says: if fire were generated, all bodies would have to be generated. This shows how one wrong step can lead to absurd conclusions. In your own spiritual life, is there a small compromise or incorrect assumption that, if followed to its logical end, would lead to something far worse? Will you trace it and repent?

The Condition of Regularity in Generation and Its Counterexamples
Sanusi refutes the condition of regularity by citing counterexamples from Mu'tazilite doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazilite condition of regularity by demonstrating that their own examples of generation also lack regularity.

Core Idea
Irregularity is universal—it cuts both ways.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a key objection from the Mu'tazilites: that generation (tawallud) only occurs when the cause regularly produces the effect. He turns their own criterion against them by pointing out that the very examples they accept as generated—throwing, wounding, lifting—also fail to be regular. This reveals an inconsistency in their position and defends the Ash'ari view that generation is not tied to empirical regularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the structure: objection then rebuttal.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazilites deny generation in certain cases due to lack of regularity. Understand their reasoning.
Regularity means the effect always follows the cause.
3
Analyze the author's rebuttal
The author cites three examples—throwing, wounding, lifting—where irregularity is present yet the Mu'tazilites accept these as generated.
Each example shows the effect is not constant.
4
Reflect on the logical force
Consider why this rebuttal is effective: it shows the condition of regularity is applied inconsistently.
The argument does not deny regularity altogether but shows it cannot be a valid distinguishing criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنْ أجابوا عن قولهم بعدم التولُّدِ في تلك الأمور التي ألزموها : بأنَّهم إنَّما قالوا فيها بعدم التولُّدِ لعدم اطرادِها .. قيلَ لهم : وكذلك ثبت عدم الاطرادِ فيما ادعيتُموهُ مُولَّداً ؛ كالرمي والجَرْحِ ورفعِ الثقيل وشيله ، وغيرِ ذلكَ ممَّا وقعَ فيهِ النزاعُ . أمَّا الرمي : فإِنَّ الإنسان فيصيبُ الغرض تارةً ولا يصيبُ الغرضَ أخرى ، والجرحُ : قد يفضي إلى السريان تارةً وقد يندملُ أخرى ، ورفع الثقيل وشيله : قد يرتفع للشخص تارةً ولا يرتفع أخرى .
And if they respond to their statement denying generation in those matters they were compelled to admit — that they only said that they do not generate due to lack of regularity — it is said to them: Likewise, the lack of regularity is established in what you claim is generated, such as throwing, wounding, lifting the heavy and carrying it, and other matters about which there is dispute. As for throwing: a person sometimes hits the target and sometimes does not hit the target. As for wounding: it sometimes leads to spreading and sometimes heals. As for lifting the heavy and carrying it: it sometimes rises for the person and sometimes does not rise.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage addresses the Mu'tazilite objection that generation is denied in certain cases because those cases lack regularity (i.e., the effect does not always follow the cause). The author turns the argument around: the very same lack of regularity is found in cases that the Mu'tazilites themselves claim are generated, such as throwing (missing the target sometimes), wounding (sometimes healing without spreading), and lifting heavy objects (sometimes succeeding, sometimes failing). Therefore, their criterion of regularity is not a valid reason to deny generation in the disputed cases, since it applies equally to their own examples. This is a classic rebuttal showing inconsistency in the opponent's position.

Key Terms
اطراد (regularity)
تولد (generation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Regularity is a necessary condition for generation to occur.
Correction: The author shows that even admitted generated acts (throwing, wounding, lifting) exhibit irregularity, so regularity cannot be a necessary condition.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the Mu'tazilite claim that generation is denied due to lack of regularity?
A) By proving that regularity does not exist at all.
B) By showing that the same lack of regularity occurs in cases they consider generated.
C) By arguing that regularity is not a valid condition.
D) By denying that the examples given lack regularity.

Key Takeaway
The condition of regularity is not a valid criterion for generation because irregularity appears in both disputed and undisputed cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you throw a ball or lift a heavy object, do you assume that your skill alone guarantees the outcome, or do you acknowledge that Allah is the ultimate cause of every success and failure? How often do you thank Him for the 'hit' and still trust Him when you 'miss'?
?
The Mu'tazilites applied a standard to others that they did not apply to themselves. Where in your own life do you demand regularity and consistency from others—family, friends, or institutions—while excusing your own irregularity? Will you ask Allah to help you be fair in your judgments today?
?
Reflect on a recent moment when something you expected to happen did not—a task you thought would succeed failed, or a habit you thought was certain proved inconsistent. Did you attribute it to 'error' or 'chance,' or did you see the hand of Allah breaking your expectation? What would change if you truly believed that every irregularity is a direct act of God calling you to depend on Him alone?

Mu'tazilite Views on Lifting Heavy Objects
Mu'tazilite views on whether lateral movement suffices for lifting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite debate on whether the same movement can both move an object sideways and lift it, and the empirical reasoning used by Abu Hashim to argue for additional movements.

Core Idea
Lifting requires extra movements beyond lateral motion, says Abu Hashim.

In this lesson, we explore a specific debate within Mu'tazilite kalām about the nature of movement. While early Mu'tazilites believed that the same leaning (iʿtimād) that moves an object sideways also lifts it, Abu Hashim argued that we must add extra movements for lifting. His reasoning is based on what we actually observe: a person might move an object sideways but be unable to lift it. This passage shows how Mu'tazilites used empirical experience to ground their theory of generation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice that the text presents two contrasting views on the same issue.
Pay attention to the logical structure: a premise, then a disagreement, then an argument.
2
Identify the two positions
Look for the early Mu'tazilites' view and Abu Hashim's view. The early ones say the same leaning lifts; Abu Hashim says additional movements are needed.
3
Analyze Abu Hashim's argument
His argument is signaled by 'because' (li-anna). He uses the criterion of generation: we sense things proceeding according to our motives. He gives empirical evidence: a person can move sideways but not lift, so the cause differs.
This is a key methodological point for Mu'tazilites.
4
Reflect on the empirical basis
Consider how Abu Hashim relies on observable regularities. This shows that Mu'tazilite kalām is not purely speculative but grounded in common experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب المعتزلة في تحريك الأشياء الثقيلة : أنَّ تحريك الثقيل يمنة ويسرة بالاعتماد عليه ودفعه ، وإذا أُريد رفعُهُ وإقلاله اختلفوا فيهِ ؛ فذهب المتقدّمونَ : إلى أنَّ الاعتماد الذي يحرِّكُهُ يَمنةً ويَسرةٌ بِهِ يُرفَعُ إلى جهة التصعدِ . وقال أبو هاشم ومتَّبعوه : ليس ذلك بصحيح ، بل لا بدَّ مِنْ زيادةِ حركاتٍ على الحركة التي تحرَّكَ بها في جهة اليمنة واليسرة ، قال : لأنَّ معتمدنا في التولدِ ما نُحِسُّهُ مِنْ جريان الأمر على حسبِ دواعينا وقُصُودِنا ، ولا نشك أنَّا نجدُ مِنْ شخص قدرة على تحريكه يمنة ويسرة ولا يقدر على رفعِهِ ، فلزمَ أَنَّ ما بهِ يُحرِّكُهُ ليسَ ما بهِ يرفعُهُ
And the Mu'tazilite doctrine regarding moving heavy objects: that moving a heavy object right and left is by leaning upon it and pushing it. And if one intends to lift and raise it, they disagreed about that. The early scholars held that the leaning which moves it right and left is the same by which it is raised upward. Abu Hashim and his followers said: That is not correct; rather, there must be additional movements beyond the movement by which it moved in the right and left direction. He said: because our basis in generation is what we sense of things proceeding according to our motives and intentions, and we do not doubt that we find a person capable of moving it right and left but not capable of lifting it, so it necessarily follows that that by which he moves it is not that by which he lifts it.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite position on the movement of heavy objects and an internal disagreement. The common view is that moving an object laterally involves leaning (i'timad) and pushing. The dispute arises when lifting is intended. The early Mu'tazilites argued that the same leaning that moves it sideways also raises it upward. Abu Hashim and his followers disagree, using the criterion of generation: we observe that a person can sometimes move an object sideways without being able to lift it, so the cause of lateral movement cannot be the same as the cause of lifting. Therefore, additional movements are necessary for lifting. This argument relies on empirical observation and the principle that effects should follow our intentions in a regular way (the basis of generation according to Mu'tazilites).

Key Terms
اعتماد (leaning/dependence)
تولد (generation)
دواع (motives)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that the same force moves an object in all directions.
Correction: Abu Hashim shows that empirical evidence contradicts this: a person can move an object sideways without being able to lift it, so the causes must be different.

Checkpoint
What is Abu Hashim's main argument for requiring additional movements to lift a heavy object?
A) The leaning that moves it sideways is insufficient for upward motion.
B) A person can move an object sideways without being able to lift it, so the causes differ.
C) The early scholars were wrong because they did not consider regularity.
D) Heavy objects require more force to lift than to push.

Key Takeaway
In Mu'tazilite thought, observable differences in ability (e.g., moving sideways vs. lifting) indicate distinct underlying causes, challenging the notion that a single movement suffices for both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it mean for you to 'test' your faith like they tested movement?

Disagreement on Group Lifting a Heavy Object
Two Mu'tazilite opinions on collective lifting when each can lift alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite debate about how multiple agents contribute to a single effect when each is independently capable.

Core Idea
Collective causation raises the question: do agents act on distinct parts or share influence over the whole?

This brief but crucial passage records a disagreement among Mu'tazilite scholars about the nature of collective action. When a group lifts an object that each member could lift alone, how do their individual powers combine? The answer has implications for theories of causation and divine action. Let us examine the two positions and what they entail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: first a general statement, then two opinions introduced with 'فقال' and 'وذهب'.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find the first opinion attributed to al-Ka'bi and 'Abbad al-Saymari, and the second opinion of other Mu'tazilites. Write down the key difference: each carries a different part vs. each affects every part.
The first opinion avoids overlap, the second affirms shared causation.
3
Understand the underlying issue
Ask yourself why this disagreement matters. It relates to how causal power is distributed among agents. Does each agent have a discrete portion of the effect, or does each agent's power extend to the entire effect?
This connects to broader debates about the nature of causality and God's relation to created actions.
4
Consider the common misunderstanding
Read the common misunderstanding in the source: one might think that when many lift an object, they all share the same part. The first opinion corrects this by insisting on distinct parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلفوا أيضاً : إذا رفع جماعةٌ ثقيلاً وكلُّ واحدٍ يستقل على الانفراد بحملِهِ ؛ فقالَ الكعبي وعبَّاد الصيمري وأتباعهما : يحملُ كلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ الأجزاء ما لم يحملهُ الآخرُ ، ولا يشتركان في حمل جزء ، وذهب غيرُهم مِنَ المعتزلة : إلى أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مِنَ الجماعةِ يُؤثر في كل جزء
And they also disagreed: when a group lifts a heavy object and each one is independently capable of carrying it alone — al-Ka'bi, 'Abbad al-Saymari, and their followers said: each one carries a part that the other does not carry, and they do not share in carrying any part. And others among the Mu'tazila held that each one of the group affects every part.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage records a dispute among Mu'tazilite scholars about the nature of collective action when each individual is independently capable of lifting the object alone. The first group (al-Ka'bi and 'Abbad al-Saymari) argue that each person carries a distinct part, so there is no overlap in their actions. The second group holds that each person's power affects every part of the object, implying a kind of shared causation. This is a technical discussion about how multiple agents can contribute to a single effect, relevant to theories of causal power and generation.

Key Terms
استقلال (independence/capability)
جزء (part)
أثر (effect/affect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When multiple people lift an object, they all share the same part and their actions overlap.
Correction: At least one Mu'tazilite view (al-Ka'bi and 'Abbad al-Saymari) holds that each person carries a distinct part with no overlap. The second view also allows for overlap, but the first explicitly denies it.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bi and 'Abbad al-Saymari, how does a group lift a heavy object when each can lift it alone?
A) Each person carries a different part, and they never share a part.
B) Each person affects every part of the object.
C) They lift it together using shared causation.
D) Only one person lifts it while others assist.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite debate on collective lifting illustrates two models of causal contribution: distinct partitive action versus shared influence over the whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazilite disagreement on collective lifting presents two models: each affects a distinct part, or each affects every part. In your own experience of working with others—whether in da'wah, family, or community—have you assumed your contribution is distinct or shared? How might your assumption affect your sense of responsibility and your reliance on others? Are you ready to re-evaluate that assumption in light of this debate?
?
If you can accomplish a task alone, do you still choose to involve others? When you do, do you fear losing individual recognition or do you embrace shared causation? This lesson challenges you to confront your motives for joining collective action: is it for the shared reward or a desire for exclusive credit? Examine your heart.
?
The first opinion isolates each agent's effect to separate parts, preserving causal independence. The second allows multiple agents to affect every part, blurring boundaries. Which model resonates with how you see your own influence in the world? Does your answer make you more or less accountable for the collective failures of your community? Let this question sit with you.

Mu'tazilite View on Generated Actions and Lifting Heavy Objects
Sanusi presents Mu'tazilite examples of generated actions and their disagreement on lifting heavy objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite concept of generated actions (tawallud) and the internal debate on whether the same reliance (i'timad) causes both sideways movement and lifting of heavy objects.

Core Idea
Can the same reliance move an object sideways and lift it? Abu Hashim says no.

This passage explores the Mu'tazilite theory of generated actions through concrete examples like throwing and wounding, where outcomes vary. It then focuses on a disagreement: does the same reliance that pushes an object sideways also lift it? The early Mu'tazilites thought yes, but Abu Hashim argued from sensory experience that a person may move sideways but not lift, proving distinct causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples of generated actions
Read the examples of throwing, wounding, and lifting heavy objects. Notice that each sometimes succeeds and sometimes fails, illustrating their generated nature.
These examples set up the concept of tawallud.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite doctrine on sideways movement
For moving heavy objects sideways, reliance (i'timad) is the cause. This is agreed upon.
3
Contrast the two positions on lifting
The early Mu'tazilites say the same reliance lifts upward; Abu Hashim says additional movements are needed.
Abu Hashim's position is based on empirical observation.
4
Analyze Abu Hashim's argument
He argues that a person may be able to move an object sideways but not lift it, so the cause of sideways movement cannot be the same as the cause of lifting.
This reasoning relies on sensed differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا الرمي: فإِنَّ الإنسان يرمي فيصيبُ الغرض تارةً ولا يصيبُ الغرضَ أخرى، والجرحُ: قد يفضي إلى السريان تارةً وقد يندملُ أخرى، ورفع الثقيل وشيله: قد يرتفع للشخص تارةً ولا يرتفع أخرى. ومذهب المعتزلة في تحريك الأشياء الثقيلة: أنَّ تحريك الثقيل يمنة ويسرة بالاعتماد عليه ودفعه(١)، وإذا أُريد رفعُهُ وإقلاله اختلفوا فيه؛ فذهب المتقدّمونَ: إلى أنَّ الاعتماد الذي يحرِّكُهُ يَمنةً ويَسرةً به يُرفَعُ إلى جهة التصعدِ. وقال أبو هاشم ومتَّبعوه: ليس ذلك بصحيح، بل لا بدَّ مِنْ زيادةِ حركاتٍ على الحركة التي تحرَّكَ بها في جهة اليمنة واليسرة، قال: لأنَّ معتمدنا في التولدِ ما نُحِسُّهُ مِنْ جريان الأمر على حسبِ دواعينا وقُصُودِنا، ولا نشك أنَّا نجدُ مِنْ شخص قدرة على تحريكه يمنة ويسرة ولا يقدر على رفعِهِ، فلزمَ أَنَّ ما بهِ يُحرِّكُهُ ليسَ ما بهِ يرفعُهُ.
As for throwing: man throws and hits the target sometimes and misses the target other times. And as for wounding: it may lead to infection sometimes and may heal other times. And as for lifting and carrying a heavy object: it may be lifted by a person sometimes and not lifted other times. The doctrine of the Mu'tazilites concerning the movement of heavy objects is: that moving a heavy object right and left is by reliance (i'timad) upon it and pushing it, and when its lifting and raising is intended, they differed concerning it. The early ones held that the reliance by which it moves right and left, by it it is raised upward. And Abu Hashim and his followers said: that is not correct, rather there must be additional movements beyond the movement by which it moved in the direction of right and left. He said: because our basis in generation (tawallud) is what we sense of the course of things according to our motives and intentions, and we do not doubt that we find a person capable of moving it right and left but unable to lift it, so it necessarily follows that that by which he moves it is not that by which he lifts it.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of generated actions (tawallud) through everyday examples: throwing, wounding, and lifting heavy objects are actions whose outcomes sometimes succeed and sometimes fail, indicating that they are not directly caused by the agent but rather generated by the act. Sanusi then presents the Mu'tazilite doctrine on moving heavy objects: movement sideways is achieved by reliance (i'timad) and pushing. When it comes to lifting, the early Mu'tazilites held that the same reliance that moves sideways also lifts upward. Abu Hashim and his followers disagreed, arguing that additional movements are required because empirical observation shows that a person may be able to move an object sideways but unable to lift it, proving that the cause of sideways movement is distinct from the cause of lifting. This is the first of two Mu'tazilite disagreements on generation.

Key Terms
tawallud (التولد)
i'timad (الاعتماد)
Abu Hashim (أبو هاشم)
generated actions (التولد)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Hashim's argument proves the reality of tawallud.
Correction: The passage only presents his reasoning without endorsing it; it is still a point of debate.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hashim, why must there be additional movements for lifting beyond those for sideways movement?
A) Because lifting requires more force than sideways movement.
B) Because a person can move an object sideways but not lift it, so the two must be distinct.
C) Because the early Mu'tazilites were wrong.
D) Because the object is heavier when lifted.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite concept of generated actions is illustrated by variable outcomes, and their internal debate over lifting heavy objects shows how empirical observation was used to argue for distinct causal mechanisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself unable to lift a heavy burden that you could easily push sideways, do you ever consider that the ability required is different? How does this observation challenge your assumption that your own power is uniform?
?
Abu Hashim grounds his argument in what we sense and observe in our daily actions. When you act, do you pay attention to the patterns of success and failure in your generated actions? What might they teach you about the true source of your abilities?
?
The Mu'tazilites used empirical observation to argue about the nature of causation. Have you ever let a common experience—like failing to lift something—reshape your understanding of how things happen? If not, what keeps you from doing so?

Mu'tazilite Views on Group Causation
Sanusi presents two Mu'tazilite views on group causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Mu'tazilite positions on causal contribution when a group lifts a heavy object that each could lift alone.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites split on whether joint action involves shared causality or divided parts.

The Mu'tazilites debated the nature of causality in joint actions. This passage presents two opposing views on how to attribute causal efficacy when a group lifts an object that each individual is strong enough to lift alone. Understanding this disagreement sets the stage for Sanusi's subsequent refutation from a Sunni perspective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note that the author first states the scenario, then presents two views.
The scenario is a group lifting a heavy object where each person can carry it alone.
2
Identify the first view
Identify the view of al-Ka'bi, 'Abbad al-Saymari, and their followers: each person carries distinct parts without sharing any part.
This view avoids causal overlap by dividing the object's parts.
3
Identify the second view
Identify the view of the majority Mu'tazilites: each person affects every part, resulting in a shared causal contribution.
This view allows for multiple agents acting on the same part simultaneously.
4
Compare the implications
Consider how each view understands causal agency. The first emphasizes exclusivity; the second emphasizes cooperation.
Sanusi will later critique both views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلفوا أيضاً: إذا رفع جماعةٌ ثقيلاً وكلُّ واحدٍ يستقل على الانفراد بحملِهِ؛ فقالَ الكعبي وعبَّاد الصيمري وأتباعهما: يحملُ كلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ الأجزاء ما لم يحملهُ الآخرُ، ولا يشتركان في حمل جزء، وذهب غيرُهم مِنَ المعتزلة: إلى أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مِنَ الجماعةِ يُؤثر في كل جزء والشَّرْكةُ حاصلة! وهذا مذهب معظم المعتزلة.
And they also differed: when a group lifts a heavy object and each one is independently capable of carrying it, al-Ka'bi and 'Abbad al-Saymari and their followers said: each one carries from the parts what the other does not carry, and they do not share in carrying any part. And other Mu'tazilites held that each one of the group affects every part, and sharing occurs! And this is the doctrine of most Mu'tazilites.
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite disagreement on generated actions: when a group lifts a heavy object and each individual is strong enough to carry it alone. Two views emerge: (1) al-Ka'bi and 'Abbad al-Saymari argue that each person carries a distinct set of parts, with no overlap — each carries what others do not. (2) The majority of Mu'tazilites hold that each person affects every part, so they all share in carrying the whole object. This is the state of the debate before Sanusi will refute both positions.

Key Terms
الكعبي
عبَّاد الصيمري
الشركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Both Mu'tazilite views on group lifting are theologically valid.
Correction: The passage sets up a refutation; Sanusi will argue that neither view correctly explains causal agency according to Sunni doctrine.

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Mu'tazilites, what happens when a group lifts a heavy object and each can carry it alone?
A) Each carries a different part.
B) Each affects every part, sharing the load.
C) Only one person actually lifts it.
D) The object splits into parts.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites disagreed on causality in joint actions: some favored division of parts, others favored shared causality across all parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you work with others to achieve something, do you tend to think your contribution is unique (like al-Ka'bi's view) or shared (like the majority's view)? How does that belief affect your sense of responsibility and humility?
?
The Mu'tazilites debated whether each person affects every part or only distinct parts. In your own teamwork, do you operate as if your contribution is partial or total? How might that affect your accountability for the outcome?
?
Consider a recent group success you were part of. Do you more naturally assume you carried a distinct part or that you and others shared in every part? What does that assumption reveal about your view of causality and your reliance on others?

Refutation of Mu'tazilite Generation in Lifting Heavy Objects
Sanusi refutes the first Mu'tazilite disagreement by affirming the true doctrine and arguing against both views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of both Mu'tazilite positions on generated movement and how the Sunni doctrine of direct divine causation resolves the issue.

Core Idea
The true doctrine attributes all possibilities directly to God, refuting the very principle of generation.

This passage addresses a specific Mu'tazilite dispute about whether lifting a heavy object involves generated movements. Sanusi first states the Sunni position—abolishing generation entirely—and then, for the sake of argument, grants generation to show that both Mu'tazilite views are internally contradictory. By following the argument, you will see how careful definition of movement and conditions leads to refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing on terms. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: true doctrine → hypothetical grant → refutation of ancients → refutation of Abu Hashim → further argument.
The passage uses dialectical reasoning (if we grant... then...).
2
Identify the true doctrine
Find where Sanusi states the Sunni position: abolishing tawallud (generation) and attributing all possibilities directly to God. Understand that this removes any difficulty (la ishkal).
This is the foundation of the refutation.
3
Understand the first refutation (ancients)
Sanusi says that if we grant generation, then the early Mu'tazilites' view is invalidated by Abu Hashim's argument. What is that argument? It involves claiming that sideways movement and lifting are not generated by the same reliance.
The text doesn't expand, but the key is that Abu Hashim refuted the ancients.
4
Understand the second refutation (Abu Hashim)
Abu Hashim's own view is refuted because it requires the conjunction of two similars (ijtima' al-mithlayn) – two similar movements in the same object – which is impossible.
Even if we accept that conjunction, there is a further problem.
5
Grasp the final argument even if two similars are allowed
Sanusi shows that even if we allow two similars, generating one movement in the object necessitates it moves; otherwise, movement would subsist in a stationary body, contradicting the definition of movement as emptying and occupying space.
Thus, Abu Hashim's requirement for extra movements is unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما إذا قلنا بالمذهب الحق ـ وهو إبطال أصل التولُّدِ، وإسنادُ الممكناتِ كلّها ابتداءً إلى الله تعالى - فلا إشكال، وإِنْ سلَّمناه جدلاً فيبطل مذهبُ الأقدمين في المسألة الأولى بما ذكر أبو هاشم فيها، ويبطل ما ذهب إليهِ أبو هاشم بأنَّ فيهِ اجتماع المثلين؛ لقوله: لا بدَّ من زيادة حركاتٍ، وهو محال(١). سلَّمنا جواز اجتماع المثلين، لكن يُقالُ لَهُ: إِذا وَلَّدَ الرافع حركة واحدةً في هذا الثقيل.. استحالَ ألا يتحرَّكَ؛ إِذْ يلزمُ منهُ قيام حركة بجسم وهو ساكن بحيّزه، وفي ذلك إبطال حقيقة الحركة؛ إذ الحركة لا بد فيها مِنْ تفريغ وإشغال، فاشتراطه زيادة حركة في جهة التصعدِ على ما به يتحرَّكُ إلى سائر الجهات.. اشتراط لما يتحقَّقُ المشروط بدونِهِ، وذلك ينافي حقيقة الشرطِ.
As for when we affirm the true doctrine — which is the abolition of the principle of generation (tawallud) and the attribution of all possibilities initially to God — then there is no problem. But if we grant it dialectically, then the doctrine of the early ones in the first issue is invalidated by what Abu Hashim mentioned concerning it, and what Abu Hashim held is invalidated by the fact that it involves the conjunction of two similars (ijtima' al-mithlayn); because he said: there must be additional movements, and that is impossible. (Even) if we grant the permissibility of the conjunction of two similars, still one says to him: when the lifter generates one movement in this heavy object, it is impossible for it not to move; because it would necessitate a movement subsisting in a body while it is stationary in its place, and that invalidates the reality of movement; for movement necessarily involves emptying and occupying. So his requiring an additional movement in the upward direction beyond that by which it moves to other directions is a requirement of that without which the conditioned is realized, and that contradicts the true nature of a condition.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite views on generating movement when lifting heavy objects. Sanusi first states the true Sunni doctrine: abolishing the principle of generation (tawallud) entirely and attributing all possibilities directly to God, which avoids any difficulty. Then, for the sake of argument, he grants generation and shows that both Mu'tazilite views are invalid. The ancients are refuted by Abu Hashim's own argument that sideways movement and lifting cannot be caused by the same reliance. Abu Hashim's view is refuted because requiring additional movements leads to the conjunction of two similars, which is impossible according to Sunni theology. Even if one concedes the possibility of such conjunction, a further argument shows that if the lifter generates a single movement in the object, the object must move; otherwise, a movement would exist in a stationary body, which contradicts the definition of movement (as it requires emptying and occupying space). Therefore, Abu Hashim's requirement of extra movements is unnecessary and violates the nature of a condition. Thus, both positions are refuted.

Key Terms
true doctrine (المذهب الحق)
generation (التولد)
conjunction of two similars (اجتماع المثلين)
movement (الحركة)
emptying and occupying (تفريغ وإشغال)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Sanusi's refutation depends on accepting tawallud (generation) as a valid principle.
Correction: Sanusi clearly states that the true doctrine is the abolition of tawallud entirely; the subsequent refutation is only dialectical (jadalan), not a concession of its validity.

Checkpoint
What is the true Sunni doctrine regarding generated actions according to Sanusi?
A) Actions are generated by the agent.
B) All possibilities are directly attributed to God.
C) Some actions are generated, some are direct.
D) Humans create their own actions entirely.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi demonstrates that the Sunni doctrine of direct divine causation avoids the internal contradictions of Mu'tazilite theories of generated movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you lift a heavy object, do you pause to recognize that the very movement is a created accident from God? Or do you credit your own strength and skill? Your answer reveals where your heart truly relies.
?
Sanusi shows that logical contradictions arise if we hold any view that compromises God's direct causality. Are there beliefs in your life—perhaps about 'luck' or 'natural laws'—that implicitly deny God's all-encompassing power? Will you purge them today?
?
If every possibility (including your own actions) is directly created by God, what does that mean for your sense of independence? Can you truly accept that your every move depends on Him, or does pride still cling to imagined autonomy?

The True Doctrine: Rejecting Tawallud
Sanusi states the true doctrine and the nullification of tawallud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the true doctrine of attributing all possibilities directly to Allah and the logical consequence of rejecting the principle of tawallud.

Core Idea
All possibilities are directly from Allah; tawallud is a false principle.

Sanusi begins by stating the correct position: all possible things (mumkināt) are created directly by Allah, without intermediate generated acts. Any concession to the principle of tawallud leads to contradictions. This lesson establishes the foundational stance of Ahl al-Sunna on causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of Sanusi's statement of the true doctrine.
Pay attention to the key phrase 'إبطال أصل التولُّدِ وإسنادُ الممكناتِ كلّها ابتداءً إلى الله تعالى'.
2
Understand the true doctrine
The true doctrine is to completely reject tawallud and attribute all contingent things directly to Allah.
3
Grasp the hypothetical concession
Sanusi concedes tawallud for argument but shows it nullifies both older and Abu Hashim's views.
This sets up the refutation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage warns that departing from truth leads to bewilderment.
This is a recurring theme.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزء والشَّرْكةُ حاصلة ! وهذا مذهب معظم المعتزلة ، وكلام جميعهم في المسألتين باطل. أما إذا قلنا بالمذهب الحقِّ ـ وهو إبطال أصل التولُّدِ ، وإسنادُ الممكناتِ كلّها ابتداءً إلى الله تعالى - فلا إشكال ، وإِنْ سلَّمناه جدلاً فيبطل مذهبُ الأقدمين في المسألة الأولى بما ذكر أبو هاشم فيها ، ويبطل ما ذهب إليهِ أبو هاشم بأنَّ فيهِ اجتماع المثلين ؛ لقوله : لا بدَّ من زيادة حركاتٍ ، وهو محال(۱). سلَّمنا جواز اجتماع المثلين ، لكن يُقالُ لَهُ : إِذا وَلَّدَ الرافع حركة واحدةً في هذا الثقيل . . استحالَ ألا يتحرَّكَ ؛ إِذْ يلزمُ منهُ قيام حركة بجسم وهو ساكن بحيّزه ، وفي ذلك إبطال حقيقة الحركة ؛ إذ الحركة لا بد فيها مِنْ تفريغ وإشغال ، فاشتراطه زيادة حركة في جهة التصعدِ على ما به يتحرَّكُ إلى سائر الجهات .. اشتراط لما يتحقَّقُ المشروط بدونِهِ ، وذلك ينافي حقيقة الشرطِ (۲). وأمَّا اختلافهم في المسألة الثانية في الجماعة إذا حملوا ثقيلاً وكلُّ واحدٍ منهم يستقل بحملِهِ : فقد قيل لعبَّادِ الصيمري القائل بالقول الأَوَّلِ فيها : الجزء الذي يختص بهِ بعضُ الحاملينَ مُعيَّنٌ أو مُبهم ؟ وارتفاع الجزء المبهم محال ، وهو ظاهر ، وارتفاع الجزء المعيَّن أيضاً محالٌ ؛ إذْ ليس تعيين جزء بأَولى مِنْ جزءٍ ، والفَرْضُ أنَّ هذا الحامل إذا كانَ بانفراده يستقل بالحمل لجميع الأجزاء ، فما وجه انفراده بجزء دون جزء ؟ فقال (۱) : لا أعرفُ وجْهَ الاختصاص ، وهـذه حيرة نشأت مِنَ التمسُّكِ في أصل التولدِ بمحض التوهمات الفاسدة (٢). قيل للآخرين القائلين بالقول الثاني : عينُ ما تولَّدَ مِنْ فعل أحدِ الحاملين تولَّدَ مِنَ الآخر أم لا ؟ فإنْ كانَ الأَوَّلُ لزم وقوع أثر واحدٍ مِنْ مؤثرين ، وهو محال ، وإنْ كانَ الثاني فارتفاع الجسم قد حصل بأحدهما ، ولزم أن يكون الزائد لا فائدة له. وبالجملة : فالخروج عن الحقِّ ، وتحكيم الأوهام والخيالات .. يؤدي إلى أنواع مِنَ الحيرة والفسادِ لا حصر لها ، والله سبحانه الهادي مَنْ يشاء إلى صراط مستقيم. (1) لأن اجتماع المثلين مفض إلى اجتماع الضدين ؛ لجواز عدم أحدهما ، فيكون قد اجتمع المثل ولا مثل في محل واحد (2) والحاصل : أن الرافع للثقيل إذا ولد فيه حركةً من رفعه له .. وجب أن يتحرك بتلك الحركة إلى أي جهة كانت حتى لجهة العلو ، فلزم من ذلك أنه يتحقق تصعده وارتفاعه بتلك الحركة ، فاشتراطه في تصعده وارتفاعه لحركة أخرى اشتراط لما لا يتوقف عليه المشروط . ( حامدي » ( ص ۳۷۲ ) . (1) أي : عباد الصيمري . « عكاري » ( ق ۲۹۸ ) (2) انظر ) شرح الإرشاد » ( ص ٣٩٤ ) ، و ( حاشية المنجور » ( ص ۱۹۱ ) .
A part and partnership is established! This is the doctrine of most Mu'tazilites, and all their speech in the two issues is false. But if we say the true doctrine—which is the nullification of the principle of generation (tawallud), and the attribution of all possibilities (mumkināt) initially to Allah Most High—then there is no problem. And if we concede it hypothetically, then the doctrine of the predecessors in the first issue is nullified by what Abu Hashim mentioned concerning it, and what Abu Hashim held is nullified because it involves the conjunction of two similar things (ijtimā' al-mithlayn); for his statement: 'There must be an increase of movements' is impossible (muḥāl). We concede the permissibility of two similars conjoining, but it is said to him: If the lifter generates one movement in this heavy thing, it is impossible that it does not move; for it would necessitate the existence of a movement in a body while it is stationary in its space, and that involves the nullification of the reality of movement; for movement necessarily involves vacating and occupying. So his condition of an additional movement in the direction of ascent beyond that by which it moves to other directions is a condition for that which is realized without the condition, and that contradicts the reality of a condition. As for their disagreement in the second issue concerning the group when they carry a heavy thing and each one of them is independent in carrying it: It was said to 'Abbād al-Ṣaymarī, who holds the first opinion on it: Is the part (juz') that belongs specifically to some of the carriers determinate or indeterminate? The raising of the indeterminate part is impossible, and that is apparent. And the raising of the determinate part is also impossible, because no one part is more entitled to determination than another, and the assumption is that this carrier, when alone, is independent in carrying all the parts, so what is the reason for its singling out a part to the exclusion of others? He said: I do not know the reason for the specification, and this is bewilderment arising from clinging to the principle of generation (tawallud) through mere corrupt fantasies. It was said to the others who hold the second opinion: Is the very thing that was generated from the act of one of the carriers also generated from the other, or not? If the first, then it necessitates the occurrence of one effect from two agents, and that is impossible. If the second, then the raising of the body has been accomplished by one of them, and it necessitates that the extra action is of no benefit. In sum: Departing from the truth and giving authority to fancies and illusions leads to types of bewilderment and corruption without limit. And Allah, Exalted, guides whom He wills to a straight path. (1) Because the conjunction of two similars leads to the conjunction of two opposites; due to the possibility of the absence of one of them, so the similar would have been conjoined with no similar in one place. (2) The conclusion: That the lifter of a heavy thing, if he generates a movement in it from his lifting it, it is necessary that it moves by that movement to any direction whatever, even to the direction of height. So it follows that its ascent and elevation are realized by that movement. Therefore, his conditioning in its ascent and elevation another movement is a condition for that which is not contingent upon the condition. (al-Ḥāmidī, p. 372). (1) i.e., 'Abbād al-Ṣaymarī. ('Akkārī, folio 298) (2) See: Sharḥ al-Irshād, p. 394, and Ḥāshiyat al-Manjūr, p. 191.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud (generated acts). The author begins by stating that the true doctrine (al-madhab al-ḥaqq) is to reject the entire principle of tawallud and to attribute all possible things (mumkināt) directly to Allah from the outset. If this true view is accepted, there is no problem. Then, for the sake of argument (jadalan), the author hypothetically concedes the principle of tawallud to show that even then the Mu'tazilite views are self-contradictory. First, the older Mu'tazilite view is nullified by the argument of Abū Hāshim, and Abū Hāshim's own view is nullified because it entails the conjunction of two similars (ijtimā' al-mithlayn)—he requires an increase of movements, which is impossible. The author then further concedes the possibility of two similars conjoining, but raises an objection: if the lifter generates one movement in the heavy object, the object must move by that movement; it cannot remain stationary. Requiring an additional movement in the upward direction beyond that which already moves it is a condition for something that can be realized without that condition, thus contradicting the nature of a condition. Next, the author discusses the second issue: a group carrying a heavy object where each individual could carry it alone. The question put to 'Abbād al-Ṣaymarī is whether the part specific to each carrier is determinate or indeterminate. Both options lead to absurdity: an indeterminate part cannot be raised, and a determinate part has no basis for specification since all parts are equal. 'Abbād admits he does not know the reason for specification, showing that this bewilderment stems from clinging to tawallud through corrupt fantasies. Then, the author addresses those holding the second opinion: ask them whether the same generated effect comes from one carrier or from the other. If the same effect comes from both, that would be one effect from two agents, which is impossible. If the effect comes from only one, then the other's action is superfluous. The passage concludes by warning that departing from the truth and following fancies leads to endless bewilderment and corruption, and Allah guides whom He wills.

Key Terms
تولد (tawallud)
اجتماع المثلين (ijtimā' al-mithlayn)
ممكن (mumkin)
مؤثر (mu'aththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud is a rational necessity.
Correction: The passage shows it leads to logical absurdities even on its own terms.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the true doctrine (al-madhab al-ḥaqq) regarding the generation of acts?
A) Acts are generated from other acts via natural causation.
B) All possible things are directly attributed to Allah from the beginning.
C) Some acts are generated, others are directly from Allah.
D) The lifter generates movement in the lifted object.

Key Takeaway
The true doctrine is to reject tawallud completely and ascribe all contingent things directly to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a success or a failure, do you instinctively trace it to a human cause (your effort, someone else's action) or directly to Allah's creation? Which is more real to you in that moment? Will you train yourself to say 'Allah created this' even when a visible cause is present?
?
The passage says all possibilities are initially from Allah. Is there a 'possibility' in your life today—a need, a hope, a fear—that you treat as though it is outside Allah's direct control? What would change if you truly believed He creates every outcome without any generated intermediate?
?
If tawallud is false, then you cannot claim that your actions generate results by themselves. How does this reshape your understanding of reliance on Allah? Are you willing to let go of the illusion that your efforts produce outcomes independently, and instead see every effect as a direct gift from Him?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Condition Argument
Sanusi refutes the condition for additional movement in ascent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the Mu'tazilite condition that an additional movement is required for a lifted object to ascend.

Core Idea
If a movement is generated, the object must move; an extra condition contradicts the nature of a condition.

Sanusi continues his hypothetical concession by addressing the specific argument about the lifter and the heavy object. He shows that requiring an additional movement for ascent is self-contradictory because the generated movement itself should cause motion in any direction. This lesson unpacks the notion of a condition (shart) and why the Mu'tazilite condition fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the scenario of the lifter generating one movement.
2
Understand the scenario
The lifter generates one movement in the heavy object; Sanusi argues it must cause motion.
Contrast with the claim that an extra movement for ascent is needed.
3
Grasp the logical objection
If the object moves by the generated movement, it cannot remain stationary. Requiring an extra movement makes a condition for something already realized.
This violates the definition of a condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزء والشَّرْكةُ حاصلة ! وهذا مذهب معظم المعتزلة ، وكلام جميعهم في المسألتين باطل. أما إذا قلنا بالمذهب الحقِّ ـ وهو إبطال أصل التولُّدِ ، وإسنادُ الممكناتِ كلّها ابتداءً إلى الله تعالى - فلا إشكال ، وإِنْ سلَّمناه جدلاً فيبطل مذهبُ الأقدمين في المسألة الأولى بما ذكر أبو هاشم فيها ، ويبطل ما ذهب إليهِ أبو هاشم بأنَّ فيهِ اجتماع المثلين ؛ لقوله : لا بدَّ من زيادة حركاتٍ ، وهو محال(۱). سلَّمنا جواز اجتماع المثلين ، لكن يُقالُ لَهُ : إِذا وَلَّدَ الرافع حركة واحدةً في هذا الثقيل . . استحالَ ألا يتحرَّكَ ؛ إِذْ يلزمُ منهُ قيام حركة بجسم وهو ساكن بحيّزه ، وفي ذلك إبطال حقيقة الحركة ؛ إذ الحركة لا بد فيها مِنْ تفريغ وإشغال ، فاشتراطه زيادة حركة في جهة التصعدِ على ما به يتحرَّكُ إلى سائر الجهات .. اشتراط لما يتحقَّقُ المشروط بدونِهِ ، وذلك ينافي حقيقة الشرطِ (۲). وأمَّا اختلافهم في المسألة الثانية في الجماعة إذا حملوا ثقيلاً وكلُّ واحدٍ منهم يستقل بحملِهِ : فقد قيل لعبَّادِ الصيمري القائل بالقول الأَوَّلِ فيها : الجزء الذي يختص بهِ بعضُ الحاملينَ مُعيَّنٌ أو مُبهم ؟ وارتفاع الجزء المبهم محال ، وهو ظاهر ، وارتفاع الجزء المعيَّن أيضاً محالٌ ؛ إذْ ليس تعيين جزء بأَولى مِنْ جزءٍ ، والفَرْضُ أنَّ هذا الحامل إذا كانَ بانفراده يستقل بالحمل لجميع الأجزاء ، فما وجه انفراده بجزء دون جزء ؟ فقال (۱) : لا أعرفُ وجْهَ الاختصاص ، وهـذه حيرة نشأت مِنَ التمسُّكِ في أصل التولدِ بمحض التوهمات الفاسدة (٢). قيل للآخرين القائلين بالقول الثاني : عينُ ما تولَّدَ مِنْ فعل أحدِ الحاملين تولَّدَ مِنَ الآخر أم لا ؟ فإنْ كانَ الأَوَّلُ لزم وقوع أثر واحدٍ مِنْ مؤثرين ، وهو محال ، وإنْ كانَ الثاني فارتفاع الجسم قد حصل بأحدهما ، ولزم أن يكون الزائد لا فائدة له. وبالجملة : فالخروج عن الحقِّ ، وتحكيم الأوهام والخيالات .. يؤدي إلى أنواع مِنَ الحيرة والفسادِ لا حصر لها ، والله سبحانه الهادي مَنْ يشاء إلى صراط مستقيم. (1) لأن اجتماع المثلين مفض إلى اجتماع الضدين ؛ لجواز عدم أحدهما ، فيكون قد اجتمع المثل ولا مثل في محل واحد (2) والحاصل : أن الرافع للثقيل إذا ولد فيه حركةً من رفعه له .. وجب أن يتحرك بتلك الحركة إلى أي جهة كانت حتى لجهة العلو ، فلزم من ذلك أنه يتحقق تصعده وارتفاعه بتلك الحركة ، فاشتراطه في تصعده وارتفاعه لحركة أخرى اشتراط لما لا يتوقف عليه المشروط . ( حامدي » ( ص ۳۷۲ ) . (1) أي : عباد الصيمري . « عكاري » ( ق ۲۹۸ ) (2) انظر ) شرح الإرشاد » ( ص ٣٩٤ ) ، و ( حاشية المنجور » ( ص ۱۹۱ ) .
A part and partnership is established! This is the doctrine of most Mu'tazilites, and all their speech in the two issues is false. But if we say the true doctrine—which is the nullification of the principle of generation (tawallud), and the attribution of all possibilities (mumkināt) initially to Allah Most High—then there is no problem. And if we concede it hypothetically, then the doctrine of the predecessors in the first issue is nullified by what Abu Hashim mentioned concerning it, and what Abu Hashim held is nullified because it involves the conjunction of two similar things (ijtimā' al-mithlayn); for his statement: 'There must be an increase of movements' is impossible (muḥāl). We concede the permissibility of two similars conjoining, but it is said to him: If the lifter generates one movement in this heavy thing, it is impossible that it does not move; for it would necessitate the existence of a movement in a body while it is stationary in its space, and that involves the nullification of the reality of movement; for movement necessarily involves vacating and occupying. So his condition of an additional movement in the direction of ascent beyond that by which it moves to other directions is a condition for that which is realized without the condition, and that contradicts the reality of a condition. As for their disagreement in the second issue concerning the group when they carry a heavy thing and each one of them is independent in carrying it: It was said to 'Abbād al-Ṣaymarī, who holds the first opinion on it: Is the part (juz') that belongs specifically to some of the carriers determinate or indeterminate? The raising of the indeterminate part is impossible, and that is apparent. And the raising of the determinate part is also impossible, because no one part is more entitled to determination than another, and the assumption is that this carrier, when alone, is independent in carrying all the parts, so what is the reason for its singling out a part to the exclusion of others? He said: I do not know the reason for the specification, and this is bewilderment arising from clinging to the principle of generation (tawallud) through mere corrupt fantasies. It was said to the others who hold the second opinion: Is the very thing that was generated from the act of one of the carriers also generated from the other, or not? If the first, then it necessitates the occurrence of one effect from two agents, and that is impossible. If the second, then the raising of the body has been accomplished by one of them, and it necessitates that the extra action is of no benefit. In sum: Departing from the truth and giving authority to fancies and illusions leads to types of bewilderment and corruption without limit. And Allah, Exalted, guides whom He wills to a straight path. (1) Because the conjunction of two similars leads to the conjunction of two opposites; due to the possibility of the absence of one of them, so the similar would have been conjoined with no similar in one place. (2) The conclusion: That the lifter of a heavy thing, if he generates a movement in it from his lifting it, it is necessary that it moves by that movement to any direction whatever, even to the direction of height. So it follows that its ascent and elevation are realized by that movement. Therefore, his conditioning in its ascent and elevation another movement is a condition for that which is not contingent upon the condition. (al-Ḥāmidī, p. 372). (1) i.e., 'Abbād al-Ṣaymarī. ('Akkārī, folio 298) (2) See: Sharḥ al-Irshād, p. 394, and Ḥāshiyat al-Manjūr, p. 191.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite theory of tawallud (generated acts). The author begins by stating that the true doctrine (al-madhab al-ḥaqq) is to reject the entire principle of tawallud and to attribute all possible things (mumkināt) directly to Allah from the outset. If this true view is accepted, there is no problem. Then, for the sake of argument (jadalan), the author hypothetically concedes the principle of tawallud to show that even then the Mu'tazilite views are self-contradictory. First, the older Mu'tazilite view is nullified by the argument of Abū Hāshim, and Abū Hāshim's own view is nullified because it entails the conjunction of two similars (ijtimā' al-mithlayn)—he requires an increase of movements, which is impossible. The author then further concedes the possibility of two similars conjoining, but raises an objection: if the lifter generates one movement in the heavy object, the object must move by that movement; it cannot remain stationary. Requiring an additional movement in the upward direction beyond that which already moves it is a condition for something that can be realized without that condition, thus contradicting the nature of a condition. Next, the author discusses the second issue: a group carrying a heavy object where each individual could carry it alone. The question put to 'Abbād al-Ṣaymarī is whether the part specific to each carrier is determinate or indeterminate. Both options lead to absurdity: an indeterminate part cannot be raised, and a determinate part has no basis for specification since all parts are equal. 'Abbād admits he does not know the reason for specification, showing that this bewilderment stems from clinging to tawallud through corrupt fantasies. Then, the author addresses those holding the second opinion: ask them whether the same generated effect comes from one carrier or from the other. If the same effect comes from both, that would be one effect from two agents, which is impossible. If the effect comes from only one, then the other's action is superfluous. The passage concludes by warning that departing from the truth and following fancies leads to endless bewilderment and corruption, and Allah guides whom He wills.

Key Terms
تولد (tawallud)
حركة (ḥaraka)
شرط (sharṭ)
تفريغ وإشغال (tafrīgh wa-ishghāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a lifter must generate an additional movement for the object to rise.
Correction: The generated movement itself suffices; an extra condition is invalid.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue about the generated movement in the heavy object?
A) It is not sufficient for ascent.
B) It necessitates the object moves, contradicting any extra condition.
C) It requires an additional movement for each direction.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
A condition for something already realized is invalid; thus the Mu'tazilite condition of extra movement fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a promise to Allah, do you ever add extra conditions to it, thinking your promise is incomplete without them? This lesson shows such conditions contradict the reality of the promise. Will you repent from that habit today?
?
Consider an act of worship you perform. Do you believe it is valid only if you feel a certain emotion or meet a personal standard? That is like the Mu'tazilite condition. Are you willing to accept Allah's account?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's decree is complete, would you still add conditions to your trust in Him? What condition are you clinging to right now that you need to drop?

The Problem of Multiple Agents and Indeterminate Parts
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazilite views on multiple carriers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical problems that arise when multiple agents each independently capable of an act are said to generate a shared effect.

Core Idea
If each carrier can carry alone, specifying a part or effect leads to absurdities.

The final part of Sanusi's refutation deals with a scenario where several people carry a heavy object, each strong enough to carry it alone. The Mu'tazilites debated whether the generated effect comes from all or just one. Sanusi shows that both options lead to contradiction, revealing the bankruptcy of the tawallud theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation about the group carrying a heavy object.
Notice the two opinions presented.
2
Understand the first opinion
Each carrier causes a specific part of the lifting. Sanusi asks: is that part determinate or indeterminate?
Both options are impossible.
3
Understand the second opinion
A single generated act from all carriers. Sanusi asks: is the generated effect the same from both or different?
Both options lead to impossibility or uselessness.
4
Reflect on the moral
Sanusi concludes that departing from truth leads to endless bewilderment.
This reinforces the true doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Requiring an additional movement in the upward direction beyond that which already moves it is a condition for something that can be realized without that condition, thus contradicting the nature of a condition. Next, the author discusses the second issue: a group carrying a heavy object where each individual could carry it alone. The question put to 'Abbād al-Ṣaymarī is whether the part specific to each carrier is determinate or indeterminate. Both options lead to absurdity: an indeterminate part cannot be raised, and a determinate part has no basis for specification since all parts are equal. 'Abbād admits he does not know the reason for specification, showing that this bewilderment stems from clinging to tawallud through corrupt fantasies. Then, the author addresses those holding the second opinion: ask them whether the same generated effect comes from one carrier or from the other. If the same effect comes from both, that would be one effect from two agents, which is impossible. If the effect comes from only one, then the other's action is superfluous. The passage concludes by warning that departing from the truth and following fancies leads to endless bewilderment and corruption, and Allah guides whom He wills.

Key Terms
جزء (juz')
مُعيَّن (muʿayyan)
مُبهم (mubham)
أثر (athar)
مؤثر (mu'aththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a single effect can be produced by multiple agents together.
Correction: The passage shows it leads to impossibility or redundancy.

Checkpoint
What is the problem with the first opinion that each carrier causes a specific part?
A) The part must be indeterminate.
B) The part must be determinate but there is no reason for specification.
C) Both A and B lead to impossibility.
D) The carriers cannot all be independent.

Key Takeaway
The theory of tawallud leads to absurdities when applied to multiple agents; only direct divine causation is coherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life have you been insisting on a 'system' of thought or action that, when pressed, leads to bewilderness and confusion? Will you let go of that illusion today?
?
Sanusi shows that straying from truth gives authority to fantasies. Is there a belief you hold or a habit you defend that, honestly, is based more on your own whims than on clear truth? What would it cost you to surrender it?
?
The extra carrier's action was 'of no benefit' if the effect was already caused. Are you adding 'extra actions' to your life—busyness, distractions, routines—that, in light of Allah's sufficiency, are actually futile? Name one thing you can stop doing this week to focus on what truly matters.
