Refuting Specification in Generation Theory
Sanusi refutes an objection about specifying a part to one carrier in generation theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible to specify a part of a generated effect to one carrier, and how this leads to a dilemma for proponents of divided generation.

Core Idea
Specifying a part to one carrier is impossible because no reason can justify it.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses an objection against those who claim that when two agents generate a motion, the motion is divided among them. He presents a logical dilemma: either the part specified is ambiguous (which cannot exist) or determined (which cannot be justified). He then extends the argument to show that the entire theory leads to absurd consequences. Let's read the passage closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once, noting the structure: objection, answer, and concluding remark.
Notice the use of rhetorical questions.
2
Identify the two dilemmas
The passage presents two separate arguments: first about the part (determined vs ambiguous), second about the generated effect (same or different).
Each dilemma is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Analyze the impossibility
Understand why both horns of each dilemma lead to contradiction. For the first, neither ambiguous nor determined part is possible. For the second, both options (same effect or different) are impossible.
This shows the flaw in the theory of divided generation.
4
Examine the author's conclusion
Sanusi states that such confusion arises from clinging to corrupt imaginations and abandoning truth.
He attributes the opponent's ignorance to a flawed method.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how kalam uses logical dilemmas to defend the principle that a single effect cannot have multiple true agents.
This reinforces the unity of causal agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأَوَّلِ فيها : الجزء الذي يختص بهِ بعضُ الحاملينَ مُعيَّنٌ أو مُبهم ؟ وارتفاع الجزء المبهم محال ، وهو ظاهر ، وارتفاع الجزء المعيَّن أيضاً محالٌ ؛ إذْ ليس تعيين جزء بأَولى مِنْ جزءٍ ، والفَرْضُ أنَّ هذا الحامل إذا كانَ بانفراده يستقل بالحمل لجميع الأجزاء ، فما وجه انفراده بجزء دون جزء ؟ فقال (۱) : لا أعرفُ وجْهَ الاختصاص ، وهـذه حيرة نشأت مِنَ التمسُّكِ في أصل التولدِ بمحض التوهمات الفاسدة. قيل للآخرين القائلين بالقول الثاني : عينُ ما تولَّدَ مِنْ فعل أحدِ الحاملين تولّدَ مِنَ الآخر أم لا ؟ فإنْ كانَ الأَوَّلُ لزم وقوع أثر واحدٍ مِنْ مؤثرين ، وهو محال ، وإنْ كانَ الثاني فارتفاع الجسم قد حصل بأحدهما ، ولزم أن يكون الزائد لا فائدة له. وبالجملة : فالخروج عن الحقِّ ، وتحكيم الأوهام والخيالات .. يؤدي إلى أنواع مِنَ الحيرة والفسادِ لا حصر لها ، والله سبحانه الهادي مَنْ يشاء إلى صراط مستقيم
The first of them: is the part that is specific to some carriers determined or ambiguous? And the removal of the ambiguous is impossible, which is apparent, and the removal of the determined is also impossible, since no part is more deserving than another part, and the assumption is that this carrier, if alone, is sufficient to carry all the parts, so what is its being alone with a part without another? So he said (1): I do not know the aspect of specification, and this is confusion that arose from clinging in the principle of generation to mere corrupt imaginations. It was said to the others who hold the second saying: that which is generated from the action of one of the two carriers is generated from the other or not? If it is the first, then the occurrence of a single effect from two agents is necessary, which is impossible. And if it is the second, then the removal of the body has occurred by one of them, and it follows that the additional one has no benefit. And in summary: leaving the truth and giving authority to whims and imaginations leads to types of confusion and corruption without limit, and Allah is the Guide to whom He wills to a straight path.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer within a larger discussion on the theory of generation (tawallud). Sanusi presents a dilemma for those who claim that when two carriers (agents) generate a motion in a body, the motion is divided among them. The objection asks: Is the part that is specific to one of the carriers determined or ambiguous? If it is ambiguous, its removal (non-existence) is impossible because an ambiguous part cannot be the specific effect. If it is determined, then why is that particular part chosen over any other? Since each carrier alone can carry all parts, there is no reason for specifying one part to one carrier. The opponent (here, 'Abbad al-Saymari) admits ignorance, which Sanusi says stems from reliance on corrupt imaginations. Then Sanusi presents a second argument against the other opinion (that the effect is divided): either the same generated thing comes from both or different things. If the same, then one effect from two agents, which is impossible. If different, then one agent suffices and the other is redundant. The conclusion is that abandoning truth for whims leads to endless confusion.

Key Terms
حامل
تولد
جزء
اختصاص
معين
مبهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that specifying a part to one carrier is arbitrary; the argument shows it is impossible because no preference exists.
Correction: The argument demonstrates that specification cannot be arbitrary—there must be a differentiating factor, but none exists when carriers are equally capable. Hence, the theory is incoherent.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why is specifying a determined part to one carrier impossible?
A) Because the part is ambiguous and cannot exist
B) Because no part is more deserving than another, and no reason for preference exists
C) Because the carrier cannot bear any part alone
D) Because the effect must come from both carriers equally

Key Takeaway
The attempt to divide a generated effect among multiple agents leads to logical contradictions, affirming that a single effect can only have one true cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this passage, the opponent admits ignorance because he cannot justify his arbitrary specification. When you hold a belief without evidence, what would it take for you to admit that you are relying on 'corrupt imaginations'?
?
The argument shows that there is no reason to prefer one part over another. Think of a decision you made recently where you chose one option over another for no clear reason. How does this principle challenge you to demand valid reasons before committing?
?
Sanusi warns that abandoning truth for whims leads to endless confusion. Are there areas in your life—your thoughts, habits, or relationships—where you have prioritized your own desires over objective truth? What one step can you take today to submit to the truth, even if it is uncomfortable?

Responding to the Geometrical Objection on Perceiving Large Objects as Small
Sanusi presents a geometrical objection about vision and the reply of Ahl al-Haqq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ahl al-Haqq refuted the geometrical objection that large objects appear small due to unequal distances of their parts from the eye.

Core Idea
Distance alone does not determine visibility; observation disproves the geometrical argument.

The opponents used a geometrical triangle argument to explain why a large object can appear small. They claimed that the parts of the object are at different distances from the eye, so only some parts are seen at full size. The people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) responded with a simple observation: if the object were moved to the farther distance, it would become invisible by their logic, but in reality it remains visible. This lesson presents the objection and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the geometrical objection
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi explains how the triangle is formed and why the endpoints are farther than the middle, causing the object to appear smaller.
Pay attention to the terms: right angle, hypotenuse, base.
2
Learn the response of Ahl al-Haqq
Read the second part where they argue using the same logic: if the object were moved to the distance of its endpoints, it should become invisible, but observation shows otherwise.
This refutation uses reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this objection fails and what it teaches about causation and observation.
This connects to the broader topic of causality in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بوسط تلك القاعدة ؛ فإنَّهُ تحدثُ فيهِ زاويتان قائمتانِ ، ويكون كلُّ واحدٍ مِنَ الخطينِ الواقعين على الطرفين وتراً للزاوية القائمة ، وقد تبيَّن في الهندسة أنَّ وتر الزاوية القائمة التي في المثلَّثِ أطولُ مِنْ كُلِّ واحدٍ مِنَ الخطّينِ المحيطين بها ، فالخطَّانِ الواقعانِ على الطرفين أطولُ مِنَ الخط الواقع على وسط الجسم المرئي ، فتكون الأجزاء التي وقعَ عليها الطرفانِ أبعد عن البصرِ مِنَ الأجزاء التي يقع عليها الخطّ الأوسط ، فنسبة الأجزاء إذاً ليسَتْ متساوية في القرب والبعدِ ، فلذلك صح أن يُرى بعضُ الجسمِ دونَ بعض ، فيُرى الكبير صغيراً ، وهذهِ صورة المثلثِ : الرائي. الوتر الأيمن قاعدة المرئي الوتر الأيسر. أجابَهم أهلُ الحقِّ رضيَ اللهُ تعالى عنهم : بأنَّهُ إذا كان البعد الحاصل بين المرئي والناظرِ مئة ذراع مثلاً ، والذي بين طرفيه زائداً على المئة قدر ذراع .. فكان يجب - إذا انتقل ذلك الجسم الكبير الذي رئي صغيراً إلى مسافة الطرفين ؛ وهي مئة ذراع وذراع - ألا يُرى ألبتة كما لم يُرَ الطرفان الكائنانِ في تلك المسافة ، لكنَّ المشاهدة تُكَذِّبُ ذلك ، فبطل ما ذكروه .
By means of that rule, two right angles occur in it, and each of the two lines lying on the two extremities becomes a hypotenuse for the right angle. It has been shown in geometry that the hypotenuse of the right angle in the triangle is longer than each of the two lines surrounding it. Thus the two lines lying on the two extremities are longer than the line lying on the middle of the visible body. So the parts on which the two extremities fall are farther from the eye than the parts on which the middle line falls. Therefore the ratio of the parts is not equal in nearness and distance, so it is valid that part of the body is seen without another, so the large is seen small. This is the form of the triangle: the viewer, the right hypotenuse, the base of the visible, the left hypotenuse. The people of truth, may Allah be pleased with them, answered them: that if the distance between the visible and the viewer is, for example, one hundred cubits, and the distance between its two endpoints exceeds the hundred by a cubit, then it would be necessary—if that large body which was seen as small were moved to the distance of the two endpoints, which is one hundred and one cubits—that it not be seen at all, just as the two endpoints located at that distance were not seen. But observation contradicts that, so what they mentioned is invalid.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection regarding the perception of large objects as small. The opponents had argued that when a large object appears small, it is because the eye sees it through a triangle where the base is the object and the vertex is the eye. The rays from the eye hit different parts at different distances, causing the parts to appear unequal in size. The response from the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) uses a counterargument: if the object's size reduction were due to the farther parts being at a greater distance (by one cubit), then moving the entire object to that farther distance should make it completely invisible, since the endpoints at that distance are unseen. But experience shows that the object is still visible, thus the original explanation fails. The commentary follows the author's order: first the geometrical setup, then the objection, then the response, and finally the conclusion that the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
الزاوية القائمة
الوتر
القاعدة
الأشعة
المرئي
البعد
الحِسُّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Distance alone determines visibility, so objects appear smaller because their parts are at different distances.
Correction: The Ahl al-Haqq show that if that were true, moving the entire object to the farther distance would make it invisible, but it remains visible, indicating other factors are at play.

Checkpoint
What is the core of the response from Ahl al-Haqq to the geometrical objection about seeing large objects as small?
A) The large object appears small because the rays bend around it.
B) If the object were moved to the distance of its farther parts, it would be invisible, but observation shows it is still visible.
C) The eye can only see a small portion of the object at any time.
D) The triangle formed by the rays is not isosceles.

Key Takeaway
The geometrical objection against perception of large objects as small fails because its own logic would lead to invisibility of the object at a slightly greater distance, contradicting observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you held onto a logical argument or belief even though it contradicted something you directly observed? What made you cling to that argument?
?
The People of Truth used simple observation to overturn a sophisticated geometrical proof. In your own life, what 'self-evident' truths of your faith have you neglected because they don't fit your intellectual framework?
?
If you were faced with a contradiction between a cherished theory and what your senses clearly tell you, would you have the courage to abandon the theory? Give an example from your personal experience where you did or did not do so.

Refuting the Ray-Contact Theory of Vision
Sanusi refutes the refined theory that only objects which receive rays or are connected to them are visible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi dismantles the opponents' theory of vision by showing that accidents and colors are seen without direct ray contact, and why their refined theories still fail.

Core Idea
Seeing motion and rest disproves the claim that vision requires ray contact.

The opponents of kalam argued that vision occurs only when rays from the eye connect with the object. But we see things like motion, rest, and colors that cannot physically contact rays. In this passage, Sanusi systematically dismantles their theory through a series of objections and responses, exposing the flaws in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the back-and-forth structure between the refuter and the opponents.
The passage is a dialectical exchange; note each objection and response.
2
Identify the opponents' original claim
The opponents initially claim that the visible is that to which rays connect. Find this statement and understand its implication.
3
Follow the first refutation
Notice how Sanusi refutes this by pointing out that accidents (like motion and rest) are seen, yet rays cannot connect to them because rays are bodies and accidents cannot touch bodies.
This is a key counterexample.
4
Trace the refined theory
The opponents then refine their claim: the visible is what the ray connects to or what inheres in what it connects to. See how this is still refuted using tastes and smells as examples.
5
Observe the final counterexample
The opponents further restrict to 'things that admit visibility,' but Sanusi brings up the example of a distant object seen without its color, showing the theory still fails.
The passage ends abruptly, but the argument's direction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما ينقص عليهم : رؤية الأكوان مع أنَّ الأشعة لم تتصل بها قالوا : المرئي ما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ ، أو قام بما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ قلنا : فيلزم أنْ تُرى الطعوم والروائح ؛ لقيامها بما اتَّصلَتْ بهِ . قالوا : إنَّما ذلك فيما يقبلُ الرؤية . قلنا : ها هو البعيدُ يُرى دونَ لونِهِ . هذا ممَّا نقض عليهم قولَهم بأنَّ سببَ الرؤيةِ اتصال الأشعة بالمرئي ، وأنَّهُ إِنَّما يُرى ما اتصلَ بهِ الشعاع ، وهذا قول الأقدمينَ منهم ، فقيل لهم : قد رُئِيَتِ الأكوان - وهي : الحركة والسكون ، والاجتماع والافتراقُ - والألوان ، والأشعة لا تتصل بها ؛ إذ الأشعة أجسام ، والعرضُ يستحيلُ عليه مماسَّةُ الأجسامِ . فأجابوا بالرجوع عن قولهمُ الأوَّلِ ، وقالوا : المرئي ما اتَّصلَ بهِ الشعاع أو قام بما اتَّصلَ بهِ الشعاع ، فقيل لهم : فيلزم أن تُرى الطعوم والروائح ؛ لأنَّها قائمة بما اتَّصلَ بهِ الشعاعُ ، فقالوا : إِنَّما نقولُ : ما قام بما اتَّصلَ بهِ الشعاعُ يُرى إذا كانَ ممَّا تجوزُ رؤيتُهُ وهذا الذي أوردتُم .. عندنا لا تجوزُ رؤيتُهُ ، فقيل لهم : فالجسم إذا
And among what refutes them: the seeing of accidents (akwan) even though the rays did not connect to them. They said: 'The visible is that to which the rays connect, or that which inheres in that to which they connect.' We said: 'Then it would be necessary that tastes and smells be seen, because they inhere in that to which the rays connect.' They said: 'That is only for things that admit visibility.' We said: 'Here is the distant object seen without its color.' This is part of what invalidates their statement that the cause of vision is the connection of the rays to the visible, and that only what the ray connects to is seen—this is the statement of their early ones. So it was said to them: 'Accidents—which are motion, rest, conjunction, and separation—and colors have been seen, but the rays do not connect to them, because rays are bodies, and it is impossible for an accident to touch bodies.' So they answered by retracting their first statement, and said: 'The visible is that to which the ray connects or that which inheres in that to which the ray connects.' So it was said to them: 'Then it would be necessary that tastes and smells be seen, because they inhere in that to which the ray connects.' So they said: 'We rather say: what inheres in that to which the ray connects is seen if it is among things whose vision is possible. And that which you have cited... according to us, its vision is not possible.' So it was said to them: 'Then if a body...'
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and responses concerning the theory of vision based on rays emanating from the eye. The opponents initially claimed that only objects directly contacted by rays are visible. The refutation points out that accidents like motion, rest, conjunction, and separation (al-akwan) and colors are seen, but rays cannot contact them because rays are bodies and accidents cannot touch bodies. The opponents then refined their theory: the visible is either what the ray contacts or what inheres in what the ray contacts. The refutation counters that if that were true, tastes and smells (which inhere in bodies) would be visible. The opponents further restrict to things that 'admit visibility,' but the refutation notes that distant objects are seen without their color, showing the theory still fails. The passage ends with an incomplete question about a body. The commentary follows the author's order: first the objection about accidents, then the opponent's answer, then the objection about tastes/smells, then the opponent's further restriction, then the counterpoint about distant objects and colors, and final partial question.

الروائح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponents believe that vision requires either direct contact of rays or inherence in something that contacts rays.
Correction: The visibility of accidents and colors disproves direct contact, and the visibility of distant objects without their color disproves the inherence condition.

Checkpoint
Why do the opponents' first two theories about vision fail according to this passage?
A) They cannot explain how we see colors and accidents that do not contact the rays.
B) They assume rays are not physical entities.
C) They ignore the role of the brain in processing vision.
D) They rely on geometric proofs that are flawed.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' physicalist theory of vision is falsified by the visibility of accidents and colors, which do not make contact with rays.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see motion or change around you, do you ever pause to realize that you are witnessing something immaterial—an accident—that cannot be captured by physical touch? How does this convict you of relying too much on material explanations for everything?
?
If the opponents' theory failed because it could not admit that we see what is distant without its color, where in your own life do you limit what you believe based on what you can 'touch' or physically verify? Are you willing to accept truths that reason and revelation show, even if they lack sensory proof?
?
The debate shows that even refined human reasoning about how we see falls short. Do you trust that Allah's knowledge encompasses all vision, even before you open your eyes? What would change in your daily gratitude if you truly believed that every sight is a direct act of His creation?
